HEADLINE: No Toll Increases Without Electronic Scanners

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The announced intention of the Triborough Bridge and Tunnel Authority to increase bridge and tunnel tolls (news article, Nov. 17) seems to defy economics. Planners who seek to discourage commuters from driving, seem baffled that frequent toll increases have done little to induce people onto mass transit. 
 The vast majority of commuters drive because of the absence of efficient public transport that will meet their needs. Ineffective planning has left us with a transit system that does not meet our needs. I cite the thousands of people who drive from Queens to the Bronx and the lack of commuter buses on these routes. I cite local bus routes that do not synchronize their schedules with commuter rail lines they can easily serve. I cite countless instances where the costs of motoring, tolls and parking are endured not for pleasure, or even choice, but of necessity.
Perhaps, this time, with clean air regulations about to force action throughout the region, the time has come for a quid pro quo: no toll increases to be approved unless the authority and all toll-collection agencies install electronic scanners that permit toll accounts to be debited without impeding the flow of traffic.
The motoring public is forced to wait in long, time-wasting lines, while vehicles spew pollution into the atmosphere. The bridge and tunnel agency must take the lead to temper its planned toll increase with technology to speed traffic through its facilities.
 
DONALD E. SIMON
Bronx, Nov. 17, 1992

HEADLINE: Doubled Sales;
With That Hair, Barbie Is Looking Like a Billion

BYLINE: By ANDREA ADELSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Nov. 25

 BODY:
The Barbie doll in all her variations will breach the $1 billion sales mark this year, in no small measure because of the popularity of Totally Hair Barbie, the nation's fifth-best-selling toy.
Mattel Inc., which was preoccupied with financial problems in the early 1980's, has more than doubled sales of its perennial favorite since 1987, when Barbie sales were $430 million. 
 John W. Amerman, chairman and chief executive, said today that the company expected record revenue and profits this year, in part because sales of low-price toys have resisted the recession. The company, which will sell 65 million dolls this year, earned $118 million on sales of $1.65 billion in 1991.
Besides the $12 Totally Hair Barbie, projected to sell eight million units, Mattel says it is having trouble keeping in stock Li'l Miss Magic Jewels, priced at $30; Baby Rollerblade, selling for $35, and Gak, a compound that is a cross between Silly Putty and Play-Doh and costs $3.
Mattel items based on characters from Disney's "Aladdin" movie are also disappearing fast.
At the nation's toy stores this month, top sellers are Teenage Mutant Ninja Mutations, California Roller Baby, Nerf bow and arrow sets, and Treasure Trolls, according to The Toy Book's What's Hot List. Rollerblade Barbie is ranked No. 8 and Mattel's Baby Rollerblade is No. 12.
These same toys are expected to remain best-sellers through the holiday season for the $13 billion-a-year industry, said Maria Ramos, editor of the trade magazine.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Mattel expects Barbie to bring more than $1 billion in sales this year. The Happy Holidays Barbie is among this year's models. (Mattel)

HEADLINE: Immigration Reform Holds Key to Economy

 BODY:
To the Editor:
There is a striking omission in "How to Lift the Poor" (news analysis, Nov. 10), your overview of the Clinton Administration's agenda to aid those in need: immigration reform. Over the last decade, the United States has received the largest number of immigrants in our history -- close to 10 million people. In the recessionary year 1991, more than 1.8 million people were granted permanent resident status -- also the highest number in our history.
More than one-third of the annual growth in the labor force comes from this collective source (that is, legal immigrants, illegal immigrants, refugees, asylum seekers and foreign nationals who are legally permitted to be employed as nonimmigrant workers). The preponderance of the immigrant flow has been into urban areas, especially to central cities. 
 This human infusion has been disproportionately characterized by people who are unskilled, poorly educated and often not English-speaking. Not only have many such immigrants added to the magnitude of the country's poverty population, but they have also contributed, through increased competition, to a worsening of employment and income experiences for needy citizens.
What sense does it make to have an inflexible immigration policy that admits record numbers of people who must find jobs to survive without any regard to the state of the domestic economy? What is the justification for a policy that admits (or permits in the case of illegal immigration) mass entry of people whose human capital attributes most closely match those of citizens who are themselves experiencing the highest incidences of poverty and unemployment?
What is the logic of a policy that annually pours hundreds of thousands of such people into the central cities, which are already overburdened with chronic shortages of jobs, training opportunities, health services and affordable housing?
Immigration reform is not a panacea. But unless our immigration policy is changed, any urban programs are doomed to failure that seek to raise the rewards of working for the urban poor; to reduce the magnitude of adult illiteracy; to improve the quality and retention power of urban school systems -- to name only a few laudable goals.
Immigration reform, the omitted option among the urban reforms that you describe as being under discussion, would be the least costly to implement and the quickest to produce beneficial results. Moreover, such action would greatly improve the prospects for other elements of the proposed urban agenda to make a difference in the quality of life in our cities.1
 
VERNON M. BRIGGS JR.
Professor, N.Y.S. School of Industrial
and Labor Relations, Cornell U.
Ithaca, N.Y., Nov. 11, 1992

GRAPHIC: Drawing

HEADLINE: Innovative Program in California Aids Those With Outdated Skills

SERIES: Finding What Works -- Fourth article of a series: Retraining Programs

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SANTA CLARA, Calif.

 BODY:
For almost four years, Mark H. Johnson was a chemical technician for a microfilm company, doing skilled work that paid him a good salary of $30,000 a year. But the company's three shifts were cut to two, and he was on the third. Soon he landed another skilled job paying $27,000, as a high-speed packaging mechanic. But a year and a half later production was cut, and with it Mr. Johnson.
So about five years ago, Mr. Johnson joined the ranks of the underemployed, settling for a series of low-wage jobs because his talents were no longer valued enough to assure him a secure and well-paid position. 
 But now, at 37 years of age, Mr. Johnson has found the right job: machining, a vital craft for manufacturing industries. What rescued him was an innovative retraining program that the State of California offers to tens of thousands of workers each year.
About one million experienced workers nationwide are laid off every year as the economy undergoes a fundamental restructuring and finds itself needing employees with more sophisticated skills. President-elect Bill Clinton has made job creation and training central elements of his plans for economic growth. Among those needing retraining and new jobs are workers with outdated skills like Mr. Johnson.
 
Success in California
 If the nation is to stand up to international competition, experts say, the economy needs more skilled workers. But, they add, most state and Federal programs intended to help those with waning skills are small or ineffective.
California seems to be an exception, as Mr. Johnson and 150,000 other workers have found over the 10 years since the state Legislature established the retraining program.
In 1990, Mr. Johnson went to work for McNeal Enterprises, a thriving machining shop of 25 employees in this recession-worn Silicon Valley town. He started at $8 an hour. Now he makes $11, or $22,000 a year, and he can aspire to $46,000, the salary of McNeal's top machinist.
Machinists make pieces of machines and whole machines that make machines. They fashion the models of industry's new products, along with the tools and molds to produce them. On the job, Mr. Johnson shapes fist-sized chunks of clear plastic into the core components of medical devices, like blood analysis instruments used in testing for AIDS.
"It's good work," he said. "It's clean work. It makes you think. You work with numbers all the time. Degrees. Circumferences. All kinds of numbers. You're actually creating something. You look at a blueprint and make it into what the blueprint calls for."
 
Help for the Skilled
 The California Legislature created the Employment Training Panel for experienced workers like Mr. Johnson, not the chronically unemployed or the unskilled youth that other programs help with more basic training.
Some other states, like New York, Illinois, Maryland, Delaware and Alaska, have retraining agencies, but they are much smaller and less comprehensive. Just this year, New Jersey started a program intended to approach the scope of California's. And through the Job Training Partnership Act, the Federal Government also tries to retrain laid-off workers. But California has achieved the most impressive results, experts say.
The program starts with a hard-nosed assumption that reverses the wisdom of the 1970's and early 80's. It does not try to turn middle-aged blast furnace operators into advanced computer programmers. Some people like Mr. Johnson are taught completely new skills, but only after passing aptitude tests. More often, the program tries to beef up the workers' existing skills long before they lose their jobs.
"Interestingly, the most effective training is for people who are working," said Anthony P. Carnivale, chief economist of the American Society for Training and Development, an organization of training professionals.
 
The Overview
Skills Change With Economy
 As the American economy evolves, there is widespread agreement that if Government wants to create more employment opportunities for everyone, it must do a better job of retraining experienced workers whose skills have grown less valuable. Unlike the old days, when an auto or steel worker knew his abilities would be needed through his working life, now few businesses stand still, and Americans who do not master new skills are at the mercy of foreigners who do.
"We have a rapidly changing economy where workers' skills have been getting out of date," said Evelyn Ganzglass, director of employment and social policy studies at the National Governors Association.
A result is the erosion of the standard of living that Mr. Clinton repeatedly mentioned in his campaign. Workplace skills are not the only problem. Inattentive corporate management, Federal economic policy and the general weakness of the economy affect the nation's competitiveness, too. But the huge deficit in foreign trade is in large part a measure of the deficit in workplace skills. By learning new skills, employees raise their productivity, which means they produce more and better goods and services without working harder.
"We want to step in to train people to be more productive while they are working," Ms. Ganzglass said. "That's our big aim, and the kind of training that works best."
 
Training for 50 Million
 Of 117 million working Americans, it is hard to say how many need retraining, though certainly the one million employees whom waning industries let go each year ought to be counted. Mr. Carnivale also estimates that 50 million workers still on the job need additional training. In factories, machine operators need to master computerized equipment, and office workers need to learn the ever-expanding abilities of their computers.
Government now does little to meet those needs. The largest Federal program, the Job Training Partnership Act, falls well short, experts say, offering no training for employed workers who do not face imminent layoffs and reaching only 30 percent of the one million people who do lose their jobs to plant closings and permanent layoffs each year. For those, it pays for a range of subsidized training programs, job counseling and unemployment benefits.
Assessing the program's performance is difficult. The General Accounting Office found that 71 percent of the workers who entered one part of it in 1990 were in new jobs 90 days later, earning an average of $8.64 an hour. But it could not ascertain how that compared with the pay in their former jobs.
Bob Cook, a public policy analyst in Boston, said the Federal program is most effective when administrators get involved in a company before a plant closes. But Linda G. Morra, director for education and employment issues at the accounting office, said the programs usually do not reach workers until three or four months after they lose their jobs.
 
California
Innovative Project With Possibilities
 It is hard to gauge the extent to which California's program helps the economy. The state is in a deep recession; unemployment stands at 9.4 percent. Nonetheless, in giving people new skills -- about 50,000 are now in retraining programs -- the state may be helping lay the ground for a robust recovery.
Advocates say the California program is an obvious model for the Federal job training program that Mr. Clinton says he will propose. And it does seem to be consistent with the President-elect's promises to go easy on taxpayers and the budget deficit. To pay for its program, California skims off a tenth of one percent of the unemployment insurance premiums that employers pay to support workers who lose their jobs.
The program works, experts say, because of the discipline the state imposes on the companies and training schools that receive its subsidies. The state training panel requires that each trainee land a job using his new skills and hold that job at least 90 days. If not, the state takes back all the money it paid to train him -- even if the trainee dies during the course. Studies show that about 70 percent of the participants fulfill the state's requirements.
"Start bending that rule, and where do you stop?" asked Gerald Geismar, the panel's executive director. The Federal Government sets performance goals for its programs, too, but none are as rigorous.
Cheryl Strecher, president of a consulting firm in Los Angeles that studied the program for the California Legislature, said, "We came to feel that this is the kind of program the Government should be doing more of."
 
Better Results
 Graduates of the California retraining projects do better than those in Federal programs. Richard W. Moore, a professor at California State University at Northridge, followed 46,946 workers who took part in state-financed retraining projects that ended between mid-1989 and mid-1990.
Among workers who retrained while still on the job, he found that those using their new skills a year later were earning an average of $28,538, or $3,897 more than they had before. And the formerly unemployed workers were earning an average of $19,358 a year, or $8,263 more than they had in their previous jobs.
Still, Professor Moore and Ms. Strecher say there is room for improvement. They say the training panel should be more of a tool of industrial policy, to guide the state's growth. Instead, they say the panel sometimes bends to pressure from local legislators trying just to keep people on the job.
In addition, employers who try to take part are ensnared with so much red tape that in 9 of the last 10 years, the panel has been unable to spend all of its annual budget, currently about $97 million.
Some analysts also suspect that some well-off companies are given aid, even though they probably have the resources to retrain their own workers. Indeed, Mr. Geismar says, "We don't look into financial performance." But he said a company does have to meet its performance guidelines and show a need to protect or save jobs through retraining.
Besides, he said, the money -- the unemployment insurance the companies pay -- is theirs anyway, not the taxpayers'. Barbara Hanscom, head of the panel's San Mateo regional office, also contended that intervening early, before a company is on the ropes, averts bigger spending later.
 
FMC Corporation
Seeking New Skill After Cold War
 If not for the California training program, the Ground Systems Division of the FMC Corporation would be a bleaker place. A large and diversified company, FMC makes Army tanks like Bradley Fighting Vehicles and M113's. But the tank business in the post-cold-war era is sinking fast.
From a peak employment of 7,000 in the mid-1980's, the division has shrunk to 3,000. In two more years, it expects to stop makingtanks altogether, abandoning most of the plant's blue-collar workers and many of its engineers.
Ted Krekelberg, a 36-year-old mechanical engineer earning $40,000 a year, is nervous about the future of the industry, which is not surprising given his experience at FMC.
He had been helping to design a common chassis that would serve a variety of armed vehicles. But the Army abandoned it in early October. He was then reassigned to work on a lightweight tank that could be dropped from airplanes. He has helped make six of them, and the next step would be an order for 300 more. But he knows that order may never come. So Mr. Krekelberg has begun thinking of other work.
"My objective is to get out of defense systems," he said.
 
Hopes for Retraining
 That hope hinges on the training he has been receiving for more than a year, as part of the Employment Training Panel, which is spending $2.5 million to retrain workers at FMC. The company says it is spending $12 million of its own, and through the Job Training Partnership Act, the Federal Government is providing $1.5 million more for related programs.
Mr. Krekelberg is learning computer-aided design, known as CAD. It lets him plot his design for a tank's component into a computer, then allows him to see how the component looks in three dimensions and how it fits in relation to the designs of other components. Everyone helping to design the tank can see instantly how their work affects that of other designers.
"There is a major requirement for CAD skills in the commercial sector," Mr. Krekelberg said. "The jobs are out there."
He reaches into the desk drawer where he keeps a sheaf of help-wanted advertisements. One is for an engineering job that would require him to pay for his health insurance and his pension. In addition, the job could end in a couple of years. But it would pay between $80,000 and $100,000 a year.
That might interest him, he said, and with his new training he believes he could become a strong candidate.
 
Solectron Corporation
Training Workers For Better Results
 Sales of the Solectron Corporation, near Santa Clara in Milpitas, have soared right through the recession, from $93 million in 1986 to $407 million in the 1992 fiscal year, and its profits have climbed even more. State-subsidized retraining is a good part of the reason, executives there say. Without it, they might have had to lay off some workers.
Solectron's 3,500 employees assemble printed circuit boards, which are sold to firms like I.B.M. and Apple.
In 1986, as the quality of its products slipped, Solectron suffered a $3.2 million loss and ordered a large layoff. Management decided that to survive, the company needed a more reliable production system, known as statistical process control. To learn it, the workers would need special training.
"We used to have a buddy system," said Richard W. Allen, the company's director of quality assurance. "You would sit with someone and watch what they were doing. That's how you would learn what to do." But as the complexity of the boards increased, employees were not able to do the job properly. "We could have hired other people," he said. "Instead we chose to train our own."
Toward that end, the state provided $1.4 million to subsidize a two-year training project for about 600 employees. That project has been completed, and with $650,000 more, the company is training another 600. The subsidy is less this time because the training panel expects the company to assume all its training costs eventually.
At work stations along the production lines, computers continually sample the work and print out their measurements so operators can see instantly when something has gone awry. They can then adjust the machinery to prevent mass production of defective boards.
Anna Ramos, 55, a graduate of the introductory course, sits at the end of a line that makes boards for computer tape drives. She is a quality inspector.
"I inspect for soldering, the height of parts, the values of the parts," she said, seeming confident of her contribution to Solectron.
A chart near her testing bench shows why. In early July, it says, when the company began producing the tape-drive boards, 8,000 parts per million were defective. By Aug. 4, the rate had dropped to 378.
 
Machining School
Out of Work, But Optimistic
 Workers who lose their jobs are the hardest to help. Living on only their unemployment benefits, typically half their former pay, most of them spend their days looking for another job, leaving them with little time for retraining classes.
But about 1,000 apply each year for the four-month courses at Mr. Johnson's alma mater, the training center of the National Tooling and Machining Association in Santa Clara. Bert H. Shuster, the executive director, says about 100 are accepted.
The current class is a cross-section of the state's unemployed. Some are young, but most are skilled older workers. John Coyle, 52, was laid off from one engineering job that paid $56,000 a year and then from another paying $45,000. John Kovalski, 46, was a technical support manager paid $52,000 until he was laid off. And Nancy Nielson, 41, earned about $30,000 as a survey map maker and structural designer in the now-moribund construction industry.
Now all of them see machining as their best hope for jobs paying more than the minimum wage. Mr. Kovalski expects that in four or five years, he will be be earning up to $14 an hour, just over half his former earnings. Still, as a machinist, at least he expects to survive recessions.
"Middle management is not a good place to be," he said.
For all these students, California's retraining program could pay off. "We're in a desperate situation," says Bob L. McNeal, Mark Johnson's boss and president of McNeal Enterprises. "There's not enough young ones. I just ran an ad for a machinist-plastics fabricator. I ran it for 14 days. I had 66 applicants. Eleven I felt I could talk to. Two were qualified. I hired one."

SECTION: Section D; Page 9; Column 6; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 768 words

HEADLINE: GATT Pact Is Sought By Year-End

BYLINE: By ALAN RIDING, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PARIS, Nov. 26

 BODY:
After being deadlocked for almost a year by disagreements over agriculture, world trade negotiations resumed today in Geneva, with representatives of 108 nations aiming to conclude a new trade liberalization package before the end of this year.
The way was opened for an early conclusion of the global trade accord when the United States and the European Community settled their longstanding dispute over farming subsidies last week. France has denounced the deal but could not block resumption of the global talks. 
 These trade talks, known as the Uruguay Round, are widely viewed as a key to reviving economic growth, particularly in the industrialized world. They have been going on since September 1986 under the auspices of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade.
 
Missed Deadlines
 Although negotiators have missed several previous deadlines, Arthur Dunkel, GATT's director-general, said today that he was encouraged enough to propose achieving "a successful political conclusion" by the end of this year. The 108-nation Trade Negotiating Committee approved the target date.
"We're going to do everything we can to get agreement within the deadline set by Dunkel," Rufus Yerxa, the Deputy United States Trade Representative, said after this morning's session. "You can count on full U.S. participation."
The talks cover 15 areas not tackled in previous GATT "rounds," including services, textiles, intellectual property and industry, as well as agriculture. Economists believe an agreement to reduce tariffs and other trade barriers could add $200 billion a year to world trade by 2002.
While progress has been achieved in many areas and a draft final accord has long existed, the talks ground to a halt because the 12-nation European Community refused to bend to demands by the United States and other leading food producers to cut its subsidies to farmers and food exports.
Then, earlier this month, a new breakdown in farm talks and an American threat of punitive sanctions against some European Community exports suddenly raised the specter of a trade war. Last week, the community backed off and accepted a 21 percent reduction in its subsidized food exports.
France, which is Europe's largest food exporter, promptly rejected the farm-trade agreement worked out by the community's executive commission with Washington. Unable to veto the pact, France has now warned its European partners that it will veto any final GATT accord harmful to its interests.
But since the community is represented in trade bargaining by its executive commission and takes its decisions as a bloc, France's lonely stance is likely to have little impact on the GATT talks. Many trade experts also question whether France would veto a GATT package that would benefit many nonfarming sectors of its economy.
 
'3-Dimensional Chess Game'
 In his statement restarting the global talks, Mr. Dunkel emphasized that different aspects of the Uruguay Round were interlinked, a reference to the fact that concessions in one area could be exchanged for concessions in another area. "Nothing is final until everything is settled," he noted.
Above all, he has called for "intensive, nonstop" negotiations to settle outstanding differences over market access for goods and over liberalization of services. The procedure is immensely complex because bilateral and multilateral deals must be worked out simultaneously.
"It's a bit like a three-dimensional chess game involving 108 governments," one GATT official explained. "And you have to remember that everyone complains all the time. Governments never say they are happy when they extract an advantage. That would not be good bargaining. But in the end, it's all a matter of give and take."
 
Fast-Track Approval
 Mr. Dunkel aims to achieve broad agreement on the so-called Final Act of the Uruguay Round by the end of this year, but he acknowledged that it might not be possible to work out each country's schedule for removing specific barriers within that time.
GATT officials said that while the year-end deadline for a political agreement had a symbolic ring about it, failure to meet it would in fact bring serious new complications, not least to plans to obtain ratification of the package by the United States Congress.
They noted that authority to seek Congressional ratification by the so-called fast-track procedure -- under which Congress can accept or reject the accord, but cannot offer amendments -- ends on June 1. But Congress must also be allowed 90 days for reflection, which means any GATT accord must be ready by March 1.

HEADLINE: Somalia Aid Workers Split on Troops

BYLINE: By JANE PERLEZ, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOGADISHU, Somalia, Nov. 26

 BODY:
With the popping of gunfire a daily occurrence, Mogadishu is always a jumpy city, but the disquiet was aggravated this afternoon by reports that United States troops might be coming and by fears of the effects of such intervention.
Aid workers were clearly divided over whether military intervention would help relief shipments reach starving Somalis, or ignite further chaos and bloodshed in a country plundered by clan warfare and withered by famine. 
 Peter Stocker, head of the International Red Cross operation here, said United Nations peacekeeping troops should be increased only in coordination with the warring clan leaders.
"If that isn't done, the United Nations would not attain its objective of building up confidence for possible political dialogue," he said.
 
A Possible Reversal
 Many officials and workers said they feared an unwelcome outside force would unite Somali factions. The result, they said, could be a shooting war between the United Nations and unified Somali forces or the kind of indiscriminate killings that characterized the clan war between November and February.
The appearance today of armed trucks, jeeps and a tank with mounted artillery at the headquarters of the International Medical Corps, an American agency, signaled the opposition of Gen. Mohammed Farah Aidid, the most recalcitrant Somali leader, relief officials here said.
The offer of troops, made Wednesday by President Bush, came as clan fighters and greedy gunmen stepped up attacks on the relief agencies struggling to feed nearly 2 million hungry Somalis.
The United States would most likely use Air Force planes to transport troops to Somalia and deploy 2,000 marines off the coast, a senior Administration official said today.
 
Food Just Offshore
 The rise in violence here, including the shelling Tuesday of a United Nations ship with 10,000 tons of wheat as it tried to enter port, has rapidly depleted stocks across the famine zone, aid officials said.
The International Red Cross said today that it had cut rations to one-quarter at its 300 kitchens in Mogadishu, serving only 523 calories' worth of rice, oil and beans, the equivalent of a small bar of chocolate.
Even at this level, only three days' food remained in Red Cross warehouses in Mogadishu. With the airport closed because of violence, aid workers could foresee no clear way of getting sizable quantities of food.
At the same time, two ships with 3,500 tons of rice each waited offshore, as they have for days. The United Nations ship carrying wheat was diverted to Mombasa, Kenya.
A senior United States official said that as food deliveries by plane and ship increased in the last month, a higher proportion was looted.
 
Looting Rate Doubles
 The official, Andrew S. Natsios, the aid coordinator for the Agency for International Development, who has been directing the United States program in Somalia, said 80 percent of the donated food was "being looted or used as protection money," up from about 40 percent in mid-October.
"Either the United Nations has to reconsider the tactics on security, or the entire relief operation should be closed down," Mr. Natsios said Wednesday in a telephone interview from the United States. "You can't tolerate 80 percent of the food being stolen. It's an outrage."
He said food stolen at gunpoint was being exchanged in neighboring Ethiopia for weapons. "The relief food should not be a treasury to buy more guns," he said, speaking shortly before Washington's offer of troops became public.
The reference to protection money underlined one of the more bizarre aspects of the famine: the security arrangements among the relief agencies, which hire private gunmen to protect their houses, warehouses and convoys of food. The hired guns, usually paid in food and cash, have often turned on agency personnel or looted the food they are supposed to guard.
 
500 Wary Pakistanis
 So far, only 500 United Nations troops have arrived to guard food convoys; three months ago, the Security Council approved a contingent of 3,500.
The initial force, from Pakistan, arrived in early October, but only slightly more than 100 have been deployed, at the main airport. The other 3,000, from Canada, Belgium, Egypt and Nigeria, are to be sent to other areas.
So far, General Aidid has prevented the Pakistani troops from being stationed at the port, the main entry point for food, United Nations officials said.
 
'You're Going to Get Shot'
 But Mr. Natsios was also referring to the limited mandate assigned to United Nations troops so far. Mr. Natsios said he understood the Pakistanis had only side arms and were more concerned with protecting themselves than with protecting food convoys.
"You attack a relief shipment and you're going to get shot," should be the message and method of United Nations troops, he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Reports of the possible use of foreign troops to insure aid deliveries in Somalia have divided the opinions of relief workers. Malnourished women and children received food last summer in a relief camp in Baidoa. (Fiona McDougall for The New York Times)
 
Chart: "A Nation in Tatters"
 
THE LAND
 
Somalia, a nation of more than 4.5 millikon people ion the eastern edge of the Horn of Africa, has been torn byfierce clan fighting since President Mohammed Siad Barre was ousted last year.
 
THE WAR
 
The two main militia leaders are Gen. Mohammed Farah Aidid, a Soviet-trained military officer who lays claim to being the rightful leader of Somalia, and Mohammed Ali Mahdi, a mild-mannered hotelier who goes by the title Interim President of Somalia. They conspired in January 1991 to oust President Siad Barre, then later had a falling-out and fought a war earlier this year that virutally destroyed Mogadishu and closed the capital's port.
 
THE FAMINE
 
More than two milion Somalis have been forced off their lands by the warring militias, who have prevented farmers from planting crops and killed much of the nation's livestock. Aid officials say as many one-third of the Somalis, or even more, may die in the next six months without food, yet aid officials estimate that 80 percent of the food that is sent is being stolen.

HEADLINE: In Swank Shops, Lebanon Seeks the Gougers

BYLINE: By IHSAN A. HIJAZI

DATELINE: BEIRUT, Lebanon, Nov. 25

 BODY:
Lebanese involved in business were shaken this week after Government inspectors backed by police escorts raided and closed a number of exclusive stores in the capital, accusing them of defrauding the public.
The raids were videotaped and shown on television. Stunned shopkeepers with their jaws dropping and their eyes staring in surprise were reprimanded by police officers for failing to show proper invoices for their merchandise and for overpricing. 
 The raids were one of numerous measures used recently by the authorities in their attempt to inject discipline in a country used to the anarchy of 15 years of civil war.
In another part of the campaign, traffic violators were given a 24-hour warning in the form of a communique from the traffic department, after which tow trucks went into action removing double parked cars. Traffic violation fees are doubled if not paid in 10 days.
"The next mission is to clear side walks from car vendors," promised a senior traffic official.
Pedestrians can no longer use pavements because squatters selling used cars imported from Germany and the United States have taken them over.
People stealing electricity by stretching wires from their houses to power cables on the streets have been fined. "The next time they do this they will have no electricity at all," warned John Lyon, the new director of the state power grid. Mr. Lyon sent teams of collectors to thousands of homes to collect unpaid power bills.
Fouad Siniora, minister of state for finance, said: "We are determined to collect all that is due to the treasury. Not one cent will be allowed to go unpaid."
Throughout the civil war, very few Lebanese paid income tax. Now businesses have received notices to prepare their books and have them ready for inspection by tax officials.
After the army took over law and order duties when all militias were disarmed and dissolved in March 1990, troops seized public buildings and offices occupied by gunmen.
"No security violations or plundering of public property will be tolerated in the future," the army commander, Gen. Emile Lahoud, said.
President Elias Hrawi told the troops in an independence day speech this week: "Destroy any one who may try to challenge you."
The day of the raids on the stores, President Hrawi warned "greedy merchants" that they will no longer be allowed "to suck the blood of the people." He said he was one of the citizens complaining about runaway inflation.
Airing his views in a television interview to mark the halfway point of his six-year term, the President said there was no justification for the cost of living to remain so high when the value of the Lebanese currency has appreciated by nearly 30 percent in relation to the United States dollar and other currencies. "Prices should fall by the same proportion," he demanded.
They did not. The main public complaint has been that when the Lebanese pound rate depreciated against the dollar, shops raised prices. But when the reverse happens, the price does not come down.
The formation of a new Cabinet last month under a billionaire, Rafik al-Hariri, has injected confidence in the economy, and sent Lebanese converting their holdings in dollars and other foreign currency into Lebanese pounds.
In a laissez-faire society, Lebanese businessmen are free to do what they like. But President Hrawi warned them this time that if they do not stop their greed, the Government would interfere and apply what he called "guided economy." Information Minister Michel Samaha was quick, however, to point out that the President did not mean that Lebanon would end its traditional policy of free enterprise.
The raids on the shops were supervised by a department in the Ministry of Economy for the protection of consumers. Among the stores affected were Beirut's two most exclusive super markets, Mandarine and Goodies. The owners were allowed to reopen their doors after pleading with Economy Minister Hagob Demarjian.

HEADLINE: Blockaded Iraqi Kurds Face Fearsome Winter

BYLINE: By CHRIS HEDGES

DATELINE: DOHUK, Iraq, Nov. 25

 BODY:
Soft, wet snow has begun to fall over northern Iraq, laying a mantle over the desolate steppe and signaling the start what may well be one of the most difficult periods for the Iraqi Kurds since they were freed from the grip of Saddam Hussein's Government 18 months ago.
The Kurds, crippled by a 15-month-old blockade by Baghdad that has left them bereft of kerosene for heating, food and basic supplies, will depend on the $200 million in relief assistance, including $43 million from the United States Agency for International Development and $20 million from the European Community, that is just beginning to be trucked into the north. 
 The United Nations hopes to deliver at least nine million gallons of kerosene by the end of December and 13,000 tons of food. But its officials in northern Iraq fear that the program, hastily planned and beginning only now, may not be enough to avert disaster.
"We are way behind," a United Nations official said, "and we have grossly underestimated what the Kurds will need. Even if we had enough to hand out, which we do not, I do not think we could get it distributed in time."
The 4 million Kurds live in a security zone set up by the allied forces following an abortive rebellion just after the Persian Gulf war. The zone, which runs roughly along Iraq's 36th parallel, was created to convince 1.5 million Kurdish refugees in Turkey and Iran that it was safe to return home. It is monitored by a small team of military observers in the border town of Zakho and frequent flights by warplanes based in Turkey.
The withdrawal of Iraqi forces from the north and Baghdad's decision to impose a strict embargo, refusing even to pay pensions or forward letters, speeded the Kurds' decision to form their own government this summer.
But the de facto Kurdish state, with meager revenues and few resources, has presided over a steady deterioration in living standards.
Roads and bridges are crumbling into disuse. There are frequent power outages. Gasoline can be bought only on the black market. And with a host of materials from window panes to spare parts for machines now unavailable, printing shops, mills, bakeries and garages have all had to shut down, swelling the ranks of the unemployed.
Prices for basic items like kerosene have risen more than 100 times what they were before the Iraqi blockade.
"We are grateful for what the United Nations is doing," said Massoud Barzani, the head of the Kurdistan Democratic Party, one of the two main Kurdish factions, "but what they are providing is little and will not solve the problems we face. The U.N. and international agencies should, in addition to relief, help us with some long-term development projects."
At least 8,000 families live in tents; tens of thousands will huddle in gutted buildings with only thin plastic sheeting covering the empty window frames.
The Kurds have chopped down most trees around the urban centers, with many families even digging out the roots, to stockpile fuel for the winter.
Families that have subsisted on vegetables for months now watch in horror as prices spiral upward and the volume in the markets decline, leaving only cans of imported goods that are priced beyond their reach.
"As we get closer to winter, we are beginning to see increasing numbers of children who have some degree of malnutrition," said Dr. Ghayat al-Din Obeid Ala, the general director of health for Dohuk Province. "Every day there is less and less to eat."
Fatima Ibrahim stood on a small concrete landing outside the one-room hovel where she lives with her husband and five children. For several weeks her family has survived on a cold porridge of chick peas and tomatoes while her husband scrounges for odd jobs.
But jobs are as scarce as food, and he usually returns with no earnings and cold feet.
Inside the room, two of Mrs. Ibrahim's young sons, wrapped in tattered gray blankets, lay on the floor, racked by high fevers. One was so sick he could no longer walk.
"It is the winter that scares us the most," she said. "Before we could at least exist, but now we do not see a way out."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Economy
Productivity has grown sharply in the last 18 months, a sign that the recovery is likely to endure. The average American worker now turns out more than $28 in goods and services an hour, the highest ever and nearly a dollar above the amount in the spring of 1991, when the recession hit bottom. [Page D9.]
Hesitancy over the economy is the dominant mood in Reading, Pa., which was surveyed for the eighth time since the onset of the recession in 1990 as representative of the Northeast economy. Although the election of Bill Clinton brought a temporary surge in consumer activity, Reading's business community is unsure whether a true economic rebound will occur. [D9.] 
 The American weapons industry has entered a phase of consolidation forced by declining Pentagon spending. It is expected that by 2000, less than half of the current contractors will remain independent or leaders in their fields. But some say that is positive because it reduces overcapacity. [D12.]
 
International
Nomura Securities will pay nearly $200 million to compensate customers for losses on bonds that it had promised would never fall in value. The world's largest brokerage said its brokers sold $390 million of bonds that plummeted in value when the American real estate market declined. [D9.]
World trade negotiations resumed in Geneva after being deadlocked for almost a year by disagreements over agriculture. The way was opened for an early conclusion of the global trade accord when the United States and the European Community settled their longstanding dispute over farming subsidies last week. [D9.]
OPEC appeared near deadlock and oil prices tumbled as the organization struggled to reconcile competing demands to sell more oil within an output ceiling low enough to push prices higher. World oil prices, down 10 percent since mid-October, plunged to a seven-month low in European markets. [D10.]
 
Companies
Digital Equipment is preparing to accelerate its layoffs before the end of the year. People inside the company said that by Dec. 7, at least 7,500 more employees, and perhaps 8,800, would be laid off worldwide, many in New England. [D10.]
The F.C.C. is agonizing over what to do about the raunchy remarks of Howard Stern, the New York disk jockey who, with his employer, the Infinity Broadcasting Corporation, is struggling with regulators over "indecent" programming. [D14.]
 
Markets
The Global Settlement Fund is a novel 24-hour money transfer system that might someday be used by all kinds of businesses for sending payments to one another. While securities and currencies are traded 24 hours a day in London, New York and Tokyo, there had been no way for traders to send or receive payments outside of business hours in their local market. [D12.]
The dollar inched up in overseas dealings thinned by the Thanksgiving holiday in the United States. Treasury bonds rose slightly in London, where stocks also advanced. [D12.]
 
Today's Columns
By making a public offering in American Re only two months after buying the reinsurance company from Aetna, Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts is taking advantage of a window of profitability opened in the wake of Hurricane Andrew and other recent natural catastrophes. Kurt Eichenwald: Market Place. [D12.]
A filtered version of venerable Chesterfield cigarettes is being brought out by the Liggett Group. Ads for the cigarettes mention the Chesterfield Cigarette Company -- a faux corporate parent. The strategy, a common ploy in selling image-driven products, aims to revive the lagging brand. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D14.]

HEADLINE: U.S. Output Per Worker Is Growing

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
The nation's productivity has seen a much-needed growth spurt over the last 18 months, a compelling sign that the economic recovery is likely to endure.
After slipping for two years, productivity of American workers has been rising at a solid 2.6 percent annual rate. Indeed, since the economy is believed to have grown during the summer at a faster rate than previously thought, productivity probably surged more than 3.5 percent in the third quarter. 
 Higher productivity means that the average American worker -- still the most efficient in the world -- now turns out more than $28 worth of goods and services an hour. The figure, adjusted for inflation, is the highest ever and nearly $1 above the level in the spring of 1991 when the recession finally hit bottom.
 
Done Without Adding Jobs
 The productivity rebound has been so strong that it means all the additional output of the United States economy in the recovery so far has been achieved without adding any jobs or work hours. Since the spring of 1991, annual output has grown by $150 billion.
The fact that businesses can produce more with the same or fewer people is painful for those who are unemployed, and it poses a challenge for the new Clinton Administration, which has promised to create more jobs. But it is also a crucial sign of improving economic health.
Whether the recent rebound in output per worker hour signals a permanent turnaround in the nation's long-run productivity prospects remains to be seen. In the meantime, though, the pickup to date is a compelling sign that the economic expansion is developing staying power.
 
A 'Classic' Signal
 Productivity typically spurts early in recoveries, as companies better utilize employees who, because of soft sales, were not laid off but who were also not working flat out. It is also during this period that companies typically are too cautious to hire new help.
"It's a classic recovery signal," said Lawrence H. Kudlow, an economist at Bear, Stearns & Company.
Productivity gains are holding down the price of services to consumers and businesses. They are also keeping labor costs flat, making businesses more competitive. And most significantly, rising productivity has pushed up corporate profits, despite so-so sales; that means companies will eventually be able to hire more workers and investment in new equipment.
The gains in productivity appear to be far more widespread than during the early part of the 1980's expansion. A rough calculation using published Government data suggests that the services sector of the economy -- broadly defined to include construction as well as banking, fast-food restaurants and retail stores -- may have contributed as much as two-thirds of the rise in productivity in the last 18 months.
Throughout the 1980's, by contrast, productivity did not grow at all for the service sector, considered as a whole.
"I see tremendous changes taking place in some service industries," said Martin N. Baily, a productivity expert at the University of Maryland, citing a wave of restructuring in industries like banking, retailing and railroads.
The big question is whether productivity will continue to increase or will slacken when the economy fully recovers from the recession.
"We see a lot of layoffs going on, a lot of restructuring," said Robert Z. Lawrence, an economist at the Kennedy School at Harvard and the author of several books on productivity. "Maybe this is the kick-in-the-pants productivity needs, and this is a sustainable. Or maybe companies are just very pessimistic about the recovery."
Productivity's contribution to long-term economic well-being would be hard to exaggerate. Over a matter of decades, productivity growth determines how fast living standards can rise and how quickly the economy as a whole can expand.
Workers, ultimately, can only consume as much as they produce. That's why increasing the sluggish growth of productivity -- on average, less than 1 percent a year in the last two decades -- was at the heart of the economic programs put forth by Ronald Reagan's supply-side theorists and now by President-elect Bill Clinton's public-investment gurus.
Thanks to the power of compounding, seemingly small differences in productivity growth rates, if they persist long enough, have dramatic consequences for long-term growth in living standards.
The difference between 0.8 percent, the trend in the past 20 years, and the average rate for the past century, 1.7 percent, is huge. If productivity is growing close to 2 percent, living standards double every 35 years. If growth is closer to 1 percent, it takes 70 years.
Stephen S. Roach, an economist at Morgan Stanley & Company, is among those predicting a permanent shift toward faster productivity growth. "On the basis of the striking improvement in productivity growth in the last year, I'd say we're heading back toward a long-term productivity growth trend of 1.7 or 1.8 percent," he said.
Productivity optimists like Mr. Roach argue that as inflation is gradually squeezed out of the economy, service companies are finding it harder to pass on cost increases to customers.
Foreign competition is also affecting services, both directly and indirectly -- as more and more companies from abroad enter the American market and, probably more importantly, as American manufacturers insist on higher quality at lower cost. Moreover, the greater competition created by deregulation is forcing consolidation and automation in everything from airlines to banking.
"A lot of the downsizing we're seeing will endure," Mr. Roach said.
But productivity pessimists take the opposite view, arguing that nothing fundamental, like the pace of technological innovation, has changed. All that is happening, they contend, is that companies are correcting the excesses -- overoptimistic hiring and sloppy management -- that crop up when times are good for a long time, as they were in the 1980's.
The recent productivity growth "is nothing to cheer about," said Robert H. Gordon, an economist at Northwestern University. "It's going to grow faster than normal because it has a lot of catching up to do."
 
What Both Sides Want
 Most economists remain on the fence, pointing out that only time can settle this debate. "Based on my long experience watching these numbers," said Mr. Dean at the Bureau of Labor Statistics, "I hesitate to say that a trend that's lasted five or six quarters will last even one or two more."
Regardless of their views, the optimists and pessimists alike want the incoming administration to press ahead with growth-oriented policies aimed at improving the skills of the work force and boosting both public and private investment in everything from roads to research and development.
"We shouldn't rest on our laurels," said Stanley Fischer, a Massachusetts Institute of Technology economist. "Productivity is growing very fast. But a revival in living standards really depends on some revival of investment."

HEADLINE: OUR TOWNS;
United in Love for Homemade Songs

BYLINE: By Andrew H. Malcolm

DATELINE: SHARON, Conn.

 BODY:
SO you're a respected research chemist who lives with precise equations and predictable reactions. And your daughter Caroline, the graduate student, comes home one day with a blue-jeaned fellow carrying a guitar and a weathered look like those country singers who've clearly lived a full life, maybe two, before 40. Turns out this guy is an itinerant folk singer who's temporarily unemployed. (Aren't they all?)
Daddy, says your beloved daughter, I'd like you to meet Sandy Paton, my husband. 
 How do you do, Mr. Pa ---- Your what?!
Apparently, that consenting chemist, Reuben Swenson, knew a little about human chemistry, too, in 1957 when Sandy and Caroline were united in love for each other and for the homemade ballads, ditties and laments of everyday life that say so much in song about the society that spawns them.
Caroline Swenson had never been part of the bubblegum-chewing, Fabian-worshipping crowd anyway. In fact, she was supposed to be taking ski lessons when a friend dragged her to an evening of folk songs in Berkeley where Sandy Paton hadn't planned on being either. But even hitchikers need a few dollars now and then.
So there he was singing the Riddle Song and nodding, as always, to the audience to join the refrain when he hears this lovely voice in the second row coming from a knockout brunette in a dirndl and peasant blouse. (Remember, this is pre-60's.)
He's trying to think of a good pickup line when she strides up and says, "Would you teach me that unusual version of the Riddle Song?"
"Come on back to my friend's house," he suggests. (Is this column beginning to sound like a folk song?) Three days later he asks for her hand. One week more and he hires a band. Now, 35 years after their wedding and thousands of recordings and sing-alongs in theaters, church basements, classrooms, coffee shops and kitchens across the country, Sandy and Caroline Paton have been named Connecticut's official State Troubadours for 1993.
To match the official salary, there are no official duties. It's just a yearlong honor bestowed by the legislature recognizing two lifelong singers of folk songs for, well, folks.
"We appreciate it," said Caroline, who's 60 years old. "Maybe we'll get more bookings." They sang yesterday for Thanksgiving diners at the nearby Interlaken Inn and appear tomorrow evening at West Hartford's First Church of Christ Congregational (the basement, of course).
After the 1960's folk scare when the Kingston Trio and other awfully clean groups in pressed pants and striped shirts took folk songs to unprecedented heights of slick, packaged popularity that shocked traditionalists, folk music has returned to a comfortable obscurity and a presumed purity.
Being a folk singer is not your standard 9 to 5 job, unless you mean 9 P.M. to 5 A.M. when the crowd, the guitar and the wine are good. The Patons are folk singers with a cordless phone, their own 800-number (836-0901) and a label (Folk Legacy) with more than 100 recordings available from their 19th-century carriage-house near here.
And there's more to the Patons' 1,000-song repertory than those mind-numbing choruses of Michael Rowed the Boat Ashore that Peace Corps volunteers have carried to every corner of the globe.
Let's face it, more folk music was written in the barn than the parlor. It was intended for private porch performances at a time the country's isolated pockets of population made their own entertainment or did without, not for a wired era of widespread enjoyment when global satellites simultaneously televise recordings with warning labels.
Folk songs tell of brave heroes, pure maidens, sadness and glee. But like some of Shakespeare's lilting literary licentiousness, old-fashioned folk music, despite its often jaunty rhythms, can also be violent (spouses murdering one another), immoral (doing deals with the devil) and euphemistic, even for its day. It doesn't, for instance, take Madonna to suggest that, in original versions, more may have been going on in Dinah's kitchen than someone strumming on an old banjo.
But that's what fascinates the Patons (no, not that -- the revealing changes in lyrics over generations). "There's no end to the world's folk songs," said the 63-year-old Mr. Paton, who has spent decades combing rural America and the British Isles for them, then tracing their evolving themes and lyrics. "And every folk song is always alive, always changing, as singers adapt them for different times and audiences."
The Patons adjust songs and performances for specific audiences, even adding their own lyrics. Last summer, one spectator tried to request her favorite old folk song but couldn't remember the title. She sang a verse.
"I know it," said Mr. Paton. "I wrote it."

HEADLINE: Beekeepers Face a Myriad Of Challenges

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SYRACUSE

 BODY:
Members of the Empire State Honey Producers wanted to sweeten their morning oatmeal during a recent conference here. So Fred Thompson dashed out the restaurant door and returned with a jar of honey.
If the beekeepers in New York, New Jersey and Connecticut could only solve the rest of their problems with such ease. Experts and beekeepers at the conference said the industry is facing some of the toughest challenges in recent memory. 
 Beekeepers are battling insects, black bears, diseases, honey exports from China and other problems.
Eight years ago, the biggest challenge beekeepers faced was a disease called American Foulbrood, a bacterial infection that can be controlled with antibiotics.
Now they are faced with even larger disease and pest problems, including the infestation of two types of mites that have destroyed large portions of the bee populations in the three states.
 
Number of Colonies Declines
 "From about 1984 through 1992, we have lost about a third of our bees," said Robert Mungari, director of the Division of Plant Industry for the New York State Department of Agriculture. Between 1985 and 1986, Mr. Mungari estimated, New York State had about 120,000 bee colonies. "We estimate that we are now down to around 80,000 colonies due to the honeybee tracheal mite," he said.
Beekeepers must also protect their bees from bears, which love to devour hives full of honey. Robert Morse, a professor of agriculture at Cornell University in Ithaca, N.Y., has encouraged New York beekeepers to fight to retain a provision in the state law that allows beekeepers to shoot bears that are in the process of destroying hives.
Beekeepers in Connecticut are not allowed to shoot the bears, said Carol Lemmon, Deputy State Entomologist for the Department of Agriculture in Connecticut, so some have turned to electric fences to keep bears away from the hives.
Bear damage to hives in New Jersey has been limited to the area north of Trenton, said Walt Wilson, an apiary inspector for the state. Beekeepers in New Jersey also use electric fences, since the state prohibits the shooting of bears.
But economics may pose an even bigger threat to beekeeping.
According to a recent industry study conducted at the College of Agriculture at Cornell University, more than 16 percent of the nation's beekeepers have costs that exceed their incomes.
Federal subsidies for beekeepers -- which began in 1954 to provide adequate pollination for agricultural crops by maintaining a viable beekeeping industry -- have been increased in the face of competition from China, which has grown increasingly stiff as honey exporters in different provinces have begun competing with each other, Dr. Morse said. But those subsidies, along with other Federal agricultural supports, have come under attack from some members of Congress, and beekeepers fear they may lack the political clout to protect their interests.
Despite all the problems, though, the beekeepers stay in business because beekeepers love what they're doing. Ms. Lemmon has four hives she maintains as a hobby. "You become very attached to your hives and your bees," she said. "They are almost like pets."

HEADLINE: Uncertainty and Hope on Main Street

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: READING, Pa., Nov. 26

 BODY:
For George S. Reppert, general manager of Kachel Ford near here, the election of Bill Clinton three weeks ago removed one uncertainty and replaced it with another.
Even if not everyone in Berks County, which George Bush narrowly carried, is pleased with the election's outcome, "at last we have somebody," Mr. Reppert said. 
 But now Mr. Reppert and legions of other area business people must decide as they plan for 1993 whether to bet that the modest upturn in consumer activity since the election will produce a full-fledged rebound in sales and profits.
 
 
Sense of Hesitancy
 "There was a certain upsurge," said Mr. Reppert, describing a flurry of showroom traffic and sales in the week or so after Nov. 3. But activity has since died down, adding a sense of hesitancy to Mr. Reppert's outlook.
Indeed, hesitancy is the dominant mood in this southeastern Pennsylvania city and the surrounding area of Berks County, which was surveyed for the eighth time since the onset of the recession in 1990 as representative of the Northeast's economy.
At Kachel Ford, in suburban Shillington, the hesitancy takes the form of holding up plans to invest $60,000 in a new computer system and $40,000 for shop equipment because of uncertainties over new tax credits for business investments promised by the incoming Clinton Administration. Mr. Clinton listed the tax credits as a priority, and they seem likely to meet relatively little opposition in Congress.
"We're waiting to find out what's happening," Mr. Reppert said as he sat in his showroom near a 1925 Model T that his wife's grandfather sold in his first year as a franchisee. Decades later, the dealership repurchased the car and restored it. "We don't want to make a dumb investment and miss it by a week or a month," Mr. Reppert added, referring to the effective date of any new investment tax credits.
Mr. Clinton has proposed providing tax breaks to companies that buy a certain amount of business equipment. Economists say such investment tax credits will help stimulate the economy and create jobs. But it is undecided how much equipment companies will have to buy to qualify for the credits or when the credits will take effect.
There are, of course, already signs of a measurable pickup in the Reading area's economy. Louis F. Lauer, manager of the Pennsylvania State Job Service office on Cherry Street, said his caseload of unemployment claims runs just over 5,000 these days, down from about 8,000 in early summer and 6,500 in February.
"The openings are coming in a little better," Mr. Lauer observed, pointing to the seasonal increases at Godiva Chocolate and other employers in the county's prodigious candy business. He also cited a handful of plant expansions, like the one at Sweet Street Desserts, a fast-growing specialty food operation.
And Metropolitan Edison, the dominant electric utility, finds industrial sales of electricity, adjusted for weather, running 2.7 percent ahead of the 1991 pace so far this year. And for October alone, commercial and residential demand is running ahead of the year-to-date average. Industrial power, a gauge of manufacturing activity, fell 2 percent in 1991.
There are even some companies, like the Morgan Corporation, a builder of truck bodies hard by the Morgantown interchange of the Pennsylvania Turnpike, that are enjoying particularly strong rebounds in sales.
Donald Griffin, executive vice president, said business had jumped some 50 percent this year, bouncing back from a poor 1991 after Ryder, Hertz Penske and other fleet owners found themselves unable to defer buying new equipment any longer.
"Everybody tried to postpone capital appropriations," said Mr. Griffin, whose headquarters plant here turns out up to half of Ryder's East Coast fleet. "Then they started getting eaten alive with maintenance expenses."
But even as the company prepares to recall 30 remaining laid-off workers for the peak winter-spring production cycle, Mr. Griffin frets that the bulk of the company's recovery has probably already occurred.
This could be somewhat ominous, since Morgan's business, according to Mr. Griffin, tends to move up or down before the economy at large.
With its myriad outlet and traditional malls, four Kmart stores, three Wal-Mart outlets, a new "designer outlet center" and other national chains, Berks County is one of the most dense per-capita sales territories on earth. But for the county's retailers, the mood is hardly sanguine, despite hopes for an improved 1992 holiday season.
Charles R. Thamarus, manager of Joseph's Clothier on Penn Street in Reading's downtown, said business perked up in October but that November had so far been "spotty," trailing the year-earlier period and providing little incentive to build inventories.
Fern Schmehl, who with her husband Daniel runs the Iron Horse Hobby House, reports no improvement in a business that has been dismal all year and has historically thrived in hard times.
"We should be very busy with the trains right now," Mrs. Schmehl said. With more apparent hope than expectations, she added, "I'm waiting for Clinton to turn the economy around."
 
 
Sign of the Times
 One sobering sign of consumer sentiment over the last several weeks is the relative lack of interest among students at generally affluent Governor Mifflin High School in signing up for the annual Easter break trip to Europe.
In recent years, long lines formed on the first day of school to obtain one of the coveted 54 places reserved for juniors and seniors. But over several weeks this fall, the trip leader, Michael T. Kostzewa, was able to round up only 34 students despite opening the trip to 10th-graders.
"I definitely think it's related to the economy," said Mr. Kostzewa, a social studies teacher, adding that putting a few Eastern European cities on the itinerary for the first time may also have slightly diminished the appeal. "They don't seem to want to spend the money."
The nine-day trip in early April will cost $1,400, up about $75 from last spring.
But the Berks County economy, like any other, is a swirl of crosscurrents and seemingly contradictory signals that make it nearly impossible to gauge the present condition precisely, much less project the future.
Such crosscurrents have swept over individual businesses like the Singer Equipment Company, a $25 million-a-year supplier to the food service industry.
Orders to outfit new restaurants are "very, very dead," said Henry D. Singer, president of the family-owned company, whose core market extends about 100 miles from Reading. Spending at restaurants by the elderly, by middle-income workers and by those on expense accounts has fallen substantially and remains depressed, the owner of a local chain of restaurants recently told Mr. Singer.
 
 
'Wave of Optimism'
 On the other hand, Singer Equipment is enjoying brisk sales for new or renovated eating facilities in places like retirement communities, country clubs, fast-food operations, hospitals, bars, churches, schools and caterers.
"There is a definite wave of optimism, not the dark and gloomy atmosphere like there was in the first quarter" of 1992, Mr. Singer said.
But profit margins are being squeezed ferociously -- "The greatest pressure we've ever seen," he said -- and banks are still largely unwilling to finance new ventures, notoriously risky in the restaurant business.
Mr. Singer, like Mr. Reppert, the manager of the Ford dealership, hopes to benefit soon from new business investment credits that in the past have proved "great impetus" for his customers to place orders. But for the present, there is the hesitancy.
"Some of them have told us that if it happens, they certainly will upgrade their equipment," he said, "but they're holding off to see what's going to happen."

HEADLINE: A Fund-Shifting System That's Open All Night

BYLINE: By MICHAEL QUINT

 BODY:
The Global Settlement Fund may sound like a common money market fund, but behind that bland title is a novel 24-hour money transfer system that might some day be used by all kinds of businesses for sending payments to one another.
While securities and currencies are traded 24 hours a day in London, New York and Tokyo, there has not been any way for traders to send or receive payments outside of business hours in their local market. 
 The Bankers Trust Company, creator and manager of the new fund, began offering it earlier this month to members of the Chicago Mercantile Exchange. The exchange, whose 87 members include most of the nation's leading banks and securities firms, recently changed its rules so members can satisfy daily margin calls by sending the fund's shares to the exchange rather than cash or Treasury bills.
 
 
A 24-Hour Cash Exchange
 Shares in the fund are the equivalent of money that can be transferred instantly to another shareholder, 24 hours a day, without a transaction fee.
The fund invests only in Treasury securities. Shares may only be bought by corporations and institutions that have the required computer connections with the fund's control room in New York. The minimum purchase is $100,000.
The rapidity of payment and round-the-clock access was important to the Chicago Merc, which started nighttime trading of futures contracts in June. The exchange had been seeking a way to collect margin during hours that the banking system and Federal Reserve were closed.
"Right now, there are a lot of people on the sidelines waiting to see it work," said Robin B. Perlin, vice president of cash management at the Chicago Research and Trading Group, a Chicago Merc member firm that has decided to buy shares in the fund. "Hopefully, all the exchanges are going to approve the fund, which will give us a lot more flexibility to move funds between our accounts."
 
Designs Beyond Traders
 Bankers Trust, which spent five years developing Global Settlement and obtaining the necessary approvals from banking and securities regulators, has grander visions for the fund than just a back-office tool for securities firms and futures traders.
Eileen Bedell, the company's director of global settlement services, expects the fund to appeal to corporations that are tired of paying bank fees, which average about $7 for every money transfer, and the uncertainty of not knowing exactly when those transfers will occur.
There are no transaction fees when Global Settlement Fund shares change hands, and the fund's projected expenses of about 65-hundredths of one percentage point of assets are about average for other money market funds. Like other funds, Global Settlement will pay interest to shareholders depending on the interest rates the fund earns on its investments.
 
Expands on Fed's Role
 Currently, a corporation transfers large amounts of money by telling its bank to send money to a particular account at another bank. The sending bank instructs the Federal Reserve, operator of the electronic payment system known as the Fed wire, to transfer money from its reserve account at the Fed to another bank which also maintains a reserve account at the Fed. The Fed charges for each transaction, and the banks pass that cost on to their customers.
That system has worked well enough to handle 65 million transactions worth $192 billion last year, up from 4.3 million transactions and $7.2 billion in 1970. But the Fed system, like the payment systems in other financial centers like London and Tokyo, operates only during the business hours of its local market.
Ms. Bedell of Bankers Trust said the Chicago Options Clearing Corporation would soon consider a rule to allow its members to use the fund.
She also expects similar Bankers Trust funds for Japanese yen and British pounds to commence operations soon. When the yen and pound funds become available, shareholders should be able to trade foreign exchange at any time by advertising their desire on an electronic bulletin board, and negotiating a trade with a shareholder in a fund denominated in another currency.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
A Bright Outlook For Reinsurers

BYLINE: By Kurt Eichenwald

 BODY:
WITH hurricanes, a typhoon and a riot, this has not been a great year for the insurance industry. So, as the claims stemming from all these troubles weigh down insurers and reinsurers, who would pick now to take one of these companies public?
Try Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts & Company, the New York buyout firm that purchased the American Re Corporation two months ago in a $1.4 billion buyout. The timing of the offering of 24 percent of the reinsurance company, which essentially acts as an insurer for insurance companies, is not as irrational as it might appear. Indeed, Kohlberg, Kravis may be muttering thanks for the natural disasters, because they seem to have improved the prospects for higher profits. 
 The planned offering, which is expected to raise about $301 million, marks the fastest Kohlberg, Kravis has ever gone to the public markets with any company it has purchased. Usually, the firm's practice is to take a company private using a little equity and lots of debt, spend years investing in it and improving its value, and then sell a portion back to the public at a huge profit. That is how the firm scored home runs with Duracell International and Motel 6, among others.
But by turning to the public markets about two months after buying American Re from the Aetna Life and Casualty Company, Kohlberg, Kravis is taking advantage of a window of profitability opened in the wake of Hurricane Andrew and other recent catastrophes.
The reason? The fallout from the disasters has resulted in the biggest shake-up in the reinsurance industry in more than five years, leading many analysts to predict that the industry, which has been suffering through a long recession, is poised for explosive growth.
American Re said just that in the offering documents it filed with the Securities and Exchange Commission this week. The company noted that several factors, including the impact on the industry of Hurricanes Andrew and Iniki, the Los Angeles riots and a typhoon in Guam, "may favorably influence property and casualty pricing, as well as reinsurance buying trends, in the future."
For proof, just look at the stock prices of other reinsurance companies, which have been booming in the last three months. Shares in the General Re Corporation, for example, have climbed about 25 percent since August, closing on Wednesday at $111 a share, up $2.125, on the New York Stock Exchange.
The boom stems from the heavy disaster-related claims that have hit the insurance and reinsurance industries. This year, claims for damages from various catastrophes have reached $17 billion, according to Gary Kerney, the director of catastrophe services for Property Claims Services, which monitors the totals for insurance claims.
This has had an impact on pricing, the strength of competition in the reinsurance industry and the number of customers and the volume of business they demand.
"The losses will do what we refer to as harden the market," said Frank Nutter, president of the Reinsurance Association of America in Washington. "It will cause some of the soft pricing that has existed for several years to be dropped. So these catastrophes have an almost counterintuitive effect on values."
But the price increases are coming too late for some in the industry. A number of reinsurers, battered by heavy losses, have begun to trim their sails, essentially ceding part of their business to stronger competitors. For example, the Scandia Group Insurance Company recently announced plans to cut back on its volume of reinsurance business, and other companies have tried unsuccessfully to sell their reinsurers outright.
That means that some business is up for grabs. And considering that reinsurers tend to retain their clients for a long time, any new business gained in this difficult year means stronger profits in years when the claims go back to normal levels.
The disasters have also shaken up the insurance companies that are the reinsurers' customers, industry experts said. In recent years, as insurance companies have been faced with numerous losses in their real estate and junk bond investments, some have decreased their costs by doing without reinsurance. Essentially, the companies decided they could assume more risk for a time until their financial picture looked better.
In the wake of the catastrophes, however, this attitude is changing. More and more insurers are actively searching out reinsurance to help lighten the risk load for fear of another big disaster.
 With all of those factors converging, the Kohlberg, Kravis decision to make a public offering opens up an opportunity for American Re: the ability to write more policies. Under insurance company rules, companies can write policies in direct proportion to their leverage. That means that the higher the debt, the fewer policies that can be issued.
So, with this public offering -- which the S.E.C. document says will decrease the reinsurer's debt to $875 million, from $1.1 billion, -- Kohlberg, Kravis is essentially getting out of the way to let one of its properties catch what experts predict could be a tidal wave of business.
