HEADLINE: Books of The Times;
A Daunting Father, A Brother's Shadow

BYLINE: By MICHIKO KAKUTANI

 BODY:

J. F. K.
Reckless Youth
By Nigel Hamilton
Illustrated. 898 pages. Random House. $30.
The outline of John Fitzgerald Kennedy's early years is well known to most Americans: the love-hate relationship with his domineering father, Joseph P. Kennedy; the privileged childhood in Boston and New York; the years of academic promise at Harvard; the harrowing survival of the sinking of PT 109 during World War II, and the successful run for Congress in 1946. 
 Now, in "Reckless Youth," the first installment of a projected three-volume biography, the British biographer Nigel Hamilton tries to fill in the specifics of that outline. Despite the author's credentials (Mr. Hamilton's life of Field Marshal Bernard L. Montgomery won the Whitbread Award in 1981), "J. F. K" often reads like a trashy novel, one of those epic family sagas peopled with villains and heroes, and animated by stark, primordial emotions. The writing is frequently sensationalistic ("Kennedy then entered Swanson's suite at the Hotel Poinciana and raped her," he writes of Joseph P. Kennedy's first night with Gloria Swanson); the insights into the familial psychology of the Kennedys are crudely and superficially Freudian.
What overcomes these highly noticeable flaws is the sheer accumulation of detail Mr. Hamilton has amassed: his minute, often day-by-day chronicle of young Kennedy's life is based on 2,000 interviews and access to previously unpublished documents, and it slowly but steadily steamrollers over the reader's doubts.
As depicted by Mr. Hamilton, Jack Kennedy emerges as a charismatic but conflicted young man, physically hampered by a frail physique and emotionally crippled by his childhood in a dysfunctional family, a childhood that would leave him detached, wary of intimacy and unable to form lasting attachments to women.
His mother, Rose, comes across, in Mr. Hamilton's telling, as a chilly, remote woman, obsessed with the Roman Catholic Church, personal hygiene and designer clothing, and incapable of giving her children the affection they craved. Her husband is painted in even darker colors: in this volume, he resembles a malevolent movie Godfather, or the nefarious character played by John Huston in "Chinatown."
Using adjectives like "ignoble," "ruthless" and "odious" and nouns like "coward" and "blackmailer" to describe him, Mr. Hamilton dwells, at length, on Joseph Kennedy's manipulation of the stock market, his promiscuity, and his obsession with money and power. Mr. Hamilton's account of the elder Kennedy's advocacy of appeasement of Nazi Germany -- while serving as the United States Ambassador to Britain -- is thoroughly damning, as is his depiction of Joseph Kennedy's frequent use of payoffs and bribes. He also contends that Joseph Kennedy retained private detectives to keep tabs on his children, and that he frequently propositioned his daughters' friends. At one point, Mr. Hamilton goes so far as to compare the elder Kennedy to Stalin, noting that they "even shared the same first name."
It is the young Jack Kennedy's herculean efforts to come to terms with this daunting father that form the dramatic focus of "J. F. K.," as well as the impetus behind the fledgling politician's eventual drive toward worldly achievement.
In his earliest years, Jack's pursuit of his father's approval tended to take the form of competition with his older brother, Joe, whose untimely death in a World War II flying mission would later lead to Jack's assumption of the family's dynastic ambitions. Whereas Joe Jr., Mr. Hamilton reports, dutifully conformed to his parent's wishes -- he was neat, orderly and responsible, and later slavishly echoed his father's most reprehensible political views -- Jack was more inclined to stand politely apart. As a teen-ager, Jack was sloppy and careless, relying upon his easy charm and wit to ingratiate himself with others; and while he eagerly emulated his father's hectic pursuit of women, he was more cautious when it came to embracing his ideological stands.
Although Jack, like his father, began by espousing a doctrine of defeatism -- an unsigned editorial he wrote for The Harvard Crimson in the fall of 1939 naively expressed the hope that Hitler could be made to disarm -- Mr. Hamilton argues that he gradually came to realize the dangers of isolationism and became an interventionist before Pearl Harbor.
"While his elder brother merely parroted their father," Mr. Hamilton writes, "Jack's political grappling was his own, and all the more moving because, symbolized in his struggle to confront the underlying issues facing Western democracy and his own country in the 'fog of war,' there was too the age-old struggle of a son seeking to emerge from his father's shadow."
That shadow, of course, would be difficult to escape. According to Mr. Hamilton, Joseph Kennedy helped squash Jack's affair with the Danish journalist Inga Arvad (who seems to have been the first woman to capture Jack's heart); he helped Jack secure a PT boat assignment, despite his sickly physique, and he spent an estimated $250,000 to $500,000 to try to buy Jack's election to Congress (although many observers believed Jack could have won on his natural political gifts alone).
In the end, Mr. Hamilton argues, Jack Kennedy's contentious relationship with his father shaped not only his ambitions, but also his deepest beliefs. "It would be fanciful to suggest that young Jack Kennedy's political philosophy was rooted in his servility at home," he writes. "Yet to ignore the parallel would be to miss the key to Jack's political heart."
Although he had led a sheltered life of privilege, although he had never had any firsthand experience of economic hardship or political oppression, Mr. Hamilton writes, Jack Kennedy "had experienced oppression of another kind, in the tyrannical, Stalinesque figure of a father he both loved and resented." The result, glimpsed years later in his concern with civil rights and his ardent opposition to the brutalities of the Soviet regime abroad, was "the one moral crusade that stirred his soul: the defense of freedom."

HEADLINE: Immigration Reform Holds Key to Economy;
Easy to Get In

 BODY:
To the Editor:
It is deeply alarming. Hordes of illegal immigrants are flooding New York City. The Immigration and Naturalization Service may even be collaborating, for a man from Java boasted to me that upon arriving at Kennedy Airport an official simply told him to "keep moving." A Mexican youth also informed me that he had come all the way from Mexico City by train without a ticket and was never once challenged by either a conductor or border guards.
The quiescence of unions is also extraordinary. Several months ago The Atlantic published a fine article on the impact of illegal immigration upon Los Angeles, "Black vs. Brown." The writer reported that the black hospital workers' union had been smashed, with the former employees replaced by Mexican illegals. 
 Mexico has adequate reasons for encouraging flight. The Mexico City population, 7 million in 1970, is now 20 million and will reach 28 million by the end of the decade. The city's water supply and waste disposal are both already inadequate.
What will be the impact, in a few years, of a half-million and more illegal and illiterate immigrants on our battered city?
 
SIDNEY SHANKER
Forest Hills, Queens, Nov. 10, 1992

HEADLINE: At the Bar;
At Columbia Law School, an uncommon ebullience and evangelism at the top.

BYLINE: By David Margolick

 BODY:
For as long as anyone can remember, Columbia Law School has been regarded as a very prestigious, very dull and very unpleasant place to be. Students are happy to get in, happy to get out and say they went there and unhappy about almost everything in between. Then the school named Lance Liebman as dean.
Mr. Liebman, 51 years old, has been at Columbia's helm for barely a year, hardly enough time to change an institution's personality. Indeed, maybe no law school can ever be fun, least of all one in New York to which people often repair only after rejections from Cambridge, New Haven or Palo Alto, sites of what are generally rated as the nation's three top schools. 
 But under Mr. Liebman, a weary refugee from war-torn Harvard, something unprecedented has come to Morningside Heights: joie de vivre. Surely no one -- not Harlan Fiske Stone, not Charles Evans Hughes, not any of the other deans whose portraits hang grimly on the office walls -- can have approached the job with as much energy and ebullience.
Mr. Liebman gossips with elan, has a goofy and infectious laugh and exhibits a sense of humor worthy of Calvin Trillin's second cousin. He drops names aplenty, but naturally and inoffensively. Law professors, judges, journalists, politicians -- Mr. Liebman knows them all, either from his days in P.S. 89 in Elmhurst, Queens, or as chairman of the Yale Daily News, president of the Harvard Law Review, law clerk to Justice Byron R. White or adviser to Mayor John V. Lindsay of New York. His Rolodex must rival that of Bill Clinton -- whom, miraculously, he has never managed to meet, at least not yet.
In contrast to his predecessor, Barbara Black, whose only visibility during her six-year tenure came when her appointment as Columbia's first female dean was announced, Mr. Liebman is suddenly ubiquitous. He evangelizes endlessly for Columbia, whether before groups of Federal judges on the shores of Lake George or over lunches with Columbia graduates at New York's 30 largest law firms, with barristers in London or with bengoshi in Tokyo.
The Liebman administration can already point to several achievements: some new and unorthodox faculty appointments; increased fund-raising among alumni; a requirement that students perform 40 hours of public-service work in their last two years and participate in an experimental program on professional responsibility. Mr. Liebman has also begun the latest and most ambitious plan to renovate the school's congenitally uncongenial building, the late architect Wallace K. Harrison's curse on generations of Columbia law students.
Many graduates consider the building so dreadful that it should be razed, that renovations should begin at ground zero. But even with his formidable fund-raising skills, Mr. Liebman views this option as unrealistic under current economic conditions. Instead, there are plans to fix up the entrance and build a student lounge -- in Mr. Liebman's words, "a place with a glass roof and a cappuccino machine."
At the same time, his goals include getting students out of the awful building more often. Even though New York is the capital of the American legal community, Mr. Liebman complained, Columbia has historically turned its back on -- or its nose up at -- the place. Another aim is to expand career options, so the school stops sending so many of its own to the large law firms, only to watch them leave disillusioned a few years later.
For all his efforts to seem solemn and speak like a dean -- "It could hurt Columbia Law School if I said something stupid," he explained -- Mr. Liebman is a naturally extroverted, exuberant sort. At least part of his enthusiasm may really be just relief over his daring escape from Harvard Law School, where he spent 24 years.
Nothing pleases Mr. Liebman so much as contrasting his old institution with his new one. He brandished a copy of Robert Clark's recent letter to Harvard alumni, in which the Harvard dean described sit-ins in faculty offices, apologized for the "thoughtless and cruel" parody of a murdered law professor's work published last year by the Harvard Law Review, and announced the creation of a panel intended to create a stronger sense of community at the school.
By contrast, in his letter to Columbia alumni Mr. Liebman recounted how "grand" his first year had been. Judging from it, the greatest crisis he faced came last June, when the Latin word for "dean" was misspelled on all 350 diplomas.
Mr. Liebman was passed over for the Harvard deanship in 1989. It was perhaps his first professional setback, and, as it turned out, a great professional break, for it left him available when Columbia came calling.
Some people find New York cold and brutal, but Mr. Liebman is finding it positively placid. "Only someone coming from Harvard Law School," he said, "would view New York as a haven of civility."

LENGTH: 311 words

HEADLINE: Help the U.N. Arm for Peace

 BODY:
When warring clans cause starvation in Somalia or Serbia invades Bosnia and threatens Kosovo, the American impulse is to call in the United Nations, not send in the Marines. But the U.N. has no military forces -- except those its members are willing to provide ad hoc. And it has no standing capacity to transport or pay them.
The U.N.'s ability to promote peace needs to be strengthened by placing standing forces, and funds, at its disposal. And it needs a capacity to act before wars break out, through preventive deployments. 
 Secretary General Boutros Boutros-Ghali is seeking just such a capability. But the U.S. has been slow to respond. Indeed, the Pentagon shudders at the thought of placing personnel under a U.N. command.
Now an independent commission offers four sensible recommendations on how the U.S. might organize itself to provide American military muscle to U.N. missions:
 Establish a new U.S. military command to plan for and train troops for U.N. operations.
 Have the Pentagon designate one or two U.S.-based brigades for such contingencies.
 Shift funding for U.N. peacekeeping operations from the State Department's budget to the Pentagon's.
 Put two high-ranking officials, an Under Secretary of State and an Under Secretary of Defense, in charge of America's involvement in U.N. and other international peacekeeping and peacemaking operations.
The study commission, co-sponsored by the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace and the Institute for International Economics, consisted of 30 distinguished former Federal officials of both parties. They know the territory; they also have the right idea.
By giving strong backing to these proposals, President-elect Clinton could help himself, and the world, by making it easier to arm the U.N. to intervene in places like Kosovo before conflict turns to war.

HEADLINE: More Cuts Expected Soon at Digital

BYLINE: By GLENN RIFKIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MAYNARD, Mass., Nov. 25

 BODY:
The Digital Equipment Corporation is preparing to accelerate its layoffs before the end of the year, as the company attempts to rebound from billions of dollars in losses.
While a Digital spokeswoman would not confirm the exact number of layoffs that are planned, people inside Digital said this week that by Dec. 7, at least 7,500 and perhaps closer to 8,800 more employees would be laid off worldwide, with a significant percentage of those being cut in Massachusetts and New Hampshire where more than half of the company's American employees live and work. 
 Digital, the nation's second-largest computer maker, currently employs a total of 108,500 in the United States and abroad.
 
'It's Painful'
 Robert B. Palmer, Digital's president and chief executive, told stockholders at the company's annual meeting in November that the company would be aggressively cutting costs and increasing the number of layoffs to get its costs in line. The company laid off 5,300 last quarter.
But while Mr. Palmer instructed company managers to accelerate the cutting, many had been hesitant to comply. With the deadline looming, thousands of employees are expected to get termination notices in the next week or so.
"It's painful," one Digital manager said. "But Palmer has been telling the managers that either 20,000 to 30,000 people feel the pain, or 110,000 people will feel the pain."
Digital employs more than 26,000 workers in Massachusetts and New Hampshire, and more layoffs are likely to depress the region's economy further. In the middle- and upper-middle-class communities between Routes 128 and 495 that are dotted with Digital plants and offices, this holiday season is filled with a sense of anguish.
"We have a number of families with more than one member working at Digital," said Pastor Richard Leavitt of the Boxborough, Mass., United Church of Christ. "The constant anxiety about being laid off is taking a tremendous toll on people's lives."
 
'Dreadful to Watch'
 Don Johnson, town manager in Acton, Mass., where many Digital employees live, said, "When Digital is hurting, business is hurting and the town is hurting."
Once a haven for lifetime employment, Digital, like I.B.M., is trying to survive the current industry slump with cuts and organizational changes. The inner turmoil at Digital has never been higher. "People are terrified," one employee said. Even those who manage to survive this round know more layoffs will almost certainly follow.
"It's absolutely dreadful to watch what is happening at Digital," said David Smith, a consultant at the International Data Corporation. He noted that much of the turmoil was being caused by the way some departments are handling the cutbacks.
"Some are very humane and others are awful," he said.
 
Deep Uncertainty
 Mr. Smith said cuts have been ordered across the board, but in some groups, employees simply do not know who is going and who is staying. Some are being told that parts of their group might be salvaged if another group takes them in. But such vague promises only heighten the anxiety.
In addition, Mr. Smith said, rumors abound that Digital's generous severance package will be cut sharply or altogether after this round of layoffs. "People don't know whether they want to stay or go now," he said.
The layoffs were not unexpected. Digital has already pared more than 25,000 employees over the last two and a half years through early-retirement incentives, layoffs and attrition. But that has not been nearly enough, in light of the stagnating economy and Digital's own problems selling its computer systems. Analysts say Digital needs to slim down to the size of the Hewlett-Packard Corporation, a competitor with similar revenues but nearly 20,000 fewer employees.
 
'Exodus of Software Engineers'
 Mr. Palmer acknowledged in an interview in October that 25,000 more layoffs were likely over the next two years. At the end of the fiscal first quarter, Sept. 30, the company had $1.3 billion in reserve to cover the costs of the cutbacks. Digital is also in the midst of a sweeping restructuring, with many departments being consolidated down to 8 to 10 business units and countless projects being cut.
At Mr. Palmer's behest, significant cutbacks are being made in the company's $1.8 billion engineering budget, particularly in the software development area. "They are expecting an exodus of software engineers from Spitbrook and Nashua," said Terry Shannon, a consultant with Gander Resources, referring to Digital sites in New Hampshire. He said Digital was apparently abandoning many development projects for its VAX computer and VMS software product lines.
George Colony, a consultant with Forrester Research in Cambridge, Mass., has watched the company cut costs before. "It feels different this time," he said, "because they are no longer cutting the fat, the nonperformers; they are cutting into the muscle.
"But this is Palmer's charter. This is what the board told him to do. It's a General Motors-type of situation: they've got to cut deep, hard and fast."

HEADLINE: Q & A

BYLINE: By Leonard Sloane

 BODY:

 Higher Guaranteed Interest
Q Now that returns on many certificates of deposit have fallen to the 3 percent level, where can investors get a better rate of interest but still get a guaranteed return of their money?
 
WILL BRIDGER, HILTON HEAD, S.C.
Treasury bills are short-term Government securities that often yield as much as or more than most short-term certificates of deposit. William K. Joseph, a senior vice president of Shearson Lehman Brothers, said: "They are safer than any bank C.D.'s and free from state and local taxes. But if you sell a bill before maturity, there is the potential for either gain or loss." 
 Treasury obligations of one to five years are also competitive with C.D. rates and should be considered as alternatives, Mr. Joseph noted. "In addition, you can purchase more than $100,000 worth of Treasuries without being concerned about Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation limits. Finally the Government will hold your Treasury securities for you at no charge," he said.
Banks and brokerage firms usually charge a small fee to purchase Treasury securities for their customers. But you can get them without any commission at a Federal Reserve bank or branch.
 
Guardian for Children
Q When our children were young, my wife and I designated a guardian in our wills to manage our estates on their behalf. Now the children are moving into their 20's and we wonder if a guardian is still needed?
 
HOYT SPELMAN 3d, MANHATTAN
A Many people name a guardian in their wills for their minor children, just as you did. Tricia Bonner, a partner in the New York law firm of Thacher Proffitt & Wood, said: "If the children are adults at the time of your death -- in New York State, the age of majority is 18 -- the guardianship provision has no effect. Therefore it is not necessary to write a new will or codicil to revoke that provision. Once a child becomes an adult, he or she would have full control over any inheritance unless you take legal steps to prevent it, such as creating a trust."
 
Tax on Fund's Dividends
Q If you invest in a mutual fund and your dividends are automatically reinvested, must you pay income tax on the dividends each year or only when you sell the shares?
 
LEONARD KRESCH, SEARINGTON, L.I.
A You pay income tax on the dividends only once. "You must pay income taxes on these dividends each year, whether they are withdrawn or automatically reinvested, because the income is available to you," said Jacob S. Dreyer, chief economist of the Investment Company Institute. "From a Tax Code point of view, automatic reinvestment is equivalent to withdrawing the dividends and investing them again."
However, when mutual fund shares are sold, investors may incur a capital gain or a capital loss, depending on whether the sales price is above or below the cost of acquisition. "You have to pay taxes on capital gains, unless you have other losses elsewhere that can be used to offset the gains on the mutual fund shares," he added.

HEADLINE: Shoppers Fill Stores, but Many Are Just Looking

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM

 BODY:
Glenda Harry arrived at the A & S Plaza in Manhattan yesterday at 8:30 A.M. But she hadn't come to do her Christmas shopping; she came to sign up for a contest to win $1,000, sponsored by the mall's operator, the Simon Management Company.
On the day after Thanksgiving, the busiest shopping day of the year, consumers thronged shopping areas from Herald Square in New York to State Street in Chicago to Union Square in San Francisco. Although many were just looking, strolling through stores, checking prices and searching for gift ideas and good deals, retailers were delighted and relieved by the turnout. 
 The period between Thanksgiving and Christmas is crucial to retailers. Many stores make as much as 40 percent of their annual sales and 50 percent of profits during those 20-odd days.
 
Much Window Shopping
 Retailers said that the holiday-shopping crowds were bigger than they had been in the last two or three years and that shoppers seemed to be spending more than in the past. But most retailers will not total up the actual sales figures until Sunday or Monday.
In spite of retailers' threats that lean inventories will lead to early sellouts of popular items this year, few people seemed in any rush yesterday to buy gifts. The number of people toting shopping bags was only a fraction of the number who turned out to window- shop. And many of those who were buying were doing so at discount stores or at sales.
Despite recent signs of an economy edging upward, many shoppers said they would still be spending less on gifts this year, placing strict limits on their purchases, as they have in recent years, or buying household necessities instead of frivolities.
"Things are so tight these days you have to cut back," said Miss Harry, an accountant. "I have a job, I have my health, I have strength. But I have a lot of friends who are out of work with trouble putting food on the table, and that makes me think twice about spending a lot of money."
Not everyone, of course, is cautious. Some people questioned yesterday said they would spend about as much as they did last year, and a few even said they would spend more. And some retailers said they saw increased sales of higher-priced merchandise and more buying with credit cards.
Pat Doyle finished her Christmas shopping at 11 A.M. yesterday. Standing outside the newly renovated Marshall Field's flagship on State Street in Chicago, she estimated that she had spent 20 percent more this year. "I don't like shopping with crowds, so I started three weeks ago," she said.
She said the state of the economy was not an issue for her. "My husband owns his own company, and he has done well this year," Mrs. Doyle said.
Most retailers believe sales will increase this holiday season anywhere from 4 to 6 percent, compared with little more than 1 percent last year, but all are quick to hedge their projections because consumers have fooled them so many times over the last two years. Although sales surged in late September and October, no one is quite sure that the buoyancy will continue through the holiday season. Sales also picked up steam in January and February before stalling out in the spring and summer.
 
'Started With a Bang'>
 Merchants took heart yesterday, though, as they described the season's kickoff, with notes of enthusiasm creeping into their voices for the first time in two years.
"It started with a bang," said Ken Dardis, the operational vice president for Home Depot's stores in the Northeast. "Today was an absolute zoo; by 9 o'clock, the stores were just rocking."
Clark Johnson, the president and chief executive of Pier 1 Imports, a specialty store chain selling housewares, said sales yesterday might exceed last year's day-after-Thanksgiving sales by 15 percent. He said the average sale would also be somewhat higher than last year because shoppers were buying more expensive merchandise.
The co-chairman of R. H. Macy & Company, Myron E. Ullman 3d, said in a telephone interview from the Macy's store at Roosevelt Field on Long Island that shoppers were using the Macy's charge card to make purchases more than any time in recent memory. "We saw the uptick start in October and it has continued, which means consumers think things are likely to get somewhat better," he said.
Mr. Ullman said strong sales of more expensive pots and pans, knives, china and crystal also indicated better consumer confidence levels. Macy has planned on flat sales this holiday season, but Mr. Ullman said the company was "cautiously optimistic" that it would exceed its own projections.
Thomas Shea, district manager of the Kmart stores on Long Island and in the boroughs of New York City, interpreted heavy sales of higher-priced merchandise like 14-carat jewelry and compact disk players as a sign of more optimism among consumers.
He has a less orthodox method of measuring his customers' mood: "When customers aren't feeling good about things, they'll pick an item up and throw it down so that by the end of the day, the store looks like a cyclone hit it," Mr. Shea said. "The stores I've been in today didn't get so wrecked. It'll be an easy recovery job tonight."
Retail consultants and analysts predict that off-price stores like Men's Wearhouse and discounters will get the lion's share of business this holiday season because shoppers are so sensitive to prices. Tactical Retail Solutions, a retail consulting firm, has estimated that discounters like Wal-Mart and Target will see a 6 percent increase, while off-price outlets will see as much as a 7 percent increase in their business. In contrast, the company projects that specialty stores that sell apparel may see only a 1 percent sales increase and department stores a 2 percent gain.
 
Pattern in Detroit
 Foot traffic in the malls around Detroit yesterday morning and early afternoon reflected that pattern. The Somerset Collection, a cluster of 70 stores like Barney's, Tiffany & Company, Saks Fifth Avenue and Neiman Marcus in the Detroit suburb of Troy, had fewer shoppers a couple of hours after it opened than it does on a normal weekday. Parking was available as close as four spaces away from the main entrance.
But at a Kmart store in Troy, shoppers were lined up outside before the 7 A.M. opening time, and by 7:15 the large parking lot was half full of cars.
Five minutes after the opening, Lloyd Wold sailed out pushing a cart filled with a 20-inch General Electric color television set he bought for $199 during a sale that was on between 7 to 11 A.M. He and his wife, Pat, used a credit card to pay for the television set because they could not afford to spend cash on such a large purchase.
"We just don't have that kind of money," Mrs. Wold said. "Just buying food is hard."
Union Square in San Francisco had a somewhat better experience than the Somerset mall in Troy. By 9 A.M. bright red Macy's bags were bobbing about the square, and shoppers queued up at F.A.O. Schwarz and Gump's, a specialty store stocked with pricey Oriental tchotchkes, fine jewelry and other exotic gifts.
Asked what people are buying this year, Becky Stein, the general manager at F.A.O. Schwarz, grabbed a three-foot-tall stuffed gorilla by a furry brow. "Big and inexpensive," she said. "We're selling lots of these."
 
Christmas Gift Budget
 Bruce and Mindy Walker ventured into San Francisco from nearby Richmond for a special holiday shopping spree. Although the shops and stores that line Union Square are far from inexpensive, Mrs. Walker said she would hold to a budget of $500 to $600 for Christmas gifts, about the same as she spent last year. "We're not going lavish," she vowed.
The Walkers said they planned to buy practical gifts like kitchen utensils and socks. "We just wanted to see how the other half lives," Mr. Walker said.
Merchants have gone all out this year to emphasize their low prices. Large inserts in newspapers touted Thanksgiving sales, television commercials hawked "holiday specials" and display tables and racks were crowned with signs heralding lower-than-normal prices.
And shoppers will turn out for a bargain. Pat Timm, a payroll clerk from Roseville, Mich., rolled into the Oakland Mall near Detroit in a wheelchair, undaunted by the casts that encased her legs after recent surgery.
 
Not Charging This Year
 "I don't miss a day-after-Thanksgiving sale," she said. "They've got these specials that they don't have at any other time."
She said she would spend less this year because she was insecure about her job. "I usually charge the purchases, but not this year," Mrs. Timm said. "I'm going to spend cash because I don't want to get into trouble."
While most shoppers at the start of this holiday season seemed to feel more secure, if not optimistic, about their prospects, several told the now-familiar, heart-wrenching stories of lives wrecked by the realignments shaking the economy. Santa Claus will not arrive in every home this year.
Vivian Martin, a data processor who lost her job in August, headed for Marshall Field's in Chicago because the retailer was handing out coupons worth 10 percent off items that were previously discounted 20 and 30 percent. Her husband, Preston, is also unemployed, and the toys and clothes she bought for her 4-year-old son cost less than $100.
"My husband and I haven't budgeted anything for gifts for one another," Mrs. Martin said. "If we do, it will be something small, pretty insignificant."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Crowded sidewalks yesterday on Broadway at Macy's in Herald Square marked the traditional first shopping day of the Christmas season. (Lee Romero/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: U.S. PLAN TO GUARD CONVOYS IS BACKED BY SOMALI GENERAL

BYLINE: By JANE PERLEZ, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOGADISHU, Somalia, Nov. 27

 BODY:
In an uncharacteristically conciliatory gesture, the leader of one of the principal factions in the fighting in Somalia said today that he welcomed an American initiative to send thousands of troops to protect food convoys for the starving.
The faction leader, Gen. Mohammed Farah Aidid, who in the last year has sent his fighters across central and southern Somalia and who had threatened to send United Nations troops home in body bags, said at a news conference here that he viewed the American move as "constructive." 
 At the same time, he said, "military intervention will not solve the problem." He emphasized the economic and political benefits that he said would accrue from an American presence in a country devastated by clan warfare and a famine that relief officials estimated has killed 300,000 people since January 1991, when the Government of Mohammed Siad Barre was toppled.
 
'We Will Protect Them'
 The main obstacle to improving the appalling plight of nearly two million hungry Somalis has been the looting by clan gangs, many of them led by General Aidid's men, of food destined for the starving.
If General Aidid does not challenge the American military, United Nations and other officials here said the task of opening the port and airport of Mogadishu for food deliveries and protecting convoys would be made much simpler.
They said that if the general did not resist, 5,000 rather than tens of thousands of troops would be sufficient to do the job.
[The size and composition of the force envisioned by Washington remained unclear, Bush Administration officials said. Also undetermined was whether the United Nations and the United States' allies would subscribe to the plan, which would place the Somali operation firmly under American control. Page 6.]
Stephen Tomlin, the field director of the International Medical Corps, a Los Angeles-based agency, said General Aidid's chief financier, Osman Ato, came to Mr. Tomlin's Mogadishu house this morning to tell him that the general and his advisers had been up all night debating the issue of the American troops.
"He said, 'Please reassure your colleagues in the non-governmental organizations not to be concerned, we will protect them,' " Mr. Tomlin recounted. "And he asked me to get a message to Ambassador Peter de Vos in Washington, who has been covering Somalia, to say that the general welcomed the Americans."
 
High Regard for Americans
 The nearly 400 foreign aid workers in Somalia, who have been striving under treacherous security conditions to feed the starving, appeared relieved that General Aidid went out of his way to pledge their safety. There had been fears among some workers that they might become targets of even more violence if the American troops were deployed against the general's will.
The reaction from the Aidid camp was interpreted by foreign officials and Somalis as a sign of his growing military and political weaknesses within the changing clan alliances in Somalia. Last month his forces were defeated at an important rural base, Bardera, and he appears about to lose control of a major coastal city, Kismayu.
They feared that General Aidid was becoming isolated as his alliances fell apart and that he probably looked at the Americans, whom he is said to hold in high regard, as his salvation.
Officials here said that General Aidid, who at best might have several thousand fighters armed with machine guns and some artillery, had no chance of obstructing the tens of thousands of well-equipped troops that the Bush Administration said might be sent to Somalia.
 
Fluctuating Death Rate
 A United Nations official said that a senior figure in General Aidid's group reacted to the American plan by saying: "We had better swim with the tide. We have no chance against 20,000 to 30,000 troops."
The White House decision to offer American soldiers to lead a beefed-up United Nations contingent was based in part on the premise that the death rate from starvation among Somalis was going up again, Administration officials said on Thursday.
But senior relief officials who have worked in Somalia throughout the crisis said the number of deaths had dropped, from thousands a day in May, June, July, August and September to hundreds a day.
That was because so many weak people had already died but also because, despite obstruction by looters, the relief effort of the last month had made a difference.
 
Corn Fields and High Hopes
 In Baidoa, the death rate reached a high of more than 300 a day in September. But it had dropped to fewer than 100 a day this month, Mr. Stocker said.
In Bardera, the death rate soared to more than 200 a day in September as thousands of destitute people struggled into the town from the countryside. The death rate went down in early October but soared again to more than 200 after General Aidid fled the city and aid workers evacuated. But two weeks ago, the daily death toll had dropped to just around 100 a day after a new airlift started, according to the Red Cross.
Mr. Stocker said the Red Cross feeds about 1.5 million Somalis every day at 900 kitchens. He disputed a contention by Andrew S. Natsios, the relief coordinator of the United States Agency for International Development, that there were many hidden deaths in areas not reached by aid agencies. Mr. Stocker said that Mr. Natsios's estimate that 80 percent of the donated food in the last month was looted or paid in protection money was overstated.
Like Mr. Stocker, Mr. Tomlin said the death rates were lower.
 
Breakdown in Security
 There was "hope in Somalia," he said. "There's corn high in the fields from Baidoa to the lower Shabelle Valley."
But at the same time, Mr. Tomlin said, an increase in attacks on food convoys and the closing of the Mogadishu port because of fighting had diminished the improvements.
"If something is not done by bringing troops we would lose the gains we have made so far," Mr. Tomlin said.
Mr. Tomlin said a breakdown in security was the main reason an ambitious plan by the United Nations to deliver 100,000 tons of food in 100 days had fallen miserably short.
 
Precarious Pakistani Troops
 "It is now Day 45 of the 100-day plan, and to date, 9,263 metric tons have come through the port of Mogadishu and nothing through the port of Kismayu," he said. "Much of this did not reach the intended recipients."
The plan to airlift 15,000 metric tons was progressing more successfully, he said.
What many aid and United Nations officials here do not understand is why Washington did not prod the United Nations to sharpen its flailing military operation in Mogadishu before making the quantum leap of committing large numbers of American troops.
The 500 Pakistani troops sent by the United Nations in early October to secure the airport, port and goods out of the capital had little hope of success, United Nations officials said.
The troops were too few, their equipment inadequate and they were so underfinanced that their commander, Gen. Imtiaz Shaheen, had to beg aid agencies of the United Nations, rather than the military arm, for money to help supply them, the official said. The troops, based near the airport, have become so vulnerable that snipers fired at their tents this week, a United Nations official said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The United Nations has deployed 500 Pakistani troops to secure the airport in Mogadishu, Somalia, where they are vulnerable to snipers. Maj. Saqlain Afzal, a Pakistani officer who was shot Wednesday, was recovering at a hospital yesterday in Nairobi, Kenya. (Fiona MacDougall for The New York Times); Gen. Mohammed Farah Aidid, right, a Somali factional leader who had once threatened U.N. troops, spoke in Mogadishu in support of the United States' sending troops to protect food convoys. SECTION: Section 1; Page 1; Column 3; National Desk

LENGTH: 1292 words

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Glancing Over His Shoulder, Clinton Watches Perot Tide

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
Just as Ross Perot managed to reshape the 1992 Presidential campaign merely by hovering around its edges, his shadow is now influencing the emerging strategy of the Clinton Administration.
Mr. Perot is back in Texas, quiet for the moment, and the potential power of his backers, who are reorganizing themselves, is still uncertain. But aides to President-elect Bill Clinton are acutely aware that one in five voters chose Mr. Perot, whose backers have already warned that they intend to lobby the Clinton White House for their programs. 
 With that in mind, Mr. Clinton's team is tailoring parts of its public relations and governing strategy to win those voters.
"I can't believe I am talking about 1996 before Governor Clinton has even been inaugurated," said his communications director, George Stephanopoulos, "but assuming that 1996 will be a two-person race, then the Perot voters will be central to that election. They have to be part of our thinking. They will be the difference between winning and losing."
Considering that Mr. Clinton got 43 percent of the vote, Mr. Bush 39 percent and Mr. Perot 19 percent, said David Wilhelm, director of political affairs for Mr. Clinton's transition team, "it is an important political objective of ours to reach out to those folks in the coming months."
Reaching them, though, may not be so easy, because Clinton and Perot advisers have very different views about how to attract the Perot constituency: the hard-core followers who are trying to create a political organization as well as the millions of sympathizers whose political affiliation and future involvement are uncertain.
Clinton aides say that to transform those voters into Clinton supporters in four years, the President-elect will use a variety of Perot-style techniques, from town hall meetings to toll-free phone numbers, to appeal to their appreciation for unmediated, direct contact with decision makers.
At the same time, the aides say, Mr. Clinton will heavily stress issues that appeal to the hunger of Perot voters to "clean up the mess" in Washington -- matters like overhauling campaign finance laws and limiting special-interest lobbying.
Mr. Clinton wants to adopt some of these techniques and tackle some of these issues for his own reasons, partly to get around the news media and partly because the issues are ones he cares about. But they are being given added attention because of the Perot factor, aides say.
While Mr. Clinton intends to focus on reducing the deficit and national debt, Mr. Perot's primary issues, he is still open to the possibility of increasing the deficit in the short term to stimulate the economy and seems uncertain about how quickly and deeply he intends to cut the national debt.
But the Clinton team believes that for Perot voters these issues are symbols of the mess in Washington. They say they can avoid taking them on immediately, provided they convey a sense that they are energetically and effectively addressing national problems like health care, job growth or welfare.
 
Dispute on U.S. Debt
 Mr. Perot was not available over the long holiday weekend to discuss the Clinton plan. But his supporters strongly disagree with the President-elect's advisers. The Perot supporters argue that the $4 trillion national debt and the soaring deficit are not just symbols of government in gridlock; they view those problems almost theologically -- like elements of a looming apocalypse that could undermine the foundations of the republic if not immediately addressed.
"You can't pay them off just by going after lobbyists or the Japanese," said James Squires, Mr. Perot's former spokesman. "You have to go after the deficit and debt. If you look at the Perot coalition you find that it contained people from the far left and far right. They were a political zoo representing every trend. We found that the one thing, the only thing, that they could all agree on was dealing now with the debt and the deficit. Trying to attract them with anything else will be folly."
Mr. Squires said Mr. Perot's followers were organizing themselves into a formal political movement, "United We Stand," which will have a full-time staff and dues-paying members. Its first order of business, he said, will be to help Mr. Clinton get the Congress to make tough choices.
If there is not "a legitimate effort at fair-shared sacrifice to deal with the debt and the deficit," Mr. Squires said, Mr. Perot and United We Stand will try to influence the 1994 Congressional elections by backing candidates who share those priorities.
Which is why Clinton aides say that they are interested not only in appealing to the Perot voters as future Democrats but also, in the short run, in harnessing their energy for Mr. Clinton's own agenda.
"These people are alienated and angry," Mr. Stephanopoulos said. "But they are not apathetic. The question is, How do you take the negative energy that motivated their vote for Perot and turn it into positive energy to help you govern?"
Mr. Clinton has not made up his mind about how soon and how aggressively to tackle deficit reduction; the question is whether the other policies he intends to offer will really attract the Perot following.
"I am of the view that Perot's specific policies were not that important," argued Stan Greenberg, Mr. Clinton's chief pollster. "Deficit reduction was symbolic for cleaning up the mess in Washington. That is why I don't think you have to take a Perot platform and simply graft it to President-elect Clinton's. He has a lot of room to maneuver -- as long as he is maneuvering forward and giving a sense that he is addressing and solving problems with a coherent plan and focus."
Clinton aides hope to convey that momentum through both style and substance.
"In terms of communications," Mr. Wilhelm said, "we have instituted within the transition operation a public outreach department, and a big part of that is to consciously reach out to Perot voters, and all voters who have not always felt that they have had access to the administration or the political process."
Clinton aides say one policy issue the President-elect has been stressing is overhauling campaign finance laws. That emphasis is based in part on the assumption that one of Mr. Perot's most evocative appeals was his complaint that decision-making in Washington had been hijacked by special-interest groups and lobbyists, who own the politicians.
Campaigning in Georgia for Senator Wyche Fowler Jr., who was defeated in a runoff on Tuesday, Mr. Clinton said, "People who come to work in my Administration are going to have to sign an agreement not to go to work for other interests and lobby their Government and make a profit out of their public service."
 
Nature of the Protest
 Another substantive appeal to the Perot vote, Clinton aides say, is simply getting something done -- anything -- that begins to improve the economy and gives a sense that the Congress-White House stalemate is being loosened.
"The Perot vote," Mr. Stephanopoulos said, "was as much about ending gridlock in Washington and simply getting action and reforming the system, as about any specific issue, such as deficit reduction."
Maybe, but maybe not, said John White, who had been the issues coordinator for Mr. Perot's campaign and who later went to work for Mr. Clinton.
"The Perot voters are people who are very skeptical and hard to convince," Mr. White said. "I don't mean this as criticism, but they tended to be a little naive. These are people who were attracted to simple solutions, and that is why the Perot thing worked. Appealing to them with anything other than black and white solutions for the debt and deficit is not going to be easy."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Economy
Consumers thronged to stores and malls on the first day of the crucial shopping season between Thanksgiving and Christmas. But despite the large crowds, there was a sense that people were not rushing to buy gifts. And many of those who did buy were doing business at discount stores or picking up items on sale. [Page 1.]
Americans' personal income grew 1 percent in October, the fastest rise in 10 months, and consumer spending posted a healthy gain of 0.7 percent. Analysts cautioned, however, that the figures overstated the rise because of a number of one-time factors. [33.]
 
Markets
Investors poured money into stock mutual funds in November, and the industry's net cash inflow in 1992 is likely to be its highest ever. Figures show that October was reasonably good for stock funds but the worst month in nearly two years for bond funds. [33.]
Stock prices moved higher, as Wall Street ended a week of solid gains. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 15.94 points, to 3,282.20, a gain of 54.84 for the week. A number of indexes that measure the broader market hit record highs. [33.]
Treasury bond prices fell in reaction to the unexpectedly strong rises in personal income and spending. But analysts cautioned that the thinness of post-holiday trading made for volatile prices, which may not indicate long-term market trends. [41.]
The dollar ended firmer in a quiet session, helped by both technical factors and fresh signs of economic strength. [42.]
Copper futures prices bounded higher. The value of copper often moves up with improvements in the economy.[42.]
 
International
Japan's Government said it would not relax its ban on rice imports. But then it sent a subtle signal to its rice farmers that protectionism that could harm the nation's trading relationships may not be sustainable. Japan's ban on rice imports could become an obstacle to a new global trading treaty. [33.]
OPEC reached a new production accord that provides for a cut in total output of perhaps 400,000 barrels daily, or about 2 percent. Oil prices are expected to inch a bit higher. The deal reflects the production of 12 members, as Ecuador withdrew from OPEC. [34.]
 
Companies
British Airways was given clearance by the European Community executive commission for its proposed purchase of a 49.9 percent stake in the small French regional carrier TAT. The British carrier agreed to conditions meant to assure that competition is not reduced by the linkup. [35.]
A former investment banker was acquitted of conspiracy and fraud charges arising out of the marathon Guinness fraud trial. The case against Lord Spens was one of several stemming from the 1986 takeover of a Scotch whisky company, Distillers, by the brewing giant Guinness. [35.]
The Dabah family filed petitions under Chapter 11 of the Federal Bankruptcy Code. The family is the largest shareholder in Gitano, although the clothing company itself was not involved in the bankruptcy filings. [35.]
The civil war in Yugoslavia has forced an end to production of the humble Yugo automobile, costing tens of thousands of jobs. Now the factory makes little more than rifles and pistols. [35.]
 
Your Money
Certain prosperous couples should use life insurance to insure that their heirs will have enough liquidity to pay estate taxes. This approach is particularly recommended for couples with estates in the range of $2 million to $3 million. [31.]
Monthly brokerage statements usually lack numbers that let an investor see instantly whether each security is a winner or a loser or that show the total cost of maintaining the account. [31.]
Medical bills should be scrutinized carefully. One report said that as many as 95 percent of hospital bills contained errors. [32.]

HEADLINE: Clinton Chats With Reagan, Then Heads Out to the Mall

BYLINE: By MICHAEL KELLY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Nov. 27

 BODY:
In a gesture of good manners that was also good politics, President-elect Bill Clinton came here today to drink coffee and chat with a man he spent the last year depicting as the founding father of just about everything wrong with America.
Mr. Clinton's visit with former President Ronald Reagan opened a four-day vacation trip to Southern California by the President-elect, his wife, Hillary, and their daughter, Chelsea, who will all spend the weekend at the Summerland house of the television producers Harry Thomason and Linda Bloodworth-Thomason. But it also set the tone for a day that Mr. Clinton's aides described as devoted to courtesy calls but that doubled as state-of-the-Presidential-art politicking. 
 After the meeting with Mr. Reagan, Mr. Clinton went on a publicity-minded mall hop that was remarkable even by the standards of suburban Southern California, the land that raised indoor shopping to the level of life style.
At the Glendale Galleria, a huge mall about 6 miles north of Los Angeles, tens of thousands of screaming, cheering people jammed two floors, leaving only a narrow corridor for Mr. Clinton and his entourage. The President-elect was equipped with a wireless microphone that broadcast on the mall's public address system the snippets of conversations he had with those who managed to squirm their way to the front of the rope lines.
 
Forgiving the Original Sinner
 Only a matter of weeks ago, candidate Clinton was denouncing Mr. Reagan as the architect of "failed trickle-down economics," the proponent of plutocratic politics and the original sinner of the Decade of Greed.
Today, all was sunshine and no hard feelings between the once and future Presidents, who met for more than an hour in Mr. Reagan's 34th-floor offices in the spectacular Century City skyscraper where the movie "Die Hard," the Bruce Willis thriller, was filmed.
As the two men posed for the cameras before their private meeting, Mr. Reagan handed his successor a jar of red, white and blue jelly beans, saying his "history with jelly beans" had "prevented me from becoming a cigarette addict."
"I substituted these," Mr. Reagan said, "and I just thought that -- patriotic colors and all -- that maybe you might like to have them."
"Thank you," Mr. Clinton said, throwing back his head in laughter. "What a wonderful gift. I love it. I'll need it."
 
No Argument Here
 Afterwards, Mr. Clinton recounted the highlights of a conversation notable for its avoidance of the unpleasantries of the past. Indeed, an uninformed listener might easily have assumed from the description that Mr. Clinton and Mr. Reagan were of one mind on practically everything.
"We talked about the line item veto, which as you know he likes a lot, and he was glad that I was supporting it," Mr. Clinton said. "We talked about some ways to restrain Government spending in areas where I want to hold it down."
Mr. Reagan, Mr. Clinton recounted, "said that he hoped I'd have more cooperation from Congress than he did, and he thought I might. And I said I was working on it hard."
On the potentially embarrassing subject of the economy, the words "trickle down" and "tax and spend" apparently went unsaid. Instead, the two men found common ground in agreeing that the deficit ought to be brought down, Mr. Clinton said.
The talk ended on a rather poignant discussion of the prison of the Presidency.
"He said that he thought Hillary and Chelsea and I should make ample use of Camp David," Mr. Clinton said. "He said that it's about the only place where you can really walk freely, you know, and be alone, virtually unencumbered. He said, 'It will help you keep your peace of mind.' "
The Clinton camp was insistent in depicting the informal Presidential meeting as motivated solely by Mr. Clinton's desire to be polite and to pick up a few pointers from Mr. Reagan.
Bruce Lindsey, a senior adviser to Mr. Clinton, said the call on Mr. Reagan "was not calculated for political gain," but he acknowledged that such gain might be forthcoming.
"I would hope Reagan supporters and Republicans would be glad that Governor Clinton felt it was worthwhile to consult with President Reagan," he said.
 
Meet and Greet at the Mall
 For the third time since the election, Mr. Clinton returned to a favorite mode of campaigning, what his aides call "a meet and greet," in which Mr. Clinton, surrounded by cameras, moves among a crowd, touching, talking and listening.
The President-elect made a very public plunge today, spending more than an hour walking in the Glendale Galleria. Although billed by Mr. Lindsey as a way for the President-elect to "remain in touch with people" through personal conversations, the eventwas indistinguishable from a campaign rally; his local supporters had been encouraged to attend.
The President-elect's stroll was to a degree far more than had been expected and to a degree almost frightening. By the time Mr. Clinton arrived at the mall at 2:30 P.M., at least 30,000 people were waiting, lined up 30 deep along the rope lines set up along the route where he would walk from store to store.
As Mr. Clinton made his way through the mall, the screaming crowd surged forward in great waves. The noise was deafening, the pandemonium not far from the point where control is lost.
Mr. Clinton's bodyguards were clearly worried, and made an impromptu effort to ferret out anyone who might have a gun. As the President-elect approached each fresh section of the crowd, a line of Secret Service agents and police officers preceded him, yelling to the people, "Show us your hands! Show us your hands!" and waggling their own in the air in example. The crowd, obliging, waved hands and wiggled fingers.
"I've been here since 9 o'clock this morning, and I'm not going to leave, come hell or high water, till I see him," said Mary Ann Pokorny, as she pushed her slight weight back against the crush 20 deep behind her. "I voted for him, so it's my right to see him."

GRAPHIC: Photo: President-elect Bill Clinton greeting a crowd of cheering shoppers during a walk through the Glendale Galleria mall, north of Los Angeles. (Jim Wilson/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Serbia Copes With Its Own Refugees

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BELGRADE, Yugoslavia

 BODY:
In the sad and ever growing annals of refugee experience, the scene was somewhat unusual.
Six Serbians -- a bank employee, a lawyer, a businessman and their wives -- expelled by invading Croats from the southern Bosnian city of Mostar, sat in the living room of a small but warm apartment here the other evening watching TV and drinking tea. 
 While the place is crowded, it is a kind of home nevertheless -- instead of a bleak refugee camp with naked light bulbs and rows of iron beds.
"I've got nothing against any ethnic groups" said the apartment owner, Vlada Kovacevic. "These are all relatives staying with me now. But before they came I had strangers, including Croats and Muslims."
Serbia is a country in disgrace these days, accused of fomenting an ethnic civil war in Bosnia and Herzegovina and cut off from normal world contact by an economic blockade.
 
Absorbed 600,000 Refugees
 But it has taken in some 600,000 of the more than two million refugees the Balkan civil war has created so far, with more coming in at a rate of 500 to 2,000 a day. Moreover, Serbia has lodged 96 percent of all these refugees with families in their private homes -- a development almost without precedent, relief officials say.
Most but not all are Serbs fleeing the "ethnic cleansing" carried out against them by Muslims and Croats. But these days a sizeable number of Serbian refugees are coming from so-called "liberated" Serbian areas of Bosnia.
The Serbian Commissioner for Refugees, Dobrica Vulovic, said: "Of course it helps that most of the refugees are Serbs and usually have relatives living somewhere here. But we've benefited from plenty of disinterested generosity with people giving accommodation to refugees off the street."
What rankles Mr. Vulovic and other Serbian officials, though, is that while Serbia plays host to about a quarter of all refugees, it gets only about 14 percent of the aid Western countries are donating to help care for them.
The United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees tries to take an even-handed approach in aid to each country in the area according to the scale of the refugee crisis it faces.
The biggest beneficiary is Bosnia and Herzegovina itself, which is estimated to have more than 1.3 million refugees or needy people, followed by Croatia with more than 600,000. Serbia comes next and is expected to receive about $80 million in United Nations assistance between November and next March.
The United States and the European Community are denying Serbia the additional aid that they are giving for Balkan refugees. Western diplomats here say public opinion would not stand for giving aid to refugees living relatively safely in the country held responsible for the tragedy in preference to those experiencing far greater hardships in Bosnia itself.
Serbian officials see this as just another example of the Western world's bias against them and its failure to understand the Balkan conflict.
"Since we are always blamed for every evil here we get no help," says Belgrade's Mayor, Slobadank Gruden, with unconcealed bitterness. "But refugees are always refugees."
Commissioner Vulovic says he has some 545,000 registered refugees but estimates that there are an additional 120,000 unregistered ones.
The government can no longer afford to offer them financial assistance. But they get food and medicine from the United Nations and other aid agencies.
As the exodus from Bosnia continues, Serbian officials find that the public's generosity is drying up. And they are now being forced to fall back on more traditional methods of caring for them -- bleak refugee centers in sport centers, disused army barracks and summer recreation camps.
"Often the host family thought the refugees would only be with them a few weeks but now it's seven or eight months and tempers get frayed," Mrs. Gruden explains. "Then everybody is getting poorer as a result of sanctions. Factories are closing and salaries are being cut. So it's becoming a bigger economic strain to help other people."
With the winter coming on and the fighting continuing, Commissioner Vulovic is bracing for a further big influx as conditions in Bosnia get worse. "We expect at least 200,000 this winter," he said. "But if Sarajevo falls it could be more." He said that in the besieged Muslim town of Travnik, there are already believed to be nearly 50,000 refugees seeking shelter.

HEADLINE: Some Legal Experts See Intolerance as H.I.V. and Sex Are Linked to Crime

BYLINE: By ADAM NOSSITER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEW ORLEANS, Nov. 27

 BODY:
The prospect of dying in a Louisiana prison made Salvadore Gamberella's hands shake and his voice tremble. He is only 28 years old, and at most his crime will cost him 20 years in jail.
But it will likely be a life sentence nonetheless. Mr. Gamberella has H.I.V., the virus that causes AIDS, and is not expected to live out the term. Last week Mr. Gamberella, an unemployed shipyard welder with a troubled past, became the first person to be prosecuted and convicted under Louisiana's five-year-old criminal AIDS law. 
 It took the State District Court jury in Houma, La., only 12 minutes to find Mr. Gamberella guilty of putting his former girlfriend, Darla Trosclair, at risk. Ms. Trosclair has tested positive for the human immunodeficiency virus. Did Mr. Gamberella tell the 18-year-old woman, the valedictorian of her high school class, he was infected? It was his word against hers, and the jury believed Ms. Trosclair, who said he did not tell her.
 
Rise in Prosecutions
 To Mr. Gamberella, free on bond in his trailer at the edge of Houma, the jury's quick decision was grim new evidence of his outcast status in the coastal city of about 35,000 residents. But it was also a sign of what experts say is a new, harsher wave in society's response to the AIDS epidemic.
In the last year, there has been a surge of prosecutions involving people with H.I.V., said Lawrence Gostin, executive director of the American Society of Law, Medicine and Ethics, and head of the United States AIDS Litigation Project. Mr. Gostin said about 20 convictions had been logged around the country since the epidemic started.
The man who prosecuted Mr. Gamberella, Steven Callahan, the Terrebonne Parish assistant district attorney, says the case tells people with H.I.V. "they have to be more responsible about their sexual behavior."
But others wonder. Many of these cases, particularly those resulting from criminal AIDS laws like the one here, trouble legal experts who say they reflect a new intolerance, demonize people with AIDS and may even discourage people from seeking to learn whether they are infected with the virus. To know, these experts say, is to leave oneself open to prosecution.
Most criminal AIDS cases are still brought under existing laws. One such case ended on Wednesday in Portland, Ore., with the conviction of an H.I.V.-infected man who was charged with attempted murder for having unprotected sex with a 17-year-old girl.
Half the states now have specific laws similar to Louisiana's, which makes it a crime when a person who knows he has the AIDS virus does not inform his sex partner. The law here, with its penalty of 10 years at hard labor and a fine of $5,000, is one of the toughest in the country.
The law is "motivated by punitive or retributive motivations, not what's good for public health," said Mr. Gostin, who is also a professor of health law at Harvard University.
Until now, prosecutors have had trouble winning AIDS cases brought under the existing criminal laws. There have been about 300 cases, Mr. Gostin said, and in many it has been difficult to prove that one person intended to harm another.
The case in Oregon involved Alberto Gonzalez, 28, who was on probation after pleading no contest in a similar case last October. The teen-age girl in the current case has tested negative for H.I.V., but the woman in the earlier case has developed AIDS. A third woman who had unprotected sex with Mr. Gonzalez died of AIDS.
 
Proof of Intent to Harm
 While the prosecution was able to obtain a conviction of Mr. Gonzalez, the jury was split 10 to 2, with one juror, speaking on condition of anonymity, saying they had great difficulty determining whether he intentionally tried to pass the virus. Juries in Oregon do not have to be unanimous to convict on attempted murder charges.
As a result of the difficulties facing prosecutors in obtaining convictions under conventional law, legislatures got tougher, passing laws specifically for AIDS cases.
Under laws like Louisiana's, no proof of intent to harm is necessary. The state needs to show only that one person knew of the infection and did not tell the partner. That was the essence of the case against Mr. Gamberella.
In an interview last week, Mr. Gamberella readily admitted to his past: a prison term on a burglary conviction, marriage to a prostitute, intravenous drug use. Mr. Gamberella insisted that after he had met Ms. Trosclair he told her that he feared he might be infected with H.I.V..
According to court records and Mr. Gamberella, he met Ms. Trosclair at a blessing of the fleet ceremony in Chauvin, La., in the spring of 1989. They became friends. Because of his record, Mr. Gamberella had a difficult time finding a job, and in June 1989 he tried to earn some cash by selling his blood at a blood bank.
Through the blood bank he found out he had the virus that causes AIDS and, he says, told Ms. Trosclair. Several months later, they began having sex. A brief the American Civil Liberties Union filed on behalf of Mr. Gamberella said the couple regularly used a condom during sex.
On one occasion, though, they did not use a condom, and on that occasion Mr. Gamberella told Ms. Trosclair that they should stop, according to the brief. But Ms. Trosclair, the brief said, "told Mr. Gamberella she did not want to stop." It added, "She said that if he was going to die she wanted to 'die with him.' "
Ms. Trosclair's account is different from Mr. Gamberella's. In testimony in Houma last week, Ms. Trosclair said that Mr. Gamberella never told her that he was infected, even early in 1990, when she discovered that she was pregnant. Mr. Gamberella now faces a second count of violating the AIDS law for exposing the child. The son is 2 years old and has tested negative for H.I.V. Ms. Trosclair said she did not find out she was infected with the virus until early this year.
Ms. Trosclair refuses to discuss the case with reporters.
While no trial date has yet been set for Mr. Gamberella in the case involving the child, a challenge to the Louisiana law is set to be heard in State District Court on Tuesday. The civil liberties union, which is representing Mr. Gamberella, says it will argue that the law, with its pivotal reliance on the word "intentionally," is unconstitutionally vague. The law, according to a brief the group filed in the constitutionality case, fails to define exactly what must be intended, simply having sex or inflicting criminal harm.
The civil liberties union also says the law does not specify that the accused must be aware of his or her condition.
"I really do see this law as unworthy of our society," said Denise LeBoeuf, a civil liberties union lawyer who is representing Mr. Gamberella. "There is a natural fear response that we can fight AIDS by fighting people with AIDS. We can't."

HEADLINE: Time Warner Holds Talks

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
Time Warner's magazines unit and the American Express Company are in talks about possible business deals, a Time spokesman said yesterday. But he declined to comment on a Wall Street Journal report that American Express's magazine publishing operations may be put into a joint venture with Time Warner.
Quoting unidentified executives from both companies, The Journal said they were near an agreement for Time Warner's magazine unit to operate the joint venture through its Time Inc. Ventures. 
 The magazines include American Express's Travel & Leisure, Food & Wine and Departures.
A Time spokesman said the companies were "having discussions on a variety of topics." American Express executives were not available for comment.
American Express has been experiencing sluggish revenue growth in its core credit card business because of the weak economy. For the latest quarter, the travel, financial and information services company reported a net loss of $205 million, against a profit of $31 million in the third quarter of 1991. It closed one magazine, New York Woman, earlier this year.
Time Warner, the world's largest media company, has been seeking business alliances to cope with the heavy debt taken on when Time bought Warner Communications Inc. for $14 billion in 1989.

HEADLINE: FUTURES/OPTIONS;
Copper Prices Move Higher On Bullish Economic News

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
Copper futures prices bounded higher yesterday on the Commodity Exchange in New York in reaction to this week's bullish economic news.
The value of copper, an industrial metal, often moves in unison with improvements in the economic climate. 
 The Government reported fresh evidence yesterday of a recovery, as Americans' incomes rose 1 percent in October and spending climbed seven-tenths of a percent. On Wednesday, the Commerce Department reported a surprisingly strong 3.9 percent increase in the third-quarter gross domestic product.
Fred Demler, metals analyst at Paine Webber Inc., said the encouraging economic data this week and production problems in Russia all contributed to the rally in copper. Also supporting it were rumors of production problems at a Chilean copper refinery and of demand from China and Japan, traders said.
The actively traded March copper contract rose 2.50 cents, to 99.60 cents a pound.
Chicago grain and livestock futures markets were open yesterday, but trading volume was light as many traders took off the day after Thanksgiving.
Chicago Mercantile Exchange cattle futures closed lower as traders realized that a snowstorm that sped through the Great Plains on Wednesday probably had not harmed the cattle herds there.
December live cattle closed down 0.1 cent at 76.35 cents a pound.
New York energy futures markets were closed yesterday for a long Thanksgiving weekend, delaying until Monday any market reaction to OPEC's announcement of a production ceiling. Traders said they expected Nymex crude oil prices to remain fairly steady on OPEC's action. The group set an output ceiling of 24.582 million barrels a day.
In London, January Brent crude futures rose 22 cents, to $18.97 a barrel.
"It is not something that is going to drive up the price by $3 per barrel, but it will reduce the risk of a major drop in prices," said Gerry Protti, president of the Canadian Association of Petroleum Producers.
The OPEC cap is about 400,000 barrels less than what Western economists had estimated as OPEC's recent output.

HEADLINE: Fire Destroys 4 Businesses In Queens

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS

 BODY:
The first fire that struck the squat brick shopping center in Queens last May caused more fear than damage in the vegetarian restaurant Abhay Mehta and his family opened two years ago. The second fire, early yesterday morning, devastated it.
The fire, which began at 4:20 A.M. and quickly went to four alarms, tore through the small shopping center on Broadway at 43d Avenue in Elmhurst, destroying four businesses, , including Mr. Mehta's restaurant, Jhupdi, and a vacant storefront. Fire officials said the blaze, which broke out two doors away from Mr. Mehta's restaurant, was suspicious because it began in the same place as the fire in May. 
 "What could be the purpose?" Mr. Mehta said, as he stood in the center's parking lot yesterday, sorting through the broken glass and charred remains of the restaurant.
The fire, which burned out of control for more than three hours, swept through a shopping center as ethnically diverse as the neighborhood around it. In addition to Jhupdi, the fire gutted the Restaurante Las Palmas, Singas Famous Pizza, and Anna Bhandar Indo-Pakistani Groceries. The center's anchor supermarket, Key Food, suffered smoke damage.
 
Economic Impact Concerns
 Dan Andrews, a spokesman for Borough President Claire Shulman of Queens, said her office was concerned that fire could have a harsh economic impact in the neighborhood, where thousands of new immigrants have moved in the last decade. Scores of ethnic shops, bodegas and restaurants have opened despite the recession that has left many stores vacant in other parts of the city.
Worried that the owners of the stores destroyed in the fire would face an arduous struggle rebuilding, Mr. Andrews said, officials in the Borough President's office of economic development had already begun looking into ways to assist them, possibly arranging low-interest lows.
More than 180 firefighters from 38 fire companies fought the blaze, which appeared to have begun in the basement beneath Las Palmas and Singas Famous Pizza, bringing it under control at 7:38 yesterday morning. No one was injured, officials said.
A Fire Department spokesman, Robert Broderick, said that fire marshals were still investigating the blaze and that they had no suspects. Mr. Mehta, who emigrated from Bombay in 1988, said he and five relatives had pooled their resources to open Jhupdi two years ago.
 
Insurance, Yes. But Enough?
 As they slowly redecorated, hoping to recreate the ambiance of an Indian village, they encountered resistance from the city's inspectors, who objected to the straw huts they wanted to build inside. The flame-retardant straw was the only thing that did not burn yesterday.
As workers cleaned debris from the sidewalk and hammered plywood over the storefronts, Mr. Mehta, who is 27 years old, said he had insurance but probably not enough.
"The atmosphere inside was so good," he said, picking up a charred, soaked newspaper clipping of a favorable review. "It was a lot of work, eight months. The insurance doesn't cover the work we did."
