HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
In Place of Yugos, AK-47's and Pistols

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: KRAGUJEVAC, Yugoslavia, Nov. 27

 BODY:
The humble Yugo automobile meant money and jobs for this town in the center of Serbia. But the civil war has forced an end to production, costing tens of thousands of jobs.
Now the factory makes little more than rifles and pistols. 
 "We had our chance, but we missed it," said Rodoljub Micic, the plant's general director. "Our worry now is what to do with thousands unemployed. They could start a social revolution."
Factory workers cried with joy when the first shipment of Yugo cars, the pride of Yugoslavia's state-run industry, left for the United States in 1985. Now, hopes of prosperity based on the 55-horsepower compact are gone.
 
 
30,000 Laid Off
 "So many people are out on the streets without work in Kragujevac," said Dragica Petrovic, who used to work on the production line. "How are we going to survive?"
As the country has disintegrated in civil war, car production at the Red Flag factory in this town of about 150,000 people 60 miles south of Belgrade has all but ceased.
Management has laid off 30,000 of 49,000 employees. Of the remaining workers, 2,000 are using leftover parts to assemble the last 5,000 Yugos for the domestic market.
The rest are producing AK-47 assault rifles, Magnum-type revolvers and other large-caliber pistols popular on the war fronts in neighboring Bosnia-Herzegovina and elsewhere in war-torn Yugoslavia.
The 100-year-old Red Flag arms factory is the biggest in the Balkans; car production began there only in the mid-1950's.
 
 
'Deal of the Century'
 Under a contract touted in Yugoslavia as "the deal of the century," the plant started exporting Yugos to the United States in July 1985, making them the first cars from a Communist country on the American market. Yugos were also exported to Britain, Greece, Spain and Egypt, but the United States was the largest market.
Sales in the United States peaked at 49,000 in 1988 before plunging. Although the no-frills Yugo was inexpensive -- it began selling for $3,999 -- the car had recurring technical problems.
In the first quarter of 1992, Yugo America Inc., based in Upper Saddle River, N.J., sold only 1,400 cars. Yugo America filed for bankruptcy in April and is no longer operating. One of the founders of Yugo America was Lawrence S. Eagleburger, Acting Secretary of State and a former Ambassador to Yugoslavia.
Sanctions were imposed by the United Nations last May on Serbia and Montenegro -- the two remaining Yugoslav republics -- to punish Serbia for fomenting war, first in Croatia and now in Bosnia. Yugoslavia's economy had already been crippled by mismanagement and the burden of financing military campaigns, but the sanctions created a crisis. About 500,000 workers have lost their jobs since May, and it is expected the plant will be closed.
 
 
'We Cried With Joy'
 "I remember, we cried with joy when the first Yugo left our factory for America," Ms. Petrovic said. "Now, many of us are crying because tomorrow we may not have bread to eat." She lost her job in August and receives about $50 a month in welfare.
The huge assembly lines are virtually deserted now as thousands of Yugos without parts stand idle, rusting on a parking lot.
"Success requires the effort of all, while only one person is sufficient for failure," reads a large red banner still hanging in the main factory. Many of the jobless workers consider that one person sufficient for failure to be Serbia's nationalist President, Slobodan Milosevic, who is widely blamed for the violent breakup of Yugoslavia.
"If he was not here, Yugoslavia would be together now," said Zoran Stojkovic, an auto worker discharged early this year. "We would be selling our cars in the other republics and maybe America. And there would be no sanctions."
"Now, the factory is producing only machine guns, which kill our former brothers," he added, sipping a plum brandy in the smoky Srbija restaurant.

HEADLINE: With a Big Claim, a Tiny Tribe Seeks Aid

BYLINE: By CONSTANCE L. HAYS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BRIDGEPORT, Conn., Nov. 25

 BODY:
In the beginning there were rocks and rivers, forests and meadows, cornfields and leaping deer. Now there is downtown Bridgeport, and the Golden Hill Paugussetts, an Indian tribe based here, want it back.
In a class-action suit filed earlier this month in Federal District Court, the 100-member tribe asserted its ownership of 88 acres that include much of this city's core, encompassing City Hall, the main post office and the Hi-Ho shopping mall. Citing violations of agreements made in the 18th century, first with the British and then with the Americans, tribal leaders have proposed a settlement that includes $750 million, 80 acres of land, support for the tribe's efforts to become recognized by the Federal Government as an official tribe and state permission to operate a casino. 
 It is a tall order, especially for an economically beleaguered city and state, but one that tribal leaders say they are entitled to after a history of poor treatment. "This is based on the value of the land," said Aurelius H. Piper Jr., the tribe's 47-year-old council chief, who is also known as Quiet Hawk. "I want the whole thing, all of it," he added. "Any white man in this country with a billion-dollar claim would be as hard-nosed as I am."
Some have wondered why anyone would want anything in downtown Bridgeport, an aging industrial city that tried to declare bankruptcy last year. Others have scoffed at the tribe's claim, suggesting that its real goal is simply to open a casino of its own. Another Connecticut tribe, the Mashantucket Pequots, opened a successful casino near Ledyard in February. Various parties, including Las Vegas developers like Steven A. Wynn, have proposed building casinos in Bridgeport as well.
But regaining its land has been a tribal priority for generations, Mr. Piper said. "It's something that has passed down, leader to leader," he said. "We have continuously gone after our property, but we haven't had the money to finish it. The tribe is very poor and small." Besides Bridgeport, the tribe is considering staking claims to other land it inhabited before Europeans arrived, in Orange, Stratford, Milford and Trumbull.
The Golden Hill Paugussetts have existed "since time immemorial," according to the lawsuit, and now occupy two reservations in Connecticut. One, covering about 107 acres in Colchester, was bought with a Federal Housing and Urban Development grant in the 1970's, Mr. Piper said. The other, only a quarter-acre, is in Trumbull. Mr. Piper's brother, known as Moonface Bear and a tribal leader himself, lives on the Colchester reservation; his father, Aurelius Sr., whose Indian name is Big Eagle, lives on the Trumbull property.
Mr. Piper said he had tried to negotiate with city officials but got nowhere, which forced him to file the suit. A lawyer for the city, Mark Anastasi, refused to comment on the case because it is pending. Legal experts said that in settlements of Indian claims, the bulk of the awards are usually paid by the Federal Government, although state and local governments are required to contribute.
The tribe contends that agreements between its ancestors and the British Crown, as well as the fledgling American Government, were violated. English colonists arrived in the area of what is now Bridgeport, where the Paugussetts were concentrated, in the 1630's. To resolve a dispute over property that arose in 1658, the tribe agreed to accept an 80-acre reservation in Bridgeport, which became known as the Golden Hill Reservation, the lawsuit states.
But by 1760, the reservation had been nibbled away by more and more settlers, prompting an investigation by a committee of the colony's Assembly, said Bernard Wishnia, a Roseland, N.J., lawyer who is representing the Paugussetts. The investigation, completed in 1765, found in favor of the Indians but allowed the settlers to keep 68 acres, giving the Indians two lots totaling 20 acres along with some corn and a pile of blankets. The entire 88-acre tract is the subject of the suit.
Most of the land subsequently was taken from the Indians by local officials, according to the suit, even though Federal law specified that that could not be done without the consent of Congress. "The Tribe therefore retains the title and right of possession to the said land," the suit states. Among the several pages of defendants are dozens of individual and corporate property owners, as well as Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr., the United States of America and Mayor Joseph Ganim of Bridgeport.
The points raised in the suit and proposed settlement highlight some of the complex issues facing Indian tribes, which often include social problems like alcoholism and unemployment in disproportionate numbers. Raising enough money to sustain even a small tribe is difficult; hence tribes turn to bingo or casinos to draw big spenders. Certain Federal education and health programs are available specifically for Indians and not for other ethnic or racial groups.
 
Certification Process
 But to be entitled to such benefits, tribes must go through a Federal certification process with criteria that many of them find demeaning as well as difficult to satisfy. Tribes must prove, among other things, that they have had a long, continuous history; that they have maintained a "contemporary community," and that the tribal government has exercised continuous authority over its members. The Golden Hill Paugussetts have been trying to gain Federal recognition since 1982.
The certification process "creates an expectation that modern-day tribes, who have never received any kind of Federal support and in many cases have been actively suppressed, must show the same sorts of characteristics as tribes that have had land set aside for them and received other kinds of support," said Henry Sockbeson, a senior lawyer for the Native American Rights Fund in Washington. Tribes most often fail the community aspect of the test, he said, because many of their members are scattered across a wide area and have little contact with each other.
Only one case of a tribe's seeking to prove its status has ever gone before a jury, Mr. Sockbeson said. That involved the Mashpees, a tribe in Massachusetts, during the 1970's, and the tribe lost.
Other tribes have been successful in winning Federal recognition. Among them are the Mashantucket Pequots, based near Ledyard, in the eastern part of the state, and the Penobscots, the Maine tribe to which Mr. Sockbeson belongs.
Dale T. White, a Boulder, Colo., specialist in Indian law who is representing the Golden Hill Paugussetts in their recognition bid, said he is optimistic that the tribe will succeed. After all, he noted, the tribe has been recognized by Connecticut since the middle of the 17th century, and is listed in various documents. But Mr. Piper, whose mother was black, said he is repelled by the whole process. "It's insulting, condescending and racist," he said. "I have to prove that I'm an Indian, and yet they can decide whether I am an Indian or not."
And he faces resistance not only from the Government, but from other tribes as well, he said. "The more people who are federally recognized, the smaller the pie for everyone else," he said. "There are good ol' boy politics inside the tribes, too. I can understand it, but I don't necessarily agree with it."

GRAPHIC: Photo: "I want the whole thing, all of it," said Aurelius H. Piper Jr., the council chief of the Golden Hill Paugussetts. Earlier this month, the tribe had filed a class-action suit against the city of Bridgeport, Conn., to assert its ownership of 88 acres that include much of the city's core. (Stephen Castagneto for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Artist of the Possible;
Brenda Bacon, in Trenton Vortex

BYLINE: By WAYNE KING, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TRENTON, Nov. 25

 BODY:
Like everyone else involved in restructuring New Jersey's health care system for the uninsured, Brenda Bacon, who as Chief of Management and Planning for the State of New Jersey was one of its chief architects, is not happy with the new plan, which goes to a vote in the Legislature on Monday.
"This is the best package that can be accomplished, given all the circumstances," she said in an interview in the cabinet room just off the Governor's office this week. "We had to deal with the Federal court decision, we had to deal with a Legislature that is totally tax averse and we had to deal with a crisis in how health care is delivered in this state and this country. We can all sit down and design a perfect system on paper, but it isn't going to be reality." 
 The sweeping health care package is "fast tracked," scheduled to go to the floor of both houses of the Legislature on Monday, with the outcome uncertain. The plan, which Gov. Jim Florio concedes is "not everybody's first choice" but which he says he will sign into law if nobody comes up with something better in the time remaining, would create a new health care financing system by tapping the state's $2.4 billion unemployment compensation fund.
 
Court Ruling
 A new system must be developed because a Federal court last May decided in a civil suit brought by 14 labor unions that the state's method of financing health care for the uninsured was unconstitutional. That program, called the Uncompensated Care Trust Fund, set up in 1980, raised its money, amounting to $644 million last year, by adding 19 percent to every hospital bill. The money was used to pay for health care for the indigent and the uninsured.
The court's ruling meant that the state had to come up with a new way to pay for that care, and the Governor and the Legislature agreed to produce a new plan by Nov. 30. They have been arguing about how to do it since the ruling in May and they came up with the compromise plan only last week.
Tapping into the unemployment fund directly is illegal, so the plan, devised by the Republican majority in the Legislature, does it by indirection, cutting the unemployment tax while creating a new health care tax that would raise an equivalent sum. If the $2.4 billion unemployment fund dips below $1.5 billion, a new tax on employers, six-tenths of 1 percent of their payrolls, would kick in.
The plan is intended to avoid the immediate imposition of a new tax, which the Republican leaders consider politically unacceptable. The Republicans won large majorities in both houses of the Legislature in November 1991 by campaigning against taxes imposed two years ago by Governor Florio and the Democrats who then controlled the Legislature.
 
At the Vortex
 Reality in Trenton is harsh these days. Fiscal crisis is a perpetual state of affairs; the stubborn Republican majority, in total control of the the Legislature, is at sword's point with an equally stubborn Democratic Governor, and the list of problem areas -- from welfare to education to automobile insurance to health care -- is mounting. At the vortex is Ms. Bacon, who is both a longtime friend and aide of Mr. Florio and the third-ranking member of his administration. She is also a friend of long standing and a former business partner of the No. 2 man, Joseph C. Salema, the Governor's Chief of Staff. "Oh, yes," says Ms. Bacon, who is 42 years old, "I remember the Governor when he looked like Bruce Springsteen."
And Jim Florio in 1974 was a boyish, new member of the House of Representatives. He met Brenda Bacon, then in her 20's and carving out a career in health care after an early start as a social worker, when they attended meetings aimed at finding ways to provide affordable health care for everyone. After two decades of hard work and intensive study, Ms. Bacon and the Governor concede, that is still the elusive goal.
"Brenda and I go back a long way," the Governor said, "back to the days of the Health Services Agency," a Federal program in which both were involved. "The people doing it were mostly health care professionals, and I was very impressed, not only with Brenda's knowledge but with the respect she commanded from the professionals."
 
'We Share a Philosophy'
 She has been an adviser to the Governor in Washington and Trenton, on and off, ever since. When Mr. Florio took office as Governor in January 1990 after 16 years in Congress, she was one of his first appointees."
"We share a philosophy that the object of government policy is the public interest," the Governor said. "All too frequently," he added, "you have academics who can't manage or people who can manage but who have not thought through policy. She does both."
As the chief shaper of both policy and administration, Ms. Bacon meets daily with the Governor and four days a week with key members of the cabinet and the Chief of Staff.
"Brenda is smart, likable, but she is tough as nails," said Assemblyman Wayne R. Bryant of Camden, the deputy minority leader. Mr. Bryant held the seat on the Camden County Board of Freeholders to which -- at his urging and that of Mr. Florio -- Ms. Bacon was appointed for a year before being elected to a two-year term in the early 1980's.
"I was only the second minority member of the Camden County Freeholders," said Mr. Bryant, "and the black community said they should continue to have a voice. We had never had a black woman on the board. Brenda was the first, and she was elected to a second term."
Despite her reputation for relentless and passionate advocacy for the things that she and the Governor believe in, she maintains a cheerful delight in human foibles and laughs as easily as she argues.
 
'Lucky to Have Her'
 State Senator C. Louis Bassano of Union, the chairman of the Senate Health Committee and the chief sponsor and Republican negotiator on the health care plan, recalls telling Ms. Bacon when the negotiations ended how tough she is. "I meant it as a compliment," he said, "and I think she took it that way. She is tough, she is a good negotiator and I don't always agree with her, but the Governor is lucky to have her. She understands health care, knows it well."
"When it got too intense," he added, "she would just say, 'I've had enough; I'm leaving.' "
Ms. Bacon concedes that she walked out of a few meetings to cool off. "These were not nice meetings," she explained. "There are many things we negotiated out of the bill." But she always came back, because, she said, one cannot just walk away.
If the warring camps had not worked together, she said, "On Nov. 30, you were going to have hundreds of thousands of people who were not going to have medical care, a hospital system where hundreds of people were laid off, bond defaults and all sorts of horrible things.
"So where maybe $600 million is not enough to solve the urban hospital problem, zero dollars is a lot worse."
 
 
PROFILE
Brenda Joyce Bacon
Born: April 27, 1950, in St. Mary's County, Md.
Hometown: Voorhees, N.J.
Education: Elizabeth Seton High School, Bladensburg, Md.; B.A., Hampton Institute, Hampton, Va.; M.B.A., Wharton School, University of Pennsylvania.
Career Highlights: New Jersey Health Planning Council, 1976-81; president, Southern New Jersey Health System Agency, 1978-80; Camden County Board of Chosen Freeholders, 1982-85; president, HealthTeam Inc., health-care consultants, Mount Laurel, 1985-89; Chief of Management and Planning, State of New Jersey, 1990-present.
Family: Married to Roland L. Bacon; children, Kevin, 9, and Jessica, 7.
Hobbies: Reading mysteries and techno-thrillers; children's activities.

HEADLINE: Japanese Officially Reaffirm Support of Rice Import Ban

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Nov. 27

 BODY:
Japan's Government said today that the nation would fight to maintain its ban on the import of rice, a stance that may threaten to disrupt world trade talks.
But after the official position was stated, Japan's Foreign Minister seemed to leave the door open for a compromise, sending a subtle signal to the country's rice farmers that protectionism that could harm Japan's trading relationships may not be sustainable. 
 With the United States and the European Community's having reached an agreement, however tenuous, on agricultural subsidies, Japan's ban on rice imports could become an obstacle to a new global trading treaty. Despite France's opposition to the farm accord and a possible French veto of a global trade pact, negotiators agreed in Geneva on Thursday to resume the long-stalled Uruguay Round of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, with the goal of reaching a basic treaty by the end of the year.
Japanese Cabinet ministers held a special meeting today to discuss the nation's negotiating position, and they reaffirmed Japan's opposition to rice imports.
But at a news conference immediately after the meeting, Foreign Minister Michio Watanabe seemed to suggest that Japan might have to compromise. "I wonder if we can stick to our policy of not importing even a grain of rice," he said.
The statement, as well as other seemingly conflicting remarks by officials earlier in the week, reflected to some extent the divisions in the Government. While the Agriculture Ministry seems firmly opposed to rice imports, the Foreign Affairs Ministry is more interested in seeing that the rice issue does not ruin Japan's standing in the world or relations with trading partners.
The International Trade and Industry Ministry and big business groups here are also said to believe that the rice issue should not be allowed to touch off a trade war that would harm Japan's exports of cars, computer chips and camcorders -- products that represent far bigger sectors of the economy than rice farming.
 
Protecting a Staple
 Japanese officials argue that the nation needs to ban imports to protect Japanese rice farmers from being forced out of business by less expensive foreign products and to assure that the nation remains self-sufficient in its staple food.
Farmers and the powerful agricultural cooperatives are important supporters of the ruling Liberal Democratic Party. With a big political scandal and the economic recession already undermining its support, the party cannot afford to alienate farmers.
To some analysts, what has been happening here is elaborate political theater. Politicians are maintaining a strong stance in opposition to imports while occasionally sending up trial balloons hinting a compromise, thus preparing the nation for a decision that might be needed to facilitate the GATT talks. If a market opening occurs, these analysts say, politicians will be able to say they put up a good fight but were forced to give in to foreign pressure.
 
A Tariff Proposal
 Last year, Arthur Dunkel, the Director General of GATT, proposed replacing import bans with tariffs that would be relaxed gradually. Mr. Dunkel has urged that there be no exceptions.
Japanese officials say that Japan, which is already the largest food importer in the world, deserves an exception for rice. South Korea also wants to keep a ban on rice imports, and other nations are expected to seek specific exceptions.
"We will actively seek international understanding for the difficult problems that Japan faces," Finance Minister Tsutomu Hata said today, according to the Kyodo news agency. The Agriculture Minister, Masami Tanabu, said he might visit the United States and Europe to discuss Japan's position.
 
May Be Acceptable
 Still, reports have appeared here that Japan might accept a tariff plan, providing the tariff is made higher and longer lasting than under Mr. Dunkel's proposal.
Asked about this today, Foreign Minister Watanabe said that while Japan opposed the tariff plan, it might end up in a "fight over the conditions of 'tariffication.' " While the Foreign Ministry said Mr. Watanabe was speaking as an individual, his comments were interpreted by the news media here as a sign that the Government was not completely closed to a tariff on rice.
Under Mr. Dunkel's proposal imported rice might start, for example, with a tariff in Japan as high as 700 percent, declining over six years to a level as low as 450 percent. The proposal also called for Japan to allow 3 percent of its rice market to be served by imports initially, rising to 5 percent in six years.
Mr. Dunkel has argued that such high tariffs will merely substitute one form of protection for another. But Japanese officials worry that once tariffs are in place, there will be international pressure to reduce them.
The United States has called on Japan to accept the tariff plan. Some newspapers here have also been calling for a liberalization on imports.

HEADLINE: Bonds Drop as 2 Indicators Advance

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Treasury bond prices fell yesterday after the Government reported an unexpected rise in the nation's personal income and spending.
The Treasury's main 30-year bond fell 21/32 point, or $6.56 per $1,000 face amount. Its yield, which rises when prices fall, grew to 7.59 percent, from 7.53 percent after a shortened session on Wednesday. No trading took place Thursday. 
 Bond prices opened slightly higher but began falling at midmorning after the Commerce Department reported that Americans' personal income shot up 1 percent in October, the largest gain in 10 months. Personal spending rose a strong seven-tenths of 1 percent, pushed higher by a number of special factors.
 
Strength Ahead
 The market had been expecting rises of about five-tenths of 1 percent in personal income and six-tenths of a percent in consumption.
The income and spending figures were the second signal this week that the economy is rebounding. They follow a report on Wednesday that the nation's gross domestic product rose at a surprisingly robust annual rate of 3.9 percent in the third quarter.
The two numbers together suggest that "strength is likely to continue into the fourth quarter," said Ram Bhagavatula, chief financial economist at Citicorp Securities Markets.
Bullish economic figures usually send bond prices lower because they signal that inflation, which erodes the value of long-term investments, might heat up. They also make the Federal Reserve less likely to lower interest rates.
 
'We'll Probably See a Rally'
 But the thinness of yesterday's post-holiday trading made for volatile prices that did not necessarily indicate long-term market trends, Mr. Bhagavatula said.
For one thing, inflation is running at a comfortable 2.5 to 3 percent, which should not be enough to disturb bond prices long term, he said.
In addition, the economist pointed out, the percentage of manufacturing capacity currently in use was 78.5 percent in October. "Until that rate gets up to 85 percent, there is little cause for concern," he said. "My nagging suspicion is that Monday we'll probably see a rally."
In the secondary market for Treasury securities, short-term maturities fell 5/32 to 15/32 point and intermediate maturities fell 17/32 point, the Telerate financial data service reported.
The Lehman Brothers Daily Treasury Bond Index, which measures price movements on all Treasury issues outstanding with maturities of a year or longer, fell 5.22, to 1,242.33.
The discount rate on three-month Treasury bills rose 1 basis point, or hundredth percentage point, to 3.24 percent; on six-month bills it rose 3 basis points, to 3.46 percent. The discount rate on one-year bills rose 5 basis points, to 3.64 percent.
The Federal funds rate, the interest on overnight loans between banks, rose to 3 3/8 percent, from 3 1/4 percent late Wednesday.
In the tax-exempt market, the Bond Buyer index of 40 actively traded municipal bonds closed at 97 1/8, unchanged from Wednesday. The average yield to maturity was 6.4 percent, also unchanged.

HEADLINE: The Hard Fact of Life Of a School's Closing

 BODY:
The sermon was appropriate for the season: try to find something to be thankful for.
But as the upper school students at St. Hilda's and St. Hugh's School filed out of their daily chapel service on a gray Manhattan day, few of them could identify that something. 
 The school's Board of Trustees announced this month that the upper school, which now has only 45 students, will close at the end of the school year. The cost of maintaining faculty, classrooms and activities for so few students, many of whom are on partial or full scholarships, has become a drain on the thriving lower classes. But as the older students face leaving their tiny community of friends and teachers, many of them feel anger and betrayal, or sadness.
 
'Cried for Two Days'
 "When I first heard I was so upset," Amanda Nazario, a 10th grader, said. "I cried for two days."
Amanda, 14 years old, came to St. Hilda's and St. Hugh's last year as a ninth grader. She said the atmosphere just seemed "ideal." Her mother called the school "a little piece of heaven for the kids."
In a city that boasts the largest group of independent schools in the country, St. Hilda's and St. Hugh's is a paradigm of both the educational genre and the economic problems such schools have faced in the last five years.
It was founded as a kindergarten in 1950 in a Morningside Heights brownstone by the Reverend Mother Ruth, an Episcopal nun who named it after her college at the University of Toronto, St. Hilda's. The school grew a grade a year, with students taught primarily by the nuns in the Community of the Holy Spirit, which Mother Ruth also founded.
 
A School Grows
 The school grew to include nursery through high school students, as well as boys. When the institution moved into a beige brick building on 114th Street near the Hudson River, it acquired a second name, that of St. Hugh, whom Mother Ruth chose as a patron saint for her male students.
Slowly, economic pressures and fewer nuns teaching for a small stipend (there are none at the school this year) have meant sharp increases in tuition and equally sharp decreases in the upper school's enrollment.
In the 1970's, when tuition was about $1,000 a year, 150 students attended the high school. In the mid-1980's, with tuition at $3,000, the school had around 100 upper schoolers. Now, with base high school tuition around $10,000, the upper school has lost 10 students in the last year. There are 211 students in the lower grades.
But Rick Fernandez, one of six seniors, said he did not consider the size of the classes to be a limitation. "We are not six cookie-cutter people," said Rick, who is 17. "We are really different from each other, but our personalities mesh."
"What we like about the school is the smallness," said Martha Bagnall, 14, who has been at St. Hilda's and St. Hugh's since nursery school. "So for it to be closed because of its size is even harder."
 
Racially Diverse
 Martha also likes the racial diversity of the school, which belies its size. Her group of 10th-grade friends consists of Amanda, who is half Puerto Rican and half Dutch, and "one Caucasian, one black, two Dominicans and Jennifer." "She's Mandarin," Martha said.
Jennifer Chen, 16, has attended St. Hilda's and St. Hugh's since fourth grade, when her family moved to the United States from Taiwan. "I learned to speak English here," she said. "I feel really attached to this place. I can't picture myself anyplace else."
But the group has begun to look elsewhere, aided by a school fair, which administrators hastily assembled last week.
"None of them match up to St. Hilda's and St. Hugh's," Amanda said of the other schools she has visited. "They pale in comparison to the education I'm receiving here."
The students remain angry and somewhat defiant, albeit in a tame fashion. Some asked permission to wear bluejeans (forbidden in the dress code) as a form of protest. More practically, several of the 10th graders called schools that end in the eighth grade to recruit students for next fall.
 
Learning About Power
 In many ways, the last month has provided an important, if not a pleasant, lesson. "I never knew there was a board of trustees that could make these decisions without asking us," Martha said. "Their picture was always at the front of the yearbook, but I didn't know they had that kind of power."
Of course, going to such a high school is bound to be different, though the students insist none of the differences are bad. The newspaper, The Courant, is not published, but posted on a bulletin board article by article. Few bother going to dances. And fielding sports teams gets harder and harder.
For instance, the boy's soccer team won the league championship last year, but almost could not play this year. Although 13 of the 19 high school boys agreed to play, that left little room for injury, and teachers had to forbid students who were hurt from playing.
As Lynne M. Allen, the headmistress, explained, in a phrase that may describe the entire controversy over closing and not just the soccer team, "Sensibility always comes up against adolescent enthusiasm."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Amanda Nazario, left, a 10th grader at St. Hilda's and St. Hugh's School in Manhattan, said she "cried for two days" when she heard that the upper school would close at year's end because of financial pressures. She talked on Tuesday with Martha Bagnall, center, and Jennifer Chen. (Keith Meyers/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Income, Spending Up Sharply

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Americans' personal income grew at its fastest clip in 10 months during October, and consumer spending posted a healthy gain, the Government reported yesterday.
The Commerce Department said incomes grew 1 percent, to a seasonally adjusted $5.13 trillion, in October, the biggest gain since December 1991, when incomes rose 1.2 percent. The gain last month followed a half-point increase during September. 
 Consumer spending rose seven-tenths of a percent in October, or three-tenths of a percent after inflation. That followed a gain of nine-tenths of a percent in September.
 
A Skewed Perspective
 Analysts said the October income figures overstated the rise, and that recent gains in consumption could not be expected to continue at more than a moderate rate until the economy started generating new jobs.
Much of the October gain in income stemmed from special nonrecurring circumstances, including a rebound in Hawaiian rental incomes after Hurricane Iniki, retirement incentive payments to Postal Service employees, and payments to auto workers. Excluding these one-time factors, income rose just four-tenths of a percentage point.
"None of the growth in income is coming from job growth; it is all due to special factors," said Edward F. McKelvey, a senior economist at Goldman, Sachs. "And you are not going to get any meaningful income growth until you get some sort of upward trajectory to job growth."
Gains in personal income include wages and salaries as well as income from sources such as dividends and interest.
Private sector wages and salaries rose six-tenths of a percent in October, to an annual rate of $2.94 billion, after falling one-tenth of a percent in September. The gain had been expected, largely because workers put in longer hours.
Similarly, analysts said the rise in consumer spending, which accounts for two-thirds of all economic activity, would be difficult to sustain unless more jobs were created.
"Consumption had a good third quarter and is starting off the fourth at a fairly high level," said James O'Sullivan, an economist at J. P. Morgan. "But from what we already know from auto sales and retail store surveys, it looks like spending slowed in November. And if consumption is to stay strong, income has to pick up, which means employment has to pick up."
Despite the caveats, analysts said the higher consumption is likely to translate into a brighter Christmas selling season for retailers.
"The point I have tried to make about Christmas is that the increases we have seen in consumer spending make a good Christmas season almost inevitable when compared to a year ago," Mr. McKelvey said. "But while modest, single-digit gains may sound good to retailers, they don't signal a real spending boom."
During October at least, Americans did not pay for current consumption out of savings.
The Commerce Department said the personal savings rate, which shows savings as a percentage of disposable income, rose to 4.7 percent in October from 4.4 percent a month earlier. Despite the improvement, Mr. O'Sullivan said the savings rate is a full half-point lower than it was six months ago.
The income and consumption numbers are the latest in a series of figures suggesting the economy is showing signs of life. Most recently, earlier this week, the Government revised upward its estimate of third-quarter growth in the gross domestic product, to 3.9 percent, from an earlier estimate of 2.7 percent. The new figure represents the best growth rate in nearly four years.
But many analysts say the stronger third-quarter growth represents merely a shift from activity that otherwise would have taken place in the current quarter. A good indication of whether the momentum is being sustained comes next Friday, when the Labor Department is expected to issue the November employment report.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Up in Quiet Trading; Gold Prices Also Advance

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
The dollar ended firmer yesterday in a quiet session, helped by technical factors and fresh signs of an improving economy.
The dollar found some support in personal income and spending figures. "The numbers were fairly good, but a holiday market was not willing to take the dollar up," said Hugh Walsh, assistant vice president at ABN/AMRO. 
 Many dealers extended the Thanksgiving holiday, and activity was extremely light, dealers said.
Gold prices ended higher. On the Commodity Exchange, gold bullion for current delivery settled at $334 an ounce, up 50 cents from Wednesday. A late quote from the Republic National Bank said gold closed up 75 cents at $334.60.
The dollar rose overnight, but failed to puncture technical resistance at 1.6080 marks. Dealers said the dollar's repeated failure at that level lent credence to reports the Bundesbank was selling the United States currency for marks, building its reserves for future intervention to support the European Exchange Rate Mechanism.
The Bundesbank deputy president, Hans Tietmeyer, said he saw no reason for the Irish pound or Danish crown to be devalued. But many dealers see a devaluation as inevitable now that Irish elections are over.
Dealers said the Bundesbank's decision at its fortnightly council meeting to hold interest rates steady weighed on the dollar. Mr. Tietmeyer's remarks that German interest rates were "not so high" also were viewed as an indication German rates would not be coming down soon.
"It appears now that Germany will just stick to its guns," said Belal Khan, senior customer dealer at the Bank of Tokyo.
He said the dollar's close below 1.60 marks, a key technical level, could portend further losses next week. The dollar was at 1.5985 marks in late New York trade, up from 1.5880 on Wednesday.
In Tokyo, the dollar closed at at 124.20 yen, up from 123.60 yen on Thursday. Later in London, it rose to 124.40 yen. In late trade in New York, the dollar was at 124.45 yen, up from 123.75 yen on Wednesday.
In London, the British pound softened to $1.5090, from $1.5249 on Wednesday. It edged higher, to $1.5105, in New York.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Wednesday rates, included: 1.4400 Swiss francs, up from 1.4270; 5.4275 French francs, up from 5.3820, and 1,394 Italian lire, up from 1,375.

HEADLINE: HOW THEY DO IT;
Mrs. Halvorsen Finds Lasting Benefits in an Unexpected Job Loss

 BODY:
Donna Halvorsen, a 42-year-old systems analysts, had worked for I.B.M. for 22 years when the company closed its Franklin Lakes, N.J. office and eliminated her job. She was offered a buyout or a job in Connecticut. Rather than uproot her family -- her husband, Drew, and 3-year-old son Jason -- she chose the buyout. Her main concern was the loss of benefits, because her husband, an oil-refinery pipe designer, is self-employed.
 After careful study of the 49-page buyout summary, which offered a variety of options, she came away with a generous severance package, including long-term insurance and a substantial sum of money to offset the loss of income. 
 After losing her job in July, she found a silver lining: She has more time with her son, and a change to develop a hobby designing fabric-covered picture frames (like the ones at right) into a small business.
 
AFTER THE BUYOUT
Benefits (many); losses (a few)
 
INSURANCE
 Group medical, dental and life insurance coverage continues at no cost except deductibles until 1999.
 
SEVERANCE
 One week's pay for every six months worked: $711 x 44 = $31,284
 Unused vacation pay: $1,000
 
401(k)
 Funds invested in 401(k) while at I.B.M. remain intact.
 
DISABILITY
 Coverage ended when she left I.B.M. She can purchase a disability policy for $60 a month if her business venture succeeds (if she earns a steady income for six months).
 
EASING HER CASH FLOW
 She now uses dividends of a life insurance policy, previously reinvested in the policy, to pay its monthly premiums ($145) and those of her husband's disability polices ($267).
 
UNEMPLOYMENT INSURANCE
 Because her job was eliminated by I.B.M., she may be eligible for unemployment compensation. Her case is pending; if approved, she will receive $308 a week for 13 weeks.

HEADLINE: FOOTBALL;
With Sadness in His Eyes, Toon Bids Farewell

BYLINE: By AL HARVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HEMPSTEAD, L.I., Nov. 27

 BODY:
Coach Bruce Coslet of the Jets called it a sad occasion and a happy occasion. So did Jane Toon, the wife of Al Toon, the Jets' wide receiver, who retired from pro football today at the age of 29.
The force behind Toon's decision stemmed from the concussion he received after being hit by Broncos linebacker Michael Brooks in the Jets' loss in Denver almost three weeks ago. What clinched the decision was the consensus among a half-dozen doctors, after a battery of tests, that Toon, who has had five concussions in the last six seasons, should not risk another blow to the head. 
 "You go to a doctor and get an opinion and you go to more than one doctor and get more than one opinion," Toon explained, misty eyed, during a news conference at Weeb Ewbank Hall at the Jets' training center. "You get multiple opinions. If all are the same, or concur with the decision, you have to rely on it."
 
'Didn't Think I'd Cry'
 Toon's retirement ended an eight-year career in which he set Jets records for receptions in a season (93) and in consecutive games (101). He finished second to Don Maynard in receptions for a career with 517 and third to Maynard and Wesley Walker in receiving yardage (6,605).
"I didn't think I'd cry but it's been a big part of my life," said Toon of the years he spent in New York after the Jets made him their first-round draft choice, out of Wisconsin, in 1985. Three times he was voted to the Pro Bowl team; no other Jets player made it that often during Toon's eight years.
"Al is is the consummate team player," said Coslet, who was hired before the 1990 season. "Right out of the box I made him team captain. You could just see the leadership qualities he had oozing from him the very first day.
"It's sad because one of the great players in the N.F.L. is retiring, and it's a happy occasion because he's O.K. and there's no harm to his life."
 
New Contract in February
 Toon led the Jets in receptions for six straight years through last season, and signed a new $4.1 million contract through the 1994 season last February. The Jets are planning to pay him in full for the remainder of this year, approximately $1.4 million, plus the $65,000 required by the league as a career-ending-injury settlement.
He had 31 catches for 311 yards and 2 touchdowns this year, third best on the team.
When asked to recall the highlight of his career, Toon went back to 1988, the season in which the 5-yard touchdown pass he caught with only 37 seconds to play in the season-ending game with the Giants knocked the Giants out of the playoffs.
But Toon cited a bigger personal highlight.
"I remember being in the hospital the night my son, Nicholas, was born," said Toon, who also has two daughters, Kirby, aged 2, and Molly Elizabeth, born last May. "I got very little sleep that night and then went and caught 13 passes against Indianapolis.
"My biggest regret? Not being able to finish like I want to."
At Wisconsin, Toon set school career records for receptions (127), yardage 2,055 and touchdowns (19). Toon, who made the dean's list his last four semesters, has a degree in economics and obtained a Wisconsin real estate license in the off season. He plans to stay on Long Island until the end of the season and then return to his home in Madison, Wis., and devote full time to business.
When asked who had made the final decision to retire, Toon's wife, Jane, said: "Oh, he takes care of things like that pretty much by himself. This is about as emotional as I've ever seen him get.
"For the first couple of days it was like any other concussion, but when the condition hung on and got worse over a time, that's when we knew, when we suspected something different. I'm happy he's in one piece. I'm sad to be leaving my friends."
Chris Burkett, the wide receiver who usually entered games to replace Toon, said he had no idea his teammate would retire so soon.
"I talk to Al every day," he said. "We're more friends off the field than anything else, and when we talk we basically don't talk football too much. From what I know it was a decision that had to be made and I have to be supportive of him, because he's not just a football player on this team. He's my friend."
Dale Dawkins, who replaced Toon on the roster after Toon was put on injured reserve, said:
"I was one of the guys who was inspired by him. After my first game I called him when I got back. I talked to him about 10 minutes, told him what I did on my first play, and all he did was just support me and was happy."
 
Hidden Value
 Chip Myers, the receivers' coach, discussed one of Toon's talents that never showed up in the statistics of the 6-foot-4-inch, 204-pound player.
"The biggest problem I have with Al Toon is he tried to do everything too perfectly," Myers said. "You don't ask wide receivers to block ordinarily, but we asked him to block because we were going to get the running game cranked up. And he's one of the best blockers that every played the game. I mean he just doesn't miss. Ever.
"He knew what to do as far as catching the ball and running with it. He's a potential big play on every down, but the funny thing was he never really believed he could block like he did."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Jets wide receiver Al Toon announcing his retirement yesterday after an eight-year career with the Jets (pg. 27); Wide receiver Al Toon hugging Coach Bruce Coslet as Toon made his way to the podium to announce his retirement from football yesterday. (pg. 30) (Ozier Muhammad/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: ON COLLEGE FOOTBALL;
It's Time to Create Financial Options

BYLINE: By Malcolm Moran

 BODY:
Early this evening, as a national cable-television audience watches, Marshall Faulk, the gifted running back from San Diego State, might or might not be able to push his sprained knee in a meeting with Miami and a transcontinental Heisman challenge against Hurricane quarterback Gino Torretta.
Faulk might or might not become the first sophomore to win a Heisman Trophy, and he might or might not eventually leave school unnecessarily early for the financial security professional football can bring. 
 In a game that manufactures uncertainty and pain, there is this much that the people who care about Marshall Faulk can count on: Ready or not, he has already been pushed into the marketplace despite an archaic system that treats athletes as waifs who must be protected against the evils of commercialism while their universities cut their deals.
And Faulk, because of the unreasonable restrictions imposed by those outdated rules, could soon become the kind of academic statistic that Dick Schultz and the reformers within the National Collegiate Athletic Association have been working to reduce.
Why is it, in the marketplace of the 1990's, that an admired Olympic competitor can build the security of a trust fund while pursuing an athletic goal while a college student must take a vow of poverty? Why must talented college athletes with the highest profiles -- particularly those like Faulk, from difficult economic backgrounds -- face a choice between continuing their educations and developing some financial stability?
Defenders of the system point to free college educations and the opportunities they provide as more than fair compensation. This might have made sense before the recent explosion of sports marketing. Athletes in big-time football and basketball programs occupy a place where Olympic-caliber athletes once found themselves. It is not a good place. There is more than enough evidence in campus bookstores, arena souvenir stands and neighborhood malls.
Small children at Notre Dame football games wrap themselves in official mesh jerseys with the No. 6, which just happens to belong to Jerome Bettis, a powerful and personable fullback.
A souvenir stand at the Palace of Auburn Hills, where a capacity crowd recently watched Michigan's basketball team play an exhibition game, displayed Detroit Piston items next to Michigan souvenirs. An Isiah Thomas jersey cost $39. A blue Michigan shirt with the yellow No. 44, which is worn by Chris Webber, the most famous of the sophomore starters known as the Fab Five, was listed at $65. A similar shirt, through mail order, is even more expensive.
Last summer, a walk through a mall in the Midwest included a pass through a sports memorabilia store with autographed pictures and items: Mickey Mantle. Muhammad Ali. Sandy Koufax. Nolan Ryan. Jose Canseco. The Fab Five. An autographed basketball, which included the signatures of the five Michigan freshmen who reached last year's N.C.A.A. championship game, was selling for $300.
Where does the money go?
This is not about getting rich. If a college athlete is more interested in becoming wealthy than earning a degree, that's fine. Goodbye, good luck, and get a good agent. This is about the attainment of a reasonable piece of the pie.
The N.C.A.A.'s Presidents Commission has taken an aggressive approach to pushing through changes that give athletes a better chance to earn meaningful degrees. Insurance policies available through the N.C.A.A. now protect valuable commodities from the economic damage of a career-threatening injury.
A next step could reduce the influence of the sleaze factor and the flesh peddlers, and establish a carefully regulated system that puts the money -- at long last -- atop the table.
If an athlete wishes to go beyond tuition, room and board, he or she can approach a marketing representative of the university. The question that is already asked in too many clandestine conversations -- what can you do for me? -- could become an acceptable part of the recruiting process. Proposals can be made. The marketplace can be analyzed. A paper trail would account for every dime.
And here are the rules: The athlete must be academically eligible. The amount of the athletic scholarship would be repaid and placed in a general scholarship fund, available to all students.
Any additional income would fall into two categories: living expenses for the athlete and his immediate family, with the remainder divided between the university and a trust fund that would not become available to the athlete until all eligibility has been completed.
The alternative is obvious. College teams will continue to lose their biggest attractions prematurely, and nervous athletes will leave still semesters away from attaining degrees.
Or the environment could become nasty. After the player protests this football season at South Carolina, Memphis State, Oklahoma and Morgan State, how long will it take for athletes to challenge a system that directs the profits to everyone but them?
P.S. The schools could make more money, too. That ought to get someone's attention.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Haitian Refugees: Don't Panic. Plan.

 BODY:
Bill Clinton's promise to ease the Bush Administration's punitive policies toward Haitian refugees has raised excessive expectations in Haiti and excessive fears here. All the President-elect proposes is to return to traditional interpretations of international practice and U.S. law, assuring Haitians who claim political persecution a fair hearing.
That's only decency, not reckless generosity. The new Administration is not about to open America's doors to every Haitian seeking a better life. It merely intends to abandon the Bush policy of turning back Haitian boats on the high seas without giving their occupants a chance to demonstrate legitimate claims to asylum. 
 The new approach will burden American facilities and resources. But with prompt planning, the load can be managed. Departures from Haiti are up already and could soar even before Inauguration Day if the Supreme Court strikes down President Bush's authorization of pre-emptive interceptions.
Clinton spokesmen talk of carrying out asylum interviews in other countries or on shipboard. That seems unrealistic. No other countries in the region have been willing to admit large numbers of Haitians, even temporarily. Coast Guard and Navy ships can't accommodate large groups awaiting processing for long periods. That leaves American bases like the one at Guantanamo Bay, supplemented by scattered-site facilities on the U.S. mainland.
Without a major change in Haiti's political situation, applicants could number 100,000 or more. Judging from interviews at Guantanamo earlier this year, only about a third will pass initial screening. Most of these will eventually qualify for something called Temporary Protected Status -- revocable in theory should repression in Haiti ease.
Diplomatic efforts to restore Jean-Bertrand Aristide, deposed as President 14 months ago, are now stalemated. A regional economic embargo has been too porous to exert real pressure. And the Organization of American States has hesitated to apply tougher measures.
That leaves scope for a Clinton Administration to increase diplomatic pressure on the Haitian military, the real power behind Prime Minister Marc Bazin. It could lean on European and other allies to join the hemisphere's embargo. It could use the Navy and Coast Guard to tighten enforcement. And it could restrict the travel and economic privileges of close collaborators with the Haitian dictatorship when they come to the United States.
None of this is guaranteed to produce quick results, given the bitterness of the Haitian quarrel and the reluctance on all sides to compromise. Meanwhile, it's time to begin preparing for the inevitable increase in refugees.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

LENGTH: 2081 words

HEADLINE: Without Plumbing, Villages In Alaska Face Health Crisis

BYLINE: By JASON DePARLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: KOTLIK, Alaska

 BODY:
This tiny fishing village on the Bering Sea offers most of the conveniences of modern American life. People here can get the Disney Channel on cable television, dial out on touch-tone phones, and rent "Wayne's World" at a local store.
But the one technology they lack is creating one of the nation's most poignant public health problems. 
 They do not have toilets.
 
Disease and Resentment
 Permafrost and poor soils make sewage systems extremely expensive. So natives here and in many Alaskan villages must haul drinking water in barrels and go to the bathroom in buckets.
The combination of primitive technology, a growing population and a Government policy that encouraged Alaskan Indians to consolidate into villages has produced a major sewage and water crisis. It is causing outbreaks of disease, deepening racial resentments and underscoring the shortcomings of the United States' health system, which brings modern miracles to some of its people while leaving others to want for the simplest care.
"The most basic components of health care are clean water and adequate sanitation," said Dr. Donn Kruse, medical director of the Yukon-Kuskokwim Health Corporation in Bethel, which serves 48 villages in western Alaska. "We send Peace Corps volunteers to solve these problems around the world, but we haven't solved them here."
State officials say that only about half of the approximately 200 villages in Alaska have flush toilets and running water inside natives' homes. And with tight budgets in both Washington and Juneau, no one expects the situation to change soon.
But one fact of life, in Kotlik and many other villages, has not escaped local notice: the white people in town, about a dozen teachers, have expensive, fully modern systems, paid for by the Federal Government. Their housing, built by the Bureau of Indian Affairs to attract teachers to the village, has heated pipes that pump in running water and whisk away the sewage to a specially built lagoon.
Water and sewer problems did not afflict previous generations of natives, most of whom lived in small nomadic groups and did not settle in sufficient numbers for sewage to accumulate. The introduction of a cash economy and Government schools brought a consolidation of these groups into villages. Now the villages are undergoing a population explosion, and no one knows where to put the sewage.
In the last decade, the 500 residents of Kotlik have experienced epidemics that seemed to have leapt from the pages of Exodus. First a wave of boils swept over town, raising sores the size of silver dollars.
Then came an outbreak of viral meningitis, causing about 1 person in 10 to be flown to the nearest hospital, 200 miles away, while officials worried it would sweep the state. And this summer in nearby Hooper Bay, the contamination of a public well left one person dead and hundreds ill.
The conditions in Kotlik have made sanitation a civic fixation, with people here aspiring to toilets the way other communities dream of landing a Madonna concert or a professional football team.
No one feels the shame and frustration more than Joe Uisok, the hapless sanitation worker who empties the village's raw sewage bins. His work brings him coughs, colds, stinking clothes and snickers from the townspeople, who have so little public money they hold charity bingo games to help pay his salary. "And," he complained "there's no health insurance if I get sick."
 
Created by Government
 Kotlik is largely a Government creation. Until a generation ago, the Yupik Eskimo people who inhabit this pond-dappled delta of the Yukon River 475 miles west of Anchorage, lived in small clans and moved among favorite hunting and fishing spots.
Now most Kotlik residents live in trim wooden houses and shop in stores that brim with the offerings of American life, from boxes of Triscuits to tapes of Garth Brooks. There are no roads, but tiny planes arrive several times a day from Bethel, the delta's hub. Many families still hunt and fish, but unemployment, food stamps and neediness run high.
A major change began in the 1960's, when the Federal Government required native children to attend school, quickening the consolidation of subsistence outposts into a burgeoning villages. Until 1962, Kotlik was home to only a handful of Yupiks, one of the five major groups of natives who were living in Alaska before settlers arrived from Russia and the United States. The opening of a school that year brought in Yupik families from nearby Pastolik, Chaneliak, Hamilton and Bill Moore's Slew.
As a public-health story, the move to villages, which occurred at different times across the state, is mostly a tale of success, since it brought better housing and nutrition. For instance, Dr. Kruse said, the life expectancy of a Yupik male has grown to 67 years today from 47 years in 1960.
But Dr. Kruse calls sanitation a major piece of unfinished business in village life, a villainous circumstance without a clear villain.
"The problem with water and sewer is that there's nothing that could be called a moderate-price fix," Dr. Kruse said. "We either have to spend a fortune or accept third world living conditions."
The costs are driven by climate and isolation. Permafrost keeps the ground frozen year-round in some villages and a high water table and poor soils also make underground piping impractical. So the pipes must run above ground, wrapped in thick insulation and heated by wires. With snow usually on the ground for nine months a year, the building season is short. And supplies must be shipped by plane or barge.
Most of the teachers say they could not live under the conditions their students endure.
"If I didn't have sewerage and water, I'd be gone," said Richard Nathan, a white teacher who has taught in Kotlik for 14 years.
It is hard for people accustomed to modern plumbing to understand what living without it can mean. Several years ago, Mr. Nathan's high school class sent a letter to every member of Congress, to the President and to other political leaders. "We are living in conditions that are as bad or worse than many of the migrant labor camps of the 60's," they wrote. No one answered.
Last year, Rita Okitkun, 15 years old, traveled to Washington as part of a student tour and met with members of the state's Congressional delegation. She said her plea for modern sanitation fell on deaf ears. "We don't mean nothing because we're just these Yupik people out in the middle of nowhere," she said.
State officials say the situation in Kotlik is not unusual. "There are places more primitive than Kotlik, if you can believe that," said Keith Kelton, director of facilities construction at the state Department of Environmental Conservation.
The Anchorage Daily News recently estimated that state and Federal programs have already spent $1.3 billion to modernize sanitation in the villages, which are home to about 45,000 Alaskan natives.
The Federal Government estimates it would cost another $1 billion to put modern plumbing in every village. But the state, strapped by declining oil revenues, spends only about $25 million a year to upgrade systems, and various Federal programs contribute approximately $40 million more.
And construction is just the beginning, since maintenance ussually runs about $100 per month per household, which few village families can afford. Plus the systems require a technical expertise that many villages lack. Stories of squandered sanitation money abound in rural Alaska. In Mountain Village, for instance, a $2 million water system froze in the first winter, bringing a $750,000 repair bill, Dr. Kruse said.
 
Lack of Expertise
 In Kotlik, Joseph Mike, the utilities manager, is the first to announce his modest qualifications for monitoring the single public well that provides the village's drinking water. "I didn't even finish the sixth grade," he said apologetically.
A notice from state officials posted near Mr. Mike's office warns that the town water "may be contaminated" and urges residents to boil it. But Mr. Mike was paying it no more attention than anyone else. "I don't know -- too much chlorine or something," he said, when asked about the warning.
The dangers of malfunctioning systems were tragically illustrated this summer in Hooper Bay, which appears to have produced the nation's first death from a fluoride overdose in drinking water. The exact causes of the poisoning are being sorted out in a $3 million lawsuit by the victim's family. But it appears that both faulty equipment and untrained operators were to blame.
The mechanical problem was a broken pump that sent the well's fluoride to a level 40 times the Federal limit. But a report by the state epidemiologist states that health officials in Bethel had spotted the rising fluoride level three weeks earlier and had ordered the well shut down, apparently to no avail.
The report makes a point of noting that the well operator had no formal training and that the village had had no government administrator for the previous year.
 
Speedway of Contamination
 Kotlik, like much of rural Alaska, muddles along with what is delicately known as the honey-bucket system. Residents use five-gallon buckets lined with plastic bags and topped with a toilet seat. The bags are then tied, lifted and carried to one of the 27 large vats that Mr. Uisok hauls to a lined lagoon.
A central washing complex provides treated water, which many residents haul home in barrels, though others, recoiling from the chlorine, prefer rain water. The complex also contains a $2.50 sauna, 75-cent showers and a couple of public toilets that empty into the teachers' lagoon.
The problem with sewage buckets and water barrels is that they combine to form a speedway of contamination, whisking disease from one household to another. Buckets slop. Bags break. Children track waste across floors, furniture, counters and utensils. Dirty hands get dipped in water barrels. And once a barrel is contaminated, it stays that way.
During the boil outbreak in 1985, Federal investigators came all the way from Atlanta, to try to isolate the site of infection. "But the germ was everywhere they looked -- in the sauna, in people's bedding, in their noses," Dr. Kruse said.
Diarrhea and impetigo are chronic, with the table always set for something more serious. In June 1990, residents were still emptying their honey buckets into wooden bunkers, which overflowed with the spring thaw. Suddenly one person after another succumbed to violent headaches, vomiting and high fevers.
 
A Harsh Job
 Forty-nine people were flown to the hospital in Bethel, where they were found to have viral meningitis, and Dr. Kruse said dozens more had the symptoms and stayed in Kotlik. State officials geared up for 1,500 cases, but an informal quarantine halted the spread.
After the outbreak, Kotlik got a $50,000 Federal grant to switch from bunkers to the 27 plastic vats that line the wooden walkways, awaiting Mr. Uisok. He pulls them onto a trailer, drives an all-terrain vehicle to the edge of town and backs to the edge of a lined lagoon. He removes the lids, takes aim and tilts. The plastic bags splash out with frightening imprecision.
"I threw up when I first started," Mr. Uisok said.
The job is so messy that Mr. Uisok wears a rain suit. His daily path can be tracked by a trail of decontaminating lime, which marks his mishaps like so much fingerprinting dust. Despite temperatures that can reach 10 degrees below, he prefers to work in winter, he said, when his haul "pops out like an icicle."
It is not duty that drives Mr. Uisok, who is 24 years old, but economic necessity. He moved to Kotlik from a neighboring village when he got married, and he now has four children. He said there are no other jobs in town.
Behind his back, the villagers call Mr. Uisok the "anuk man," employing a unflattering Yupik term for sewage. Mr. Uisok insists he is worth more than the $10 an hour he gets, and he resents having to pay for the daily sauna and shower he takes after work.
But Kotlik recently rejected a proposed sales-tax increase, by a vote of 55 to 21. So the village must rely on donations from charity bingo games, held four times a week, to meet Mr. Uisok's current wage.
 A caption with a picture accompanying the article in some editions, showing children playing in a flooded street, misidentified the town shown. It was Bethel, not Kotlik.

SECTION: Section 7; Page 23; Column 1; Book Review Desk

HEADLINE: 'My Enemy Brother'

BYLINE: By Clyde Haberman; Clyde Haberman is the Jerusalem bureau chief of The New York Times.

 BODY:

WITH FRIENDS LIKE YOU
What Israelis Really Think About American Jews.
By Matti Golan.
Translated by Hillel Halkin.
184 pp. New York:
The Free Press. $19.95. 
 MATTI GOLAN, a former editor at two Israeli newspapers, wastes no time shooting his target between the eyes: American Jews, he says right away in "With Friends Like You," are more of an enemy to Israel than Yassir Arafat.
How's that again?
No, Mr. Golan says, he is not in rhetorical overdrive. At least with Mr. Arafat he knows where he stands. Not so with American Jewry -- "my enemy brother."
As far as he is concerned, Jews in the United States undermine his country insidiously, most of all by refusing to emigrate to Israel, thereby depriving it of energy and know-how that it sorely needs. The fact is, Mr. Golan argues, Israelis and American Jews are separate peoples who happen to share a religion but whose relationship is based on bad faith and hypocrisy. How can they be real partners, he asks bitterly, when one of them sends 18-year-olds off to college while the other waves goodbye, perhaps forever, to sons setting off for military duty in Gaza?
Ably translated by Hillel Halkin, this slender volume, which could have been even more slender with tighter editing, is hardly the first indictment by an Israeli of American Jews. But its attacks are so unsparing -- and often so uncomfortably on the mark -- that there will be squirming in the chairs at Hadassah chapters all across the United States. Maybe that is good. Mr. Golan gets a few things off his chest that should be heard, even if they are sometimes overstated.
His technique is an imaginary conversation between two middle-aged Jews, an Israeli named Israel and an American named Judah. Israel, being an Israeli, swiftly dispenses with the notion that they like each other. "I get on your nerves," he tells Judah. "Honestly, isn't that what you say about me when you're alone with your friends? That I'm arrogant? Aggressive? Loudmouthed?"
As for the American, Israel complains, he thinks that writing a fat check to the United Jewish Appeal somehow gives him the right to tell Israelis how to run their affairs. Sure, Judah says he only has Israel's best interests at heart. But his own interests matter even more. What he really craves is a country that makes him look better to gentiles: oh, for the Jewish Camelot of six-day wars and Entebbe raids, not this place worn down by an endless Palestinian uprising and horrible images of Israeli soldiers breaking the bones of Arab children.
Actually, the bloom slipped off the Israeli rose for American Jews well before the intifada, Israel says. It started with the 1973 war, when the Jewish state's vaunted heroes were caught flat-footed. Then Arabs sent oil prices soaring. Global terrorism and other dislocations became ways of life. In no time, he maintains, an unflinchingly pro-Israel stand fell out of fashion.
But what about the hundreds of millions of dollars that American Jews give each year to Israel? Big deal, Israel shrugs.
For starters, the money amounts to an insurance premium, paid by overseas Jews who look upon Israel mostly as a potential haven should the Cossacks turn up at the door again. Besides, Israel argues, the sums are not all that vast. Much of the cash goes to Jewish institutions in the United States, with the state of Israel (and sometimes even the Holocaust) used by fund-raisers as a hook. It is a lot easier, after all, to get a donation from someone who thinks he is helping the embattled Jewish state, not buying bedpans for the local hospital.
"You've made us your clowns, your vaudeville act," Israel rails. And the worst part is that the Israelis themselves have become willing accomplices, performing handstands to milk the American "money machine" and to win expense-paid fund-raising trips to the United States so that they can briefly escape daily lunacies back home.
Some readers will dismiss Mr. Golan as an over-the-top crank. Indeed, he is not always fair: he gives the back of his hand, for example, to Jewish lobbyists who help assure the United States military and economic aid -- $3 billion every year -- on which Israel is hopelessly hooked.
But the author responds that the lobbying would be superfluous if American Jews would only emigrate to Israel in waves instead of rivulets. Israel would be so strengthened, he says, that it would not need all that help from Washington. As a bonus, he argues, emigration might even rescue American Judaism, which is threatened not by anti-Semitism but by a rampant secularism that has produced a menacing intermarriage rate of 50 percent.
The way things are going, Mr. Golan's Israel warns, "I am now too tired and weak to preserve Israel and you are too comfortable and well padded to be able to preserve your Judaism." It does not sound as though he expects much to improve soon.
