PUB-DATE: November 29, 1992

SECTION: Section 13CN; Page 1; Column 1; Connecticut Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 1819 words

HEADLINE: New Corporate Credo Addresses Society's Ills


BYLINE: In one of the city's poorer neighborhoods, the Rev. Clyde Joyner, pastor of St. Peter's Baptist Church, is closer to the problems of crime, unemployment, drugs and the homeless. Like the sparkling chrome and glass of the People's Bank building downtown, Mr. Joyner's small one-story white church serves as a beacon for a smaller community -- those Bridgeport residents who are struggling to keep their families together.
By KATHLEEN SALUK FAILLAFROM the top of downtown Bridgeport's tallest building, where People's Bank established its corporate headquarters in 1989 at a time when most businesses were leaving, its president, David E. A. Carson, has an unobstructed view of the state's largest city. The bird's-eye view does little to distance him, however, from the problems that plague Bridgeport. Nor would Mr. Carson want it that way.

 BODY:
 On the surface, there is little to connect the two men. But in reality, they are part of a movement among business people who recognize that society's problems cannot be solved by government alone.
All across America, corporations are finding themselves increasingly coming to the aid the nation's social welfare and educational systems. In Connecticut, more and more businesses, large and small, are reaching out to their communities, providing help in a variety of ways. From direct financing for early-childhood health care to restructuring school curriculums, corporations are taking on a role traditionally entrusted to government.
While there is no overall figure available on how much money is being spent in community outreach programs, it is evident that momentum is gaining throughout the state. Employers like Aetna, Champion International, Travelers and Xerox have steadily increased their commitment to programs that not only help their employees with such services as day care but also improve the quality of life in the community. For example, the Aetna Foundation, with fewer dollars to spend this year because of business conditions, has redirected its efforts from housing to health care for young children.
In September, it announced a $1 million grant to the Hartford Childhood Immunization Project, which plans to increase the immunization rate of Hartford's children against preventable diseases, like whooping cough, measels, polio and diphtheria.
In Bridgeport, the president of People's Bank is a graduate of the University of Michigan School of Business, who is on a first-name basis with politicians and business leaders alike. Mr. Joyner, a minister for 32 years and founder of St. Peter's, has worked at several blue-collar jobs. He has been employed as a mechanic by the Aquarion Company since 1969, when it was known as the Bridgeport Hydraulic Company.
 
Government Forced to Scale Back
 Differences aside, Mr. Carson and Mr. Joyner are painfully aware of the social and economic changes taking place in Connecticut, and Bridgeport in particular. They recognize that government has been forced to scale back and, in many cases, no longer meet the needs of the families, particularly the children. Education budgets are being slashed at a time when the school system is under critical review. Students, unprepared to meet the needs of the work place, continue to graduate from schools. And the ranks of the unemployed are increasing, adding further to the state's social ills.
More importantly, they know that Connecticut cannot stand alone in addressing these needs without help from the business community. Individually, they are urging businesses to make a commitment by taking a more active role in the problems of their communities.
The Acting Commissioner of the State Commission on Children, Elaine Zimmerman, said that simple cost-effective programs, like improved health care and day care, could do much toward assuring that students would succeed in school. The commissioner said that for every dollar invested in young children, the state would save an average of $4.75 per taxpayer in terms of the expense of more costly problems like illness, school dropouts, welfare dependency and teen-age pregnancy.
Mr. Carson is serving as chairman of a business advisory committee for Kids Count, the commission's early-childhood project designed to prepare children for school. The success of this committee can be measured by its influence. In this year's session of the General Assembly, Mr. Carson and other business leaders supported bills ranging from free immunization to preventive health care and full-day Head Start.
 
Care for Uninsured Children
 Their support was vital in the passage of such legislation that standardizes training for child-care providers, provides intervention measures for crack babies, supports preventive health care for uninsured children, and phases in a full-day Head Start program, Commissioner Zimmerman said. "I think these business leaders helped us move an agenda faster. We've gained a few years and we've capitalized on an opportunity that might have been closed."
The role business can play is also evident to Mr. Joyner. Unlike Mr. Carson, who got involved with Kids Count after a call from the Commission on Children, Mr. Joyner's call came in a different way.
Earlier this year, while praying, he said he was directed to use his expertise as a minister to help people in the community beyond his church. He sought to do this by involving his company, and he met with the company's president. As a result of that meeting, the company's chief executive officer, Jack E. McGregor, considered ways the company could expand its current involvement in the community. Aquarion was already involved at the Barnum Elementary School through the Adopt a School program. Mr. McGregor approachedthe Bridgeport Police Department, where Mr. Joyner's full-time help in community service work was welcomed.
 
Crime Victims and Crime Prevention
 Mr. Joyner helped the police in their DARE program, advising youngsters on the risks of drugs and alcohol. He counseled crime victims and their families, and helped elderly people with crime prevention. After six months, he returned to his company, where he was promoted to the new job of community relations representative. He is now working as an executive on loan to the United Way.
The associate executive director of the United Way of Eastern Fairfield County, Sherbie Worthen, said there has been a surge of new interest by business and community programs. "We tend to put business on the shelf as not knowledgeable about these issues and not wanting to get involved, well that's not true," Ms. Worthen said. She said business understood that social problems would cost more to fix later.
"It's not just philanthrophy at work here -- it's enlightened self-interest," maintained Timothy H. Smith, executive director of the Interfaith Center on Corporate Responsibility in New York. Companies are being pressured by shareholders, politicians and community leaders to become more socially responsible, he said.
 
Immunization as 'Centerpiece'
 In addition to a desire to be a good citizen, corporations have learned that it makes good business sense to focus on social service. For example, the director of corporate public involvement for Aetna, Thomas Q. Callahan, said his company decided to make the immunization grant its "centerpiece" in corporate giving because it fit in with their business as an insurance company. "I wouldn't say it's purely altruistic, in that it's related to one of the businesses we're involved in," Mr. Callahan said.
Although some businesses have long had policies relating to social consciousness, for example, giving money for scholarships, the realization in recent years that education is important to the pool of qualified workers has caused many companies to expand their traditional roles.
Companies across the state are increasingly becoming involved in the schools, either on the local or state level. Local programs include the Adopt A School model program in Norwalk, Champion International's partnership with Stamford educators to improve the middle school curriculum, and a variety of mentoring and tutoring programs throughout the state.
 
Statewide Initiatives
 Several statewide initiatives are also in place, the most prominent being the Connecticut Business for Education Coalition, which is lead by Robert D. Kennedy, chairman and chief executive officer of Union Carbide. He is a leader in the American Business Roundtable's work to help public education meet President Bush's national goals. In Connecticut, the broad-based coalition of business leaders is promoting revisions in education.
In Hartford, the Connecticut Business and Industry Association is working with the Hartford Alliance for Mathematics and Science Education to test a new math curriculum that, if successful, would be used nationwide. The association helped obtain a $3.7 million grant from the National Science Foundation for the project. John Rathgeber, the association's executive vice president, said Connecticut's business leaders were concerned that entry-level employees lacked the necessary language and math skills and sense of teamwork to help keep Connectiut companies competitive.
The high cost of training employees without basic skills has thrust many companies into the forefront of educational revisions. "Business has been involved in minor ways, up until now," said George Bellinger, chief executive officer of Bar-Pat, an electronics company in Bridgeport. The urgency has developed, he said, because "business believes the products the schools are producing, the children, are not business ready." He takes his colleagues to task for complaining and not getting involved. "Business people are at fault if they haven't concerned themselves with something as simple as education."
Mr. Bellinger is a member of the business advisory committee of Kids Count and also teaches eighth-grade students in the Junior Achievement program.
 
Toward a Broader Perspective
 Mr. Carson of People's Bank said business people and educators must work together to develop a broader perspective on the issues. "The U.S. has the finest higher education system in the world, but we haven't been as successful in providing quality education in the younger years," he said. There is much talk today, he continued, about improving school performance by introducing the concept that has worked well for business called "Total Quality Management," whose goal is to encourage employee performance through teamwork.
A decade ago, most business people would not have anticipated the extent of their involvement in social and educational issues. Indeed, neither did legislative leaders, who during the last session of the General Assembly voted to establish an additional commission, which brings together business leaders, educators and legislators to study the state's educational system.
Former State Representative Alice Meyer, of Easton, who served on several business and education committees in the legislature, is a member of the new commission, called the Commission on Education Excellence for Connecticut. In the past, Mrs. Meyer said, many legislators refused to listen to business. Today's economy has forced them to be more attentive, she asserted. "I think they're listening a lot more because their constituents don't have jobs and it is a lot more obvious," Mrs. Meyer said.
The growing influence of business, coupled with the state's commitment to revise education standards, may produce some substantial changes in the decade ahead, the experts say.
Meanwhile business has found itself taking on a variety of new responsibilities. "What we need to do is make success happen," Mr. Carson admonished.

HEADLINE: Talking: Paydowns;
Reducing Mortgage Principal

BYLINE: By ANDREE BROOKS

 BODY:
ABOUT two years ago, Paul Havemann, vice president of HSH, a mortgage monitoring and advisory service in Butler, N.J., decided to personally try out an idea increasingly being promoted in his industry: making additional principal payments each month so the balance of his home mortgage could be paid off faster.
He had been in his Jefferson Township house only two years and, like other homeowners, was worried that any attempt to refinance could be rejected if the value of his home had dropped too sharply. This is likely to happen when a mortgage balance loan exceeds the customary 80 percent loan-to-value limit. 
 The same heavy balance might also preclude changing over to a 15-year loan. Further, retaining a hefty loan balance, he concluded, could also generate higher closing costs if and when he refinanced.
Mr. Havemann was equally concerned that if he needed to sell in a hurry he might be left with so high a mortgage compared to what he could get for the house that he might need an infusion of cash just to pay off the balance. So every month he's been adding an extra $70.
There are compelling reasons these days to pay off a mortgage principal faster. The customary method is to add $25 to $100 each month to the required payments and note on the payment slip that the extra is for reducing the principal (not for paying part of the next month's bill in advance).
Nevertheless, the idea remains controversial. For one, it violates a sacrosanct rule that suggests people fare best when they diversify their assets.
For instance, Gary Greenbaum, president of Greenbaum & Associates of Oradell, N.J., investment advisers, argues that the average homeowner already has about 30 percent of the family's total net worth tied up in the home. To add more would be putting too many eggs in one basket. Moreover, in a declining or stagnant property market, that money is not really helping to increase the asset base. Thus any extra money may be better invested elsewhere. However, he concedes it could make sense for people who cannot cope with risk.
Lewis J. Altfest, president of L.J. Altfest & Company, financial advisers in Manhattan, disagrees. He maintains this is an ideal time to pay down a mortgage balance because so many other investment vehicles are paying such paltry returns.
Further, the interest charges that can be saved, even taking into account their tax-deductibility, create savings that in the long run are cost-effective for most homeowners. Moreover, paying down a mortgage guarantees a reduced debt load. Paying off early also makes more sense in times of low inflation.
Larry Hagemann, an engineer in Chicago, would agree. His goal was to have his mortgage paid off by the time his daughter started college in the fall of 1993. He also knew he needed a way to lock up his savings rather than have them available to spend on other things. Further, he looked around and knew he could not easily get the sort of 10 to 12 percent returns available in the 80's.
So early in 1989 he began adding $120 each month to the $360 he was already paying -- the amount he had figured he would need to pay off the $18,000 outstanding balance by August 1993. The idea also appealed to him, he said, because he had calculated he could save $27,000 in interest payments over the life of the $50,000 mortgage.
And even though he would be losing a tax deduction, it was not all that meaningful, he said, for anyone in the 28-33 percent tax bracket. "I would only save about 30 cents for every dollar I paid interest," he noted. Moreover, he figured he could always take out an equity loan if he ever needed extra cash.
However, most advisers caution that it's more important to start by paying off non-deductible debt, like credit card balances and car loans, before working on a mortgage prinicpal.
BY FAR the best benefits will accrue to the person who has bought a home in the last four or five years, according to Marc Eisenson, president of Banker's Secret of Elizaville, N.Y., a company that sells computer software and books to help homeowners make their own calculations.
This, he said, is because the savings become most pronounced when a balance is paid down fast in the early years. He cites the following example: If someone paid an extra $100 a month on a $100,000, 30-year loan during the first five years and never paid another additional cent, the term would be reduced by five years, at a saving of $38,035 over the life of the loan.
By contrast, the person who started with the same $100,000 loan but waited 10 years to begin making additional payments would reduce the term by only 2.5 years and interest charges by $16,026.
But why not get similar results by changing to a 15-year mortgage? For one, it could mean paying a new round of closing costs that could reach 5 percent of the outstanding balance. Second, it obligates the homeowner to higher payments each month irrespective of whether those payments are convenient. "There is a real advantage in tough times to being able to slack off," said Mr. Eisenson.
Moreover, if the homeowner is concerned that the optional payment method creates a temptation to skip the additional principal payments, there are other ways to prevent backsliding.
Chase Home Mortgage, for example, has a program that lets the homeowner authorize Chase to make an automatic withdrawal of the additional sum from a checking account each month. And unlike payments on a 15-year loan, the arrangement can also be halted any time, said Tony Petrizzo, Chase's vice president for customer support.
But there are caveats. Paying extra each month will not reduce the size of the monthly payments -- those terms are set by contract when the loan is taken out. And it's important to keep tabs on how much extra is being paid in (some lenders, like Chase, include this calculation in their regular statements).
To find out specifically what the saving might be you could start by asking the lender to produce some estimates based on how much you intend to add each month.
There are also books and software that can help compute tables and answers. "The Banker's Secret" (1-800-255-0899), books and software, is one source. Another is a program called "Mastering Your Mortgage" from Home Equity Software, available for about $40. So far, however, the program works only with IBM-compatible computers. The Mac version will be available late next year.

HEADLINE: In the Region: Connecticut;
South Norwalk Commercial Pump Primed

BYLINE: By ELEANOR CHARLES

 BODY:
A ROLLER-COASTER economy over the last decade has taken the rehabilitation of South Norwalk to the heights of the mid-80's, the depths of the 90's and the occasional whiplash stop.
Rather than continue at the mercy of such forces, the city decided in 1991 to include in its capital budget a $1 million fund for what it calls the Facade Improvement Program, which can -- despite its name -- include other exterior work such as landscaping. 
 By injecting this quick-fix investment into lagging business districts, the program has in less than two years been successful enough to win an award for outstanding planning from the American Planning Association, a professional organization based in Washington.
To date, 16 retail and commercial exterior rehabilitation projects have been primed with city grants ranging from $6,500 to $75,000. While property owners must put up a minimum of 35 percent of the total rehabilitation cost, and several have invested a good deal more, most say they would not have gone ahead on their own in this economy.
Edward J. Musante, Jr., director of the Norwalk Redevelopment Agency, which administers the program, said there had been a snowball effect.
"When one building is improved, others on the block tend to follow suit," he said.
His agency applies for new funds annually, subject to approval by the city's Common Council.
Joseph Caiati, a Westchester furniture retailer who has owned a warehouse in South Norwalk since 1985, called the program "an incentive and an inspiration."
"I have not seen anything as serious as this anywhere," he said. With a $75,000 grant and $118,000 of his own money he converted the site at Main and Elizabeth Streets into a retail furniture store with a 30-car landscaped parking lot.
Norwalk's business districts, running from South Main to North Main Street below I-95, and onto West Avenue and Wall Street north of I-95, have suffered heavily since the 1950's with the loss of industries that moved south and shops that moved to suburban malls.
In an initial attempt at renewal in the early 80's, Washington Street, near the mouth of the Norwalk River, was transformed by the Collins Development Corporation of Stamford, with the help of Federal tax credits, from a wasteland of derelict, burned-out brick buildings sheltering junkies and prostitutes into a tree-lined thoroughfare of shops, restaurants and condominiums.
RECLAIMING its 19th-century charm, the street became a model for similar restorations of buildings throughout the city. A prime example is the four-year-old Maritime Center on Water Street, converted from a defunct factory.
But when the economy weakened, dozens of plans for redevelopment, including a $40 million housing and retail complex to be built by the Starrett Corporation of New York around a new South Norwalk railroad station, were put on hold.
Now Norwalk is redefining itself "not as a city like Stamford," said John L. Burritt, assistant director of redevelopment, "not New Canaan or Darien, although some residential areas look like those towns. We decided to retain a small city flavor, attract small manufacturers and one-of-a-kind retailers."
That may not be easy.
"I don't have many tenants interested in South Norwalk, especially below I-95," said Peter Corbett, a retail broker at CB Commercial in Stamford. "They don't want to be pioneers."
"There is still a perception of crime south of I-95, of space sitting for a long time, of a lot of turnover on Washington Street and ugly public housing projects," he continued. "But Norwalk is taking a real pro-active stance. They are doing the right thing and if anyone can, they will pull it off. Not tomorrow or next year, but eventually."
North of I-95 on West Avenue, The Gap/ Gap Kids store constantly bustles, and a new CVS Pharmacy is expanding by 1,000 square feet, both without city assistance.
On Wall Street, David Harvey Jewelers spent $300,000 in addition to a $75,000 city grant to expand and renovate and the Fairfield County Savings Bank is enlarging into adjacent space with help from the facade program.
Last summer the city spent $100,000 on 750 linear feet of new transient boat docks at Veteran's Park, across the river from Washington Street. The idea is to link boaters and Maritime Center visitors to a network of streets that will eventually offer a wide range of shopping, restaurants and entertainment.
SoNo Cinema on Washington Street, known for foreign films, is undergoing a facelift with the help of the city program, and a 24,000-square-foot block of dilapidated North Main Street shops between Marshall and Ann Streets has been refurbished by Bruce Beinfield, the owner-architect, and his partner, Clayton Fowler, with a city grant. Rents for about 7,000 square feet remaining range from $10 to $12 a square foot.
The rear of the building, a block and a half from the Maritime Center, faces the New England Brewery, a local beer maker, pub, and beer museum that is taking advantage of the facade program to make improvements.
A VISUAL connection of the business districts north and south of the highway is taking place with the installation of brick sidewalks, Victorian streetlamps, landscaping, sheltered bus stops and benches, road repairs and some relocation of utilities underground with a combination of Federal, state and city funds.
The same continuity is being carried out with identical streetscaping southward along Water Street past Elizabeth and Tolles Streets, where SoNo Square is under redevelopment with a facade grant. The 110,000-square-foot building, covering a square block, has 45 tenants, including the Woodworker's Store, a new, innovative shop that holds seminars on carpentry and caters to seekers of exotic woods. Rents in 20,000 square feet of retail space that will become available when renovations are completed in 1993 will be about $14 a square foot.
Two years ago, M.F. DiScala, a Norwalk development firm, built a handsome apartment and office building at 49 Day Street, behind SoNo Square, after demolishing an old brick structure on the site. The project was completed before the facade program became available.
"Maybe we should have waited," said Michael DiScala, the company president, "and gotten a grant."

HEADLINE: New Hospital Housing Plan in White Plains

BYLINE: By JAMES FERON

DATELINE: WHITE PLAINS

 BODY:
A PROPOSAL by a major hospital here to build 1,200 units of town-house-style housing on undeveloped hospital property, which some residents see as the city's future "Central Park," has received a warmer welcome from the Common Council than a more ambitious project offered for the same site a decade ago.
The new proposal, which would use 90 acres of the heavily wooded 235-acre campus owned and occupied by New York Hospital-Cornell Medical Center, a psychiatric institution, is smaller in scope than the original plan, eliminating proposed office buildings, a motel and a shopping mall and reducing the planned residential space by a third. The new project would provide the hospital with at least 200 units of additional housing for its staff, as well as income needed for research and services. 
 It was presented informally to Council members on Nov. 19, but as Mary Ann Keenan, a Council Democrat, said, "We seem to be the last to know." The reference was to a strategy being pursued by the hospital that is as different from the 1983 presentation as the scope of the project itself.
The hospital's development team, which will need a zoning change to build the project, has been meeting with neighborhood associations to measure reaction and, hopefully, enlist support. They have given the impression that they will be adjusting the proposal to accommodate the city.
S. J. Schulman, a Council Democrat and former chairman of the city's Planning Board, indicated that he was impressed with the proposal. Speaking of the hospital and its development team, he said, "I can't blame them for not wanting to spend all this money on the project without getting a clear signal that the city will go along with it."
The casual approach seems to have found a welcome reception in the ornate Council chamber, where members agreed in an unusual display of accordance to hear the proposal again, this time along with the influential Planning Board, on Dec. 9.
In 1983, the planned development of the hospital grounds landed with explosive force, surprising and infuriating community leaders. It was rejected as too ambitious by the Common Council, whose members declined even to consider the request for rezoning. This time, the hospital, stung by the hostile reaction, decided to take a more studied approach with a more modest proposal.
The new plan is limited mostly to residential development. The 1983 plan called for two million square feet of office space, a 500-room motel, medical offices, a 320,000-square-foot shopping mall, a 50,000-square-foot shopping center and a six-theater movie complex in addition to 400 town houses and 1,400 apartments in 8- to 12-story buildings.
Conceived by the Robert Martin Company, Westchester's leading developer, the original complex, which covered twice the area now being proposed for development, would have been linked by a ramp to Interstate 287, which sweeps through White Plains, sparing the adjacent communities of burdensome congestion, as the developers saw it.
 
Objection to Limited Access
 But that limited access turned out to be one of the objections that Council members cited when they rejected the original plan. Mayor Alfred Del Vecchio, who also held the city's top elected post when the original plan was submitted, said last week that the ramp link was a "negative aspect" of the 1983 plan, isolating the proposed development from the rest of the city.
"The original plans had a street system and limited access, none of it impinging on the city," Mr. Del Vecchio said. "They thought it should not interfere with the rest of the city, but that was absolutely wrong. It was not what we really wanted.
"By and large I'd like to see housing there as long as the families are integrated with the rest of the city and the project does not become something apart from the rest of the city."
Under the new plan, access would be from Bryant Avenue, a city street separating the hospital development from Bryant Gardens, a 422-unit garden apartment complex.
 
A More Modest Project
 As for the current plan, Mayor Del Vecchio said, "I feel better about it than the original proposal, which was a major misuse of the land." The revamped project, he said, "does not propose to use as much land as the original project, and they make some provision for open space."
Under the new plan, the hotel, office structure, medical offices and mall and cinema cluster would disappear, and the acreage to be developed would be cut in half, to 90 acres. Proposed residential units would drop from 1,800 to 1,200. Shopping facilities would be scaled down to a size accommodating the new community.
The new plan, as outlined by William L. Porter, a noted land-use planner who worked with the architectural firm of Skidmore, Owings & Merrill, was offered to the Council with great care, and, in several aspects, with intended vagueness. For example, Mr. Porter did not say whether the 1,200 families would be buying or renting their apartments.
"Our objective," Mr. Porter said, "was to develop a master plan for the property that would preserve its most valuable buildings and natural features while allowing for future hospital expansion on the site and providing for the hospital's financial needs."
 
Historic Site Eligibility
 In that simple statement, Mr. Porter was addressing three major elements of the debate that led to rejection of the first plan. By "valuable buildings," he was apparently referring to the listing of the site on the State Register of Historic Places, a designation sought by opponents of the development. There are only three structures on the 90-acre site, none of them with historic significance.
In a letter dated May 29, Julia S. Stokes, deputy State Parks Commissioner, confirmed the site's listing in the State Register and its status as eligible for inclusion in the National Register presumably because the design of the 235-acre hospital grounds is attributed in part to Frederick Law Olmsted, revered as the father of American landscape architecture and the designer of Central Park in Manhattan.
Architects of the new plan, aware that such designation does not foreclose development of the property, have discussed their plans with the State Office of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation to make sure they are within the limits permitted under the regulations covering historic places.
Commissioner Stokes confirmed that the state listing and eligible status for Federal designation "would not restrict development of the property in a manner consistent with the natural resources of the site and the guidelines we are discussing."
 
'Developmental Sprawl'
 Mr. Porter's reference to preserving the site's natural features was presumably intended to address at least some of the objections voiced by those who are opposed to any but the minimum development of the site permitted under existing zoning, which would be several hundred private homes.
Theodore Benjamin, who formed Concerned Citizens for Open Space to seek the historic site designation as a means of banning the original plan, said that "there's a lot more openness now in listening to those resisting the plan." He said he was still opposed to the scope of the project, which he characterized as "developmental sprawl" that requires "planning on a citywide basis."
He said he would prefer that the site be left as open space. The developers say that all the significant features have been preserved, including the densely landscaped perimeter of tall trees, which will be retained to screen the housing, consisting either of town houses or garden apartments of up to three or four stories.
Mr. Porter's reference to providing for the hospital's financial needs goes to the heart of the developmental effort, with the institution seeking to insure its economic future through the conversion of its eastern sector into income.
 
Some Council Members Cautious
 Initial reaction by some Council members indicated general praise for the new presentation as much more realistic than the original plan, with comment on the plan itself ranging from enthusiastic to cautious.
Mr. Schulman, who is serving his first term on the Council, said that the new proposal "is worth serious consideration by the city." He said it was "certainly responsive to the city's own comprehensive master plan, which designated 1,200 units for that parcel."
It was he who suggested that the Council meet with the Planning Board, "whose members are more into long-term issues than we are." He said that retaining the site for open space would be expensive and unfair to the property owner, representing what he said would be a confiscatory act.
Councilman Richard S. Hendey Jr., a Republican, praised Mr. Porter for "a masterful job of analyzing" the situation but remained cautious about the hospital's need to build housing well in excess of the need for its own staff, which Mr. Porter put at 200 of the 1,200 units. Residential housing already exists on the hospital grounds for staff members, but Mr. Porter said the hospital needed additional housing for medical and administrative personnel.
Councilwoman Keenan said she was interested in learning how the neighborhood associations were reacting to the hospital plan. She said she was not convinced that the development, if approved, would necessarily succeed, "considering the real estate market and the number of unfinished housing developments we have in the city."
 
Many Questions Unanswered
 Rita Z. Malmud, a Council Democrat, saw the proposal as "an improvement over the plan suggested many years ago, but the big question is whether or not it is sufficiently attractive to approve." She said many questions remained unanswered. "When I asked Mr. Porter if they would be selling or renting the units, he said that had not been determined."
The property, bounded by Bryant Avenue, Bloomingdale Road, Westchester Avenue and North Street, has major department stores as its neighbors, including Bloomingdale's, Neiman Marcus and Saks as well as a proposed shopping mall, a branch of Nordstrom's and the Fashion Mall, a cluster of boutiques.
Other neighbors include Kraft General Foods, St. Agnes Hospital, Bryant Gardens, the garden apartment complex; a neighborhood of single-family homes adjacent to a golf course fairway, additional housing newly built on the hospital's eastern edge and mid- and high-rise apartment houses on Mamaroneck Avenue.

HEADLINE: WEDDINGS;
Allison Lewis, F. H. Brooks Jr.

 BODY:
Allison Gayle Lewis, a computer programmer, was married yesterday evening to Frederic H. Brooks Jr., a business executive. Rabbi Jordan Parr performed the ceremony at the Radisson Riverfront Inn in Augusta, Ga.
Ms. Lewis, who is keeping her name, works at the Computer Sciences Corporation, a computer software programming company in Hanover, Md. She graduated from the Rochester Institute of Technology. She is the daughter of Dr. and Mrs. Lawrence M. Lewis of Augusta. Her father, now retired, was a dentist with the United States Army there. Her mother, also retired, was the general manager of the Augusta Ballet Company. 
 The bridegroom is the president of Comtech Systems Inc., a telecommunications company in Stamford, Conn. He graduated from Pace University in White Plains. He is a son of Mr. Brooks and Dr. Jane Brooks of Old Greenwich, Conn. His mother is an adjunct professor in the graduate psychology department of Fairfield University in Fairfield, Conn., and a school psychologist with the Stamford school system. His father is the president of the Connecticut Economic Corporation, a financial consulting company in Greenwich, Conn.

HEADLINE: Business Diary

BYLINE: By Hubert B. Herring

 BODY:

THE ECONOMY
 
Maybe This Time Recovery Was Just Around the Corner
Just weeks after an election that turned on three factors -- the economy, the economy and the economy -- a flood of numbers spilled out suggesting that things may be looking up. Economic growth was surprisingly strong in the third quarter, home sales, consumer confidence and personal income rose and jobless claims fell for the sixth time in eight weeks. The election's winners and losers seemed to lapse by habit into campaign mode, with Marlin Fitzwater shouting, to the accompaniment of a roaring Presidential helicopter, "The Bush recovery is coming on strong," while Bill Clinton took more of a wait-and-see stance. News that the economy grew 3.9 percent in the quarter "could have some impact" on short-term judgments about a stimulus, he said, but it "won't change my long-term plans" to try to strengthen industrial competitiveness. Meanwhile, many economists came down on the side of caution, saying it was too early to call a turning point for a recession that has blinked repeatedly but has defiantly refused to get out of town. 

Democracy, at the Flip of a Dial
When Ross Perot entranced the nation's television audience with his economic wisdom, Bill Clinton must have been watching. Last week his much-discussed closed-door economic "summit" turned into a "retreat," and every potato (or even potatoe) with a couch is welcome to tune in. The conference will resemble the campaign's town meetings, and the "real Americans" to participate will include the heads of Apple Computer, Hewlett-Packard, Xerox and Sara Lee. Mickey Kantor, a Clinton aide, said the goal was "putting some meat on the bones" of the President-elect's economic proposals. "This is assessment and input," he said. But another Clinton aide acknowledged that "the real work is going on right now." And at the conference, Mr. Clinton will be meeting privately with Robert B. Reich, head of his economic policy team.
 
Tax-Deductible Penance
Struggling to recoup some of the money that went down the savings and loan sinkhole, the Government got from Ernst & Young what almost amounts to loose change in this multi-billion-dollar game -- $400 million. Regulators say that in its audits of numerous institutions, the accounting firm should have seen the billboard-size handwriting on the wall and sounded a warning. But by settling, Ernst may have got off easy. It faced numerous huge suits, each running as high as hundreds of millions of dollars, but both sides wanted to avoid endless litigation, so Ernst has wiped those off the slate. Still, $400 million seems like a crippling number. But look closer: insurance will pay $300 million, and the 2,000 partners are allowed to pay their (tax-deductible) share of the rest over four years. That works out to $8,000 a year apiece, out of average earnings of $200,000. No one is likely to be out on the street.
 
G.E.'s Military Discharge
The end of the cold war, which has mapmakers working overtime worldwide, continues to redraw the map of the American economy as the nation spends less on the military. General Electric last week completed its departure from the military and aerospace businesses it has held since World War I by agreeing to sell its aerospace unit to Martin Marietta for $3 billion. The aerospace businesses, which generated more than $6 billion of G.E.'s $60 billion in revenues last year, supply satellites, technical services and systems involving radar, sonar and communications. The stock market greeted the news warmly, with the stock rising $2.125 on Monday, to $82.125, and ending the week at $82.875.
In another pullback from the military priorities of another era, the Army canceled a $594 million "Star Wars" research contract with McDonnell Douglas. The Army said the shrinking Defense Department budget had forced it to withdraw the contract to develop a ground-based surveillance and tracking system.
 
Listen Up, You of Little Tax!
"Solutions to the U.S. budget situation are readily found. What is required is the will." No, that's not Ross Perot talking. It's the Organization of Economic Cooperation and Development, and it has a strong message. The group, representing 24 big nations, is fed up with America's inaction on its deficit, saying it threatens to push up global interest rates and undermine United States Government plans to raise investment. Its specific suggestions: a 25-cent-a-gallon rise in the gasoline tax and a 5 percent national sales tax. It said the Government spent far too much on current consumption that would be better used in investing in a more productive nation.
 
COMPANIES
 
A Day Older, a Whole Lot Poorer
Even into the most glittering garage-to-riches stories, some wrenches must fall. The stock of Microsoft, the world's largest software company, is worth more than 10 times what it was when it went public in 1986, with the stock market now valuing it above General Motors. Its chairman, William H. Gates 3d, owns so many of those shares that last Monday morning he had a net worth of $7.8 billion (give or take several lesser fortunes), probably making him the richest man in America. But look at the dent one lowly analyst can make: On Monday, Rick Sherlund, of Goldman, Sachs, lowered his earnings estimates for Microsoft, and a fickle Wall Street yanked back more than $500 million of Mr. Gates's paper nest egg, as the stock dropped $6.50, to $88.50, in a single day. The stock regained $3 on Tuesday after Mr. Sherlund tempered his remarks, saying his numbers may have been too harsh, and ended the week at $91.75.
 
The Numbers Looked Good
Another high-tech darling of the market fared far worse last week, proving -- if it needed to be proved -- that investors do not look kindly on executives' making up profit numbers. Comptronix, an Alabama electronics company that seemed to have all the right hot-stock stuff, suspended its top three executives after they admitted that they had inflated earnings for as much as three years. Its stock, selling at eight times its price at the end of 1990, tumbled from its perch, in a single day dropping nearly 75 percent, to $6.125, from $22. By Friday, the stock had crept up only a bit, ending at $8.375.
 
Another Headache for G.M.
General Motors' problems just keep on coming. Last week Moody's lowered its ratings on an array of G.M. debt securities, worth about $70 billion. Moody's said the auto maker was "likely to remain under pressure because of the company's high cost structure and product weakness."
 
Biting 'the Ultimate Bullet'
Westinghouse Electric lost $1.7 billion on its financial-services businesses last year, dragging its overall results deep into the minus column, and investors were not amused. Its shares, which earlier this year traded above $20, plunged below $10. So last week it "bit the ultimate bullet," as one analyst put it. The company had tried to stage an orderly retreat from the business, but in the face of continuing losses and dwindling sources of credit, its chairman, Paul E. Lego, said it could no longer "tough it out." Westinghouse said it would close down the financial services unit and sell other businesses, cutting the company's size by a third. Investors were enthusiastic. Even though the dividend was cut nearly in half, the shares jumped almost 25 percent, to $12.125, when the plan was announced on Monday, and inched up further later in the week, to a close of $12.625.
 
At ABC, Youth Serving Youth
Network television has long set its sights on younger viewers, and last week one network put a manager where its mouth was, as Robert A. Iger, 41, was named to head ABC, becoming one of the youngest people ever to head a network. Daniel B. Burke, president of Capital Cities/ABC, clearly trying to put in a new generation of top executives at the company, said Mr. Iger's "youth and energy level" were big factors. ABC's news, sports and entertainment divisions will report to Mr. Iger, who is now president of the entertainment division. His prime-time programming has often led the other networks in delivering the younger audiences advertisers prefer.
 
Analyst Speaks, Company Yells
When David R. Korus of Kidder, Peabody speaks, people listen. And when the analyst spoke of his doubts about whether Dell Computer's accounting had inflated profits, they heard "sell," and sell they did. But Dell fought back, first in sharp words from its chairman, then in asking the S.E.C. to look into trading patterns. It also said it was contemplating legal action. Defending an analyst's freedom to scrutinize, Charles R. Wolf of First Boston said, "There has to be a healthy degree of cynicism, because very few companies will say they are doing poorly."
 
INTERNATIONAL
 
Talks Resume, to French Dismay
French farmers took to the streets to protest a European accord with the United States, a pact whose sharp cuts in farm subsidies would make it harder for them to sell their goods abroad. Leading French politicians denounced the plan as well, but were unable to block it. The agreement opened the way for world trade talks to resume in Geneva after being deadlocked for nearly a year -- talks widely viewed as a key to reviving economic growth, particularly in the industrialized world. The stated goal: to finish a trade-liberalization package by year's end.
 
U.N. to U.S.: Hands Off Cuba
When the Soviet Union died, Fidel Castro instantly became an economic orphan. Not much future in that. But the United States does not want to wait around for Mr. Castro's inevitable exit, and the United Nations does not like Uncle Sam's latest method of trying to get its way. Last week, in a surprise rebuke, the General Assembly called overwhelmingly for an end to Washington's long embargo against Cuba. What prompted even America's friends to cry "Enough" was a new law that further tightens that embargo. Under the law, American companies' foreign subsidiaries that have traded with Cuba in the past can be prosecuted under American law if they do so in the future. Mexico's delegate to the United Nations called the American move "a violation of international law."
 
Royal Contribution To Public Coffers
Marital strife. Fire. What other earthly torments is royalty heir to? You guessed it: taxes. Victoria paid them, and now Queen Elizabeth, amid resentment of the royal life style, will chip in, too.

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
The Economy: Holiday Cheer, But Will It Last?

BYLINE: By Steven Greenhouse

 BODY:
LAST week's economic reports were a hearty, heartening Thanksgiving feast for statistics mavens. The economy grew by a stunning 3.9 percent in the third quarter, orders for big-ticket factory goods soared in October, and consumer confidence finally lifted itself.
Although this news provides some much-needed holiday cheer, the consensus among economists is that the 3.9 percent jump in third-quarter output overstates the economy's underlying growth rate. They expect fourth-quarter growth to be weaker, between 2 and 2.75 percent, for several reasons. Consumer spending in the third quarter grew faster than disposable income, and third-quarter military spending rocketed despite the downward trend in military outlays. In addition, business inventories grew strongly for the second straight quarter and corporations won't want to continue that trend. 
 Still, although some reports, like industrial production, point to more weakness, more and more economists say the economy is coming out of the woods. And, says David B. Bostian Jr., chief economist at Herzog Heine Geduld Inc., the Dow Jones industrials don't fully appreciate that, although he said the Standard & Poor's 500-stock Index and Nasdaq combined composite do. Both these indices hit records after Wednesday's G.D.P. news. "The economic news will help the Dow make a catch-up move between here and the end of the year," Mr. Bostian said.
As a result of last week's bullish news, President-elect Bill Clinton said he might rethink his plans to give the economy a quick jolt. He is still expected to propose a tax credit on equipment purchases. But it is not quite as clear that he will embrace a stimulus package that creates jobs through increased deficit spending. That could help firm the bond market.
What appears likely is that the faster growth is in the fourth quarter, the smaller will be any stimulus package.
Speaking about the third-quarter growth numbers, Robert F. Wescott, an economist with the WEFA group, said, "I think it lowers the amount of fiscal stimulus Clinton will do." That, too, could buoy the bond market.
But, he added, "I think he will do something as an insurance policy. I think Clinton feels there is too much of a risk of getting two or three months of positive numbers and things going soft again." Mr. Wescott sees growth of 3 percent next year, perhaps without stimulus, or if growth falters, then with one.
Mr. Bostian said Mr. Clinton's agenda should benefit capital-goods stocks, especially technology, machinery, construction and pollution-control issues. He said a stimulus package could push long-term interest rates back up to 8 percent.
But there might be a pleasant surprise, Mr. Bostian added. Growth might prove so strong that tax revenues will rise enough to offset the cost of a $40-billion public works plan, so the deficit would hardly rise. "He just might have his cake and eat it too."

HEADLINE: Sound Bytes;
A Wireless Widget Master

BYLINE: By Peter H. Lewis

 BODY:
BILL CAMPBELL is no stranger to challenges. Armed with degrees in economics, he spent six years (1974-79) as head football coach at Columbia University, there compiling a 12-41-1 record (even so, it was better than any coach since then).
Then came jobs at J. Walter Thompson, Eastman-Kodak, and Apple Computer Inc., where he rose to executive vice president. He quit Apple to start the Claris Corporation, a software company. But when Apple bought Claris in 1990, Mr. Campbell saw the handwriting on the wall -- or rather, the screen. 
 In 1991, he became president of the GO Corporation, in Foster City, Calif. GO's Penpoint operating system is being adopted by a new generation of portable computers that use the pen for such applications as note-taking and scheduling, wireless electronic mail and facsimile, as well as standard business applications.
Question: You just got off the plane from Tokyo -- your 12th trip this year -- and now you're dashing off to speak on a panel. Are things always this hectic?
Answer: I'm a madman. I work 24 hours a day. That's always been my style. I'm an early riser and I don't sleep much. I'm really pretty dull.
Q: Is there really a market for these pen-based, wireless widget things?
A: The way I look at it is, there is an inevitability. Devices are getting smaller and more personal, so the answer is more like, "Why not?" It's like local area networks a few years ago: Everybody said, "year of the LAN, one of the most overpromised technologies." But you knew that it had to happen. People want to be connected, and sooner or later they're going to have to share all this stuff. When it becomes easy to use, and practical to use, then people will use it. And that's what happened. I think the same thing is going to happen with this. If we do a good job making them simple and cheap, this will be a gigantic market. I mean, I can't think that it wouldn't be, but then again, I'm more cautious than everybody else.
Q: Why?
A: A couple of people got too hypey about this. This is not going to happen overnight, and we just have to be careful. Pen computing already has a lot of wounds from too-high expectations. I came here in '91, and you notice we've taken a lot lower profile. I'm just not interested in being out there telling everyone how good we are. I just want to do it, and let everyone look at it.
Q: What sort of gizmos do you carry?
A: I don't carry one of the pads with me. I don't use my mobile phone; the battery life is so short. Aargh! It drives me crazy. I'll use a pay phone.
Q: What kind of device would you like to carry?
A: The ultimate is one I could carry like the old Hewlett-Packard calculators. People are looking at wireless devices as notebook replacements. If these devices are going to be successful, somebody has to say, "This is as good as a notebook, but it also offers other things -- like communications." That's why at GO we've taken a completely different approach, designed to take advantage of mobility. These are truly mobile devices, not things that have to have 120-megabyte hard drives and replace the desktop. If we have to compete for the notebook replacement or desktop extension market, then we're dead, Microsoft just crushes us.
 
 
 
WILLIAM VINCENT CAMPBELL
 Born: Aug. 31, 1940, Homestead, Pa.
Education: Bachelor's and master's in economics, Columbia University
Drives: 1991 Mercedes 500SEL
Frequent-flier miles: He winces. "Over a million each on a couple of different airlines."
Ideal escape: "Palm Springs when spring training is on."
Current reading: "Journals, technical stuff. Because I'm in Silicon Valley without a technology background, I have to catch up."
Hobbies: Working out.

HEADLINE: At Work;
New Generation Feeling the Fire

BYLINE: By BARBARA PRESLEY NOBLE

 BODY:
GRADUATION day, a bittersweet time for most college seniors and parents as they look backward and forward, may have provided Derrick Diggs's parents with one alarmingly bittersweet moment last spring. They assumed their talented son had spent some part of his last year at Morehouse College in Atlanta applying to graduate schools or looking for a job, as most anxious seniors do.
No, he told them after the family returned to its hotel from a shopping trip. "They said, 'Well, this is your last party. After that you're on your own,' " Mr. Diggs said. 
 College graduates are supposed to be cosmically unsettled in this, the year of the worst job market for recent grads in 50 years. So why do neither Derrick Diggs, nor his friend Imar Hutchins, seem in the least bit jittery?
Perhaps because they know what they want. The pair opted off the future-shock treadmill to develop a national network of private preschools called Freeform Academy -- a kind of shoe-leather, from-the-ground-up version of Chris Whittle's Edison Project. One school with 15 preschoolers is already open in Atlanta. Mr. Hutchins and Mr. Diggs plan to open five more there next fall and, in the next few years, move, as they say, "concentrically" from their Atlanta base into neighboring states, up the Eastern Seaboard and across the country.
Baby boomers, well into middle age and as yet without majors, may want to pause over the example of Mr. Hutchins, 22 years old, and Mr. Diggs, 23. Though they are members of the supposedly lost and forlorn Generation X, their intensity -- if not their discipline -- feels familiar. They would like not merely to start schools, but to transform institutions.
They have been counseled to go slowly in their venture, to think small, to postpone while they credential themselves. No. "Now's the time," said Mr. Hutchins, who reads the Harvard Business Review to relax. "If you feel the fire now, do it. The fire isn't always on you."
But, unlike their befringed, be-bell-bottomed 60's counterparts, they view private enterprise as the path to transformation -- and self-sufficiency.
The two men have an abiding faith in the gospel of entrepreneurialism, instilled by their fathers who owned businesses. In the year before graduation, they talked among friends at Morehouse about possible ventures. Certain existential decisions were made. "We decided what we weren't going to do, not what we would do," Mr. Hutchins said. "We knew something would come." What they weren't going to do included going off One of an occasional series on the experiences of recent college graduates.
 to any of the prestigious graduate programs or big companies that were courting them. The confidence to take a risky, unconventional tack came in part from their experience at Morehouse, in part from family.
BOTH were economics majors, both good and active students. Mr. Hutchins, who is from Philadelphia, graduated Phi Beta Kappa. Mr. Diggs, from Wichita, was an honors student and an All-American sprinter for three years. He says, simply, "I've always wanted to be an entrepreneur like my dad." When Mr. Hutchins was seven, his father helped him start Imar's Card Company. He went door-to-door in his neighborhood, selling greeting cards his father got wholesale. "The inventory would be magically renewed when I ran out," he said. "My father set it up so I couldn't fail."
Providing opportunities for such "microsuccesses" to develop student confidence is an important part of the Freeform Academy curriculum. The idea for a school open to all and oriented to the needs of African-American children was one of several on the menu of the senior-year discussions, suggested by a fellow student, Ayize Sabater. Mr. Hutchins was intrigued, but less with the school than with the potential. "A lot of people have schools," Mr. Hutchins said. "I said, 'Wait, we don't need one school, we need a hundred schools.' " Mr. Sabater is now principal of the school and its chief resource in educational philosophy and teacher training.
The notion that a venture of such magnitude could be successful is so improbable that -- who knows? -- it might work. McDonald's was once a single entity. Mr. Hutchins, the company president, and Mr. Diggs, its chief financial officer, undertook a two-month tour in the fall to take word of Freeform to educational groups around the country. At the moment they are scouting sites in Atlanta for the five schools they intend to open next August. They and six full-time workers are living on nearly infinitesimal draws and a belief in Freeform's future. As they expand concentrically, they plan to increase the number of new schools geometrically. By the time Chris Whittle gets his first school off the ground in 1996, they point out, there should be several dozen Freeform Academies up and running strong.
 
 
 
AVERAGE VERSUS EXCELLENT
 FOR now, Freeform Academy is operating on tuition payments ($75 a week), donations, a little startup capital and a lot of sweat equity. Derrick Diggs and Imar Hutchins, the two founders, put in long hours, as do several other full-time employees. Freeform's principal, Ayize Sabater, opens the school at 7 A.M. and goes home at 8 P.M.
Mr. Diggs and Mr. Hutchins already have in mind some entrepreneurial pitfalls. To control the sirens of unfettered growth, they have delayed plans to open schools outside Atlanta next year. And though they will probably seek venture capital at some point, they decline to think in terms of big infusions just yet. "That's the least of our worries," Mr. Diggs says. "People is what we need."
Describing the type of venture he wants Freeform to be, Mr. Hutchins refers to wisdom garnered from a recent Harvard Business Review. "An average company fulfills consumer demand," he says. "An excellent company leads consumers to the market. We're trying to see the hidden demand." He has little doubt it's there. The lone Freeform in existence now has a long waiting list.

HEADLINE: Chicago's Exchanges Look Toward an Electronic Salvation

BYLINE: By BARNABY J. FEDER

DATELINE: CHICAGO

 BODY:
LAST December, after President Bush toured the trading floor of the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, he came away dazzled by the frenzy of shouts and hand signals. "I have seen the future," he said.
Officials at the Merc and its longtime neighbor and rival, the Chicago Board of Trade, were pleased by the vote of confidence. But in fact they are counting on the future looking a bit different. Separately and jointly, the two exchanges are investing heavily to modernize as they battle to preserve their leadership in the world's markets for futures, options, and related instruments. 
 The exchanges are spending millions of dollars annually on electronic systems. Some aim to make the current open-outcry trading more efficient by eliminating paper orders along with many of the clerical workers who handle them. Other systems will sidestep the pits entirely to help Chicago's traders compete with new exchanges around the world that trade exclusively on computers.
And in moves that would have seemed unimaginable a decade ago, top officials from the Merc and Board of Trade have set aside historical rivalry to travel together to Europe and Asia on sales trips, to merge overseas offices and to begin negotiations to merge the back offices that process trades.
The exchanges' products are known as derivatives because they are based on underlying commodities. The extension of the futures concept from traditional commodities like crops, animals and metals to financial commodities like stocks, bonds and currencies has transformed derivatives into a basic cog in the global economy and one of the most incredible growth industries ever. Hal T. Hansen, the Cargill Investor Services executive who is president of the Futures Industry Association, a trade group, described the industry's growth chart as looking like the path of a homesick angel.
Industry sources figure there were more than 500 million futures and options contracts traded in the world last year -- an average of nearly 1,000 per minute. Chicago became the worldwide futures king by leading the development of financial futures.
But Chicago's share of futures and options trading, estimated at about 75 percent just five years ago, is now thought to be just under 50 percent and falling. The exchanges have been losing ground to the approximately 50 exchanges outside the United States, about half of which have been founded since 1985. Off-exchange deals between banks and other institutional investors are also a rapidly growing part of the derivatives business.
Futures are an obligation to buy or sell, anywhere from a few days to several years in the future, a fixed quantity of a commodity. The commodity could be securities like Treasury bonds, 5,000 bushels of corn, or even the exchange rate between the German mark and the Japanese yen. A futures contract specifies price, date, and place and terms of delivery. Options are the right, but not an obligation, to complete similar trades on or before a specified date.
Futures and options were once regarded as dangerous financial oddities because they attracted so many speculators. It seemed that in every famous financial bust dating back to the run-up and crash in tulip-bulb prices in 16th-century Holland, derivatives had worked like gasoline thrown on a fire. In the past 20 years, though, derivatives have come to be accepted as a useful tool for any conservative business exposed to wide swings in profitability.
THE Chicago markets, and rivals like the New York Mercantile Exchange and the London International Financial Futures Exchange, allow a farmer to establish the revenue he will have after selling a crop long before it is harvested, a manufacturer to lock in the dollar value of exported products long before payment is made, or a pension fund to build a safety net of assets that generate income to match the projected pace of payments to its members.
Although no bottom-line measure of the benefits to the world economy of financial derivatives is possible, the business world has been won over. Futures trading in the United States alone went from 18.3 million contracts in 1972 to 112.4 million contracts in 1982 to a peak of 276.5 million in 1990, according to the Futures Industry Association. (Exchange officials say that increased competition might have been a factor in the decline to 262.9 million last year but that the recession was the primary reason) Options on commodities, and on futures contracts themselves, began trading in 1982 and soared to 64.1 million contracts by 1990, the trade group said.
Not included are trades on exchanges that focus on options on stocks or stock indexes. The largest is the Chicago Board Options Exchange, a Board of Trade spinoff that has a daily volume of 500,000 contracts.
IF worldwide trading continues to expand as rapidly as it has in recent years, the Chicago exchanges could prosper even as they lose market share. But they aren't taking growth in this country for granted.
In the past, the exchanges' growth has been driven by a handful of big products. The Board of Trade, founded in 1848, owes its claim to being the world's largest derivatives exchange primarily to its 30-year Treasury bond futures, introduced in 1977. The bond contract is the world's most popular way of hedging on long-term interest rates. The Merc, founded in 1919, has grown even faster. It grabbed the lion's share of bets on short-term interest rates (through Eurodollar contracts) and foreign-exchange movements (through foreign-currency futures). And with its S.& P. 500 contract, it is Wall Street's favorite place to trade stock futures.
In recent years, though, neither exchange has come up with a new-product home run. The Board of Trade is currently making the most adventurous efforts here to snap the slump, with forays into insurance and environmental futures (see box). Most outside experts are not optimistic. "All of the obvious ideas have been done," said Jack D. Schwager, director of futures research at Prudential Securities Inc.
Conceding as much, the exchanges here are working hard to market themselves and their derivatives to investors around the world. The most potent symbol of cooperation is Globex, an electronic network that allows traders to offer and buy futures contracts through their computers. It was developed jointly by the two exchanges with Reuters, to allow their products to be traded anywhere when the exchanges are closed. .
Originally seen as a two-year project, Globex has swallowed about $7 million from the two exchanges and tens of millions more from Reuters. When trading finally began this summer, interest was limited because a number of crucial features are still under development. Among them: built-in credit limits on users of the trading terminals, ability to program in buy or sell orders that are placed automatically when fast-moving markets hit a specified price and instantaneous switching to backup networks.
Some critics doubt that Globex will ever generate the trading volumes needed to justify the hundreds of thousands of dollars members have to invest in equipment, fees and staffing to use it. But Globex did teach the exchanges that they could cooperate on complicated projects, leading not only to the joint marketing efforts but also to other technology projects. An example: work on Audit, a handheld order-keeping and order-entry device being developed for pit traders (an early prototype was tested this fall at both exchanges). Recording trades and forwarding them for clearing, the device will allow clearers to quickly detect errors, unauthorized trades or disagreements about what happened. The exchanges also hope Audit devices will eventually provide traders with news and pricing aids.
LEO MELAMED, chairman of the joint venture company managing Globex, said Globex is already serving its purpose of discouraging other exchanges from setting up competing systems or attempting to trade Chicago's products. In fact, Globex has agreements with exchanges in Singapore and France to list their most popular contracts and is negotiating with the London exchange.
In addition to being an after-hours home for many exchanges' crown jewels, Globex may also serve as a forum for global experiments with new products. It costs far less to put something on a screen and see whether it trades than to develop a trading pit. For just that reason, the Board of Trade recently brought on line a computer-based trading network known as Project A that operates during the exchange's normal trading hours. It can be an incubator for contracts that may grow into pit trading, or a permanent home for niche products like barge traffic futures.
"The idea is to be ready to trade whenever the demand arises," said William O'Connor, the chairman of the Board of Trade. That sums up the driving force behind both Project A and almost everything else the exchanges are doing these days.
 
 
 
COMING SOON: NEW PRODUCTS
THE Chicago Board of Trade is hoping to end a slump in offering successful new products with moves into insurance and environmental futures. The insurance contracts start trading next month. They are intended to allow insurance companies to sell some of their exposure to catastrophic risk to futures markets. Today, the insurance industry's only routine method for spreading a portion of its risks to private investors outside the industry is to sell to investor syndicates at Lloyds of London.
The Board of Trade's first environmental products are scheduled to start trading next spring. They will allow utilities to buy and sell permits to emit specific amounts of sulfur dioxide. The Environmental Protection Agency, which chose the board over other exchanges to test the concept, hopes that the utilities that can most efficiently invest to cut such emissions below the levels allowed for their plants will do so and then profit by selling permits covering their unused emissions allowance.
The key challenge, as with all contracts, is to attract enough traders so that anyone can get a competitive bid or offer at any time. And, as with other contracts, the exchange hopes that local traders will add liquidity to the market by buying and reselling the contracts in an attempt to profit from changing perceptions of their value as the specified date for delivery of the insurance risk or pollution permit closes in.

HEADLINE: CAMERA;
Settling Disputes Without Litigation

BYLINE: By John Durniak; John Durniak is a freelance writer, editor and consultant on photography.

 BODY:
IN the current recession, the pressure is on photographers and their clients to keep costs low by making deals and cutting corners, which makes the climate ripe for disagreements. The phrase "sue me" is being heard nearly as often as "nice shot."
Working photographers, however, have an alternative to going to court: the Joint Ethics Committee, which was organized in 1945 by professionals in the graphic communications industry. 
 The committee's main function is to help settle disputes while maintaining high professional standards. This is done through whatever works -- writing letters, for example, or arranging mediation or arbitration.
The committee's resources are available to all people in the industry, including both part-time and full-time photographers. They do not have to be members of the sponsoring organizations. And although the committee is based in New York State, it takes on cases from other states if both parties to a dispute are willing to go there for hearings.
The committee is sponsored by New York chapters of the Advertising Photographers of America, the American Institute of Graphic Arts, the American Society of Media Photographers, the Art Buyers Club, the Art Directors Club, the Graphic Artists Guild, the Society of Illustrators and the Society of Photographer and Artist Representatives.
The Joint Ethics Committee is run by four unpaid representatives from each group, and it was set up to operate independently of the sponsoring groups.
All complaints are initially handled by mail and must be accompanied by a $50 administration fee ($35 for members of the sponsoring organizations; there is no charge in hardship cases).
One recent conflict that the committee helped settle involved a photographer who had completed a series of pictures for a catalogue but said he was still owed almost $5,000 because his photos had been used not just in the catalogue but also in national advertisements. Edward Meyers, a New York photographer and writer who helps represent the American Society of Media Photographers on the Joint Ethics Committee, said it took six months and a number of letters back and forth before the client agreed to pay the photographer.
"We took a neutral position throughout the negotiations by letter," Mr. Meyers said. "If there is refusal to cooperate, then they could go to court. Good business people try to settle disagreements and not let them hang."
Robert Scott Ellis, a New York lawyer who works pro bono for the committee, constantly reminds members of the group that in negotiations they must stay neutral. Mr. Meyers said that, in fact, the committee has rejected complaints brought by members of sponsoring groups.
In mediation, a case is usually heard by three members of the committee. When a dispute goes to binding arbitration, the panel is enlarged, usually including the three committee members plus two independent experts from the communications field who are not members of the committee.
As part of the Photo '92 show next weekend at the Jacob K. Javits Convention Center, the committee will give a seminar on Sunday called "Fair Business Dealings Should Be a Right, Not a Fight." (For more information on the show: (203) 852-0500.)
The committee is encouraging professionals in other states to form their own Joint Ethics Committees. Toward that end, it has published a booklet that includes the "Articles of the Code of Fair Practice -- Relations Between Artist and Buyer."
The booklet, $4 a copy, can be obtained by writing to Joint Ethics Committee, Box 179, Grand Central Station, New York 10163. Enclose a self-addressed envelope with 52 cents postage.
More information about starting a committee may be obtained by writing to that address or by calling (212) 966-2492.

HEADLINE: ABOUT CARS;
From Geo, a Second-Generation Prizm

BYLINE: By Marshall Schuon

 BODY:
PITY the poor consumer who wants an American car. Some full-size Chevies and Mercurys are built in Canada. Some of Ford's popular Escorts are made in Mexico. The Dodge Stealth is made in Japan. Pontiac's LeMans comes from Korea, and nearly every new automobile carries parts from all over the world.
Not only that, but the designs and engineering and economics also are a case of mix and match.
General Motors owns all of Lotus, half of Saab and almost 40 percent of Isuzu. Ford owns Jaguar and about a quarter of Mazda -- although its new Mercury Villager van is a joint venture with Nissan. And Chrysler, which owns all of Lamborghini and 15 percent of Maserati, holds an 11 percent stake in Mitsubishi. 
 It's hard to tell who is what anymore, and the new Geo Prizm sedan is a perfect example.
The four-door design derives from the Toyota Sprinter, a new Corolla that is sold in Japan but not here. The Prizm, however, is built in Fremont, Calif., by New United Motors Manufacturing Inc., the General Motors-Toyota partnership that also assembles Corollas for the United States.
Spokesmen for Chevrolet, which markets Geos, like to say that the Prizm and the Corolla share not a single body panel, and they are right. What they don't say much, though, is that the half-sisters are identical under the skin.
In fact, the Prizm looks more Japanese than the Corolla, whose lines and details are a bit busier. From its body-colored front bumper to its rounded roof and trunk, the Geo has the appearance of . . . well, of today's curvaceous Mazda or of the Honda Civic that is a prime competitor.
The front-wheel-drive Prizm has been the best-selling car in the Geo lineup ever since the brand was established in 1988, and the '93 model represents a second generation that takes the car out of the econobox league. There is more basic power, more safety, more creature comfort. Its exterior and interior dimensions also are bigger than before, allowing it to graduate from the subcompact category and step out as a classy compact.
Two trim levels, Prizm and Prizm LSi, are available. Last year's sporty GSi model has been discontinued, but an optional sport package teams a new and slightly larger 1.8-liter engine with a stiffer suspension. Antilock brakes are an option, and a driver's air bag is standard across the line.
In addition, the front seatbelts now have a five-position vertical adjustment to encourage safer driving by making it comfortable, a fillip normally found on more expensive machinery. And, wonder of wonder, Geo has found a way to make the entire hub of the four-spoke wheel serve as a horn, eliminating those tiny and hard-to-find buttons.
The test car, a white LSi with blue interior, had the basic 1.6-liter engine, feeding 108 horsepower through a five-speed manual transmission, and performance was fairly zippy. However, the bigger engine, which puts out 115 horses and adds 15 foot-pounds of torque, might be a better choice with either of the optional automatic transmissions, one a three-speed and the other a new four-speed with electronic control.
The new automatic, even with the larger engine, produces fuel-economy figures that are surprisingly close to those of the basic manual powertrain, which is rated at 28 m.p.g., city, and 33 highway. All of the engine-transmission combinations also have had their gearing and torque fiddled with, and the Prizm now produces most of its power in the middle range, where customers are more likely to use it.
On the road, the car is taut and quiet, riding on a wheelbase that is two inches longer than in the past. Tires also have been upgraded to match the 14-inch wheels that now are standard, and there are other niceties like full power equipment and a low-liftover trunk with 13.8 cubic feet of space and a pass-through to the passenger compartment.
All in all, the Prizm seems to have come of age. And Geo has kept the ticket where it ought to be. The base car costs $9,995 and the LSi $10,630. Options swell the price, of course, and the tester had a sticker of $14,417. But that is still about $1,000 less than a comparably equipped Corolla, and it is considerably less than a Honda Civic EX, which starts at $15,100.
Maybe this time we shouldn't pity the poor consumer.
 
Geo Sips Its Way to the Top
 PART of the competition between Geo and Honda has to do with fuel efficiency, and Geo has won in that category again, according to 1993 Government figures.
For the third year in a row, the Geo Metro XFi was the stingiest car sold in the United States, pulling 53 miles from a gallon of fuel in city driving and getting 58 m.p.g. on the highway. Honda's Civic VX hatchback was the runner-up, posting 48 m.p.g., city, and 55, highway.
The Honda, the Metro and the Metro's twin, the Suzuki Swift, took all of the top 10 places in fuel efficiency. Geo's Metro and the Swift are produced in Canada by CAMI Inc., a joint venture between General Motors and Suzuki. The Civic VX also is made in Canada.
Chrysler's new LH sedans, sold as the Dodge Intrepid, the Eagle Vision and the Chrysler Concorde, won the sweepstakes among large cars with ratings of 20 m.p.g., city, and 28, highway.
Rolls-Royce was at the bottom of the list with city-highway figures of 10 and 14. As they say, though, if you have to ask. . . .
