HEADLINE: Flood Tunnel: A Way To Avert Tragedy

 BODY:
Re: "Passaic Flood Tunnel: A Turning Point," Nov. 8:
I find it incredible that the people least affected by the flooding in the Passaic River basin are those most opposed to the only viable flood protection measure -- the construction of the Passaic River flood tunnel. 
 As a resident of Bloomingdale and an official elected to represent all the people of Passaic County, I am haunted by the catastrophe that is waiting to happen in the Passaic River basin. In 1984, I saw the homes and businesses of friends and neighbors destroyed. I saw widespread fear and terror grip my county for days as the torrential rains swept out roads and bridges and prevented people from either leaving their homes or getting to them.
That scenario can easily be repeated, perhaps as early as next spring, if the right combination of winter snowfall and heavy March rains occurs.
Yet a hard-core group of environmental extremists -- some based in Basking Ridge, others in Bergen County, both miles away from the heart of the flood problem -- opposes the remedy for our troubles and is influencing public policy.
I respect the environmental movement, but cannot support it when its goals are unrealistic attempts to push back the clock hundreds of years. Certainly, our problem would be solved if there were less building in the flood plain. But it's too late for such pie-in-the-sky dreams. We must deal with what is; and what we have is a serious flooding problem that could become a devastating tragedy if the tunnel is not built.
The buyout that the extremists cling to as a solution is just unworkable and probably far more expensive than the tunnel construction. A buyout would require uprooting thousand of families and businesses, destroying entire neighborhoods and eliminating thousands of jobs.
Secondly, all those structures in the flood plain would have to be destroyed, creating a huge mountain of garbage -- much of it toxic -- that would have to be landfilled at an extremely high cost to taxpayers. And finally, towns along the river would have to raise property taxes dramatically to make up the loss of tax ratables caused by a buyout.
The tunnel, on the other hand, is a permanent solution that is good for the local economy, helpful to the taxpayers and not disruptive to the residents. The tunnel plan will not increase building in the basin, as more than 5,000 wetland acres will be permanently preserved.
Additionally, the Passaic County Freeholder Board has already acted to limit the water runoff effects of development in the upper basin by preserving 2,000 acres of Sterling Forest in West Milford and Ringwood and keeping it forever as parkland.
After decades of study, there is no evidence that the tunnel will cause ecological harm. There is, however, well-documented evidence that continued delay on the tunnel endangers life and property. It's time for the obstructionists to step aside and allow progress and technology to rectify a horrendous problem.
 
RICHARD DUHAIME
Freeholder, Passaic County
Paterson

HEADLINE: NORTHEAST NOTEBOOK: Dover, Del.;
Highway Inn Expanding

BYLINE: By Maureen Milford

 BODY:
A HOTEL that opened in this capital city two decades ago is undergoing a $4 million expansion designed to turn the highway lodging into a regional conference center.
The 145-room Sheraton Inn Dover, on Route 13 just north of the city's historic downtown government center, is midway through the 55,000-square-foot addition that will give it a ballroom-banquet room, a catering kitchen and 11 new guest rooms, as well as 9,500 square feet of office space. 
 "We did this because we felt it was necessary to keep up with the times," said Gregory S. Kramedas, one of two principals in K & W Partnership, which owns the hotel.
He and his partner, Davis Wood of Milford, consider the expansion a first step toward the development of a multimillion-dollar civic center in this city of 27,630 residents.
For nearly a decade Kent County has been trying to promote a civic center, arena, hotel and business park to be called Liberty Center near the Sheraton Inn. Kent County is the state's middle county, between New Castle on the north and Sussex on the south.
Such a development, business leaders say, would make this city a regional attraction for conventions and concerts, drawing people from across Delaware and from Maryland's Eastern Shore.
In Wilmington, the state's most populous and developed city, similar civic center plans are being drawn up to anchor a downtown revitalization effort.
Such a complex as Liberty Center, said LeRoy Klein, chairman of the Central Delaware Economic Development Council, would give "us an opportunity to strengthen our position as the center of state activity, culturally, politically and economically."
The expansion project, by J. Roland Dasheill & Sons of Salisbury, Md., will include a ballroom and banquet room with a capacity of 1,600 when used as a theater and 1,800 for dinner.
The room will also accommodate 200 trade-show booths, and loading docks for such events are included in the design, as is an audio-visual conference room. In addition, there will be an indoor swimming pool and a health club.
Becker/Morgan Architects of Salisbury, Md., designed the space to be on the west side of the building facing what was once farmland. Before work began on the addition in May, K & W Partnership sold 33 of its 88 acres to the Wal-Mart Company, which built a discount store to the north of the hotel and a Sam's Club west of it. It was Wal-Mart's entry into Delaware.
The hotel management is already negotiating to bring in several regional trade shows and conferences when the addition is completed in the spring.

HEADLINE: Trade New World;
As It Comes Together, It's Pulling Apart

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
In these waning days of the Bush Administration, with the Clinton regime still unborn, two competing forces are at work in the world, one pushing nations closer together and the other ripping them apart.
The clash between the two forces was clearly in evidence last week as the United States and Europe reached a compromise in their incipient trade war -- and farmers, politicians and others in France reacted furiously to the deal. Both accord and discord aptly illustrated how, at the same moment, nationalistic political impulses and pressures for economic integration are increasing dramatically. The resulting conflict threatens world stability and presents President-elect Bill Clinton with potentially severe problems. 
 The end of the cold war and the collapse of the Soviet empire in Eastern Europe are partly responsible. How many times from 1945 to 1990, a senior ambassador in Washington asks, was a fight over trade or politics or weaponry defused with the comment, "We can't afford this, because it might affect the East-West balance?"
No one need doubt the intensity of the feelings -- ethnic, religious, national -- that push peoples apart. Even as Europe has moved closer together in recent decades, with an economic locomotive pulling a political train, the Catholics and the Protestants have fought an undeclared war in Northern Ireland, and Basque separatists have done something similar in Spain.
Now, as European integration approaches, there are other difficulties; France's unwillingness to accept the deal reducing Government subsidies to farmers is only the most recent, dramatized by attacks on McDonald's and Coca-Cola installations and by troops in Parisian streets. Britain refuses to accept the European Community's passport plan, fearing an influx of undesirables, and Denmark's voters, rebuffing its politicians, have voted down the whole integration plan as an intrusion on nationalism. Germany hesitates to lower interest rates, its own bitter memories of Weimar-era inflation outweighing its pan-European instincts.
In central and eastern Europe, the Parliament of Czechoslovakia on Wednesday approved the division of that country into two republics on Dec. 31. What used to be Yugoslavia and what used to be the Soviet Union continue their process of disintegration into warring or squabbling states and factions.
In Asia, the Japanese worry about their rice farmers, like the French worry about their oil-seed farmers, and opt for measures to protect them at the same time they chide the Europeans and the Americans for giving in to "regionalism" -- trading blocs that might exclude the Japanese.
In North America, Canadian voters have rejected, despite warnings of economic difficulties ahead, a hard-won agreement on national unity, and there is opposition in the United States to the North American Free Trade Accord with Mexico and Canada. Even Mr. Clinton finds fault with it, though he wants to modify it rather than scrap it.
 
'Long Run' May Be Too Long
 All of this divisiveness takes place in the face of the conviction of most economists and political leaders, first, that smaller national and regional units usually mean lower standards of living for their citizens, and second, that free trade really does accrue to the benefit of all in the long term. President Bush has stood resolutely for free trade, and so do Mr. Clinton and his advisers, but there are protectionist elements in the Democratic Party (remember the campaign ad with Senator Bob Kerrey as a hockey goalie, blocking the best shots of "unfair foreign competition"?) and on Capitol Hill.
It may be that the lengthy negotiations intended to codify this widespread belief in free interchange in a new General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade -- known as the Uruguay Round -- will reach a successful conclusion while Mr. Bush remains in office. Few diplomats believe that the French really will pull down that whole temple in wrath over the compromise in the European dispute with the United States, no matter how bellicose the words of French politicians.
But the diplomats may be wrong, and even if they are right, Mr. Clinton will face long-term problems in keeping free trade high on the world's agenda.
The problem that will vex him is the same one vexing Helmut Kohl of Germany, John Major of Britain and dozens of other leaders. Giving up farm subsidies makes farmers angry, and angry farmers vote against governments. Free trade by a rich nation with a neighbor that pays lower wages may suck jobs out of the richer nation, and jobless workers there will vote against the government. Politicians do not like to alienate voters, no matter how lofty the goals.
To put the matter another way, it is difficult enough to persuade the electorate of any nation to yield even a scrap of sovereignty (which is why President Bush was insisting late last week that any American troops sent to Somalia serve under American command). Only the awful memories of World War II prompted the western Europeans to begin doing so over the last 50 years.
 
Leading the World
 It is even more difficult to persuade the voters to do so when some are being asked to yield their present livelihoods in the expectation that, down the road, perhaps in their children's or grandchildren's time, more and better jobs will be created in many countries by liberalized trade rules. Yet that is what Mr. Clinton will try to do, not only as the American President but as the de facto leader of the world's industrialized nations.
Mr. Clinton speaks of an Economic Security Council, by which he means to symbolize the importance he attaches to creating jobs and rebuilding the infrastructure at home as an indispensable first step to strengthen the role of the United States in the world. That mission seems a sensible one to most Western leaders, who have complained that American economic weakness made it hard for this country to play its world role.
But those same leaders are troubled by the very title of the President-elect's new council. To them it has a protectionist ring. It seems to some to signify a combative approach, along with his pledge to focus "like a laser beam" on the domestic economy first.
In fact, Mr. Clinton will have to focus on the problems of the world economy at the same time he focuses on his own. The two cannot in any meaningful way be disentangled. If Mr. Bush failed to assign a high enough priority to turning things around in the United States, he also took a less than commanding approach in meetings of the Group of Seven industrialized nations -- among the major forums for coordinating economic policies.
Much will happen before then, but next summer in Tokyo, Mr. Clinton will have his best chance to take charge on the world's struggling economy, and to persuade the Japanese, the Germans and the French to try much harder to put aside their differences for the larger, common good.
Nine years ago this month, Europe was struggling with the question of where American cruise missiles should be based. There were big anti-American demonstrations in the Netherlands, Britain and Italy. This year, the struggle involves trade, not weapons -- a nice metaphor for both the blessings bestowed by the end of the cold war and the uncertainties that followed.

SECTION: Section 4; Page 7; Column 1; Week in Review Desk

LENGTH: 1216 words

HEADLINE: Conversations/Leticia Quezada;
L.A.'s Schools Chief Wants Power, And the Vote, for Immigrant Parents

BYLINE: By ROBERT REINHOLD

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
LETICIA QUEZADA remembers that day 16 years ago when, as a Mexican immigrant fresh out of college with a teaching credential in bilingual education, she applied for work at the Los Angeles schools. She was turned down "flatly," told Los Angeles did not need bilingual teachers. Today Ms. Quezada greets visitors as the first Hispanic president of the Los Angeles Board of Education, presiding over a school system in which nearly half the students cannot speak English.
She also presides over what is now perhaps the biggest crisis the nation's second-largest school system has ever faced. Six months after the civil disorders and arson that swept Los Angeles, the 641,000-student system is threatened both by insolvency and a strike by teachers furious over a recession-induced pay cut of 12 percent. 
 In all this, Leticia Quezada, who migrated to California at age 13 without speaking a word of English, the daughter of a Mexican copper miner who died of tuberculosis at age 33, has emerged as the teachers' chief nemesis and a forceful advocate for the children of the deprived.
Wednesday, on the eve of a courtroom showdown with the teachers union, Ms. Quezada put on the hard edge that sometimes comes with this territory, the complex shifting politics of the Los Angeles Unified School District, and defended the pay cut imposed on teachers.
"The union is saying 'lay off the rest of the employees to pay us the same salary,' " she said in a soft voice tinged with the accent of her native Mexico. "We have said in bad times the morale of our employees is very important. It would be a real disaster if some employees feel they've paid the entire price of the economic depression this district is facing. We have said we are not going to lay off any employees, teachers or classified because we felt this economy could not afford any more unemployed. We thought it was the right policy decision.
"There's no way for any union to say it's okay to cut salaries by 12 percent. But the reality is that bankruptcy is the only other choice. In the final analysis, this will get resolved. It will not be easy. The problems we have are so complex and so huge that most people would want to give up."
Moments later, tears flowed down her cheek as she described the plight of the children of what she calls the "forgotten America," the poor and dark-skinned.
 
Up From a Macho World
 "More and more, the color of the school system looks like my family," she said. "Unless someone steps in who has the understanding, the sensitivity and the language skills to speak not only to the children but to their parents, we are going to lose this generation. The little ones, they did not ask to be born into a poor family, to go to a school that does not have a nurse. The problems have gotten bigger. The riots brought lot of anxiety and tension to the surface. Things have gotten more intense and intense. People are beginning to say, 'Forget it, it's not worth it.' They give up and start killing, committing suicide."
Leticia Quezada is one of the first of a growing number of female political leaders to emerge from the macho world of Hispanic America. The most notable here is her erstwhile ally, Gloria Molina, elected last year as the first Hispanic member of the Los Angeles County Board of Supervisors.
Ms. Quezada's journey began in the slums of Ciudad Juarez, opposite El Paso, Tex., from which her mother emigrated to Pittsburg, Calif., near San Francisco, and worked as a waitress. The young Leticia started eighth grade without English, caught up and eventually graduated from high school with honors and attended the University of California at Santa Cruz on a scholarship, then earned a master's degree in bilingual education.
But early on, she said, she decided her calling was not teaching, but the politics of teaching. "A professor instilled in me a sense that rather than helping one child at a time there are bigger things like changing educational policy," she said. "I was always searching for a place to make a contribution to my community through changing policy."
To do that, she felt she had to move to a big city with lots of Mexicans. That inevitably led to Los Angeles, which she had never visited. After being rejected by the school system, she did job training, then in 1981 joined the Carnation Company, the Los Angeles-based food maker, as its representative to the national Hispanic community.
She eventually plunged into electoral politics, winning one of seven seats on the school board in 1987, representing the mostly Mexican-American east side of the city. Last spring, the fractious board elected her president. The politically powerful teachers union supported her re-election in 1991, but turned on her this year in a dispute over their contract. They marshalled enough support to thwart Ms. Quezada's bid for Congress in a bitter primary fight last June.
She has been the board's foremost proponent of two controversial notions, bilingual education and giving parents more control over their local schools, which have among the country's highest drop-out rates, lowest achievement scores and most violence.
 
Angering Blacks
 "We have a tremendous challenge in educating Latino students," she said. "In entry points like Miami, New York and Los Angeles, we believe the best way to educate these students, to get them to graduation, is bilingual education."
Even more contentious has been Ms. Quezada's proposal to let noncitizens vote in school board elections, which would greatly benefit Hispanic candidates inasmuch as Hispanic people are about two-thirds of the school population but only about a third of the voters in Los Angeles. Her proposal drew fire from the leaders of Los Angeles's black community, where frictions have developed with the fast-growing Hispanic population, and she was accused during the recent Congressional primary of promoting illegal immigration.
But she contends that the proposal, shelved for now, is a key to getting parents involved. "Over 70 percent of our parents are immigrants, very likely not U.S. citizens. So many parents are disenfranchised in choosing who will decide on the education of their children. At a time when all reform movements in public education are saying that the only way schools are going to do better is with better parent involvement, this is major form of parent involvement.
"Change is very threatening. If you give Latinos the right to vote, what is going to happen to the gains blacks have made? All of us have to get used to idea of change." In many schools in the riot-scarred South Central area, the schools are now about half Hispanic, half black. "Children have to learn to live together," Ms. Quezada said. "That is the reality most us in public office are going to have to start speaking.
"I am always going to be committed to the issues of large cities," she said, "to questions of policy rather than personal issues, always looking to the next generation. Education provides the most answers, because if you do a good job in educating children, most of the other issues are taken care of."
At age 39, Ms. Quezada has no children of her own and contemplates none. "No, I already have enough," she mused. "I take care of other people's children."

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
It's China Against the World, With a Great Deal at Stake

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF

DATELINE: BEIJING

 BODY:
THE American bullies, joined by the greedy French arms peddlers and the deceitful British colonists, are brazenly conspiring to overthrow our Communist system, subvert the return of Hong Kong to the motherland, and detach Taiwan as their permanent "unsinkable aircraft carrier." But comrades, what can we do about it?
That kind of bunker mentality, according to knowledgeable Chinese, hangs in the air these days behind the high red walls of Zhongnanhai, the domain of China's past and present emperors. If many Americans are frustrated by an inability to get China to moderate policies that seem outrageous, reciprocal sentiments vex leaders on this side of the Pacific Ocean. 
 China now finds itself at a turning point in its relations with the West, and its actions in the next two or three months may determine the course of its relationship with the West for years to come. No one seems to want a deterioration in China's ties to the outside world, but this could come to pass through miscalculations on both sides -- the kind of misjudgments that have been frequent during China's last 150 years of contact with foreigners.
The government continues to emit mixed signals, probably reflecting a tense debate over how China should proceed. The immediate question is how it should respond to President Bush's decision in September to sell F-16 fighters to Taiwan, and to France's apparent plans to sell Mirage 2000 fighters to Taiwan as well.
At the Foreign Ministry's press briefing on Thursday, a Dutch reporter noted that when the Netherlands sold submarines to Taiwan in 1981, the Dutch Ambassador was expelled and diplomatic relations were lowered to the level of charge d'affaires. The reporter asked, "Why is the American Ambassador still here?"
It is still possible -- though unlikely -- that China will downgrade relations with both the United States and France over the arms sales to Taiwan. Chinese leaders feel strongly that such arms sales violate promises to them and will encourage Taiwan to secede from China and declare itself an independent nation. The leaders also believe that if China does not make the United States and France pay a severe price for the transactions, then Germany and the Netherlands will be lining up to sell Taiwan military equipment as well.
So it is possible to envision a scenario for a downward spiral. One way it could start is if China downgraded relations with the United States and France and canceled an option to purchase six more Airbus passenger jets from the European consortium that makes them. Germany has hinted to China that if the Airbus option is not exercised, Germany may sell submarines to Taiwan. If that happened, China might downgrade relations with Germany as well. Meanwhile, the United States would react to the eviction of its Ambassador by adding tough conditions early next year to the extension of most-favored-nation trade status. Washington might also launch a "Radio Free Asia" to broadcast to China. Alarmed and insecure, China's leaders would increase the repression of dissidents and purge some pro-Western officials. They might increase military spending and sell missiles to Syria and nuclear technology to Iran . . .
This sequence of events is quite unlikely. But it is possible, as well as a reminder of the stakes involved.
"China is in a very unhappy situation right now," said a European diplomat. "They've got to realize that they can't keep on going like this. You can't take on everyone at once."
The diplomat said he expected China to downgrade relations with France over the Mirage sale, but to maintain ambassador-level relations with the United States.
"At some point, China's got to cool down and realize that it wouldn't take much to improve relations," he said. "If they just stopped talking about Hong Kong, then the situation there would recover and relations with Britain would be O.K. With the U.S., if they released a few political prisoners, there would be an improvement."
Western countries are much more willing to irk Beijing than they used to be, for two main reasons. The first is that China has lost its strategic weight as a counterbalance to the Soviet Union. The second is that China today is dismissed by many as a repressive regime on its last legs. Nevertheless, no one wants to take China lightly because of its continuing importance as a nuclear power, arms merchant and one of the world's fastest-growing economies.
Moreover, China has taken some important conciliatory measures in recent months. It has allowed many dissidents to leave China. It has released many Catholic priests from prison. Just a few days ago it freed a "counterrevolutionary," Bao Zunxin, from prison 19 months before the completion of his sentence. The Government caved in to American demands for opening Chinese markets. And in a bouquet to President-elect Bill Clinton, China announced shortly after the election that it would buy 2 million tons of American wheat.
China rarely gets much credit for such actions, in part because it is stunningly incompetent at public relations. While it was releasing the priests from prison, for example, it suspended its dialogue on human rights with the United States. The suspension of the dialogue -- which in any case was mostly alternating monologues, accomplishing very little -- is precisely the kind of gesture that achieves nothing for Beijing while reinforcing perceptions of China as repressive and recalcitrant.
There are some signs that both sides are trying to pull back from the precipice and open up channels of communication. Secretary of Commerce Barbara Franklin is scheduled to visit China next month, to help re-establish exchanges of high-level visits that have been largely suspended since the 1989 crackdown on the Tiananmen democracy movement. Mr. Clinton sounded a little more restrained about China at a press conference earlier this month than he had during the campaign. And early next month China is welcoming five American senators, including some of its critics, for talks and a visit to Tibet, the western region where Chinese troops have continually put down pro-independence movements.
"I think both sides are showing a fair amount of restraint here," said Harry Harding, a China specialist at the Brookings Institution in Washington. "They realize it's a potentially delicate situation and neither one wants to move precipitously in a way that could trigger a problem."

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
Poised in Haiti: 2 Kinds of Refugee With a Single Goal

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH

DATELINE: LEOGANE, Haiti

 BODY:
IN the shade of an unfinished vessel that will almost certainly one day spirit Haitians off on a clandestine journey toward the United States, two distinct voices of desperation can be heard.
Audelin Julien, a fast-speaking, rail-thin 20-year-old who supported Haiti's exiled President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, tells of how several times he has been harassed and narrowly escaped arrest by the police for his political views. Then, pointing to the beached wooden hulk nearby, he says, "By the time Jan. 20 comes, I will have enough money to buy a seat on that boat and then, God willing, I will go." 
 Under the makeshift shed where workers saw and hammer away to complete the ship, Hilaire Altidore, a gray-whiskered, unemployed man of 58, also expresses a strong desire to reach the United States. "We have a lot of problems now," Mr. Altidore says glumly. "There's no money. There's no food. There's no work."
And almost as an afterthought, he adds: "Then you have the problem of the club. Nowadays, someone can be just standing in front of your house and the police come and beat them."
The two men -- one clearly spurred by political fear and the other by economic deprivation tinged with fear -- reflect the two kinds of motives driving Haitians to flee their country. Can President-elect Bill Clinton afford to blur the distinction between them, thereby opening the gate to large scale immigration at a time when the United States, especially hurricane-wracked southern Florida, seems especially hard-pressed to absorb it?
 
An Unresolved Debate
 The dichotomy fuels the debate over which Haitians should be considered political refugees entitled to asylum under United States law. Since the violent overthrow of Father Aristide a year ago, surprisingly little has been resolved in that debate as some 40,000 Haitians have sought to flee to Florida by small boat. While most have been turned back, nearly a quarter have been admitted temporarily into the United States to pursue their asylum bids.
With many more Haitians openly preparing to leave in light of promises of more generous treatment made by Mr. Clinton during the election campaign, diplomats and human rights advocates are still arguing over whether the boat people should be intercepted, where they should be taken when picked up at sea and how they should be processed while in United States custody.
At its core it has really been a debate over whether the United States is carrying out a humane refugee policy or simply crowd control.
American diplomats insist that their actions toward the Haitians are strictly dictated by the law's criterion of "a well-founded fear of persecution." These days in Washington, that is interpreted as meaning likely punishment for specific political acts or beliefs. Mr. Julien, the first Haitian quoted above, would appear to meet the criterion.
 
A Double Standard?
 Mr. Altidore, the second, probably would not. The diplomats say that despite the climate of violence and sporadic lawlessness in Haiti, the overwhelming majority of boat people lack true political motives for their flight.
"Of the people who have come to our attention, either on the boats or at the consulate, I would say that only 5 percent or so have a reasonable fear of being killed or roughed up or brutalized because of positions they have taken," said a United States official who asked not to be identified. "For most people, this is purely an economic move, and in this regard, Haiti is no different from many other places in the region which have sent us one third of their population."
Advocates for the Haitian refugees, however, say that the Bush Administration's policy of summarily returning fleeing Haitians is just the latest strategy in an approach that has long singled out Haitians as a serious threat to the domestic order.
The advocates say that instead of impartially applying the law, Washington has deliberately set prohibitively high standards for Haitians. The contrast with refugees from Cuba is often cited. Cubans have never been the target of interception efforts. Instead, they automatically become legal residents once they reach United States shores.
Refugee advocates say that the average Cuban refugee is much like the average Haitian refugee, fleeing mainly because of deepening economic hardship although having lived in a general climate of repression. They note that Cubans face nothing like the pervasive violence in Haiti, in which hundreds of people have been killed since Father Aristide's overthrow in September 1991.
Diplomats agree that the treatment accorded Cubans is special, but insist there is no discrimination against Haitians. They point to the longstanding guarantee of asylum for people fleeing Communist rule.
With Haiti in the policy spotlight, the new Clinton Administration will face difficult-to-navigate pressures to balance treatment for refugees from the Caribbean's two largest sources of immigrants.
"We will be looking to redress past policy mistakes, and for us that means an even-handed approach, regardless of where the person comes from," said Jocelyn McCalla, director of the National Coalition for Haitian Refugees.
Stricter standards for Cubans are hardly in the cards, for they would quickly face stiff resistance from the large and influential Cuban-American population. On the other hand, relaxed standards for Haitians could touch off an unmanageable new wave of boat people, both physically perilous for the refugees and politically perilous for the Administration.
A failure on the part of the Clinton Administration to fulfill promising statements made during the campaign and transition would surely raise loud cries from Haitians, blacks in general and human rights groups. But given the realities, many refugee advocates say they are braced for more disappointments.

HEADLINE: A Foreign Notion for Washington: U.S. Needs Higher Taxes

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE

 BODY:
SOMETIMES other countries view the United States as a rich spoiled brat that not only refuses to eat its broccoli but steals precious crumbs from poorer kids. This view jumped off the pages of a new economic report issued by 24 industrial nations that scolded the United States for refusing to increase taxes to cut its gargantuan budget deficit.
The report from the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development, a Paris-based group of wealthy nations, said that because of its $290 billion deficit, Washington was forced to borrow billions in investment capital from abroad. This, the report suggested, was turning the United States into an economic ogre -- gobbling up huge sums desperately needed by third world nations and the former Soviet bloc in order to continue living beyond its means. 
 In a snippy aside, the report noted that among the 24 wealthy nations, only Turkey collected a lower percentage of gross domestic product in taxes than did the United States. Americans pay 32 percent of G.D.P. in Federal, state and local taxes, compared with more than 40 percent for the 23 other countries. The report said the United States "would remain among the least taxed even if taxes were raised sufficiently to balance the Federal budget."
The report said America's living standards had stagnated partly because of its spending on current consumption. It warned that the nation was saving far too little to invest in machines or railroads to make the economy more efficient.
American officials, Republican and Democratic, say increasing taxes could weaken the jittery economy. And, many conservatives argue, higher taxes discourage business investment.
What should America do? One suggestion was to increase the gasoline tax 25 cents a gallon, providing more than $20 billion a year -- and helping fight pollution.

HEADLINE: The Senator And the Builder: A special report.;
Gramm's Homebuilding Bargain Raises Issue of Possible Conflict

BYLINE: By JEFF GERTH with DEAN BAQUET

 BODY:
Senator Phil Gramm, the Texas Republican who is a leading member of the Senate Banking Committee, helped guide a troubled Dallas savings and loan operator in dealings with Federal regulators one year after the executive had picked up nearly half of about $117,000 worth of building expenses on the Senator's waterfront vacation home in Maryland.
Back in 1987, Jerry D. Stiles, then a prominent homebuilder and developer in the Dallas area, took on a project for Mr. Gramm and his wife, who together had bought more than 35 acres on a remote part of Maryland's Eastern Shore and needed a contractor to finish construction on the shell of a two-story house. Mr. Stiles also owned three Texas savings and loans, which later failed and which regulators say will cost taxpayers more than $200 million. 
 Mr. Stiles assembled a crew of workers in Texas, led by a skilled craftsman, and flew them to Maryland. There, lodged in hotels, they worked for a few months to finish the Gramms' 2,815-square-foot house along the banks of the Honga River.
 
Advance on Costs
 In constructing the interior of the house, Mr. Stiles advanced the $117,000 -- interest-free -- for labor, materials and travel expenses that the job was said to have finally cost. But he never billed the Senator for anything until three months after the work was finished, and then he asked for only $63,000, the maximum Senator Gramm had told Mr. Stiles he was willing to pay.
"I do not believe for a minute that the work on my house was worth more than I paid for it," Senator Gramm said in an interview earlier this month.
At least two Federal agencies, including the Federal Bureau of Investigation, examined the relationship between the Senator and Mr. Stiles, in 1989 and 1990, according to documents and interviews. But the Government decided not to investigate further, partly because the Senate Ethics Committee subsequently decided in January 1990 that the Senator had not unethically accepted something from Mr. Stiles and did not owe him any money. The inquiry, which was requested by Mr. Gramm, did not include interviews with important witnesses.
Both he and Mr. Stiles have described the extra expenses to Senate investigators as a cost overrun that Mr. Stiles willingly assumed. At one point, after learning that the F.B.I. was looking into the financing of the house, Mr. Gramm sent a check to Mr. Stiles to cover the difference plus interest. But after the Ethics Committee ruled, Mr. Gramm kept the money.
 
Other Questions Raised
 But an examination of Mr. Gramm's dealings with Mr. Stiles, who is now accused by Federal regulators of helping to cause the failure of the three savings and loans, raises questions about Senator Gramm's ties to members of an industry he helps oversee as a member of the Banking Committee.
Senator Gramm acknowledged that it was unusual to hire a Texas builder and savings and loan operator -- more accustomed to putting up entire subdivisions -- to help finish a vacation house in rural Maryland. But he said he wanted to stimulate the sluggish Texas economy by providing jobs for Texas workers. And he said Eastern Shore contractors and laborers had reputations for being unreliable. Three or four Texas workers eventually worked on the job.
"I came up with the idea of getting a couple guys from Texas who were out of work," the Senator recalled. "Sort of the hungry builder approach, as I think I said to Jerry Stiles."
Senator Gramm said he had not counted on air fares and hotel bills adding significantly to the cost because he had expected that Texas workers, hard pressed to find jobs in a recession, would pay their own way.
The examination also illustrates how the Senate Ethics Committee can help quiet possible scandal. In this case, documents show that the committee's findings discouraged further investigation by both the F.B.I. and the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation. The F.D.I.C. was trying to determine whether Mr. Stiles had paid for the work on Senator Gramm's three-bedroom house with his own money or from federally insured deposits from any of his savings and loans.
In reaching its conclusion, the Ethics Committee relied on affidavits from the Senator and Mr. Stiles, who never contended that the Senator had owed him more money. There is no record that the committee talked to anyone else. In addition, the committee's ruling described Mr. Stiles as a "prominent Dallas homebuilder," making no mention of his involvement with the three savings and loans, which, regulators say, were used to enrich the Stiles family, according to court filings in Dallas.
Senator Gramm picked Mr. Stiles as his general contractor at a time when Texas savings and loan executives were pleading for relief in Washington as their institutions were rolling up huge losses and Texas real estate values were plummeting.
The complicated story of the Senator's vacation house attracted the attention of regulators and law enforcement officials in late 1989, more than a year after it was built.
The story unfolded when Dallas F.B.I. agents assigned to investigate the failure of one of Mr. Stiles's institutions, the Hallmark Savings and Loan Association of Plano, Tex., subpoenaed some records of Ronnie J. Hawthorne, the carpenter Mr. Stiles had chosen to work on Mr. Gramm's home. After examining the records and talking to Mr. Hawthorne, they noticed that Mr. Stiles had run up a construction bill of $117,000 but had been paid $63,433 by the Senator.
Mr. Hawthorne, in an interview, said that after the F.B.I. met with him and discussed his work on the Senator's house, he called Mr. Gramm.
"The F.B.I. didn't want me to say anything," he recalled. "I was a longtime Republican. I called Gramm and told him the F.B.I. was at my doorstep."
 
Senator's Account
Offer by Gramm To Pay Difference
 Senator Gramm offered a different account in an interview. He said he had learned of the cost discrepancy when he called Mr. Hawthorne and asked if he was interested in doing some work on the Senator's house in Washington. He said that while Mr. Hawthorne might have mentioned the F.B.I., it was not an important part of the conversation.
Senator Gramm said that before talking to Mr. Hawthorne he had no idea that Mr. Stiles had picked up nearly half the cost of the interior of his vacation house, which he owns with his wife, Wendy Gramm, who is leaving as chairman of the Commodity Futures Trading Commission. He said that he immediately tried to make up the difference but that Mr. Stiles, whom he described as a major political backer, refused to accept the money.
 
 
A Cap of $63,000
 The Senator said that based on what he had budgeted for a two-story house with two decks, and the cost of the land, he placed a cap of $63,000 on the interior. Mr. Stiles, for unknown reasons, did not mention that it went far above that, though Mr. Stiles and Senator Gramm had raised the original ceiling to $63,433, from $60,000, according to Senator Gramm.
After learning of the F.B.I. investigation from Mr. Hawthorne, Senator Gramm said he got an informal estimate from a Maryland contractor to reassure himself about the value of the work. He said the estimate came in at $57,000 -- less than the $63,433 Mr. Gramm had paid Mr. Stiles. The Senator stressed that this was informal, and that the contractor did not actually look at his house. It would also not have included travel and lodging for two or three months for workers.
 
The Arrangement
An Unusual Deal: Nothing in Writing
 Mr. Stiles's arrangement with the Senator was highly unusual in the construction business. There was no written estimate or contract.
Mr. Stiles set up a separate account at Hallmark Savings and Loan and placed it under the control of Mr. Hawthorne. It was called the "Ronnie Hawthorne, Incorporated D.C. account," and Mr. Stiles replenished it from money drawn from an account at another bank whenever it ran low, according to a deposition that Federal regulators took in 1989 from Harold Wayne Ikerd, a former Hallmark official.
Mr. Stiles has not returned telephone calls left at his lawyer's office. He has no listed office or home numbers.
The Senate Ethics Committee's ruling in January 1990, which has never been made public, was issued to Senator Gramm just as five other Senators were caught up in a public firestorm over their relationship with Charles H. Keating Jr., a high-flying West Coast savings and loan operator who had made hundreds of thousands of dollars in contributions to senators. It also came just nine months before Senator Gramm won re-election that year.
In 1987, at the time Mr. Gramm's house was built, Senate rules prohibited members from accepting anything of value from people with direct interest in legislation. Any other gift had to be reported.
In early 1990, amid the Keating scandal, the rules were changed. Now members are generally prohibited from taking any gifts over $300, except from relatives.
To prove that Senator Gramm violated the rules that applied at the time, it would be necessary to show that Mr. Gramm knew all along that Mr. Stiles was going to pay for some of the construction work.
 Mr. Stiles bought Hallmark Savings and Loan in 1984. The executive, a former part owner of the Dallas Mavericks professional basketball team, saw running a savings and loan as a way to provide money for his own real estate ventures.
Indeed, earlier this year the Resolution Trust Corporation, the main agency running the Federal bailout of the savings and loan industry, sued Mr. Stiles, contending that at his direction Hallmark made millions of dollars in risky loans to a handful of his close business associates and family members, loans that led to the failure of the institution. Mr. Stiles is contesting the accusation.
 
The Campaign
Seeking to Save A Failing S.& L.
 In early 1989, as regulators determined that Hallmark was overwhelmed with bad loans and actually insolvent, Mr. Stiles started a campaign to keep the business alive with an infusion of cash from investors and the Federal Government.
He wrote to Senator Gramm and other politicians asking for support. In March 1989, Mr. Stiles visited Mr. Gramm in his Capitol Hill office. The Senator, by Mr. Stiles's account to Federal investigators a year later, offered advice on how to navigate the complex new rules that would determine which savings institutions could remain open. Mr. Stiles made his comments to F.D.I.C. investigators who looked into the Hallmark failure. An F.D.I.C. transcript of the interview was obtained by The New York Times.
Mr. Gramm, a former economics professor at Texas A&M University, said he had advised Mr. Stiles as an economist rather than as a senator, warning him that more bad economic times were coming and advising him to get out of the savings and loan business.
But Mr. Stiles told the investigators: "The purpose of meeting with Phil was the system is nothing but political. The regulatory industry is nothing but political."
According to the transcript, Mr. Stiles added, however, that the Senator did nothing to help him, and that Mr. Gramm even warned him that there was no way regulators would pump Federal money into Hallmark.
 
Letters Are Passed On
 During this same period, Mr. Gramm urged regulators to listen to Mr. Stiles's requests for aid and waivers from Federal rules. He passed Mr. Stiles's letters on to regulators, in one case calling for a response "as soon as possible."
High-ranking regulators responded to the Senator by saying that they had looked into his case and arranged meetings with Mr. Stiles to try to work out his problems.
George Barclay, president of the Federal Home Loan Bank of Dallas, which regulated savings and loans at the time, wrote the Senator that the regulatory staff was "sympathetic to Mr. Stiles's situation and is working with him," according to correspondence provided by Mr. Gramm.
Senator Gramm said it was routine to pass correspondence on to regulators.
Ultimately, Mr. Stiles's efforts were unsuccessful. The Government shut his three savings and loans in July 1989, and have portrayed them as saddled with large losses resulting from insider deals. And Federal prosecutors in Dallas are investigating Hallmark's operations, court records in Dallas show. The other institutions controlled by Mr. Stiles were the North American Savings Association of San Antonio and the New Braunfels Savings and Loan Association of New Braunfels, Tex.
 
Industry Complaints Echoed
 In the interview, Mr. Gramm insisted that unlike many Texas politicians he had not blindly supported the politically influential Texas savings and loan industry. He said regulators had praised his cooperation.
But he has also gone on record echoing industry complaints about overzealous regulators.
In 1985 or 1986, when troubles were just beginning for the savings and loan industry in Texas, the Senator said he routinely summoned regulators to his office to listen to the general complaints of Texas savings and loan operators. The Senator said Mr. Stiles was likely to have been among those invited but there was no record that he attended.
Joe Arendes, a former assistant director of the Federal Home Loan Bank Board, who sat in on one of these meetings, recalled that he had felt "flimflammed" because the Senator had not told regulators that angry savings and loan operators would be present.
"They were looking for us to ease up on regulation," he said of the savings and loan operators at the meeting, "not to take a hard line, not to close them down."
In an interview, Mr. Hawthorne, the construction crew chief, recalled that Mr. Stiles had agreed to pay him $25,000 in cash, plus all equipment and expenses, including travel and hotel, with no ceiling on the total amount.
Senator Gramm said Mr. Hawthorne told him that there had been a dispute over expenses on the Maryland job between Mr. Stiles and Mr. Hawthorne, and that this drove up the cost. But Mr. Hawthorne said he recalled no such dispute.
 
Ethics Ruling
Committee Finds No Impropriety
 Shortly afterward, Senator Gramm, who was recently re-elected head of the Senate Republican campaign fund-raising arm, was the keynote speaker at the recent Republican convention and is often mentioned as a potential Presidential candidate in 1996, got in touch with the co-chairman of the Ethics Committee, Senator Warren Rudman. Mr. Rudman, a New Hampshire Republican who will retire from the Senate next year, was a co-author with Mr. Gramm of the deficit-reduction law that bears their names. That call to Senator Rudman ultimately led to the Ethics Committee ruling.
By Mr. Rudman's account, Senator Gramm was prepared to repay Mr. Stiles but felt it was the contractor's fault if the job went over budget. Mr. Stiles, in an affidavit to the committee, supported Senator Gramm's version, saying he thought he could perform the contract for the agreed-upon price.
Senator Rudman said he remembered thinking that a Texas developer was an odd choice to build a Maryland house. "It struck us as unusual," he said. "It doesn't mean there was anything wrong with it."
On Jan. 31, 1990, the committee concluded that Mr. Gramm had done nothing wrong and did not have to pay Mr. Stiles for the work done on his house.
 
Limited Investigation
 Mr. Hawthorne, who had detailed records of his work for Senator Gramm, said he was never questioned by the Ethics Committee. Nor did the committee talk to the F.B.I. agents investigating Mr. Stiles's Hallmark Savings and Loan or the F.D.I.C. officials who had spent months interviewing Hallmark executives, and poring through records of the savings and loan.
Shortly after the committee's ruling, Mr. Gramm's lawyer got in touch with the Dallas F.B.I. agent who had interviewed Mr. Hawthorne, law enforcement officials said. He told the agent of the committee's ruling, and that Mr. Stiles had taken the blame for the cost overrun, the officials said.
At that point, the F.B.I. team decided to drop any further investigation, given the fact that Mr. Stiles and Mr. Gramm were in agreement.
The F.D.I.C. had planned on attending a scheduled meeting with the F.B.I. and Senator Gramm, the records show.
But in an internal March 1990 memorandum, an F.D.I.C. lawyer, Mark Blanchietti Hollander, wrote to top F.D.I.C. officials that the meeting never took place. "Apparently Senator Gramm was somehow able to secure approval of this and all related transactions from the U.S. Senate Ethics Committee," the memorandum said.

SECTION: Section 1; Page 26; Column 1; National Desk

LENGTH: 540 words

BYLINE: By MICHAEL KELLY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SUMMERLAND, Calif., Nov. 28

 BODY:
Taking his first vacation since winning an exhausting one-year race for the Presidency, Bill Clinton spent most of a sunny and warm Southern California day resting at a multimillion-dollar oceanside estate leased for his use by friends.
Mr. Clinton, who has scarcely gone a day since the election without doing something that put him in the public eye and who spent his first day of vacation campaigning in the Los Angeles area, seemed determined to relax today. 
 The President-elect scheduled no events that would take him far from the grounds and beach around the $8 million Tudor-style house rented by Harry Thomason and Linda Bloodworth-Thomason, producers of several television series, including "Designing Women" and "Evening Shade."
On Saturday evening, Mr. Clinton planned to go to Pasadena for a surprise birthday party for Mr. Thomason. The Thomasons, who have Arkansas roots, are close friends of the Clintons and were influential, if informal, advisers to Mr. Clinton's campaign.
The estate, which the Thomasons recently leased expressly to provide the Clintons with a holiday home, sits on a cliff on land known as Loon's Point, overlooking the Pacific Ocean. Commanding a view across the Santa Barbara Channel to the islands of Anacapa in Santa Cruz, it is about a mile from the minute downtown of Summerland, a town of 2,500 people a few miles from Santa Barbara.
Although the front of the property is close to the heavily traveled Highway 101, the house itself is some distance from the road's noise, set amid eucalyptus, cypress and pine trees. The Secret Service has established a command post in a mobile trailer between the road and the house.
The neighborhood is notably rich, even by California standards. The singer Kenny Loggins and the actor Jonathan Winters own homes on the narrow, tree-lined street of luxurious estates. In this setting, the normal commotion caused by a Presidential presence seems particularly loud. Nevertheless, Summerland seemed to be prepared to enjoy the Clintons' presence.
When Mr. Clinton arrived Friday evening -- his wife, Hillary, and daughter, Chelsea, had come earlier in the day with Mr. Thomason -- he was met by a welcome banner strung across the town's main street and by several hundred people cheering his motorcade.
The Santa Barbara area, whose economy centers on tourism, enjoyed the benefits of eight years of President Ronald Reagan's vacations to his mountain-top ranch about 20 miles from the city. Presidential visits, with their bevies of journalists on expense accounts, are especially dear to the hearts of restaurateurs, and the hope that Mr. Clinton might usher in a new flock of big spenders was evident everywhere.
In the Nugget Restaurant, where a portrait of Mr. Reagan has long hung above the Presidential booth, a recently purchased saxophone now also hangs in hope of luring Mr. Clinton in for a visit.
A local jazz bar noted the President-elect's arrival by spreading the word that he was expected to drop by sometime Friday evening for a jam session. Actually, he was not expected to do any such thing, and he did not, but the half-believing anticipation that he might seemed to add a little something extra to the evening.

GRAPHIC: Photo: President-elect Bill Clinton playing volleyball yesterday on the beach in Santa Barbara, Calif. (Agence France-Presse)

LENGTH: 1540 words

HEADLINE: Clinton Offering Few Hints Of His Plans for Spy Agency

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
When it comes to the future role of American intelligence, President-elect Bill Clinton's thinking is almost as opaque as the decision-making process of China's aging leaders.
Since the election, Mr. Clinton has said nothing about what role secret intelligence analyses will play in policy making, whether he favors more or less covert action, whether the United States should spy on corporations or what sort of person will serve as the nation's spy chief. 
 Last September in Little Rock, Ark., when Robert M. Gates, the Director of Central Intelligence, gave Mr. Clinton the traditional intelligence briefing for Presidential candidates, they talked about the state of the world, not about the art of intelligence gathering or about the risks of selling spy-quality commercial satellites.
 
Whither U.S. Spies?
 During the campaign, Mr. Clinton issued only two vague statements on intelligence needs, calling for a shift from "military bean counting" to a more sophisticated understanding of the conditions that ignite conflicts, and for better intelligence to identify nuclear, chemical and biological weapons programs.
The best indication of how the nation's intelligence agencies will fit into a Clinton Administration is found in his economic blueprint, unveiled during the campaign, which would slash $7.5 billion over the next five years from the intelligence budget. This year Congress already cut more than $1 billion from intelligence spending, leaving a $28.5 billion budget in fiscal 1993.
But Congressional Democrats who advise Mr. Clinton say he will need a more complete and timely analysis of the messy problems of civil wars, ethnic conflicts and economic competitiveness. Although in 1990 C.I.A. analysts correctly predicted the ethnic turmoil that would result from the breakup of Yugoslavia, for example, the first reports of Serbian death camps were reported last August not by covert operators on the ground but by Newsday.
 
Redefining Intelligence Needs
 "The Clinton Administration will be faced with a number of serious diplomatic challenges early on that will require more timely, objective and relevant intelligence," said Representative Dave McCurdy, the Oklahoma Democrat who is chairman of the House Intelligence Committee. "The Bosnias and the Somalias will be the rule rather than the exception in foreign policy, and that requires a different focus."
To meet those needs, Mr. McCurdy and other influential Democrats say, Mr. Clinton will have to redefine his intelligence needs. At the moment, policy makers bombard the C.I.A. and other intelligence agencies with requests ranging from big issues like North Korea's nuclear threat to minutiae like the length of a Somalian airstrip.
A broad national security review begun with much fanfare earlier this year defined the role and priorities of the intelligence agencies until the year 2005. But instead of reducing the agencies' post-cold-war missions, the review resulted in a list of 176 areas of concern ranging from Brazilian rain forests to money-laundering techniques, a scope so vast that when Mr. Gates first presented the 1993 budget to lawmakers it did not include any cuts.
 
Decision on Economic Spying
 Democrats also recommend that Mr. Clinton place greater emphasis on reporting from spies on the ground and on analysis of publicly available information. "A satellite photograph cannot detect the actions of a terrorist making explosive devices in an abandoned building," Senator David L. Boren, the Oklahoma Democrat who is chairman of the Senate Intelligence Committee, wrote recently in the journal Foreign Affairs.
Mr. Clinton is also expected to review the hotly contested issue of economic espionage as part of his campaign to make the nation more competitive. Retired Adm. Stansfield Turner, who headed the C.I.A. during the Carter years, wrote in Foreign Affairs last year, "If economic strengths should now be recognized as a vital component of national security, parallel with military power, why should America be concerned about stealing and employing economic secrets?"
But a report to be published next month by the Cato Institute, a Libertarian research group in Washington, argues the opposite. "Economic competition is not a threat to national security," it states, adding that it is not proper for the C.I.A. to spy "on economic activities taking place in a capitalist global marketplace."
Mr. Clinton has chosen two longtime Capitol Hill aides to handle intelligence issues during the transition: George J. Tenet, the staff director for the Senate Intelligence Committee, and John G. Keliher, his counterpart in the House. Both men played important roles in developing legislation introduced by their committees earlier this year to reorganize the nation's intelligence agencies. More important, they enjoy the respect of Congress and the intelligence bureaucrats.
Mr. Clinton has given no hint of his choice for the next Director of Central Intelligence, and Representative McCurdy and Senator Boren, have urged the President-elect not to rush to push Mr. Gates out of what is supposed to be a nonpolitical job.
"I didn't support him when he came in, but I've come around," Mr. McCurdy said. "Gates has tried very hard."
Senator Boren, who personally lobbied 35 senators to confirm Mr. Gates's appointment as C.I.A. chief last year, now believes he will leave a mixed legacy because of the agency's initial failure to cooperate fully with the Justice Department on a politically sensitive multibillion-dollar bank-fraud case involving Iraq.
 
Impenetrable Filing System
 Improving the interaction between intelligence and law-enforcement agencies will be crucial for the new Administration, particularly in investigations of international drug trafficking, money laundering, bank fraud and terrorism. Senator Boren recommends an overhaul of the C.I.A.'s arcane filing system, which compartmentalizes information in more than a dozen computer databases and often prevents the agency's own analysts from gaining access to information gathered by spies.
"The document-retrieval system of the intelligence community, particularly the C.I.A., is woefully inadequate," Mr. Boren said. "Obviously, you're dealing with two different cultures that don't mesh very well."
Mr. Gates has defended his record as C.I.A. Director in a speech on Nov. 14 to the Association of Former Intelligence officers. He ticked off a list of his successes, including structural changes to streamline intelligence agencies, an improvement in the quality and timeliness of analytical papers, a new emphasis on intelligence collection from human sources, the reorganization of the National Reconnaissance Office, which buys, launches and manages spy satellites, and the creation of a new office to coordinate photographic intelligence.
 
Classified Recipes
 He also boasted of moving toward "openness," saying, "There is no reason why we cannot describe to the American people what it is we do, what our priorities are, our process for producing intelligence and providing it to Congress, and the way in which we are organized to do that."
But promises to streamline the system of document classification and to become more "transparent" are hard to keep in an agency that remains so closed that its recipes for chocolate cheesecake and poached lobster with creme fraiche and caviar, published in a recent issue of Studies on Intelligence, a C.I.A. journal, are kept secret because the journal contains classified material.
To abolish that culture of secrecy, Senator Dennis DeConcini, the Arizona Democrat who will assume the chairmanship of the Senate Intelligence Committee in January, recommends appointing an outsider, perhaps a successful corporate manager, as espionage chief with a mandate to reorganize the unwieldy C.I.A. and other intelligence agencies in a dramatic way.
"Gates is a decent guy, an honest guy," Senator DeConcini said. "I just think he was the wrong guy to move the agency. What we don't need is another Gates type, another inside company person."
 
Who's Who of Unavailables
 A number of the predictable contenders have already taken themselves out of the running, some explaining privately that they have no desire to take a no-win job running secret organizations where only the failures are made public. Among those who have stated publicly that they are not interested in the job are retired Adm. Bobby Inman, a former Deputy Director of Central Intelligence, retired Adm. William J. Crowe Jr., the former head of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, Senator Boren and Representative McCurdy.
Some former senior intelligence officials are promoting James R. Woolsey Jr., a Washington lawyer who was Under Secretary of the Navy during the Carter Administration and an arms-control negotiator for the Bush Administration. Another often mentioned candidate is Morton I. Abramowitz, a former head of the State Department's intelligence bureau who is now president of the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace.
There are few illusions that the job will be an easy one. "Reforming the intelligence world is as difficult as turning around a Trident submarine," Mr. McCurdy said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Representative Dave McCurdy, chairman of the House Intelligence Committee. (Jose R. Lopez/The New York Times)

SECTION: Section 1; Page 10; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 666 words

HEADLINE: Libya Faces More Sanctions at a Precarious Time

BYLINE: By YOUSSEF M. IBRAHIM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: AMMAN, Jordan, Nov. 28

 BODY:
The French Foreign Ministry issued a statement this week raising the specter of further sanctions against Libya at a time when the country is reeling from rising economic chaos, deepening isolation within the Arab world and a new series of strange pronouncements by its leader, Col. Muammar el-Qaddafi.
In a joint communique issued in Paris on Friday, France, the United States and Britain promised "even more effective" sanctions to force Libya to cooperate in investigations of the bombings of American and French airliners in the late 1980's by delivering two of its security services agents for trial either in Britain or the United States and four others for questioning to France. Since March, Libya has been under Security Council sanctions that ban all civilian flights to and from the country and the sales of any arms to Libya. 
 The three Western nations have accused Libya of masterminding the two bombings, in which 441 people died. Libya has consistently denied the accusations.
The sanctions already imposed have hurt Libya to the point where hundreds of Government employees reportedly have not collected their salaries in months. Major construction works are being postponed. The country's oil industry is said to be deteriorating for lack of sophisticated spare parts.
Many Arab diplomats and Libya-watchers say the pressure may be taking its toll not only on Libyan citizens but also on Colonel Qaddafi, who has been Libyan leader for 23 years.
Increasingly erratic behaviour and pronouncements from Colonel Qaddafi, as reported in newspapers throughout the Arab world, are reviving talk and speculation about how well Colonel Qaddafi is coping with the pressure, at times making him almost a laughing matter in an Arab world in which he is nearly completely isolated.
In the last few days, Saudi, Jordanian, Egyptian, Algerian and other Arab newspapers have prominently displayed the Libyan leader's most recent comments, followed sometimes by exclamation points, on their front pages.
A few days ago, the Saudi daily Asharq al Awsat told its readers in a front-page article how Colonel Qaddafi decided to stop normal budget operations recently, telling Libyans in a national speech that he would instead distribute the country's yearly income estimated at $10 billion from oil directly to Libyan families in sums of $7,000 to $10,000 a year.
Colonel Qaddafi was said to have declared that the first to get the money would be the first 100,000 families that agree to emigrate to the Sudan, Chad and Egypt, countries Mr. Qaddafi referred to as "heavens rich with water that Libya does not possess," and blessed with land and opportunities.
The Sudan has been in decade-long civil war and experienced serious famines. Chad, an arid desert, is one of Africa's poorest countries. Egypt is one of the world's most overpopulated countries, so much so that nearly 6 million Egyptians work and live abroad -- including a million in Libya.
Over the last few days and weeks, the London based Arab dailies Al Hayat and Asharq al Awsat and the Paris-based satirical weekly Al Moharer have published other statements Colonel Qaddafi has made.
In chats and speeched to members of the so-called popular committees in the last three weeks, Colonel Qaddafi is said to have described Switzerland as a "neighboring country" that is "less developed than Libya"!
He referred to capitalism as "a retarded concept from a past period of history which has totally failed" and politics "another retarded concept that has been abolished in Libya"!
He also reportedly suggested that the collapse of the Soviet Union was aided by hamburgers "filled with cockroaches, rats and frogs with which the Soviet Union was destroyed" That was presumably an allusion to the opening in Moscow of a McDonald's outlet.
During the last two weeks, Colonel Qaddafi's speeches and comments went so far and wide they distracted attention in Libya and in the Arab world from the Lockerbie issue.

HEADLINE: Japan (Bush Backer) Tiptoes Around Clinton

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Nov. 28

 BODY:
The Japanese Government is spreading a quiet message to the hundreds of bureaucrats, businessmen and politicians who hop trans-Pacific flights every week to the United States: Visit your factories, play lots of golf, but stay out of Little Rock.
As President-elect Bill Clinton assembles his transition team, Japanese officials, who openly rooted for the loser, say they are fearful that an overly eager rush to build bridges with the Democrats could prompt a post-election backlash. After a campaign marked by repeated criticism of foreign lobbyists -- and promises by Mr. Clinton to raise $45 billion in new revenue by taxing foreign companies more heavily -- there are words of caution. 
 A task force at Japan's Finance Ministry is already producing reams of paper about how trade policy may change in the new Administration, and the Foreign Minister, Michio Watanabe, is itching for pre-inauguration talks with Mr. Clinton and his advisers.
But a number of officials warn that it would be unseemly to press Japan's case before Mr. Clinton's economic team is in order.
"I'm telling my people not to make haste," Noboru Hatakeyama, Vice Minister of International Trade and Industry, which has the most at stake as the new Administration comes to power. "Every once in a while the question arises, and someone wants to get in touch with the new crowd. But perhaps it is better to wait."
Japanese business executives say that they have not received any formal directives from the Government over how and when to approach the new Administration. But several executives report informal cautions against quickly and conspicuously changing lobbying firms, dumping those with close ties to the Republicans in favor of those linked to the Democrats.
"These are issues that the business community must decide for itself," said Nobuo Matsunaga, a former Japanese Ambassador to the United States. "But I don't think they need to be told" to use discretion, he said.
But in a country perpetually in policy overdrive, restraint is not easy. Tokyo is already overflowing with ideas to broach with the new Administration, including one to scrap the Bush Administration's biggest initiative with Japan: a broad, three-year effort to criticize the fundamental flaws in each other's economies, and then force reforms. The Japanese want to replace the effort, known as the Structural Impediments Initiative, with another, yet-undefined program that they say will be aimed more at the distant future and would be less awkwardly named.
But some officials concede their timing is poor. Japan's trade deficit with the United States is at all-time heights, largely because a slowdown in the Japanese economy has stalled imports.
Right now, major questions swirl around Mr. Clinton's election for the Japanese. One, oddly enough, is how it will affect Japan's domestic politics, at a time of voter dissatisfaction with the scandal-ridden governing Liberal Democratic Party.
Some young members of the party have cited the election as evidence that it is time to retire an older generation of leaders, and wrest power from a governing party that has been in for a long time -- 37 years in Japan's case. Just today a popular consultant, Kenichi Ohmae, created a new political pressure group that is explicitly dedicated to ousting the Liberal Democrats from power, arguing that they have made the central Government far too powerful.
Another focuses on the makeup of Mr. Clinton's economic team. Many say that they feel good about Robert B. Reich, the Harvard professor who is heading the economic transition team. Rather than protectionism, his books, which are suddenly being read with more care here, talk about many of the types of solutions for American industry that the Japanese have advocated.
But there are fears that more protectionist advisers are emerging, so the Japanese are also looking for reassurances that Mr. Clinton's promise to get deeply involved in the foreign-policy aspects of trade -- one of the few regions of foreign affairs that seemed to bore Mr. Bush -- would not raise a new level of tensions with Japan.
