HEADLINE: How the President Lost: a Campaign of Disorganization and Disappointment

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 28

 BODY:
By the time the Republican Party massed in Houston last August for its convention, President Bush's re-election campaign appeared a thoroughly snakebit enterprise. In just eight months it had been jolted by an ugly primary fight, a third-party challenge, two White House staff shake-ups, an urban riot and a seemingly endless economic slump. Bill Clinton led in the polls by 15 points. Yet the President, his political advisers say, was utterly, serenely certain of victory come fall.
It was the advisers who were starting to perspire. 
 As campaign officials told it in interviews this month, the Bush re-election effort was struggling with bouts of breathtaking incoherence. The convention had been planned largely by party officials outside the control of the campaign. The campaign itself was operating without help from the White House. The White House was so disorganized that simple political directions changed daily, and officials took seven months to hire a speechwriter for the President.
As for Mr. Bush, he was balking, refusing with a stubbornness born of pride and overconfidence to do what a candidate must: retail himself and his policies through the tawdry practice of politics.
Eleven weeks remained until Election Day. Mr. Bush still had $40 million to spend on advertisements, a week-long convention and the debates in which to showcase his candidacy, and he had just persuaded James A. Baker 3d to return and impose order on chaos.
There was still hope. Many Republican strategists believed that Mr. Bush held the upper hand, even in the dark days of August. "This campaign was very winnable until the late stages," said Mitch Daniels, an Indiana political adviser who was brought into the campaign at about that time.
"We didn't need the best strategy, or even the third or fourth best. All we needed was a strategy, period."
But there was no strategy, top campaign officials said, until the very end, when Mr. Bush finally threw himself wholeheartedly into the fray. And the lack of direction during the rest of the campaign manifested itself in every facet of the re-election effort.
Mr. Baker, while he imposed admirable mechanical order to the race, brought it neither purpose nor focus. The Republican convention killed off what should have been one of the campaign's major sales pitches and alienated millions of voters with its right-wing oratory. The Bush advertising effort began in confusion, consumed three-quarters of the $55 million re-election budget, and nearly collapsed twice in disarray.
Amid it all, Mr. Baker and the campaign chairman, Robert M. Teeter, erred, many argue, by attacking Mr. Clinton without explaining to voters clearly why Mr. Bush was a better choice.
 
The Convention: Dismaying Voters

The Republican convention in August was a political showcase, but not, it turned out, for Mr. Bush.
Conceived in June, when Ross Perot's candidacy was plundering the Republicans' conservative base, the convention was intended to reassure right-wing voters of Mr. Bush's solidarity. It did so superbly. The trouble was that Mr. Perot dropped out of the race a month before the convention and Mr. Bush's problems now lay elsewhere, among suburban moderates and Reagan Democrats who were swarming to Mr. Clinton.
And moderates who heard the firebrand speeches of Mr. Bush's former primary rival, Patrick J. Buchanan, Marilyn Quayle and the television evangelist Pat Robertson fled in droves.
That might have been averted, aides said this month, had convention preparations been housed within Mr. Bush's campaign, as they were in 1988. But convention organizers this time were more independent, working with but not relying on Mr. Teeter's staff for explicit instructions.
The consequences were woefully obvious.
"We were overly preoccupied," said Jim Lake, who ran the campaign's communications operation. "We didn't write Pat Buchanan's speech. We didn't have editing rights over his speech. Very few people read the speech."
In fact, the Bush camp had no censorship rights over any convention speeches, something the political director, Mary Matalin, later called "a very serious mechanical breakdown."
"We were in a deep, deep hole after the convention," Ms. Matalin said. "Even if we'd had a coherent message then, we were spending our entire time denying that the Republican Party was a bunch of homophobic bigots."
 
The Advertising: Agreeing on Nothing

While party loyalists played at the Astrodome, Mr. Bush's top campaign advisers closeted themselves at the suburban Doubletree Hotel. The talk was of advertising: there was none.
Mr. Teeter had lured Madison Avenue's top talent months ago to a new advertising "agency," dubbed The November Company, whose sole task was to create Mr. Bush's television campaign commercials. Unfortunately, over an entire summer of debate, neither the campaign nor the advertising executives could agree on what the campaign should say.
The agency workers complained that campaign officials seemed unable to describe what they wanted. Instead, proposed commercials and even topics for commercials were dispatched to focus groups -- hand-picked assemblages of ordinary voters like middle-class housewives or blue-collar men -- who registered their approval or distaste by pressing small electronic buttons or talking to group leaders.
The result: advertisements perished by the score before their most powerful elements -- the narrator's voice, music, the pictures -- were ever heard or seen.
"They had the worst sort of client-agency relationship," said one of the members of the Bush advertising team. "Teeter was clearly dissatisfied with the work of The November Company. The November Company was very frustrated that nothing they thought was worth a damn was getting through."
 
The Strategy: A Message Is Found

Determined to break the creative logjam, the strategists at the Doubletree toiled for nine hours to produce a plan for how best to use their $40 million advertising budget to promote Mr. Bush in the 75 days remaining.
It was disarmingly simple: a three-week introduction to the President's second-term economic agenda; a few weeks to compare that agenda favorably to Mr. Clinton's higher-priced economic proposals, and a concluding barrage emphasizing Mr. Bush's experience and stature and maligning Mr. Clinton by comparison.
"What was killing the President was the impression that he was indifferent or insincere in his commitment to repair the economy," said Mr. Daniels, the Indiana adviser who was assigned to bridge the canyon between the campaign and its advertising team.
At last, a November Company commercial appeared -- a high-tech, upbeat sales pitch for the President's view of the economic future. Mr. Baker's aides repackaged a dog's breakfast of White House trade and fiscal policies into an "agenda for American renewal" that Mr. Bush unveiled to wide praise at the Detroit Economic Club on Sept. 10.
But except for banners at political rallies and boilerplate explanations in his campaign speeches, that was the last anyone saw of the agenda or the strategy from which it sprang.
 
The Attack: Forced to Retaliate

Ten days after the Detroit speech, Mr. Clinton released his first negative television campaign advertisement, portraying Mr. Bush as unconcerned about the economy and showing footage of him denying that the country was in a recession.
The judgment in the Bush camp, some strategists said, was that the President had run out of time for image-polishing and had to attack in return.
The trouble was that the leaders of the November Company found negative advertisements distasteful. In August, Mr. Baker had allowed Mr. Daniels to form a "B team" of advertising experts who would take on the task. But business summoned Mr. Daniels to Japan, and by late September, when most campaigns have reels of commercials at the ready, "our inventory was pretty slim -- like, nonexistent," said Robert Gardner, a San Francisco advertising executive who was involved with the campaign.
Mr. Baker called Sig Rogich, one of the brains behind the Reagan and Bush advertising campaigns in 1984 and 1988, who had become the American ambassador to Iceland.
Mr. Rogich immediately produced six commercials. He became the architect of the campaign's increasingly negative tone on television. "He took it over," Mr. Gardner said. "He gave it some leadership."
 
The Debates: Political Soap Opera

As the advertising effort underwent upheaval, a political soap opera was developing over what many saw as Mr. Bush's salvation -- the debates.
Mr. Baker and Mr. Teeter had abandoned conventional political wisdom: that a trailing candidate engages his foe in debate as soon and often as possible. Instead, they jousted with Mr. Clinton over the number and format of the debates, dragging the dispute onto the nation's front pages for most of September before settling on three meetings.
Aides were confounded by the dithering over what they viewed as an opportunity to match a real President with two pretenders in every sense of the word.
Mr. Perot, who had returned to the race on Oct. 1, was regarded as a wild card whose political impact was unpredictable, but the hope was that the debates would expose him as irascible and unworthy of serious support. Mr. Clinton tended to turn pedantic in debates. But the President, it was thought, relished such competitions and was at his relaxed best in town hall-style meetings and news conferences.
As cramming for the match-ups proceeded, however, Mr. Bush seemed disengaged, several aides said, and the image carried to the debates themselves. Except in the final debate, where he performed well, he sat passively as Mr. Clinton wooed the audience or Mr. Perot rattled on.
Mr. Perot's presence rankled Mr. Bush. "He understood why the organizers let Perot in, but he thought it was degrading," an adviser said. "He sure didn't consider Ross Perot his peer."
 
The Final Sprint: Focusing on Trust

Mr. Teeter had argued from the start that elections are decided in the final weeks, and that agonizing over strategy was is a waste of energy. Now came the test of that theory.
With three weeks left, the President trailed Mr. Clinton in campaign polls by as much as a dozen points. Mr. Bush's solution was to cancel the briefings he was given on the polls.
Armed with a new 20-minute speech, he boarded a train in Atlanta and slugged away at Mr. Clinton with his two best remaining weapons: taxes and trust.
For the first time in the year-long campaign, a seemingly rejuvenated President and his media advisers struck the same gong, relentlessly, for weeks in a row. And something strange happened: the polls showed Mr. Clinton losing popularity, and Mr. Bush gaining it daily, in big leaps of support. On Oct. 29, a campaign survey judged the Bush-Clinton race even, at 39 percent each, with momentum flowing to Mr. Bush.
 
The Telling Blow: Scandal's Impact
 But the the tide reversed the next day. As Mr. Bush gave a speech in St. Louis, the prosecutor in the Iran-contra affair released a document that mentioned Mr. Bush and contradicted the President's statements about his knowledge of the scandal.
Mr. Clinton immediately questioned Mr. Bush's trustworthiness, Mr. Bush cried foul and the episode dominated the evening news. Mr. Clinton then gained five points in the Bush campaign's own polls and widened his lead over the weekend.
If the President felt mortally wounded, he apparently told no one. "He never entertained the possibility that he'd lose. I spent time with him," one aide said. "He never -- never -- said he thought he wouldn't win."
He did come close, once. It was on the morning of the election, as Mr. Bush stood in the Houstonian Hotel bantering with aides about the day's vote. Everyone was politely upbeat. Then there was a lull, and Mr. Bush quietly filled it.
"Well," he said to no one in particular. "Do you think we're all fooling ourselves?"

HEADLINE: Lawsuit Attacks the Segregation of Urban Schools From White Suburbs

BYLINE: By GEORGE JUDSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HARTFORD, Nov. 28

 BODY:
The de-facto segregation that divides Connecticut into poor, largely minority cities and affluent white suburbs is about to go on trial here in a lawsuit attacking the racial isolation of Hartford's public schools.
The suit, scheduled for trial Dec. 15 in Superior Court, asks a state judge to rule that segregated conditions, whatever their cause, violate the Connecticut constitution's guarantee of equal educational opportunity. 
 Lawyers in the suit, filed by the families of 17 black, Hispanic and white children from Hartford and West Hartford, say a victory could lead to a new era of school desegregation in northern states like Connecticut, where schools have become segregated not by law, but because minority groups are concentrated in a handful of cities.
 
Relying on State Constitution
 Federal courts have refused to find such de-facto segregation unconstitutional, or to address it by ordering the transportation of students across city lines.
Relying on their state constitution's provision for free public education and its guarantee of equal opportunity regardless of race, the lawyers hope to make the case that even when segregation is not intentional, it is unconstitutional, because it leads to unequal school systems.
Connecticut has been studying the effects of school segregation since the 1960's, and many state officials, including Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr. and his Education Commissioner, Vincent L. Ferrandino, are committed to promoting the integration of city and suburban students, said John C. Brittain, a University of Connecticut law professor working on the suit.
"The problem," Mr. Brittain said, "is that Connecticut institutions, the General Assembly and local school boards, lack the courage to make the changes."
No one denies the racial and economic segregation that is reflected in Hartford's schools, where more than 90 percent of the children come from minority groups and more than half are from families living below the poverty line. The state, in opposing the suit, has maintained that it did not cause the segregation.
"There is no violation of the constitution because the state has in no way caused or contributed to the residential patterns that underlie the segregation," said Attorney General Richard Blumenthal. "There are a great many complex, difficult issues involved."
"We share their basic objective of eliminating segregation to the extent it exists and of improving educational achievement," he said. "Where we part company is simply on the legal issue. We don't believe that the courts should assume control of our school systems."
The suit, known as Sheff v. O'Neill (for Milo Sheff, a Hartford fourth-grader when it was filed in 1988, and for William A. O'Neill, the Governor then), has already survived two state attempts to kill it on the grounds that there is no constitutional violation and that the social and economic conditions underlying segregation are beyond the scope of a court.
The state, however, is prepared to call experts who will offer a much stricter defense against integration itself, including testimony intended to prove the following points:
*That desegregation has not significantly affected the achievement of black students.
*That most differences in school performance between Hartford and its suburbs are due to family background and economic status, not race.
*That most people in the Hartford area live where they do by choice, not because of discrimination.
*That minority groups in Connecticut cities do not believe integration is necessary for quality education.
*And that in any case, minority groups are moving to Hartford's suburbs at an increasing rate, achieving integration themselves.
Mr. Blumenthal emphasized Governor Weicker's commitment to achieving greater integration as he explained the state's strong defense effort. "We are open to negotiation," he said. "There is a great deal of common ground. The only potential solution that the Governor has ruled out, emphatically, and unequivocally, is a court order."
Mr. Brittain and other lawyers for the families say a court order is necessary precisely because the state is responsible for public education, including the drawing of local school districts, but has studied the issue for years without taking significant action. The statistical case, in large part, will be made from the state Department of Education's own reports.
"This court should not hold another generation of children hostage to the state's good intentions," the lawyers wrote in a pretrial memorandum.
Lawyers for the families have not offered a plan for integrating Hartford's schools with those of neighboring towns, but they do want numerical goals for the mixing of white and minority students, teachers and staff throughout the metropolitan region. They say students would have to cross the city line in both directions.
Mr. Brittain, however, envisions a process that could take 20 or 30 years to complete. He is looking not for the forced, overnight integration of 90-percent minority city schools with 90-percent white suburban ones, but for what he calls "mandatory voluntary integration," in which districts would be told what direction to move in, and then, with financial incentives from the state, could decide themselves how to get there.
The best result, he said, would be a remade educational system for the entire region that would provide a better education for everyone, white or black, urban or suburban.
"I would like, in the end, a victory for collective responsibility," he said.

HEADLINE: What We Need Money for and Where to Get It;
Economic Council

 BODY:
To the Editor:
As a co-sponsor of the Commission on Government Renewal, I appreciate your strong support for our proposal for a new economic council in the White House in "The New White House Order" (editorial, Nov. 15). 
 However, your concern over our having a misplaced focus on "competitiveness" is misplaced. The Competitiveness Policy Council, whose initial report to the President and Congress last spring helped inspire the recent commission, defines that term as largely synonymous with "productivity," just as you suggest. While the huge trade deficits of the last decade are a clear indicator of America's competitiveness problem, we explicitly rejected the trade balance as defining that concept.
To avoid any possible confusion, however, we would call the new entity simply the Economic Council. We would exclude the word "security" because, as you suggest, it could connote a protectionist intent.
The Competitiveness Policy Council shares your view that America's economic problems start at home. In proposals to the President and Congress, we will emphasize increased national saving and investment; reform of the education and worker training systems; increased investment in public infrastructure; control of health care costs; improved commercialization of technology; renewed emphasis on manufacturing, and better corporate governance.
We believe that creative initiatives in these areas, coordinated through the new economic council, represent the best protection against new trade protection.
 
C. FRED BERGSTEN
Washington, Nov. 19, 1992
 
The writer, director of the Institute for International Economics, is chairman, Competitiveness Policy Council.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Letter

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (94%);

HEADLINE: What We Need Money for and Where to Get It;
Fixing Unemployment

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In this century no political party has been able to retain control of the White House after a four-year increase in civilian employment of less than 5 percent. Warren G. Harding in 1920, Franklin D. Roosevelt in 1932, Dwight D. Eisenhower in 1952, John F. Kennedy in 1960 and Bill Clinton in 1992 were all able to oust the incumbent party from the executive office when increases in civilian employment were 3.1, -15.7, 3.3, 3.1 and about 2.4 percent, respectively. 
 Since the Great Depression of the 1930's no President has been re-elected when the civilian unemployment rate for October of the election year was in excess of 7.3 percent.
Gerald R. Ford in 1976, Jimmy Carter in 1980 and George Bush in 1992 were all ousted when the unemployment rate was in the vicinity of 7.5 percent or more.
The dilemma a President faces is that the United States electorate also dislikes high inflation. Since Woodrow Wilson kept us out of World War I in 1916 no political party has been able to get re-elected when the annual inflation rate for the consumer price index was in excess of 4.5 percent. Harding in 1920, Richard M. Nixon in 1968, Mr. Carter in 1976 and Ronald Reagan in 1980 were all successful at ousting the incumbent party when consumer price index inflation rates were 4.7 percent to 15.8 percent.
How much can Mr. Clinton reduce the unemployment rate without unleashing an inflationary spiral that might be self-defeating?
Stuart Weiner of the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City noted in his 1986 discussion of the natural or "nonprice accelerating" unemployment rate (in the absence of adverse supply shocks) that estimates of this rate vary from 5 percent to more than 7 percent.
To be on the safe side, a re-election-minded Bill Clinton should probably be content not to push the unemployment rate much below 6 percent. After the civilian unemployment rate fell to 5.3 percent in December 1988, the consumer price index inflation rate rapidly accelerated to 6.1 percent in 1990 before it could be subdued or "cured" with another recession.
 
EDWARD RENSHAW
Professor of Economics, SUNY
Albany, Nov. 12, 1992

HEADLINE: POLITICAL NOTES;
In Trenton, It's the Vote No One Wants to Cast

BYLINE: By JOSEPH F. SULLIVAN

 BODY:
There will be a lot of people holding their noses in Trenton tomorrow. That's what governors and lawmakers do in New Jersey when they have left themselves little room to maneuver and are forced to sign or vote for legislation they publicly decry.
"I'm going to sign this bill, but I'm going to hold my nose while I do it," has been said by more than one governor to indicate to the public that he is being forced to do something by circumstances beyond his control and shouldn't be held accountable. 
 But the expected passage of a package of bills intended to end a crisis in financing health care for the poor is something everyone agrees is long overdue, and tomorrow's deadline was agreed upon several months ago by Gov. Jim Florio, a Democrat, and the Republican leadership of both houses of the Legislature.
The trouble is that the two sides were unable to agree on something to replace the 12-year-old system that expires on Tuesday. That system imposed a 19.1 percent surcharge on hospital bills to pay for the care of those who do not have health insurance. There are approximately 823,000 such people in the state.
In May, Judge Alfred M. Wolin of Federal District Court in Newark ruled that the surcharge violated Federal law as it applied to self-insured health plans, which cover 40 percent of all insured patients.
To replace most of the surcharge, the Governor and legislative leaders have compromised on a plan to divert $1.6 billion from the state's Unemployment Insurance Fund over the next three years to pay for indigent care. The move has drawn sharp criticism from both business and organized labor and has been called "inadvisable" by the United States Department of Labor.
With that kind of reception, the administration and many legislators have been putting on unhappy faces and practicing their nose-holding technique. The plan is expected to pass, but the vote could be a lot closer than legislative leaders predict.
Governor Florio, who has been making his unhappiness with the compromise obvious to everyone, nevertheless sent a strong signal of support for it on Tuesday through his State Labor Commissioner, Raymond L. Bramucci, a former labor leader. Mr. Bramucci's task was unhappy and it showed in the text of his statement. He reluctantly supported the diversion of money from the unemployment fund but said a broad-based tax was a better solution.
He pointed to his role as "faithful steward of our Unemployment Insurance Fund," and wanted to make it clear the diversion would continue for only three years. If anyone is holding his nose during tomorrow's legislative session, it will be Mr. Bramucci.
 
Leaving at His Own Speed
 Albany insiders snickered a year ago when an advertisement appeared in an American Society for Public Administration journal seeking applicants for the top staff position on the New York State Senate's Finance Committee. Among those who did not realize there was an opening for the job was its current occupant, Eugene K. Tyksinski.
Mr. Tyksinski, a bulldog budget negotiator who has feuded occasionally with aides to the Senate majority leader, Senator Ralph J. Marino, said after the ad appeared that he was not quite ready to leave. But many in the Capitol interpreted the ad as a rather inartful effort by Marino aides to tell Mr. Tyksinski that they considered him too autonomous and, in the words of Al Gore, it was time for him to go.
Few in Albany have succeeded in telling Mr. Tyksinski what to do during his 10 years as secretary of the Finance Committee. And it was only last week, a full year after the ad was placed, that he announced his resignation, effective Jan. 1.
No successor has yet been named by Mr. Marino, although Mr. Tyksinski said in an interview last week that he had recommended his deputy, Paul Reuss. Mr. Tyksinski, who is 57, said he was "exploring options with several groups in the financial sector." He is the highest-paid employee of the Legislature, receiving a salary of $102,988.
In representing the Senate Republican majority in closed-door budget negotiations in Albany, Mr. Tyksinski has fought for more money for schools and roads in Republican strongholds like Long Island and upstate New York while arguing for budget cuts in Medicaid, welfare and other social services programs.
When the Legislature and Gov. Mario M. Cuomo kick off the next round of budget talks, when they face a projected gap of at least $2 billion in the state's 1993-94 budget, the Senate Republicans will have lost significant experience. Not only is Mr. Tyksinski leaving, but the chairman of the Senate Finance Committee, Senator Tarky Lombardi Jr. of Syracuse, is also retiring this year.
 
A Challenge to Mailings
 He lost the election. But Michael J. Siris, a lawyer who earlier this month failed to unseat New York State Senator Michael J. Tully Jr., a Nassau County Republican, remains particularly angry at one thing: Mr. Tully's propensity for sending tax-financed newsletters to his constituents.
Mr. Siris says that he doesn't know how many Mr. Tully sent, but that he suspects they were not all free of politics. In a lawsuit filed in State Supreme Court in Albany, Mr. Siris said he tried repeatedly to force the Republican-controlled State Senate to reveal how many newsletters Mr. Tully had mailed and at what cost to the taxpayers. But the Senate refused, even when Mr. Siris filed a request under the State's Freedom of Information Law.
Justin McCarthy, a spokesman for Mr. Marino, the Senate majority leader, called the suit frivolous. "The Senate has strict guidelines regarding mailings and Mr. Tully was absolutely in compliance, as were all the other Senators," he said.
If Mr. Siris wins his case, it could force the State Legislature, which is notoriously tight-lipped about its own budget, to open its books about mass mailings for all Senators. (Assembly rules provide for the disclosure of such information). As a result, watchdog groups that have long called for more extensive disclosure by the Legislature are looking on with special eagerness.
 
Economic Salvos in Hartford
 Reviving Connecticut's economy, long dependent on defense and other faltering industries, promises to be a priority in the legislative session that begins Jan. 6. And in the first indication of what shape the debate could take, the State House minority Leader, Edward C. Krawiecki Jr. announced his party's economic plan last week.
Mr. Krawiecki, a Republican from Bristol, also called for an economic summit among Republicans, Democrats and Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr., who is an independent, that would take place before the session begins.
The Republican plan, released on Wednesday, lists job creation, a reduction in mandated requirements for businesses, affordable health care and education as its priorities. Specific proposals include tax breaks for businesses and investors, expansion of the state's enterprise zone program to include towns where the defense industry predominates, a loan program for small businesses, and new rules that would reduce the cost of unemployment benefits and workers' compensation.
A spokeswoman for Governor Weicker, Avice Meehan, said he was out of town and had not seen the plan. She added, "The state has a well-developed and well-articulated and comprehensive economic development plan, which received broad bipartisan support in the General Assembly in 1991 and 1992, and included a whole range of programs, including manufacturing assistance and funds for defense diversification."
An earlier call by Mr. Krawiecki for an economic summit was rejected by Mr. Weicker. "I don't know that the Governor views that as productive," Ms. Meehan said, "given the fact that we already have a very significant economic development program under way."

HEADLINE: In Cities and Prisons, Hispanic Gang Grows

BYLINE: By RAYMOND HERNANDEZ, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MERIDEN, Conn.

 BODY:
With commando stealth, the Latin Kings, about 50 of them, some in military fatigues, swept into the courtyard of the Mills Street housing complex in this fading industrial city, with guns, bats and knives, bent on bringing a wayward faction into line.
Three recalcitrant members of the gang were seriously injured in the attack on April 26, which sent children and other bystanders fleeing. It was perhaps the most dramatic incident in the violent growth of the Latin Kings, a highly organized band of Hispanic men and women who, law-enforcement officials say, have rooted themselves in Connecticut's cities and prisons for more than a year, cloaking criminal activity with an appeal to cultural identity. 
 Spray-painted sketches of a five-pointed crown, the graffiti insignia of the Latin Kings, mar utility poles and burned-out buildings in Connecticut's crumbling inner-city neighborhoods, neglected areas with high unemployment, violence and despair. To many, the very name arouses dread. Shopkeepers in Bridgeport fall silent when the Latin Kings are mentioned. In New Haven, parents worry their children will get caught up with the gang. In Stamford, the Latin Kings have begun selling drugs.
The gang, which traces its name to a cultural organization in Chicago in the 1940's, was founded in the Somers Correctional Institute, a maximum security prison, in early 1989 by two inmates, Pedro Millan and Nelson Millett, who make up the ruling authority known as the Supreme Crown, court records say.
The Latin Kings have carved the state into five regions, with commanders, presidents and soldiers. Its members have been linked to drug sales and scores of assaults, and authorities suspect the gang in at least six homicides, said Richard Palmer, the chief state's attorney.
It is not clear how big the group is. Mr. Millan says there are nearly 2,000 members. Other estimates range from 1,300 to 8,000.
What no one disputes is the Latin Kings' portrayal of the group as an organization for the advancement of Hispanic people. It is an image that the authorities see as insidious, but that Mr. Millan defends as a proper appeal to ethnic pride.
"We have to take care of our own," the fast-talking co-founder of the group said during a recent interview at the Hartford Correctional Center. His co-founder, Nelson Millett, is in prison outside the state.
Invoking Malcolm X, Mr. Millan portrayed his group as locked in a social struggle, saying: "We shall not be deterred in our quest to achieve our goals for our Latin community."
A problem law-enforcement officials face is that the gang seems to thrive within prisons while maintaining contact with members on the streets. Members recruited in prison swell the numbers on the streets as they are released.
 
Chief Philosopher
 Indeed, the attack in Meriden, court records charge, was orchestrated by Mr. Millan, who bears the title of chief philosopher, over a telephone from a prison in Ray Brook, N.Y. Imprisoned for assault, he had been transferred out of the Connecticut system after officials found his organization involved in a major prison disturbance stemming from a struggle for control of prison rackets. He is now awaiting trial on conspiracy charges in connection with the Meriden assault.
"In the past, once we got criminals into the prison system, they were removed and we didn't have to deal with them," said Sgt. Frank Rudewicz, the intelligence division commander for the Hartford Police Department.
"But what we're finding is that what is happening in the prisons is affecting what is happening on the streets," he added.
Throughout the pockets of Hispanic neighborhoods in the state, the gang's talk of Latin unity has impressed many young men like 19-year-old Harry Rodriguez.
Mr. Rodriguez has had a troubled life. He lives in Bridgeport's blighted east end alongside aggressive drug dealers. He has no job. He never finished high school. He said he served a year in prison for stealing a car, something he denies doing.
 
Joined Gang in Prison
 It was during his prison term, starting in May 1991, that he met and joined the Latin Kings. Mr. Rodriguez calls them brothers and says they protected him from thugs and rival prison gangs. They got him whatever he needed in jail: soap, shampoo, toothpaste, toothbrush, underwear, socks, sneakers and food.
In turn, he has given the gang his loyalty and wears its colors -- bead necklaces of black and gold -- like armor. While Mr. Rodriguez acknowledges that some Latin Kings are involved in crime, he adds: "We're not supposed to be like that. We're not supposed to start trouble."
It is not surprising that some members like Mr. Rodriguez cling to such beliefs about the gang's integrity. In outlining the group's philosophy, Mr. Millan said that he and Mr. Millett drew up a thick charter with a cover page featuring a crude drawing of a crown. Members are admonished to adhere to the principles of honor, sacrifice, obedience, love and respect.
The rambling, ungrammatical text has 57 entries, among them a membership creed, prayers, a funeral arrangement form, a history of the organization, and a goal to have 100,000 members by the year 2000.
The charter lists 10 rules, including prohibitions on drug use, lust for relatives of a member, theft, assaults on members and discussion of the organization with nonmembers. Mr. Millan said he did not approve of violence or lawbreaking and said disobedient members faced expulsion.
But documents seized from gang members portray the Latin Kings as a brutal organization, shrouded in rituals and creeds, that punishes those who cross it. The group has an enforcement arm, a military-style band called Terminators, which exacts harsh penalties, the authorities said.
The worst form of punishment is, in the gang's parlance, termination, which the authorities say has led to people being "beat down" by five or more members, stabbed or shot. In the Meriden case, the attack was ordered because the local chapter was not complying with the gang's rules, court documents say.
Several crimes demonstrate the group's power in the prison system. One man whose name appeared on a termination list obtained by the authorities was an inmate who died after being set ablaze in his cell at the Somers Correctional Institution in April 1991, court records show.
In March, a Cheshire prison guard was accused of being a regional commander for the Latin Kings and of ordering an assault on an inmate, court documents show. In May, another prison guard was arrested in the delivery of razor blades to an inmate in the Cheshire prison who was a member of the Latin Kings. Both cases are pending, said Stephen Grasso, chief inspector for the Chief State's Attorney's Office.
Given the organized nature of the gang, the authorities recently have shifted tactics in fighting the Latin Kings. Whereas the problem was once largely left to the local police, now officials swap information to track the gang's movements.
"The gang was operating throughout the state," said Mr. Palmer, the chief state's attorney. "We all needed to work together on the law-enforcement problem they posed."
Evidence shows that the gang has spread beyond Connecticut. This summer, gang members moved into Springfield, Mass., flashing their gold-and-black beads. In September, a 17-year-old was shot by a member, the police there say.
In investigating this new, mysterious gang, the Springfield police looked across the border for help. They learned that they were up against a formidable foe.
"We were surprised that a street gang would be that organized," said Sgt. Michael Reid of the Springfield police, adding, "They've got a lot of headaches in Connecticut."

PUB-DATE: November 29, 1992

SECTION: Section 1; Page 22; Column 1; National Desk

LENGTH: 1519 words

HEADLINE: As Pay for Nurses Increases, So Does the Number of Men Entering the Field

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: FRESNO, Calif.

 BODY:
Gary Welch, an industrial parts distributor, decided to change careers the night he went to Valley Children's Hospital here to watch his sister work.
"I thought it would be boring," Mr. Welch said of his sister's job. "But there was this young patient, a girl, 11 or 12, who coded. Short for code blue, cardiac arrest. The activity! The nurses, the interns, all interacting. They got the girl breathing. 'Wow!' I thought, 'this is exciting. Everything going on to preserve the life of a person.' That got me hooked." 
 So Mr. Welch, 26 years old, is going to school here to become a registered nurse. So are Michael Newsome, 33, who was an oil-field roughneck, and Robert Ribiero, 40, a former police officer in San Francisco. Xavier Flores, 33, ran a cutting machine at a sawmill, Randy Shadle, 32, was an operations manager for Target Stores, and Mike Woods, 36, whose wife is a nurse, was a clerk at Safeway.
This big farming community of central California is no more a locus of sex-role convulsion than most American towns. In the Veterans' Day parade, the marchers playing flutes and twirling batons were girls, and the boys beat drums and blew horns. "Boys have more wind," explained a woman whose son was marching in the band. Men here say high school guidance counselors do not routinely steer men into the female bastion of bandages and bed pans.
But typically, after sampling other careers, men in Fresno and across the country are surging into nursing. For years they have made up a stable 4 or 5 percent of all registered nurses, or about 90,000 of 1.7 million last year, according to the Bureau of Labor Statistics. But they make up about 10 percent, or 19,000, of the current crop of student nurses, the National League for Nursing in New York reports. In 1988, men made up about 6 percent of all student nurses.
Like Mr. Welch, men here say they find joy and excitement in nursing. But what makes women's work women's work is poor pay, and what makes nursing a career worth considering is that the pay is sharply rising. Registered nurses' wages range from $25,000 a year in the rural South to as much as $50,000 a year in New York, Boston and much of California. Here, $40,000 a year is common.
Pay in nursing started rising in the mid-1980's, as more and more women were going to work as police officers, construction workers, lawyers and doctors. A shortage of nurses ensued, and it persists with an aging population needing medical care. So after two to four years of schooling, a newly licensed registered nurse usually earns more today than established workers in historically male factory jobs.
Nurses' health benefits are also among the nation's best. In many communities nurses are being given more control over nursing's once-onerous hours and working conditions, and there is hardly a community hospital in America that is not bidding for registered nurses and even offering hiring bonuses. "You never have to be without a job, and the market is wide open for men," said Lee Cohen, a registered nurse and nursing instructor in Augusta, Ga.
 
'Quickest Way to Good Money'
 Stuart Barger, a registered nurse and vice president for instruction at Everett Community College in Everett, Wash., where about 15 percent of the nursing students are men, said, "You can make a family stay together with the wages." Grace Whitis, head of the nursing department at Arkansas State University in Jonesboro, said 14 percent of her beginning students are men. "We do not go out and actively recruit men," she said. "They just seem to be coming in. I think it's economics. It's a good career. It's stable, and it has some intrinsic rewards."
Mr. Newsome, the former roughneck and a student at Fresno City College, said he had considered becoming a teacher. "But this was the quickest way to good money," he said, "and it's something I enjoy." Besides, he said, nursing is easy enough to master. "A patient is like a car," he said. "A car needs gas and oxygen. The body needs sugar, which is carbon, like gas, and it needs oxygen."
The male nursing students of Fresno tell different stories about choosing their new careers. But the pleasures of the work and the satisfying wages are common threads. Mr. Shadle, who is married to a real estate loan processor and has two children, ages 5 and 7, enrolled in the nursing program at California State University at Fresno after some soul searching about his career in retailing at Target Stores.
"I began to examine where I was at and where I was going," he said. "The next step would have been store manager. What I enjoyed about my job was working with people. I decided to find a field where I would enjoy myself more. I started looking for a helping profession, and nursing was the best choice by far. I can be in this career and be happy." The pay was immaterial, he said, because he can earn as much from nursing as he would have managing a store.
Mr. Flores, who is divorced and has one child, is a student nurse at Fresno City College. He said he first sought a career in farming and went to college for two years to get an associate's degree in agriculture. "I found there were no jobs waiting for me," he said. "I went to work for a lumber company for seven years. We'd be laid off for two months in the winter, and it got to where we were laid off for half the year."
"I got fed up," Mr. Flores said. "I wanted a job that gave me consistent employment. I remembered there was always a job for a nurse." So he put his name on the waiting list for admission to the nursing program at Fresno City College and was admitted three years later. "Economics had a lot to do with it," he said, "but I find I really enjoy it."
As men take up nursing, stigmas, stereotypes and entrenched relationships among hospital personnel are unraveling, much as they have started to do in other fields that were once the realms of a single sex. Confronting jeering bricklayers, Michael Prosperi, 20, a student nurse at California State University at Fresno, said, "Just ask them how much they're making."
That essential quality of nursing, the primal call to nurture, may not be so exclusive to women. "I think women are socialized to be more nurturing," said Rosalee Yeaworth, dean of the college of nursing at the University of Nebraska in Omaha. "But look at farmers. Look at the nurturing they do with livestock, caring for animals when they are ill."
Often, however, men need help in tapping their reservoirs of compassion. "If they haven't had children, if they haven't done baby-sitting like girls have done, some men are just real reluctant to pick up a baby," said Ann Walzberg, an instructor in pediatrics at Fresno City College.
So Ms. Walzberg said she often leaves a male student alone in a room with a baby. "I'll find a likable baby," she said, "and then I'll leave the room for 20 minutes. Almost always, when I come back, the baby's smiling and looking at their face."
 
'Softer and Gentler Man'
 Charles Freeman, 40, another instructor at Fresno City College, a nurse and husband of a nurse, said: "There is something that happens to men in nursing. I think I've become a softer and gentler man. The more you're in it, the more you become in touch with that side of your personality."
Still, Gordon L. Ogden, a registered nurse since 1957 and head of the nursing school at Fresno City College, said men should proceed differently from women. "They're not going to be nurturing in the same way," he said. "They should be more affirming." Rather than stroke patients softly as a female nurse might, he said, "They should touch patients firmly so they won't be misinterpreted."
Mr. Newsome said that in his experience many male patients respond better to male nurses. "Some things you just understand better as a guy," he said. "A man who wants to talk dirty will open up to a guy a lot more."
Male nurses here say older female nurses sometimes use them in stereotypical ways. Mr. Woods, the former Safeway clerk, said that as the only man in a team of seven nursing assistants in his last job, "The nurses tended to use you for pushing, pulling, positioning." But none of a score of male students interviewed here complained of serious discrimination.
"I just don't see much," said Mr. Ribiero, the former police officer. "Female workers seem to want me to learn, want me to get involved." Mr. Shadle said, "I anticipated ridicule -- 'You gay?' 'You trying to pick up nurses?' But I have not had a single person at the college or the hospital question my motives."
Students and faculty members do note differences among men and women in nursing, particularly in their choices of specialities. Men seem to prefer adrenalin-pumping assignments, like emergency rooms and intensive care units, over assignments to nursing homes and maternity wards.
"They like all that equipment, and working on a team," Mr. Ogden said. Mr. Ribiero agreed. "I want to be an air-ambulance nurse," he said. "I'm real action-oriented."
Mr. Shadle was even more emphatic. "Action!" he said. "I don't want long-term care. I want trauma."

In Fresno, Calif., Michael Newsome, left, Stanley Joe Johnson 3d, center, and Mike Woods, listened to a lecture on the digestive system at Fresno City College. (Darcy Padilla for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: A Christmas Plea on the 'Lords' of Commerce

BYLINE: By ARI L. GOLDMAN

 BODY:
Saying that the commercialization of Christmas "devours some, leaves others in ruinous debt and punishes the poor," a group of 25 major Christian leaders have issued an open letter urging Americans to exercise "moderate and thoughtful giving" and "genuine charity."
The letter, signed by Catholic, Protestant and Unitarian leaders and issued Wednesday, is sharply critical of the "advertising lords" of Madison Avenue, who, it says, have "reduced Christmas to a carnival of mass marketing." 
 While such appeals have become almost routine at Christmas from individual preachers, the letter was unusual because it represented the collective concern of a prestigious group of clergy.
The group included the Rev. Theodore M. Hesburgh, president emeritus of Notre Dame University; the Rev. Calvin O. Butts, the pastor of the Abyssinian Baptist Church in Manhattan; Bishop Thomas Gumbleton of the Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Detroit, and the Rev. Joseph Lowery, president of the Southern Christian Leadership Conference.
"I hope this statement is read in every church in the country," Dr. Lowery said.
While it is hard to gauge the impact of the letter on the public, it is bound to add to the anxiety of retailers as they face another Christmas season in a recession.
"Consumption has taken on an almost religious quality," the religious leaders said. "Malls have become the new shrines of worship. Massive and alluring advertising crusades have waged war on the essential meaning of the spiritual life, fostering the belief that the marketplace can fulfill our highest aspirations."
The letter was prepared by a group called the Campaign to Take Commercialism out of Christmas, a project of the Washington-based Center for the Study of Commercialism. It was issued at the unofficial start of the Christmas shopping season.
 
'The Gift of Family'
 In it, the religious leaders do not rule out the giving of gifts, but say that the stress should be on the "altruistic and reflective."
"For some," they add, "this may mean giving the gift of family, baking and breaking bread with old and young alike. It means opening our hearts to family and friends, and more, to those we do not know personally but whose needs we recognize."
The letter also warns about the consequences on the environment of excessive gift-giving. "We must be mindful of how our addiction to consumer goods is devastating the planet," it says. "Let us instead invest in renewing our own spirits, our relationships and our natural environment."
The coalition is sending the statement to hundreds of local and regional church organizations and is encouraging the clergy to discuss the issues in their sermons as part of the coordinated effort.
Among the others who signed the appeal were the Rev. Joan Brown Campbell, general secretary of the National Council of Churches; Bishop Herbert W. Chilstrom of the Evangelical Lutheran Church in America, and the Rev. Robert Kaufman, interim senior minister of the East Shore Unitarian Church in Bellevue, Wash.

HEADLINE: Shift for Urban Renewal: Nurture the Grass Roots

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BALTIMORE

 BODY:
LaVerne Cooper and Deborah Gibson spend their days about two blocks apart in the Sandtown neighborhood, where pride is more memory than reality. But a chasm separates Mrs. Cooper, who has a job and will soon own her home, from Mrs. Gibson, a former heroin addict daunted by the demands of any given day.
Now a generation of urban crusaders is trying to bridge that divide in Sandtown-Winchester, a cluster of 71 blighted blocks just west of downtown named for the residue that gravel-laden carts once left on its streets as they traveled from a nearby quarry. 
 Over the last three years, various public and private groups have joined forces in Sandtown-Winchester to see if this neighborhood of 10,000 people can again become a place where people will want to stay. Their plans offer a view of the latest tactics in the war on poverty: not just urban renewal but a wide array of strategies, large and small, to help make inner-city residents willing and able to meet the demands of owning their own homes.
Like similar public-private projects in the Bronx, Chicago and Milwaukee, the effort here hopes to avoid the rubble of failed programs by attacking on several fronts at once. They include training a few people to monitor the health of their neighbors; hiring others to cajole their neighbors into participating in meetings about the future; creating a day-care center where mothers are asked to volunteer time in lieu of payment, and trying to enable people to buy homes and keep them.
Mark V. Levine, director of the urban studies program at the University of Wisconsin at Milwaukee, calls the strategy a mixture of ideas from the 1960's and 80's: grass-roots social-services in the neighborhood combined with the idea of public-private partnerships.
The hope, he said, is "you rebuild the physical infrastructure, the social fabric, and you create a viable neighborhood out of one that is demolished."
The planners believe that earlier initiatives failed in part because they had imposed programs that were out of step with the residents' goals or abilities.
"Experience tells you that if you bring things in from the top down, they are not going to work," said Pat Costigan of the Enterprise Foundation, a partner in the Sandtown initiative. "We believe things can be done rooted in a community base."
Among the other partners are the administration of Mayor Kurt Schmoke; a local church, New Song Community, and Habitat for Humanity, the community redevelopment group given high visibility by former President Jimmy Carter.
More than anything, the Sandtown program and others like it try to avoid overpromising. The hope is that concrete results -- lives like LaVerne Cooper's -- will speak for themselves and make success contagious. If people like Deborah Gibson believe they can do more than just survive, the advocates say, then Sandtown and other islands in urban America's archipelago of decay can be transformed.
 
The Challenge Of Transformation
 Mrs. Cooper knows from experience how much Sandtown has changed in the last generation or so, and how challenging it will be to revive it.
She was born in the neighborhood 39 years ago, when jobs were plentiful and segregated housing patterns kept the neighborhood both black and economically diverse. It is so different now, she said recently. "This couldn't be the neighborhood I grew up in, no matter what," she said. "The role models left. You can't get them to move back. But you can take what you have and work with it."
Mrs. Gibson, a 43-year-old mother of seven and grandmother of four, lives from one welfare check to the next. She smiles a lot, and her sentences are laced with thanks to God and Jesus, whose image looks down from a drawing on a crumbling wall.
In many ways Mrs. Gibson embodies Sandtown's depressing statistics. Forty percent of the households depend on welfare; almost 50 percent of working-age people were out of work during the 1990 census, before the 1991 recession. More than 1,000 of 4,500 dwellings are vacant; only 19 percent of the occupied houses are owned by the people who live in them.
"The real job the Enterprise Foundation faces," said its founder, the developer James Rouse, "is to demonstrate that these conditions are correctable and will cost less to correct than the cost of supporting the neighborhood in its current nonworking state."
Mr. Rouse, whose vision turned around the downtowns of Baltimore, Boston and Norfolk physically and financially in the 70's and 80's, is now pressing with the same passion in the Enterprise Foundation to make poor neighborhoods work.
 
Meet, Clean Up And Then Build
 Four blocks from Mrs. Gibson's home, past cracked brick housefronts and a blocklong lot strewn with a mosaic of glass shards, a dozen residents hacked away vines covering two abandoned houses one recent afternoon.
Most of the workers were middle-aged or older. They had seen urban renewal programs come and go while drug dealers came and stayed. Some were volunteers; others, like 40-year-old Jerry Cross, were paid by the partnership to be "community organizers," a link between the neighborhood and the men and women downtown with the charts and graphs and glossy "vision statements."
"I had a lackadaisical attitude at first," said Mr. Cross, a lifelong Sandtown resident. "Was this really something that could make a difference?
"What changed my mind was the statistics. I've been in this community for 35 years, and for the first time I can see, the people who are my friends are dying around me. What was going on was affecting the people who play with my kids, the people I see on the basketball court. Things are getting worse. We could remain the same and not do nothing. Or we could do this."
Four sweaty hours of work on the vines left the 1100 block of North Gilmor Street a little less bleak. Small changes, like cleanups and neighborhood garden plots, have given the partnership a chance to get residents involved.
This is part of the effort to convince people that they can change things. Another element is coordination of services. Child care and day care are integrated with job training and employment counseling; health programs are coordinated with programs for addicts or alcoholics, and home ownership is coordinated with everything.
"Our approach has to be to identify each dysfunction and take care of it," Mr. Rouse said.
Barbara Bostic, an aide to Mayor Schmoke who heads the Sandtown project for the city, has been at work for months in community meetings on health, crime, education and housing, trying to get residents to lay out their needs.
"We're not going to try and retrieve the same spirit they had in the past when Pennsylvania Avenue was the cultural center of black Baltimore," Mr. Schmoke said, referring to a main avenue bordering Sandtown. "But we're trying to build on the old things. The new homeowners will have an attachment to Sandtown."
 
Homes, Yes. Jobs, Maybe.
 Most of Sandtown's new homeowners live in 227 detached houses financed by the city, state and Federal governments through a $4.9 million grant. These houses replaced the area's biggest eyesore, the huge abandoned Schmidt's Bakery plant, which was razed, and they were bought by employed families with annual incomes of at least $12,000.
While most of those families came from elsewhere in the Baltimore area, poorer Sandtown residents have another hope for home ownership: Habitat for Humanity. In the last two years, the organization's local director, Allan Tibbels, has given keys to renovated houses to eight local residents.
Seven more houses are now being rehabilitated, and Mrs. Cooper is scheduled to take over one of them. Like the other Habitat homeowners, she will have to make a cash contribution, but it could be as low as a few hundred dollars, depending on her earning power and resources. She will also have to put in at least 300 hours of "sweat equity" like nailing in new walls and shingles.
Habitat shares its local headquarters building with the New Song Community Church, an urban ministry of the Presbyterian Church run by the Rev. Mark Gornick. A church-sponsored clinic, day-care center and after-school program are also in the building.
Mr. Gornick, who has been here about four years, is like scores of other young ministers who feel that their vocation leads them to areas that, they say, society has abandoned.
"This has been a throwaway community," Mr. Gornick said, trying to make himself heard over the thrumming of a police helicopter. "People have treated it like trash.
"To rebuild communities you've got to see them as a whole. What are the building blocks? Good schools. Job opportunities. An economic stake in the community. Homeownership."
But jobs, one of the most obvious needs in a city that has lost 40,000 in manufacturing in the last 15 years, are something no one can guarantee.
"Jobs are an enormous challenge," said Mr. Costigan from Enterprise. "You've got a chasm between where people are in terms of skills, and where the jobs are."
Experts in urban policy are also cautious.
Programs like the ones in Sandtown-Winchester "shouldn't be evaluated as a strategy to try and change the whole neighborhood," said Gary Orfield, a professor of education and social policy at the University of Chicago. "This is a maneuver against the grain of policy to provide something for families who would otherwise have nothing."
 
The Present And the Future
 Sandtown-Winchester, whose population is 98 percent black, has ample suspicion of white outsiders and their projects. Jerry Cross once thought Mark Gornick was a drug dealer; other residents, Mr. Gornick said, took him and Mr. Tibbels, the Habitat leader, for narcotics agents.
But as residents like Mrs. Cooper and a few others joined forces with the outsiders, people began to take notice. Deborah Gibson, for example, has put herself on Habitat's list of 100 or more would-be homeowners, a list that signals not only hope but a kind of faith in the neighborhood and a willingness to stay and help rebuild it.
"Everybody likes Sandtown," said Mrs. Gibson's oldest daughter, 22-year-old Octavia Teal, who has four children of her own.
Not exactly, said her sister Shanna, an 18-year-old former member of a girl gang: "They like it, but they don't like how they live. You want to print something? Print this: 'Someone help Shanna Teal!' "
Her mother said: "The whole thing is survival. The whole thing is, 'How can I live day to day?' "

HEADLINE: Immigrants' Faith Lifts the Housing Market

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK

 BODY:
When Tricia and Philip Chen landed on American soil in 1990, having endured an eight-year wait to gain entry from their native Taiwan, buying a house was low on their list of family priorities, somewhere below becoming fluent in English and obtaining credit cards.
But priorities quickly changed. 
 The Chens, now proud owners of a four-bedroom house in Park Ridge, N.J., are among large numbers of immigrants to New York, New Jersey and Connecticut who have been buying homes, even during the depths of a long housing slump that has discouraged many of their native-born counterparts.
And while no one expects the immigrants by themselves to fuel a quick recovery of the housing market, analysts attribute their aggressive buying to a bedrock faith in the long-term value of real estate, a widespread belief that home ownership represents full enfranchisement in American society and the professional skills and hefty financial resources that some -- but far from all -- recent arrivals have brought to the region from abroad.
 
Lender's Survey
 "This gives us stability," said Mrs. Chen, whose husband is an engineer with Reebok International who spends much of his time working on research-and-development projects in Asia. In English that is brisk but still slightly broken, she said she still marvels at how big their New Jersey house is, compared to the cramped quarters that even affluent families must accept in her native country.
"In Taiwan, you would have to be a millionaire," she said.
Although precise data on how many immigrants are buying homes in the region are not available, one new measure is provided in a survey of 800 people by the Dime Savings Bank of New York, a large mortgage lender.
The survey results, released this month, found that 26 percent of the homes and apartments purchased during the last 12 months in New York City, Westchester, seven counties in northern New Jersey and on Long Island were people who had been born outside the United States.
Were the same results derived from a study that was confined to New York City -- as much as ever a "city of immigrants" -- analysts said they would not have come as a surprise. Indeed, after huge levels of immigration from the Caribbean, South America and Asia during the 1980's, city records show that fully one-third of its residents were born outside the country.
But considering the fact that in the area surveyed by the Dime, immigrants make up only 16 percent of the population, the figures took on added significance.
"The study highlighted the importance of the immigrant market in helping stimulate an economic recovery," said David J. Totaro, the Dime's director of marketing. In the survey, the bank also asked respondents about their home-buying intentions, and concluded that immigrants would also make up about a quarter of the region's home buying public during each of the next three years.
In fact many immigrants profess a greater faith than other homebuyers in the national and regional economy and little fear that the housing slump will last much longer.
"Since we are in a recession, I got a better house at a better price," said V. N. Prakash, a native of India who owns a jewelry business in Jackson Heights, Queens, and bought a large new five-bedroom home here in Farmingdale in 1990. "Things have to bounce back eventually."
The region's housing market remains mired in a slump that is now more than four years old. The Dime's survey comes at a time of evidence that home sales elsewhere in the nation are beginning to emerge from the trough. On Wednesday, a report by the National Association of Realtors in Washington said that existing home sales rose by 9 percent across the nation in October, the biggest increase in six years.,But it said sales in the nine Northeastern states, including the New York region, rose by only 2 percent.
But Mr. Totaro said the Dime study also provided a warning to banks. "We have got to better accommodate the needs of immigrant buyers," he said.
The growing importance of immigrants to the housing market is forcing lenders to rewrite some of their rules, including providing the bank with a detailed credit history and a birth certificate, that have proven to be obstacles to people not born in the United States.
For instance lenders, who have long been careful not to accept down payments unless they can be sure that the money is actually that of the borrower, say they have been increasingly presented with unconventional propositions from loan applicants, some of whom have even presented themselves with suitcases filled with cash.
 
Distrust of Banks
 "We are still trying to establish a policy for mattress money," said Michael J. Burke, manager of the Affordable Housing Program at Chemical Bank, which was established in January to make mortgage loans to families with incomes no higher than $60,750, and which has dealt extensively with immigrants from the Dominican Republic and elsewhere in the Caribbean and Latin America.
Many immigrants, because of their experience in their native lands, distrust banks. They often try to keep their borrowing to a minimum by making large down payments and in some cases even offer to pay for homes entirely in cash. But they often lack the credit histories that are normal for people who have credit cards, car loans and other experience as borrowers.
Still, the financial clout of some immigrant groups -- those from Korea and the Dominican Republic in particular -- is often enhanced by cultural traditions in which money is pooled among several members of a family or groups of friends.
At Chemical, Mr. Burke said, "25 percent and 50 percent down payments are very common" among immigrant buyers, forcing the bank to liberalize its rules for monitoring the sources of cash down payments.
To make sure that borrowers have not gone deep into debt to raise the down payments, or produced the money through criminal activity, lenders have traditionally sought documentation of how the down payment funds were raised. And applications have been rejected from applicants who received money from people other than their relatives.
But now, Mr. Burke said, Chemical is far more willing to accept down payments that have been raised by groups of immigrants. In such cases the applicants provide a letter from other members of the pool explaining how the pooling arrangement works.
 
Lack of 'Green Card'
 Another problem for some immigrants is the lack of a "green card," or documentation from their foreign employers. At Dime, Mr. Totaro said a past requirement that all applicants who are not citizens provide a green card has been changed -- now the rule is waived whenever an applicant makes a down payment of at least 25 percent.
Still, the attempts by banks to accommodate immigrant borrowers have not eliminated all hurdles.
"It seems to me that banks would want to make it easier for immigrants to engage in traditional American business practices," said Orlando Acosta, deputy director of New York City's Human Rights Commission, a Dominican immigrant who is among the hundreds of Dominicans who have purchased cooperative apartments in recent years in the Inwood section of Manhattan.
He said requirements by banks for green cards and other documentation from immigrants remain excessive and unfair. "They are just making things more cumbersome and more intimidating for people who don't represent credit risks," he said.
In Park Ridge, Mrs. Chen said she, too, found the process of obtaining a mortgage loan daunting. At the time, neither she nor her husband had credit cards or sufficient credit histories in the United States to satisfy normal policies of New Jersey lenders.
They also did not have enough cash for a 20 percent down payment on their house and were forced to borrow the money from Mr. Chen's sister.
But despite the hurdles, Mrs. Chen and other immigrants said they were not surprised that people born in other countries had emerged as a strong component in an otherwise sluggish real-estate market.
"In Asian countries, people have a very different cultural attitude toward owning real estate," said Grace Lu Volkenhouse, a specialist in minority lending for the State of New York Mortgage Association, and herself a Korean immigrant.
"We take a very long view of the world," she said, adding that many buyers who have personal roots in Asia have been undeterred by the region's housing slump. "We will invest and sit it out," she said.
In this suburb in eastern Nassau County, Mr. Prakash, the Jackson Heights jeweler, shares her optimism. He purchased his house last year after arriving in New York City in 1979, starting his jewelry business in 1984 and prospering.
He said he was attracted to the Farmingdale subdivision by a fellow Indian immigrant who first showed him the house he ultimately purchased and now expects his own family's move here to be part of the first wave of Indian immigrants moving from the congestion of Queens to the suburbs of eastern Nassau County.
"Buying a house on Long Island will always be a prestigious thing to do," he said.
Now Mr. Prakash says he cannot be sure when the recession and housing slump in the region will end. But he says he is certain it will. "This is America," he said, "and I can wait."

HEADLINE: NOV. 22-27: Economic Upturn;
Too Late for Bush, Too Early for Clinton

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER

 BODY:
The economic bulletins in the waning days of the Bush Administration are proving to be good news (though much too late) for President Bush -- and a bit awkward for President-elect Bill Clinton.
Last week, the Commerce Department issued several reports showing that the economy has been much stronger this autumn than most people realized. It grew at a hearty rate of 3.9 percent a year in the third quarter, much more than the initial estimate of 2.7 percent. Americans also earned more money in October and spent more, the department said. 
 But Mr. Clinton may not find comfort in the timing of the news. For one thing, it reduces his chances of being able to claim credit some day for the economic recovery that may already be under way. It also appears to weaken any argument for a quick jolt of Federal spending to stimulate the economy.
During the election campaign, Mr. Clinton proposed a wide variety of programs, including extra money for the nation's bridges, highways and communications links. But in recent weeks he has stopped short of saying whether he is willing to widen the deficit by spending more money while delaying any tax increases.
If the economy is indeed growing strongly again, such measures could seem unnecessary; tax revenues would be expected to rise eventually even without tax law changes. This would make additional money available for domestic programs without increasing the budget deficit. And that would be one argument for waiting before embarking on any big spending programs.
For the moment, Mr. Clinton and his aides still emphasize lingering economic weaknesses. "There's some increased activity," Mr. Clinton said on Wednesday. "But manufacturing is still in some trouble. There's still a lot of structural layoffs planned and Boeing announced another layoff today."

HEADLINE: What Is a 24,000-Square-Foot White Elephant?

BYLINE: By DAVID GONZALEZ

 BODY:
28 rms. Riv vu.
That capsule description is about the only thing conceivably compact about Arlene and Gene Kule's Riverdale home, a 24,000-square-foot Tuscan-style, coral-faced mansion so secluded one can ski down the driveway. The house, which offers a majestic tower and sweeping views of the Hudson River and Palisades, went on the market shortly after the Bronx couple opted for a life style change after their three grown daughters moved out. 
 Seven years later, the house, which the deal-ready Kules say is worth $3.5 million -- is still for sale, the price negotiable. Their daughters have moved back, so disillusioned by what passes for luxury in Manhattan that they reclaimed rooms of their own under their parents' capacious roof. The Kules are even talking less about changing their life style and more about staying put and changing the decor, having learned that selling one of the city's largest houses is no easy task despite low interest rates.
"Listen," said Mrs. Kule, her optimism echoing inside the marble-floored foyer. "You got a nice family that wants a fantastic home and tradition? This house brings tradition."
 
Architect's Masterpiece
 It also brings some bizarre proposals for alternative financing. The Kules have been offered everything from a fleet of luxury cars to a chain of grocery stores in exchange for the mansion. They declined, more intent on cash, thank you.
Known as the Campagna House, the mansion was built in 1922 by Anthony Campagna, a Sicilian immigrant who made his fortune building luxury apartment houses. The Federal Writers Project declared it "one of the finest villas in the East" and the masterpiece of Dwight James Baum, the architect who designed the house and its surrounding estate, which has since been broken up for other houses.
A dozen years ago the Kules forsook both their Park Avenue duplex and their country house in Connecticut after Mrs. Kule accidentally knocked on the mansion's door. She had mistaken the stately home for her daughter's school, and set her heart on buying it when she learned it was for sale. They paid about $1.5 million.
"It was a little piece of Europe," she said. "Oh, well, a big piece."
Less than 15 miles from mid-Manhattan, the mansion sits on 3.5 leafy acres, including a long cobblestone driveway leading to a fountain in the front and an elegant pool flanked by an orangerie in the back. Inside, the house boasts marble floors, ornately gilded ceilings and thick, carved doors among its two dozen or so rooms -- Mrs. Kule isn't sure of the total. The basement features a ballroom that accommodates several hundred revelers, although Mrs. Kule admitted she hardly ever ventured down there anymore.
 
From Working Class
 Not that she has much time. She, her husband and daughters work at the Silk Club, their family-owned business in Manhattan that manufactures garments made from machine-washable silk. Her job requires her to travel a lot, visiting suppliers and factories in the Far East.
Mrs. Kule's origins are a little nearer east -- she was raised in the Vladeck Houses on the Lower East Side. Her husband hails from Brownsville, Brooklyn. They shared a working-class upbringing before marrying and striking it rich in the garment business.
Mr. Kule admitted he felt a bit odd when his friends from the old neighborhood came by the mansion to help him celebrate his 50th birthday. They walked around the cavernous house, half in shock.
"I told some of them it was rented," Mrs. Kule said. "I was embarrassed."
Nor was Mr. Kule's mother sold on the idea of such a large house, and greeted it with skepticism when she first visited.
"You going to take in boarders?" Mr. Kule remembered her asking. "But she wasn't happy when I lived on Park Avenue either. She thought it was too dark."
 
Sale Foiled
 The mansion itself is never dark, even when the Kules embark on their frequent business trips, leaving behind their live-in help. Those voyages may take them far from home, but the home is never far from their thoughts.
"When we travel throughout the world, she calls and asks 'How's our kid? How's everything about the house?' " Mr. Kule said. "She doesn't even ask about the children."
When their daughters embarked on their own lives away from home, and their rapidly growing business entailed more meetings and entertaining in Manhattan, the Kules decided to sell the house. A food corporation in search of a new headquarters was eager to move in, but that set off a wave of opposition from neighbors who feared that a business in their midst would attract too much traffic and alter the sedate nature of the area. The sale became mired in litigation and was ultimately derailed last year, bringing the Kules back to square one.
Once they were offered 200 Mercedes-Benzes in exchange for the house. Another time, they were offered a chain of grocery stores. The Kules inspected the stores, but declined the barter.
"It doesn't turn out to be a good deal," said Farrah Kule, their daughter. "You're just taking somebody's headache away. Why not sell it and give us the cash? We don't want to get into business, we just want to sell the house."
Their efforts have attracted celebrities. The Kules said the Rev. Sung Myung Moon expressed interest in the mansion.
And Farrah Kule chuckles at the memory of her high school prom night in 1986 when Darryl Hannah, who starred as the mermaid in the movie "Splash," arrived to inspect the property with the musician Jackson Browne -- just as the Kules were frantically trying to extinguish a smoky fire caused by a stuck flue in a fireplace.
"My dad was at the front door and he called her the fish or the mermaid or something," Farrah said. "The fireplace was smoking, I'm dressed for my prom with my boyfriend and we were all running to get water."
And Ms. Hannah and Mr. Browne?
"They had some fling and went to South America or something," Mrs. Kule said. No sale.
The Kules have learned how to weed out curiosity seekers who darken their doorway. Sometimes it seems that every other "buyer" is a wide-eyed gawker asking if anyone famous ever lived in the mansion.
"I think they're looking to kill a Sunday," Mr. Kule said. "A lot of Sundays."
While not wanting to deter potential buyers, the Kules are upfront about upkeep: $22,000 in taxes, $6,000 for insurance and $7,000 for utilities.
The only moving trucks to roll up their driveway lately have been the ones hauling home their daughters' belongings. Each of them has several rooms on the second floor that they treat as their own apartments.
"It seems silly when you have this house to rent an expensive apartment in the city, a cubicle, when you can live here," Farrah said.
In fact, the whole family may slowly be realizing that it may be silly to live anywhere else. Mrs. Kule is talking about refurbishing and taking a spiritual cue from the Far East, perhaps settling in for the long haul.
In her travels, she said, she has come to admire the Chinese respect for family.
"You ever see a Chinese person in an old-age home?" she said. "They just extend the family. This house could help me do that. Nobody could throw me away."
