HEADLINE: 81st Annual Campaign For the Neediest Begins

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY

 BODY:
After roaming the country as a migrant farm worker for more than two decades, Rene P. Velez returned to New York City in 1989 in search of his childhood. He spent his first 36 hours on the elevated subway lines he remembered riding as a young boy, peering out the windows for a glimpse of a familiar station, building or street.
When he found none, he felt that his identity would remain forever clouded, that the future held nothing for him because he had no past on which to build. Angry and despondent, he tried to commit suicide by taking an overdose of pills. 
 "I took the whole bottle because I didn't know how to get help," he said the other day as he reflected on a long struggle with mental illness.
Three years later, after a number of stays in psychiatric hospitals, Mr. Velez, 39 years old, has finally come to terms with his rage and depression. His life has changed markedly, he said, because of a program for the mentally ill sponsored by the Brooklyn Bureau of Community Service, one of seven charities aided by The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund.
The Neediest Cases begins its 81st annual campaign today amid a relentless recession in New York City that has heaped added burdens upon charities. Many are trying to aid growing numbers of newly unemployed while also caring for people like Mr. Velez.
 
Record Amount Last Year
 This year, the fund starts with $257,431.56. Last year's campaign was the most successful ever, raising $5,083,550.81. That figure included thousands of small donations from people who could not spare much, but gave nonetheless because they still believed in the ideals embodied by the fund.
"Despite tough times, all of us involved in the Neediest Cases Fund are optimistic that readers will respond to this year's appeal and match the record contributions of the last drive," said Arthur Gelb, president of The New York Times Foundation, which administers the fund.
"Many of the leading charities in our city," he added, "depend on these contributions to ease the suffering of those struggling to survive under unbearable hardships: the traumatized homeless, the abandoned children, the dying victims of AIDS and tuberculosis, the homebound elderly in need of hot meals, the new immigrants with no place to go. The need to alleviate their agony is urgent."
The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund was born in 1911, when Adolph S. Ochs, the publisher of The Times, decided that his readers would be eager to improve their city if only they knew about the poverty around them. This year's campaign ends on Feb. 28.
The Times pays for the fund's overhead, so all contributions are given directly to the seven charities, which must rely more and more on private donations because of strained budgets and reduced government assistance.
"We have more to do, with either a little less or the same resources," said Dr. Megan E. McLaughlin, executive director of another Neediest charity, the Federation of Protestant Welfare Agencies. "My big fear is that we keep increasing case loads. While it's not an easy thing for people to understand, that will have long-term consequences."
 
'They Listen'
 Mr. Velez enrolled in May in Project Moving On, a program at the Brooklyn Bureau that tries to help people with a history of psychiatric hospitalizations live on their own. At first, he was rebellious and disruptive, seemingly unable to overcome a distrust of authority figures that lingered from his days in mental hospitals. He often lashed out at his counselors for urging him to talk about his parents, who abandoned him when he was a child, touching off repeated bouts of depression.
But gradually, he let his guard down.
"The people here, they listen," he said. "It's one of the most basic, most important things, because when you find someone to listen to you, you have trust. When you find someone dictating to you, you have mistrust."
Mr. Velez has turned into a leader at the Brooklyn Bureau, channeling his energy into working as an advocate for the views of the mentally ill. He runs meetings in which patients in the project talk about their problems and has discussed his concerns about the mental health system with city and state officials.
"They need to hear our voices," he said. "There is a percentage of people in the mental health system who can really function like I can function. There is not enough being done to get people into independent living and functioning better."
"I feel angry at the system, but now I'm trying to do something about it," he added. "The Rene of yesterday would get angry and say the hell with it and just walk out."
Like Mr. Velez, Lawrence James has spent much of his life drowning in anger -- at his mother for being cold and distant, at the Army for a dishonorable discharge, at himself for failure after failure. For most of that time, he sought refuge in alcohol, cocaine and heroin.
"I was mad at the world," Mr. James, 53, said in an interview at his apartment in Crown Heights, Brooklyn.
Mr. James first tried to wean himself from his addiction after a violent family fight on Christmas Day in 1985. Police officers called to his home agreed not to arrest him if he sought help. But he said he was able to fool his drug counselors -- and himself -- into believing that he had really changed. When he left the rehabilitation center, he relapsed.
He finally turned his life around in 1990 after he confronted his guilt over his teen-age daughter, who had been alienated from him because of his drug addiction. He enrolled in another rehabilitation program and has now been free of drugs for 20 months.
During that time, the Community Service Society of New York has paid for food, furniture, clothing and expenses for moving from a halfway house to an apartment. He has also drawn strength from Christine Collymore, his caseworker at the society, another one of the charities assisted by the Neediest Cases.
 
'Another Chance'
 "It feels great at this age to have another chance," Mr. James said. "These are people who really made an impact in my life. They have really changed my faith in humanity. It gives you encouragement to know the whole world isn't out to get you."
After being unemployed for more than two years, Mr. James is to start a new job tomorrow, cleaning and repairing buses for the New York City Transit Authority. He is also studying alcoholism counseling at Long Island University in Brooklyn so he can help others like himself.
"For the first time, I feel that I am beginning to know who I am," he said. "I don't feel mad about things I don't have control over. My life hasn't gotten better because of material things, but because of a new feeling inside."
 
7 Charities in 81st Appeal
 Checks should be made payable to The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund and sent to P.O. Box 5193, General Post Office, New York, N.Y. 10087, or to any of these organizations:
Brooklyn Bureau of Community Service, 285 Schermerhorn Street, Brooklyn, N.Y. 11217.
Catholic Charities of The Archdiocese of New York, 1011 First Avenue, New York, N.Y. 10022.
Catholic Charities, Diocese of Brooklyn, 191 Joralemon Street, Brooklyn, N.Y. 11201.
Children's Aid Society, 105 East 22d Street, New York, N.Y. 10010.
Community Service Society of New York, 105 East 22d Street, New York, N.Y. 10010.
Federation of Protestant Welfare Agencies, 281 Park Avenue South, New York, N.Y. 10010.
United Jewish Appeal-Federation of Jewish Philanthropies of New York, 130 East 59th Street, New York, N.Y. 10022.
No agents or solicitors are authorized to seek contributions for The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund.
Contributions to the fund are deductible on Federal, state and city income taxes to the extent permitted by law.
To delay may mean to forget.

HEADLINE: ON GOLF;
Players Can Find a Sublime Simplicity in the Birthplace of the Game

BYLINE: By Jaime Diaz

 BODY:
"The game was invented a billion years ago. Don't you remember?"
Old Scottish saying. 
 As it has undergone a second tidal wave of popularity since Arnold Palmer first shook the world, golf in America has been suffering from a case of the terrible toos: too slow, too expensive, too elaborate, too self-conscious and too hard.
Too often, the packaged American golf experience begins with paying something frighteningly close to $100 for a five-hour journey in an electric cart, which, the golfer is instructed, is to remain wedded to a concrete path. The golfer maneuvers the vehicle between island greens and condominiums, stopping only for quick excursions onto a carpet of overwatered emerald grass, from where he or she will try to avoid hitting the ball into a lake, creek or someone's backyard.
As more golfers have been shunted through this sterile production line, the pendulum has begun to swing back to a more elemental approach to an ancient game. American architects have been building courses that are less penal, flow more with the indigenous terrain, cost less to play and maintain, and are more conducive to walking. With luck, golf in America will be a better game by the year 2000.
Of course, the transition would be immediate and complete if America's golfers could all take a pilgrimage to Scotland. In the land where golf was invented and has been played by the working classes for 500 years, the natives have never allowed the superfulous to intrude on the game's sublime simplicity. In Scotland, more than anywhere else in the world, there is pure golf.
That belief was bolstered on a weeklong trip that my father and I made last month. We crisscrossed the country to play five classic links courses -- North Berwick, Gullane, St. Andrews, Turnberry and Prestwick -- in three days.
On a links course in the British Isles -- and only a few true links exist anywhere else in the world -- the golfer feels like a time traveler. By standing on the first tee, he has stepped back through centuries to what is literally common ground and common experience. On the gnarled, tumbling 14th hole at North Berwick, called Perfection, the game seems so old that imagining your playing partner to be a shepherd hitting a rock with a crook is effortless.
Physically, a links is an invigorating environment, made to walk. Because less has been done to them than any courses on earth, the rumpled links have a natural, unadorned beauty. The colors are rich but muted, an almost khaki green turf blending into a gunmetal grey sky. The sounds are the dull roar of the wind, broken by the crunch of spikes on hard packed turf. The smells are a clean mix of ocean air and cool, energizing menthol from the heather, gorse and sandy soil.
For an American golfer, a links is a litmus test of skill and attitude. It asks the golfer if he has the imagination to improvise shots along the firm ground, if he can absorb with humor and wisdom the inevitable serendipity of this type of play, and ultimately, if he can put aside the preoccupation with score and enjoy the simple act of playing.
It is an approach that comes easily to the Scots, a people whose history of economic and political deprivation has made them adept at taking the bad with the good. Accordingly, they love the game in its totality, and look upon its inherent unfairness as just another facet of an overriding cosmic joke.
Armed with that philosophy, the game is played briskly, without all the fidgety indecision and general wasted motion so common to American golf. Scots do not take "mulligans," and play preferred lies only when the greenkeeper is trying to protect the condition of the turf. The arc of their swings tends to be compact, all the better to keep the ball low and out of the wind and in the fairway, from where the game can keep moving. Three-hour rounds are the standard in Scotland.
In short, the Scots seem to know something about the game that most Americans simply do not, and the quiet certainty with which they carry that presumed knowledge has entranced us.
I know that each of the six times I've been to Scotland to work and play golf, I've gone home with a wonderful sensation of reflective ease. For the first few days back, problems seemed solvable, and tasks were welcomed challenges.
Our recent trip had a similar effect on my father, a 63-year-old, 9-handicap golfer who has played golf nearly every weekend for the last 30 years.
He isn't steeped in the history of the game, but he feels the ancestral lure. Like Bobby Jones, his favorite course is the Old Course at St. Andrews, even though hidden pot bunkers frequently catch his 180-yard drives.
This time, he particularly loved Turnberry, a course off the Irish Sea whose tall dunes and jagged cliffs make it perhaps the most dramatic course in Scotland. On an idyllic morning we had the course and what seemed like the entire Ayrshire coastline to ourselves.
A few days after we returned, I called my father at home in California.
"You know, I can't get that Turnberry out of my mind," he said. "I remember every hole. I've been having dreams about it."
There was a pause on the phone, which was broken when my mother said, "Well, aren't you going to tell him?"
My father laughed, and then continued in a voice that was full of wonder.
"You aren't going to believe what happened," he said. "Three days after I got back, I had a hole-in-one." Before I could react, he stopped me. "Then yesterday, I had another one. Both of them flew in the hole."
He had only had one before. I took the news with a strange calm. Somehow, I knew it had everything to do with Scotland.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The 11th hole at Turnberry, one of the classic links courses in Scotland, where, more than anywhere else in the world, pure golf survives. (Brian Morgan)

HEADLINE: Education for the Gifted, Seen As a Luxury, Faces Cutbacks

BYLINE: By JON NORDHEIMER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ABERDEEN, N.J., Nov. 25

 BODY:
As financially pressed school districts in the region struggle with declining Federal and state aid, rebellious taxpayers and rising costs, special programs for gifted and talented students are being trimmed, gutted or eliminated.
Enrichment classes, honors courses and other educational tools intended to accelerate learning at all levels of instruction are also experiencing setbacks in many districts. Advanced courses are being dropped from the curriculum, class sizes are swelling, and teachers are working harder with fewer resources. 
 Although many school districts have been able to preserve gifted programs, they have done so only at the cost of limited enrollments and classroom hours. And administrators who have already increased the number of children in ordinary classrooms and cut staffs and supplies find it politically difficult to spare gifted programs that may serve 5 percent or less of the student body.
 
A Cost-Cutting Option
 "It's difficult to go to the decision-makers and say 'my child is chronically gifted and needs your support,' " said Joyce Rubin, president of Advocacy for Gifted and Talented Children in New York state. "We don't want to take away from children with other special needs, but this country needs to insure that its smartest kids are challenged in school."
Gifted programs are being cut across the nation, education experts say, and some express concern that the effects of these cuts will be seen in, among other things, declining college admissions scores in the future.
"Sadly, we are producing a nation of unchallenged bright kids and underachievers and the cost to society will be with us for years," said Joseph S. Renzulli, director of the National Research Center on the Gifted and Talented at the University of Connecticut.
For many school districts, however, cutting programs for the gifted and talented is one of the few ways costs can be reduced, since programs for intellectually or physically handicapped students are mandated by the Federal and state governments and cannot be cut. Schools receive special funds earmarked for the handicapped or disadvantaged students but local taxpayers pick up much of the cost.
Dr. David J. Irvine, coordinator of gifted education in New York, said state aid for gifted programs has been frozen at $13.3 million for the last few years.
Between 1989 and the last school year, the amount of money spent by the 450 school districts outside New York City on gifted programs declined from $35 million to $32 million and the number of students exposed to a variety of gifted instruction declined from 87,000 to 76,000, a trend that is expected to continue in the current school year and possibly into 1994.
"There has been a substantial reduction -- better than 20 percent -- in the number of teachers for the gifted," Dr. Irvine noted.
Dr. Rubin, who directs the gifted program in Community School District 18 in Brooklyn, called the situation "devastating" in most of the state.
 
Parents Seek Help
 "We get letters from parents pleading with us for help," she said. "Their children in elementary school are reading three or four grades ahead of their class but the gifted classes have been eliminated."
Parents who cannot afford private education may become desperate, she said, and some opt to educate their children at home. "One couple with a son and daughter extremely gifted in mathematics gave up trying to educate them in the public schools and moved to Israel," she said. "That's a big loss for America."
In Connecticut, racked by budget problems, the decline of financial support for gifted education has been dramatic for a state that was one of the nation's leaders in the field.
By 1988, when Connecticut was contributing $8.5 million a year in state aid to 160 of 166 school districts that had academically gifted and artistically talented programs, the Legislature asked state educators to develop recommendations for future needs of the programs. The next year they were handed a study recommending that the state's share rise to $20.5 million.
"But Connecticut that year had its first budget deficit," explained Alan White, a state consultant for the gifted and talented.
 
Hartford Reverses Direction
 So instead of increasing aid to the gifted and talented, the Legislature cut the existing state subsidy until it was eliminated in the current school year.
Loss of state aid, combined with the impact of the recession and the need of local districts to reduce or hold the line on spending, led to the elimination of gifted programs in 36 districts, Mr. White said, while many other programs have been trimmed.
"For example," he continued, "some districts have reduced staff for the gifted and talented, renewing the contracts of three teachers instead of six. Or they have reduced the number of grade levels that have gifted programs.
"Some districts have, in my opinion, mistakingly gone back to whole class enrichment. While that may be applauded, that is general education, not gifted education." Under budget constraints, he said, educators "may fall into the trap" of thinking that class enrichment takes care of the needs of the gifted.
The consequences of such cutbacks are dismaying to those who staunchly support special attention to intelligent and creative students, students who often lose enthusiasm for learning in normal classroom settings that fail to challenge their abilities.
 
Doing More With Lessn
 But not all educators consider the cutbacks devastating. Dr. Philip Geiger, superintendent of the Piscataway, N.J., school district, said budget cuts were forcing public schools to develop dynamic new programs that were not necessarily inferior to the ones they replaced. Gifted students, he said, are now taught in mainstream classrooms in his schools without detriment to them or other students.
The key to success in a time of reduced Federal and state aid, he said, is diverting funds from non-instructional school costs, by, for example, privatizing cafeteria services and selling the school bus system to a private carrier, and using the savings for instruction. "We are making rational decisions, cutting out the fringe stuff, and doing more with no tax increases," he said.
In gifted programs, students are selected on the basis of testing and teacher recommendations, usually representing the top 5 percent of their age group in ability.
In many districts, it is the other talented children just below this group -- smart, able but not gifted -- who are hurt most by budget cutting, increased class size, and a host of other economies.
 
Not Live Frogs, But Drawings
 "How many times can we cut enrichment programs, squeeze advanced placement courses or eliminate things like class trips to museums before we start seeing through the fabric of public education?" asked Barbara Horl, chairwoman of the Matawan-Aberdeen Board of Education in Monmouth County, N.J., a mostly white-collar suburban area less than an hour's drive south of Manhattan.
In this district, with 3,800 students, voters have rejected the board's budget in recent years, forcing hasty cuts and bitter fights over priorities. As a result, said Peggy Schoen, a parent whose two daughters went through the elementary school gifted program, "Everything has been cut and if you want money for special things, the children and the parents have to have a bake sale or fruit sale to pay for them."
Marie Jackolow, a sixth grade teacher, said: "We used to have live frogs for science class. Now we have drawings. Once a week we gave each pupil a newspaper for current events. Now there are 20 newspapers for 60 pupils, but there's no cutting and pasting because the papers have to go to the fifth Grade to look up the stock tables."
The district has managed to keep one self-contained gifted class at each elementary grade level, though money for supplies, textbooks and even periodicals for these select pupils has greatly diminished.
 
Stretching Education Thin
 But the district was forced to abandon a magnet program at its four elementary schools that directed about half of all students into ability-related study programs, such as science and technology, performing arts, language skills and mathematics. It was estimated by administrators that the magnet program today would cost the district, which has a present budget of $35 million, about $300,000 a year.
There is no gifted program in the regional high school serving the district, and budget cuts have taken their toll on honors and advanced placement courses that help students prepare for college, said Kenneth Hall, the district's superintendent of schools.
Moreover, Mrs. Horl said, the district, which is one of those middle-class districts taking a 30 percent reduction in state aid under a state plan to transfer those funds to poorer districts, is experiencing a decline in many aspects of classroom learning.
"Everyone who is part of the public schools is seeing the strain," she said. "Most of the teachers are giving above and beyond their contractural obligations. But how long we can continue holding the system together before something snaps is the big question for not only us but school districts everywhere."

HEADLINE: Keansburg Journal;
Cheers for a Football Hero's Return

BYLINE: By ARTHUR Z. KAMIN

DATELINE: KEANSBURG

 BODY:
JONATHAN EDWARDS SCHULTHEIS is back at Keansburg High School, and people here view his return as a harbinger of good things to come.
They remember him when he was the school's leading football star, back in the late 1970's, and then the only resident of this mile-square borough of 11,000 people to graduate from Princeton University. They remember that he turned down a pro football offer to pursue a career as a teacher, and to keep his Sundays for the Pentecostal Church. 
 Now the 6-foot-3-inch, 31-year-old native son is coaching his old school's football team, and residents see that as a hopeful sign for the borough's efforts to rebuild its image and economy.
"It's been an enormous shot in the arm" for the borough, the school and the students, said Pat Antonacci, who has been teaching social studies and psychology at the high school for 17 years and is also Keansburg's Deputy Mayor.
"We're working hard to bring young families into Keansburg," Mr. Antonacci said. "We're meeting with real-estate people so they know what a bargain Keansburg homeownership is. Our taxes are stabilizing. Our real-estate values are starting to increase. When someone like Jon Schultheis commits himself to this town, it builds up our self-image and our self-esteem."
Police Chief Raymond O'Hare, whose son Bryan plays on the high school team, said: "The kids know who Jon is, and they have great respect for his ability. He is a no-nonsense disciplinarian. He runs a tight ship. And he is making our job easier."
 
A District in Need of Help
 Keansburg, where the spindly frame of the amusement park roller coaster and the water slide dominate the scene along Raritan Bay, needs whatever help it can get. The welfare rolls have been climbing, and increasing numbers of homeless people have been seeking shelter. The school district receives aid under the Quality Education Act as one of the state's 30 poorest districts.
Three years ago, the borough flirted briefly with a major economic revival plan that called for building a giant condominium, conference center and marina project called Point Atlantic. Valued at about $200 million, it was to be put up on the site of the amusement park. But the recession brought the project to a halt.
Now the Mayor and the Borough Council plan to seek state Green Acres financing to help revitalize the waterfront with a park and a boardwalk that would provide vistas of the New York City skyline.
At Keansburg High School, Mr. Schultheis, the son of the Rev. James F. Schultheis and the Rev. Grace Schultheis, co-pastors of the Keansburg Pentecostal Church, was an honor student as well as a sports hero. At Princeton, he majored in history, joined the exclusive Cottage Club and was a lineman on the varsity football team.
Princeton gave him "a freedom and a depth of knowledge" he had never known before, Mr. Schultheis said, though it was also "much too liberal for my taste." He does not drink, smoke or use profanity. He was active in the Fellowship of Christian Athletes at Princeton.
Even when the football team played out of town, he skipped the postgame parties, piled into his car and returned to Keansburg to play the organ for the Sunday service. "My parents needed me, and so did the church," he said. "I couldn't let them down."
After graduating from Princeton in 1983, Mr. Schultheis was offered $40,000 to sign with the Philadelphia Eagles and $80,000 to $100,000 a season after that. But he had never played football in high school or college on Sunday, and he was not about to change. Sundays were for church.
"I did what I felt was right," Mr. Schultheis said. "I never had any regrets." He makes $4,300 a year coaching at Keansburg.
He continues to serve the 150-member church as choir director, Bible teacher for adults and Sunday school teacher for children. He sometimes leads services here and at a satellite church in Spotswood, in Middlesex County. Such church experience will give him the training necessary to be approved by a Pentecostal board and ordained as aPentecostal minister, which is his goal.
As he did while a student at Princeton, Mr. Schultheis works summers as a stagehand. "The money is good," he said, adding that he felt that his union card brought him closer to many of the working people in Keansburg.
His main occupation, though, is as a social studies teacher at the nearby Holmdel Intermediate School. Before taking the coaching job in Keansburg, he had been an assistant football coach at Holmdel High School.
He lives with his parents in Keansburg.
 
'Coach Has Done a Lot for Us'
 Members of the borough's football team speak highly of their new coach. "Coach has done a lot for us," said Steve Boyton, the team's co-captain and a fullback. "We respect him. We know how smart he is. He tells us we are the best -- and we believe him."
Keansburg has come through with a winning regular season, though it lost to Dunellen in the state football playoffs. Ray Coleman, the athletic director at Keansburg, said the season was "highly successful" and the program was getting "better and better."
The team's other co-captain, Russell Gogan, an offensive guard, said Mr. Schultheis was working to build the team members' academic qualities. There are study halls, for example, before practice sessions, with tutors for those who need extra help. "Coach is setting high standards for us," Mr. Gogan said. "He tells us not to let anyone say anything bad about Keansburg."
Mr. Schultheis will not tolerate profanity, from players or coaches. "Say something vulgar or make a profane gesture, and that means 25 laps or 25 pushups," said Paul Markulin, a senior and backup quarterback.
Kerri O'Keefe, a senior, said the Schultheis "role model" was spreading through the school. She added: "Many of us are now saying to ourselves, 'He's from Keansburg and he made it.' He's become an inspiration."
That's just what the Schools Superintendent, Joseph Caruso, likes to hear. A Keansburg native who also played on the line in football, Mr. Caruso said Mr. Schultheis's efforts "have been contagious." He added: "Jon is helping to set high standards not only for the athletes but for the whole school. And Princeton is a part of it. Many of our kids used to think of lesser schools after graduation -- mainly because of finances. Now their sights have been raised. They know they can accomplish more."
Alice Kumanov has been teaching at the 400-student Keansburg High School for almost 26 years, and she remembers Mr. Schultheis as her best German scholar ever. "He was exceptional," she said.
But she also had praise for her other students over the years. "They were not born with silver spoons in their mouths," she said. "They work for what they get." She also likes to point to the first-place finishes by Keansburg students in high school German competitions at Rutgers University.
"Our students need help and they need motivation," she said. "But we're fortunate to have a fine, dedicated faculty at Keansburg. It is important that our students feel good about themselves and their town. Jonathan Schultheis is helping us to do that now, and it is working in a very real way."

SECTION: Section 13CN; Page 1; Column 5; Connecticut Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 1556 words

HEADLINE: Foreclosures on Rise While Prices Still Falter

BYLINE: By ROBERT A. HAMILTON

 BODY:
WHILE most of the Connecticut economy languishes, one sector is thriving. Lawyers, auctioneers, real estate agents and others involved in foreclosures are doing a booming business.
Four years ago, when real estate prices in Connecticut were at an all-time high, about 20 mortgages of every 1,000 were in foreclosure. But as real estate prices plummeted and many people lost their jobs, foreclosures have now soared to 159 for every 1,000 mortgages, the fourth highest rate in the nation, according to the Mortgage Bankers Association. 
 But the worst might be over, accoring to Thomas E. Hylinski, first vice president at People's Bank in Bridgeport and a vice president of the Connecticut chapter of the Mortgage Bankers Association. He said that in the last few months he has seen a decline in the number of delinquencies: at the start of the third quarter in July, 30-day delinquencies were down 10 percent and 90-day delinquencies were down 30 percent.
"The fact is, the loss of jobs has stopped growing, things seem to be getting better," Mr. Hylinski said. Others aren't so sure. Jeffrey L. Briggs, senior vice president of loan services for Centerbank Mortgage Company, a subsidiary of the Waterbury-based Centerbank, said the things that were driving the process -- layoffs, wage and benefit concessions, among them -- were still plaguing Connecticut. As more people lose their homes, there are fewer people to buy the houses coming on the market through foreclosure and normal turnover, he said. That means the people and banks trying to sell must reduce the price even further, putting more mortgages at risk.
In better times, those who could no longer afford their mortgage payments could sell the property and move to less expensive quarters, he said.
"That option has diminished," Mr. Briggs said. "And until that changes, the experts' predictions that prices are going to continue to fall will probably hold true."
John M. Clapp, a professor of finance and real estate in the Center for Real Estate and Urban Economic Studies at the University of Connecticut, predicted that housing prices would remain depressed for a long time because the high foreclosure rate has produced a greater number of bargain properties on the market now, and because the vacancy rate in existing housing was high.
He expects that foreclosures will not stop until employers realize that they have downsized too much and they start hiring again, and when the housing stock that is out there is absorbed. "I've heard some predictions of 1997, and that seems to make sense," he said.
According to a study Dr. Clapp made of Connecticut real estate prices, prices peaked in the third quarter of 1988, when a house that would have cost $100,000 in late 1981 would have brought $248,200, a 150 percent increase over seven years. Since then, prices have fallen more than 22 percent, so that same house would cost $190,700 today, still 90 percent more expensive than 11 years ago, but representing millions of dollars in lost equity for those who bought four years ago.
In fact, a buyer who put only a modest down payment on a house a few years ago would now owe the bank more than the house is worth, Dr. Clapp noted.
That means that when a homeowner loses a job or takes a pay cut and must to move out of the house, he or she cannot afford to sell. As a result, the bank often winds up owning the property through foreclosure.
 
Cutting Lender Losses
 Mr. Briggs said that Connecticut's high foreclosure rate did not even take into account some new policies that have been designed during the recession to prevent foreclosures, even if the bank lost money. For example, most banks now have programs to authorize sales that will not satisfy the mortgage, because the real estate problem is so severe. "The lender agrees to accept less than the amount which is owed, but over all the amount of money the lender is going to lose is less than it would lose if it didn't allow the sale to take place," Mr. Briggs said.
"This is not like a recession where in a couple of weeks things could turn around and start to get better," Mr. Briggs said. "This is a restructuring. Connecticut's economy has been defense-oriented so long, and it's got to find new directions. In the meantime, we've got to adjust."
The only lenders who are not having trouble are those that offer government subsidies. John T. Cooper, housing chief for the Farmers Home Administration in Connecticut said that only 58 of the 2,618 mortgages his agency wrote in the state in the last 30 years were in foreclosure, which was an increase over the last three years but still less than 15 percent of the foreclosure rate on privately financed homes.
 
Interest as Low as 1 Percent
 For a couple of years, the Farmers Home Administration was prohibited, by court order, from foreclosing on homes. The agency, which makes mortgage loans to low and moderate income families, can offer a moratorium on payments, or reduce the interest to as low as 1 percent, for people who have financial problems.
"For anyone who's willing to work with us, we're able to offer a lot of programs and benefits that other lenders can't," Mr. Cooper said.
But other lenders are seeing troubling numbers. Presently, only Massachusetts, New Jersey and Oklahoma have higher foreclosure rates than Connecticut. The foreclosure rate, once the envy of the nation, is now 50 percent higher than the national average of 104 for every 1,000 mortgages.
Larry Moore, a spokesman for the state Judicial Department, said the foreclosures have meant an increasingly heavy burden for the courts. The number of actions has risen steadily in the last four years, from 3,482 in the 1987-88 fiscal year, to 5,376 the next year, to 9,248 in 1989-90, to 13,422 in 1990-91, and to 14,054 in the fiscal year ended in June.
 
One Day of Court's Week
 Frances Z. Calafiore, deputy director of court operations, said that in most jurisdictions judges have set aside one day of the court's weekly calendar to hear foreclosure cases.
"Housing cases, particularly foreclosures, are driving up the number of cases on the docket throughout the state," Ms. Calafiore said.
Jay B. Weintraub, a West Hartford attorney who often handles foreclosure cases, said the increase has been significant.
"Go over to the court any Monday morning and there may be 500 or 100 cases on the docket," Mr. Weintraub said. "A few years ago it would have been one-third that number."
Mr. Weintraub said that in years past the judge might order a court auction of real estate that was in foreclosure. But today, because mortgage balances often exceed the value of the property, judges are more likely to try to work out an arrangement for private sale, he noted.
"We found the auctions were bringing about 30 percent less than even the most recent appraisals, and that's if anyone bid at all," Mr. Weintraub said. "A very, very small percentage have any equity at all."
 
Mass Auctions
 Still, some banks are resorting to mass auctions to unload some of the properties they have amassed through foreclosure.
Daniel E. Anderson, sales manager for Petrowsky Auctioneers Inc. in Norwich, which this month auctioned more than 50 properties for two New London County banks, said the price a property brought depended largely on its condition and location.
"Some of the returns from auctions are darn close to what the same properties would bring at retail -- good stuff brings good money," Mr. Anderson said. "But everyone attends an auction with the thought of getting a real bargain in the back of their mind."
The traditional method of auction is to advertise and market each house separately, which is a tremendous investment of time and money, Mr. Anderson said, particularly if a bank has dozens of foreclosed properties.
"The auction is the only way to put 50 or 100 properties together and market them and still get some attention," Mr. Anderson said. "Auction advertisements generate a lot more public interest. It brings the bank real close to the customer and it gets things done quickly."
But Dr. Clapp criticized that kind of mass sale as one of the reasons prices were depressed.
 
Short-Sighted Banks
 "Banks are short-sighted to sell off these properties," Dr. Clapp said. "They'd be better off to manage them and hold onto them until they can get fundamental value."
Mr. Hylinski of People's Bank, however, said banks were often driven by their own financial need, and even when they employed auctions they offered houses that were spread out over several towns. "If they're all located in one neighborhood that would have a significant impact on the market," he said. "But typically the auctions are pretty diverse -- one condominium in this complex, two more in a complex a couple of towns away -- so the effect hasn't been that dramatic."
Still, housing prices in Connecticut have plunged much faster than in neighboring states, and more steeply than at the national level, but then, Dr. Clapp noted, prices in Connecticut also rose more quickly during the mid-1980's boom.
"There is a bright spot in this, in that housing is more affordable in Connecticut, more competitive with neighboring states," Dr. Clapp said. "But the problem is, people still need a job to afford any housing."

SECTION: Section 13WC; Page 1; Column 1; Westchester Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 1343 words

HEADLINE: Unions Brace for Fight Over County Contracts

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER

DATELINE: WHITE PLAINS

 BODY:
UNION leaders furious over the Board of Legislators' refusal to ratify their contracts -- say their rank and file feel like pawns in a power struggle, betrayed by government leaders who "sat back in their ivory tower and played with other people's lives."
"It's not fair," said Thomas F. O'Neill, president of the Corrections Officers Benevolent Association. "You're not only dealing with people's money but with their emotions, too." 
 Early in September, the Board of Legislators, claiming that fiscal belt-tightening in a tough economic climate was in order, voted no on 1992 contracts calling for pay raises for four unions representing workers in Westchester. The Board's vote came even though the contracts had been negotiated with County Executive Andrew P. O'Rourke and his negotiator, Michael W. Wittenberg, director of the county's Labor Relations Department.
In addition to the corrections officers, the unions affected by the decision of the Board of Legislators are the Westchester units of the New York State Nurses Association and the New York State United Teachers and the Superior Officers Association of the county's Corrections Department.
Although Mr. O'Neill said that Westchester "has never been more anti-union than it is today," the situation is not restricted to the county. Budget problems are generating union conflicts between public-sector workers and their employers statewide, said Richard A. Curreri, director of conciliation for the State Public Employment Relations Board, which has received complaints from three of the four unions that did not have their contracts ratified.
 
Board Is Standing Firm
 "I've seen more impasses than at any time since 1983," Mr. Curreri said. "It's happening not just in the state, but all over the country."
But the Board of Legislators is standing firm on its decision, saying that its vote was not only legal but fiscally necessary.
The chairman of the Board of Legislators, Stephen P. Tenore, said he was confident that the State Public Employment Relations Board would decide that the legislative body acted legally.
"Our job is to ratify or deny a contract," Mr. Tenore said. "Hopefully, this will lead to a greater understanding that the Board of Legislators is an independent and co-equal branch of government with the right to vote a contract up or down."
Mr. O'Rourke said he agreed that the County Board was an independent body with the right to ratify or turn down contracts. He also said that when negotiating with the unions, "nothing is written in stone" until the Board puts its final stamp of approval on the contract.
Mr. O'Rourke added, "I know people in the private sector who have gone years without a pay raise."
 
Unions Willing to Fight
 Nevertheless, union leaders in Westchester -- armed with their anger and the feeling that they have been betrayed by government leaders-- say they are willing to fight over the issue of contract agreements that were made in good faith with Mr. O'Rourke and Mr. Wittenberg.
They claim that the County Board's 13-to-2 vote to renege on those agreements was illegal, and that they are prepared to do whatever it takes to overturn that decision.
The teachers union, for one, has filed an improper-practices charge with the State Public Employment Relations Board in Albany. "We are arguing that we had a valid agreement with the County Executive and that the legislators did not have the independent authority to vitiate that agreement," said Susan Bloom Jones, a labor-relations specialist for the teachers.
That union, which represents 500 teachers at Westchester Community College in Valhalla, is taking the position that the County Charter does not give the legislators the right to undermine an agreement made with a county executive.
 
'Core of This Whole Issue'
 "That is the core of this whole issue," said Richard J. Rosell, president of the community college teachers union and an associate professor of social and behavioral sciences. "The Board of Legislators doesn't want the authority to negotiate a contract. It just wants the authority to turn down the contract after the fact."
The nurses union, too, has filed a complaint with the Employment Relations Board, also claiming that the Board of Legislators does not have the authority to reject a union contract that has already been agreed upon.
"The County and the Board of Legislators should have had all their ducks in order," said Thomas W. Darby, negotiator for the 1,300 nurses in Westchester who are public-sector employees. "We negotiated a contract; we came to an agreement, and that contract was ratified by the nurses.
"Now the Board says that's too bad. It shows a complete ignorance of public-sector bargaining."
 
'We Have to Fight Back'
 The corrections officers union, which represents 950 employees, was found guilty on Nov. 10 by a judge in State Supreme Court in Westchester County of being involved in a work slowdown, but that decision has been stayed by the Appellate Division in Brooklyn. The corrections officers, too, are seeking assistance from the Public Employment Relations Board.
The Superior Officers Association, which represents 125 employees, has not taken any action on the matter.
Whatever the legalities of the Board's decision to turn down the contracts, union representatives claim that at the very least, their rank and file have been caught in the middle of a budgetary tug of war between the Legislature and Mr. O'Rourke, a power struggle that has left them in the lurch.
"We've been pushed over the edge," Mr. O'Neill said, "and now we have to fight back."
 
Keeping Faith With the Taxpayers
 The County Board, however, intends to stand by its decision.
"Nobody likes to turn down a union contract, but we have to keep faith with the taxpayers," said County Legislator Audrey G. Hochberg, a Democrat from Scarsdale.
"These are hard times," she said. "We are not stubbornly doing something just to be difficult."
Sandra R. Galef, a Democrat from Ossining, said the Board of Legislators was being more fiscally realistic than Mr. O'Rourke. The County Executive, she recalled, said at the beginning of the year that there was no money for raises, and later recanted, saying $20 million in surplus funds was available.
"But that's only 2 percent out of a $1.2 billion budget," Ms. Galef added. "And it is a bad move to use a surplus. We had to put our collective feet down and say no."
"I admit this is a kind of power struggle," she added. "But we're not doing it to go against Mr. O'Rourke. We just can't spend money when there isn't any."
 
May Be a No-Win Situation
 One of the two legislators who voted to ratify the union contracts, Leonard N. Spano, a Republican from Yonkers, called the Board's refusal to grant pay raises to the unions "demoralizing" and said that compared with other counties in the state, Westchester was in good shape financially.
Watching from the sidelines and looking forward to contract negotiations for the coming year, a spokeswoman for another union said she was afraid those negotiations would not be made in good faith by the county. Mr. O'Rourke said recently that there would be no pay increases in the 1993 budget.
"That's very discouraging," said Anita I. Manley, who represents 6,500 laborers, janitors, public health nurses and Social Services Department workers, among others, in the county's unit of the Civil Service Employees Association, Westchester's largest municipal union.
"All we're asking is that the negotiators sit down with an open mind, ready to negotiate with honorable intent," she said. "But obviously they can't do that if their minds are already made up."
Despite that union's concerns, Mr. Wittenberg said he would go into any future negotiations "in good faith" and bargain hard for fair contracts.
Whatever the outcome of the four unions' objections to the Board of Legislators' vote, it may be a no-win situation, the Legislators said. If pay raises are granted, jobs will have to be cut.

LENGTH: 1512 words

HEADLINE: The Smart Club Comes to the White House

BYLINE: By Nicholas Lemann; Nicholas Lemann, a national correspondent of The Atlantic, is working on a book about meritocracy in the United States.

DATELINE: PELHAM, N.Y.

 BODY:
We've been reminded over and over that Bill Clinton will be the first President born after World War II and that he represents the coming to power of a new generation. True enough, but new generations come to power all the time. Another Clinton first is more important: he is the first product of the American meritocracy to reach the White House.
Only two or three times in a century does a new elite take broad control of our country. The Clinton inaugural could mark one of those times -- but it's by no means a certainty, because the meritocracy, though triumphant at the moment, is in a precarious position. 
 By meritocracy, I mean a kind of national personnel system that uses higher education to sort and slot a substantial portion of the population. Meritocrats are successful people whose first big break was getting into the right school and whose career paths since then have been fairly orderly. The evolution of the meritocracy, during and after World War II, had the effect of making routine the eternal unruly American obsession with personal ambition.
It's still pretty unruly out there for entrepreneurs and actors. Some people still have success conferred on them as a birthright, and millions have no realistic chance of getting ahead. But the surest, most predictable and often traveled avenue of upward mobility is the higher-education system. That's why getting into and paying for college and graduate school is such an obsession of the middle class.
The meritocracy has become so pervasive and so well established that it's worth reminding ourselves that the Presidents up to now didn't have much to do with it. If they went to Ivy League schools (Franklin D. Roosevelt, John F. Kennedy, George Bush), it was not because they had been plucked, Cinderella-style, out of virtuous obscurity by the admissions office but because they were, like most Ivy Leaguers before 1950, rich kids who had been enrolled in Dad's alma mater. Harry S. Truman didn't go to college at all, and Lyndon B. Johnson, Richard Nixon and Ronald Reagan went to obscure local undergraduate schools and got on a track to power later on.
The most meritocratic modern Presidents were military academy graduates, Dwight D. Eisenhower and Jimmy Carter, but for them college was the doorway only into a narrow elite, the officer corps, not the common run of leadership positions.
Bill Clinton and Hillary Clinton will be the first occupants of the White House who truly meet the big break test: the day that fat letter from an elite college (Georgetown and Wellesley, respectively) arrived in their middle class, middle-of-the-country mailboxes was the day the trajectory of their lives changed dramatically.
The Clintons met and courted on the campus of the elite Yale Law School; as the sociologist E. Digby Baltzell has observed, for meritocrats the dean of admissions functions as a de facto marriage broker.
Mr. Baltzell called America's previous elite the Protestant Establishment: it was almost entirely WASP and quasi-hereditary. George Bush grew up in the bosom of the Protestant Establishment (he met his wife at a country club), and during the 1992 Presidential campaign you could almost feel every last remaining shred of the immense deference, trust and respect his class once commanded being blown away. The sense that people like Mr. Bush cannot be trusted to look after the interests of ordinary Americans was palpable. Mr. Clinton, the meritocrat, had much more of the log-cabin President quality than Mr. Bush did.
Since Bill and Hillary Clinton stand at the head of a substantial class of people who, since graduating from school, have risen to the top of a wide range of institutions, it's tempting to regard the ascendancy of the meritocracy as a sort of "end of history" of American elites. The system that churns these people out is firmly in place. They're smart and capable. They seem to control everything. What could possibly go wrong for them?
Actually, a lot. Because of the strength of the populist strain in the American character, the power of any elite is automatically at risk. Each succeeding elite, from the Virginia squirearchy to the robber barons to the Protestant Establishment, has seen itself as having risen in the fairest system ever devised, and has expected the admiration of the rest of the nation. Instead, the rest of the nation has eventually turned resentful and the elite has been unseated. There is no reason why the meritocracy would be immune to this.
In particular, the meritocrats are expected to deliver two elusive and possibly incompatible social benefits at the same time: equity and efficiency. The meritocracy took form during a period of high democratic spirit and economic expectation; therefore, it is imbued with the idea that it is supposed to provide every American with a genuine opportunity to get ahead, and also to keep the country on an upward path of growth. The original theory was that if we opened up the leadership ranks to everyone on the basis of talent and ability, rather than background, naturally the people who rose to the top would do a great job of running the country.
But today the equity and efficiency of the meritocracy are in question. Perceptions of the fairness of the education system vary widely from class to class, ethnic group to ethnic group and region to region. The basic tools of the meritocracy, standardized tests and admissions offices, have become controversial. Public schools for the poor have lost any reputation they may once have had for being engines of upward mobility; and, at the other end of the system, the ability of colleges to admit students without regard to their parents' financial situation has eroded.
All in all, the meritocracy is getting to the point where it is regularly accused of being a disguised aristocracy. Remember that the Clinton campaign's polling last spring showed that people thought the Governor had grown up rich, simply because he had gone to Georgetown, Oxford and Yale.
On the efficiency front, the meritocracy's problem is that its rise has coincided with a great stalling of the economy. The two phenomena may be unrelated, but it doesn't help the meritocracy's image as a promoter of economic growth that a strong plurality of the graduates of the very best colleges go into law, medicine, investment banking and management consulting. Robert Reich, one of President-elect Clinton's chief economic advisers, once memorably insulted the meritocrats by accusing them of being "pie-slicers" rather than "pie-enlargers."
Bill Clinton will have the power to shore up substantially the system that made his own rise to the Presidency possible. To do so he has to accomplish two goals. First is the obvious one of getting the economy moving again. Second, and more difficult, is re-establishing the linchpin of the meritocracy's legitimacy -- the link between education and individual possibility.
During the campaign, Mr. Clinton repeatedly sketched a vision of a society in which every schoolchild would get the basic skills necessary to make a living and any adolescent who wanted access to higher education (meaning, presumably, higher success) could have it. Nothing would insure the survival of the meritocracy better than his doing this.
"The system" is a vague term. But when things aren't going well, Americans are very good at making it specific. When the system seems not to be working, it quickly becomes identified with a group or a social process to be gotten rid of. Under President Clinton, meritocracy will be the obvious candidate.
During the campaign, the Republicans tried to foster resentment against the meritocracy. President Bush said that under Bill Clinton we'd be ruled by "the best and the brightest" -- not meaning that as a compliment. Vice President Dan Quayle said, "They think they're smarter than us." And it's no accident that the draft issue kept popping up all year: the basic sorting techniques of the meritocracy were invented partly as a way of keeping the smartest people out of the line of fire during wartime.
Now that the meritocrats are about to run the country, there will surely be more of these attacks. They had their least possible potency coming from the Bush Administration, which represented an elite far more unpopular than the meritocracy.
The real competitor elite to the meritocracy is a group composed of rich, self-made Sun Belt entrepreneurs such as Ross Perot and the men who got Ronald Reagan into politics. They can say, "We may not have gone to Ivy League schools, but we understand ordinary folks and basic economics better than the people who did."
Like every Administration, Bill Clinton's will soon find itself nearly overwhelmed with specific issues. When election year comes around again, though, they will recede in importance and larger themes will come forward. You can bet that the theme of meritocracy -- the performance of the elite Mr. Clinton belongs to, especially in education and economic management -- will be one of them.
