PUB-DATE: November 29, 1992

SECTION: Section 7; Page 3; Column 1; Book Review Desk

LENGTH: 5216 words

HEADLINE: Who Speaks for Malcolm X? The Writings of Just About Everybody

BYLINE: By Michael Eric Dyson; Michael Eric Dyson, a professor of American civilization and Afro-American studies at Brown University, is the author of the forthcoming "Reflecting Black: African-American Cultural Criticism."

 BODY:
IN "The Autobiography of Malcolm X," the charismatic black religious nationalist recalls his momentous 1964 pilgrimage to Mecca, a visit that would alter the course of his life and career. For 12 years this minister of the Nation of Islam had been trumpeting a doctrine of the intrinsic evil of white people, likening the dream of American equality to a nightmare for American blacks and championing a plan to redeem black Americans by saving them from the tide of brainwashing that had drowned awareness of the black race's true superiority. But now Malcolm X writes of an incident in Jedda, in which he is treated with great hospitality by a man who, in the United States, would be considered white.
"That white man, . . . related to Arabia's ruler, to whom he was a close advisor, truly an international man, with nothing in the world to gain, had given up his suite to me, for my transient comfort. . . . He had followed the American press about me. If he did that, he knew there was only stigma attached to me. I was supposed to have horns. I was a 'racist.' I was 'anti-white.' . . . Everyone was even accusing me of using his religion of Islam as a cloak for my criminal practices and philosophies. . . . 
 "That morning was when I first began to reappraise the 'white man.' It was when I first began to perceive that 'white man,' as commonly used, means complexion only secondarily; primarily it described attitudes and actions. In America, 'white man' meant specific attitudes and actions toward the black man, and toward all other nonwhite men. But in the Muslim world, I had seen that men with white complexions were more genuinely brotherly than anyone else had ever been.
"That morning was the start of a radical alteration in my whole outlook about 'white men.' "
Malcolm's life-changing encounters were recorded in letters from Africa to family members and other followers in the United States. The revealing missives detailed Malcolm X's view of the color blindness of Muslim society and religion. This series of experiences forced Malcolm X to "rearrange much of my thought-patterns previously held, and to toss aside some of my previous conclusions."
Less than a year later, Malcolm X was gunned down by assassins in New York's Audubon Ballroom. By the time of his martyrdom, he had seceded from the Nation of Islam (after bitter disappointment in the moral failures of its leader, Elijah Muhammad, and the tensions within the sect concerning Malcolm X's own increased public presence) and begun two organizations, one religious, the other political. Each group reflected Malcolm X's transformed perspective about racial and religious matters, especially his belief that broad social engagement permitted blacks their best chance to oppose the lethal legacy of American racism.
It is around Malcolm's increasingly independent political activity during the final 50 weeks of his life, combined with his startling reversal of feeling about the possibility of redemption for white America, that so much controversy and confusion have gathered. Although Malcolm X made many overtures to a broader philosophy of human community, he simply did not live long enough to fulfill the promise of his significant yet tentative first steps. While we may conclude with certainty that Malcolm X had rejected the whites-are-devils pronouncements that helped to focus his earlier life and brought him the attention and vilification of a nation, we are brought up short when trying to deal definitively with the universal humanitarianism of his latter days.
Though Malcolm X's legacy until recently has been demonized and dismissed by the traditional academy, his popularity never flagged among a cadre of black nationalist activists, journalists and independent intellectuals who for a quarter-century have debated intensely his significance to black politics, black culture and American society. These debates have had a trickle-up effect; they created the grounds for the current war of interpretation being waged over Malcolm's meaning in our time.
IN the face of the grim recurrence of a racism many thought was gone forever, the renewed popularity of Malcolm X -- both his image and his ideology -- has taken on new importance. The signs of his ascent -- from posters, ubiquitous baseball hats and samplings of his voice on rap records, and the appearance of his reissued autobiography on the best-seller lists, to the recording of the opera "X," created by Anthony Davis and Thulani Davis; the recent release of the epic film biography directed by Spike Lee; and the forthcoming publication of his speeches, edited to reveal his ideological evolution -- all are a response to the current need, deeply felt by many, for a confrontational stance toward this country's continuing racism and the seductive mythology of the perfect black man.
Thus it is that Malcolm X's name no longer belongs to him, no longer refers simply to his tall body or to his short life. Like Martin Luther King Jr., Malcolm has come to mean more than himself. For some, he was an unreconstructed nationalist; for others, a man who wed his nationalist beliefs to socialist philosophy. Still others subject him to Marxist and Freudian analysis, while others emphasize his vocation as a public moralist. No matter how he is pigeonholed, his stature derives as much from his detractors' exaggerated fears as from his admirers' exalted hopes. He has become a divided metaphor: for those who love him, he is a powerful lens for self-perception, a means of sharply focusing political and racial priorities; for those who loathe him, he is a distorting mirror that reflects violence and hatred.
Malcolm X himself anticipated this confusion. When he announced his new beliefs he had not fully gauged the disquiet they would cause among supporters already thrown by his earlier break with the Nation of Islam. Among its powerful dramatis personae -- its leader, Elijah Muhammad; the world-champion boxer Muhammad Ali and Louis X, now Minister Louis Farrakhan, Malcolm's associate turned enemy -- it was Malcolm who had emerged as the major player in the Nation of Islam's dramatic attempts to rescue black Americans from what they saw as the bankrupt religious and social values of white society.
Yet Malcolm X has received nothing like the intellectual attention devoted to King. As the central figure of the civil rights movement, King justifiably has been the subject of extensive scholarship, but his cultural visibility also has to do with the style, content and aims of his leadership, which for most of his life were both easily translatable and largely attractive to white America. On the other hand, Malcolm X's complex leadership positions, which visited rhetorical scorn on white supremacy and which appealed especially to working-class and poor black people, have typically invited derision, caricature and dismissal -- forces that undermine extensive and balanced scholarly investigation.
To be sure, a flood of writings about Malcolm X's legacy has been published over the years in alternative and black newspapers, journals, pamphlets and books; they have been vigorously discussed throughout this country's black communities. (Nearly two dozen books about Malcolm, ranging from biographies for young adults to collections of his writings, have been reissued or published for the first time this year.) Yet with notable exceptions, both mainstream and alternative literature on Malcolm X has often missed the mark, offering praise where critical judgment is called for, trapping itself in intellectual frameworks that neither illuminate nor surprise.
Of course, "The Autobiography of Malcolm X," as told to Alex Haley, is the Urtext of contemporary black nationalism. Activists and intellectuals tote it in their back pockets and briefcases for ready reference in debates about black America, while rappers imitate its radical tones and students often quote it as scripture. As Malcolm X's faithful and creative scribe -- he had written two articles about Malcolm and interviewed him for Playboy magazine when a publisher tapped him to write the autobiography in 1963 -- Haley placed his considerable narrative skills at Malcolm's disposal. It was Haley who brought Malcolm's life into full color, crafting a classic of African-American letters. But as Malcolm opened his life to Haley, he was also reinventing it on the spot. Malcolm was improvising a personal narrative that drew from both the virtues and failures of memory. But the lapses in accuracy that he exhibited were often rooted less in mendacity than in the human need to tell stories in ways that make our lives make sense.
Despite the crucial role of his autobiography in expanding black cultural consciousness, the full meaning of Malcolm X's life inevitably must be judged using more than a single source. Thus it is especially important now to explore a select group of books published over the past two decades that offer widely divergent views of Malcolm X's complex political journey.
In "Malcolm X: The Man and His Times," first published in 1969, the first anthology dedicated to exploring Malcolm's life and thought, John Henrik Clarke, a black historian and professor emeritus at Hunter College, has edited a collection of pieces that undermine a single, unified understanding of Malcolm's place in history. What many of the essays share is a reverential regard for his black nationalist legacy.
In his contribution to this collection of 34 essays, interviews and organizational statements, the black theologian Albert Cleage (who knew and worked with Malcolm X) boldly defends Malcolm's black nationalist reputation by concluding that "if in Mecca he had decided that blacks and whites can unite, then his life at that moment would have become meaningless in terms of the world struggle of black people." Mr. Cleage seeks to rebut the contentions that Malcolm at the end of his life was becoming either an integrationist, an advocate of the internationalization of black struggle or a socialist.
In another essay from this anthology, the political theorist James Boggs criticizes Malcolm's black nationalist heirs, whom Mr. Boggs views as preoccupied with black-power sloganeering and lacking sufficient analytic depth. He also chastises "white radicals who lend a grudging support to black power," patronizing black revolutionaries as "unfinished products" who will one day see the light and recognize the superiority of Marxist theory and the necessity of an alliance with the white working class.
Charles E. Wilson insightfully discusses Malcolm's "failure of leadership style and a failure to evolve a sound organizational base for his activities," concluding that Malcolm was a "victim of his own charisma."
Mr. Clarke's anthology mattered: in the days following Malcolm X's death, its contributors addressed urban blacks and nationalist intellectuals confronting their deepening social crisis. It argued that black nationalism was an important and thriving alternative to black bourgeois protest, which held out as its goal black integration into white society. Mr. Clarke's book also countered the demonization of Malcolm. But its goal of redeeming Malcolm X's legacy rather than evaluating it lessens its value; the collection's tone suggests an exercise in beatification, with cultural interpreters working to preserve fragments of Malcolm's memory against abuse or amnesia.
FORTUNATELY, the very books that present Malcolm primarily in relation to his views on racism also supply a wealth of detail about his career and a framework for criticism. The black theologian James H. Cone's "Martin & Malcolm & America: A Dream or a Nightmare" treats the religious roots of Malcolm's moral vision; the journalist Louis E. Lomax's "To Kill a Black Man" addresses the social and political dimensions of Malcolm's moral perspective. But it is in their roles as comparative studies of King and Malcolm X that the two books seize our interest. Mr. Cone, a professor at Union Theological Seminary in New York, has been profoundly influenced by both King and Malcolm X, and his book is a public acknowledgment of intellectual debt and inspiration.
Many Americans believe that Malcolm X and King occupied violently opposed ethical universes, that their positions on the best solution to this country's racial crisis led them to a permanent parting of paths. More likely, though, they were the yin and yang of black moral responses to white racism, complementing more than contradicting each other in their last years. Mr. Cone even suggests that King and Malcolm X were in important ways converging, saying that "Malcolm and Martin moved away from the extremes of their original positions and began to embrace aspects of each other's viewpoints." Thus, King began in 1966 to emphasize black pride, explore the virtues of "temporary segregation" to foster the economic health of black communities, and became more radical about the limits of social protest. And Malcolm X for his part became publicly political, acknowledged the militancy of integrationists, and even encouraged voter registration.
Although King and Malcolm X met only once for a brief moment -- resulting in the famed photo of both figures smiling broadly at the Capitol in 1964 -- Malcolm did journey to Selma, Ala., in 1965 when King was in jail, to speak to civil rights workers before leaving for London. His remarks on that occasion to Coretta King -- less than a month before his death -- reveal his poignant awareness and acceptance of the distinct roles he and King played in the black freedom movement. "I want Dr. King to know that I didn't come to Selma to make his job difficult," he said. "I really did come thinking I could make it easier. If the white people realize what the alternative is, perhaps they will be more willing to hear Dr. King."
"To Kill a Black Man," written in the torturous aftermath of King's assassination in 1968, has all the virtues of historical immediacy -- impassioned narration, proximity to the feeling of the moment, unvarnished insight. What it lacks is the greater virtue of historical perspective, which comes from long and mature reflection upon events. This is the virtue that Mr. Cone's book, published in 1991, possesses, along with an analytical acumen in exploring King's and Malcolm X's lives and thought in larger scope and richer detail than many of the author's nationalist predecessors.
However, like them, Mr. Cone is plagued by excessive reliance on the theme of racial unity as an intellectual principle to explain his twin subjects' failures and achievements; this limits his understanding of King and Malcolm X and skews his view of their complex uses (especially King's) of history and culture. Nevertheless, Mr. Cone's book is an invaluable aid in comprehending the similarities and differences between the two towering influences on contemporary black culture.
Malcolm X lived for most of his life in opposition to the fundamental assumptions of American moral judgment: that innocence and corruption are on a continuum, that justice and injustice are on a scale and that proper moral behavior depends on making the right choices between existing options of good and evil. Such a vision is presented in "The Death and Life of Malcolm X," by Peter Louis Goldman. The author, a senior editor at Newsweek, met Malcolm in 1962 and spent hundreds of hours interviewing him. The book, published in 1973, captures with eloquence and imagination the forces of white racial oppression that made life hell for northern poor blacks, and the minimal resources apparently at their disposal before Malcolm's defiant rhetoric rallied black rage and anger to their defense.
Mr. Goldman's Malcolm is one whose "life was itself an accusation," a "witness for the prosecution" of white injustice, a "public moralist." With each aspect of Malcolm's life that Mr. Goldman treats -- whether it is Malcolm's foreshadowing of the forthcoming black power movement or his withering assaults on white society -- Mr. Goldman's narrative subtly and skillfully illumines Malcolm's career.
What the author fails to do is properly convey the mammoth scope of competing economic, cultural and historical forces that erupted during the 1940's, 1950's and 1960's, and that reflected not only racial antagonism but class conflict and sexual oppression as well. Nor does he present a sufficient analysis of the extreme political limitations placed on black militant men of either religious or secular sensibility. Still, Mr. Goldman's book, the first full-scale life of Malcolm X, remains an indispensable biography of the enigmatic leader.
If one task of biography is to cast as bright a light as possible over the shadowed areas of human behavior, then psychobiography doubles the effort, using the insights gleaned through psychological theory to illumine the full range of human experience. Over the last decade, several journal articles and two books have notably applied this approach to Malcolm X, with varying results. The social theorist and Marxist psychoanalyst Eugene V. Wolfenstein's 1981 study, "The Victims of Democracy: Malcolm X and the Black Revolution," is a work of considerable intellectual imagination and rigor that represents the best of this trend.
Mr. Wolfenstein takes ample measure of the energies that created Malcolm X and the demons that drove him. Recalling Malcolm's "earliest vivid memory" -- being traumatically awakened in the middle of the night in 1929 because the family house in Lansing, Mich., was burning to the ground -- Mr. Wolfenstein contends that Malcolm's "consciousness began in a moment of racist violence." Starting from that point, he brilliantly maps Malcolm's developing awareness both as a black man in a racist culture and as a member of a violent family. Mr. Wolfenstein says that Malcolm's father "made himself, by his own hand, both the defender and destroyer of his family."
In Mr. Wolfenstein's scheme, because Freudian theory "provides no foundation for the analysis of interests, be they individual or collective," and Marxism "provides no foundation for the analysis of desires," he uses both to explain Malcolm's life. But his book has deficiencies; besides his lack of clarity about the importance of religion in providing social cohesion among Nothern urban black communities, he focuses exclusively on biological definitions of race. Recent race theorists have maintained that race is not only a biological reality, but carries socially created meanings as well. Such an approach to race might have helped Mr. Wolfenstein explain how Malcolm and other blacks understood and employed race in their defense against white racism, and how rigid views of race held by black nationalists limited their range of social response.
Mr. Wolfenstein's major limitation lies in his excessive reliance on the autobiography for information about Malcolm. Nonetheless, Mr. Wolfenstein offers the most astute treatment to date of Malcolm's intellectual and psychological roots.
If Mr. Wolfenstein, despite his flaws, represents the best of the psychobiographical impulse, Bruce Perry's "Malcolm: The Life of a Man Who Changed Black America" (1991) may represent the worst. In Mr. Perry's eyes, Malcolm's troubled childhood holds the key to his subsequent career as a black leader. According to Mr. Perry, the physical abuse of his mother by Malcolm's father, his parents' extramarital affairs, the breakup of the family after the father's death and the mother's mental collapse, Malcolm's inheritance of his mother's obsession with color and his hatred of women, learned during childhood, all contributed to the mature Malcolm's contorted leadership style.
Mr. Perry says Malcolm's "war against the white power structure evolved from the same inner needs that had spawned earlier rebellions against his teachers, the law, established religion, and other symbols of authority." The author portrays Malcolm's family as besieged by unremitting violence, criminality and pathology. The mature Malcolm is equally tragic, a man of looming greatness whose self-destructive personality "contributed to his premature death."
TRUE enough, he does unearth new information about Malcolm. For instance, he reveals that in 1961 Malcolm X held a secret meeting with the Ku Klux Klan on Elijah Muhammad's behalf, seeking the Klan's aid (since both groups opposed racial mixing) in obtaining land for the Nation of Islam to implement its separatist philosophy. Mr. Perry also discusses Malcolm's alleged homosexual activity, both as an experimenting adolescent and as a hustling young adult "who sold himself, as if the best he had to offer was his body." But Mr. Perry's revelations are less striking for their threat to Malcolm's image as the quintessential black man than for the pedestrian interpretation he offers of Malcolm's homosexual motivations: absent male role models and a history of tyrannical females. 
Since Mr. Perry makes a plausible case for their occurrence, a discussion of Malcolm's homosexual alliances might have served as a powerful interpretive tool, a way of exploring and explaining the cultural weight of oppressive black machismo and the cruel varieties of homophobia that afflict black communities. Instead, Mr. Perry's treatment of Malcolm's sexuality fails to enlarge our understanding of black sexual politics or its relationship to social liberation.
Mr. Perry fills in details about Malcolm's last year, but again his tendentious reading of Malcolm's career mars his perspective. For instance, he concludes that Malcolm's forays onto foreign soil were an attempt to secure financial support for his fledgling organization. But this is only partly true; through his trips abroad, Malcolm X sought to expand the scope of his views about the black struggle as his philosophy took on international dimensions. And while Mr. Perry convincingly argues that Malcolm had forsaken his views about "devil" whites as early as 1959 (during his first trip to the Middle East and long before his trip to Mecca in 1964), he cynically concludes that Malcolm's press conference held at New York's Kennedy Airport after his return from Africa in May 1964 was a "fitting culmination" to a "public relations campaign."
While Malcolm X's last year was undoubtedly a year of sometimes frantic searching for the right road to take, Mr. Perry portrays it as a series of ploys to expand his support among nonblack audiences. Although the author concedes Malcolm's "extraordinary capacity for political ambiguity," he more often characterizes his subject as a "political chameleon." Mr. Perry's book finally impresses as little more than a sniping account that makes a complex figure smaller than life.
To fully grasp Malcolm X's complexity, it is necessary to probe his radical shift to a broader base of racial protest during his last year. Perhaps the most prominent and controversial interpreters of Malcolm's last year have been a group of intellectuals associated with the Socialist Workers Party, a Trotskyist group that took keen interest in Malcolm's views after his return from Mecca and sponsored some of his last speeches. For the most part, their views have been articulated by the social critic George Breitman, author of "The Last Year of Malcolm X: The Evolution of a Revolutionary" (1967) and editor of two volumes of Malcolm's speeches, organizational statements and interviews he gave during his last years, "Malcolm X Speaks" (1965) and "By Any Means Necessary" (1970).
Mr. Breitman passionately argues that Malcolm's split with Elijah Muhammad was a major shock, however inevitable. Thus Malcolm needed time to rethink his beliefs and determine his organizational direction. Mr. Breitman divides what he calls Malcolm's "brief independent phase" into two parts: a transition period, lasting the few months between the split in March 1964 and his return from Africa at the end of May of that year; and the final period, lasting from June 1964 until his death in February 1965.
Mr. Breitman maintains that this final period marked Malcolm's maturation as "a revolutionary -- increasingly anticapitalist and prosocialist as well as anti-imperialist," although Mr. Breitman acknowledges that Malcolm himself never adopted those labels. But the truth is that we have only the bare-bones outlines of Malcolm's emerging world view, including his views on socialism. In the speeches collected in these volumes, Malcolm X displays sympathy for socialist philosophy without committing himself to its practice as a means of achieving liberation for African-Americans. He confessed in an interview near the end of his life that "I still would be hard pressed to give a specific definition of the overall philosophy which I think is necessary for the liberation of the black people in this country." As a speculative study, Mr. Breitman's book is fascinating and provocative; as a definitive study of Malcolm's evolving intellectual positions, it is much less convincing. Malcolm X left behind fragments of political speech, not systematic social thought; suggestive ideological gestures more than substantive political activity. Mr. Breitman is attempting to saddle him with a set of views he did not live long enough to clarify or adopt.
With the rebirth of black nationalism since the early 1980's, placing Malcolm X in history has acquired renewed urgency, and might well serve to put into context the popularity of the Nation of Islam leader Minister Louis Farrakhan; the broad appeal and capacity to disturb that are inherent in rap music; the adoption of African hair styles, jewelry and clothing; the expression of Afrocentric ideas by scholars such as Molefi Asante, chairman of the African-American studies program at Temple University in Philadelphia, and Ivan Van Sertima, a professor of Afro-American studies at Rutgers University; and, of course, the cultural deluge that is Malcolmania.
Malcolm X is now a part of the American imagination that once relegated him to its margins. This transcendent status, however, does not free us from the obligation to demand more from our examination of the life that really was his. More than ever, we must forsake uncritical celebrations -- not by denying his myth but by taking it into account, by probing for the continuing wellsprings of his appeal and in so doing understand our need to romanticize or revile him. Battered by unprincipled opponents, smothered by well-meaning loyalists, Malcolm's past is not yet settled; it never will be. Nonetheless, he must in the end receive what every important historical figure deserves: a comprehensive examination of both word and deed. To do less would be to fail ourselves, and the history Malcolm X so boldly helped to create.
 
 
 
A MALCOLM X BOOKSHELF
 THE POLITICAL LEGACY OF MALCOLM X. By Oba T'Shaka. (Pan Afrikan Publications, 1983.) A clear exposition of Malcolm's political ideas as a revolutionary black nationalist. It is weak, however, as a critical examination of his thought.
MALCOLM X: SPEECHES AT HARVARD. Edited by Archie Epps. (Paragon House, 1991; previously published by Morrow in 1968 as THE SPEECHES OF MALCOLM X AT HARVARD.) Three speeches delivered by Malcolm at Harvard during the 1960's, edited and introduced by Archie Epps, who knew him. An imaginative introduction compares Malcolm's views to Shakespeare's plays, but almost swallows the speeches themselves.
THE ASSASSINATION OF MALCOLM X. By George Breitman, Herman Porter and Baxter Smith. (Pathfinder Press, third edition, 1991.) A collection of essays and book reviews, most of them by Mr. Breitman, that purport to prove a Government conspiracy to assassinate Malcolm.
MALCOLM X: THE FBI FILE. By Clayborne Carson. Edited by David Gallen. (Carroll & Graf, 1991.) Extensive excerpts from Malcolm X's F.B.I. files, arranged in 19 sections, provide invaluable information about his activities and progress as an activist and thinker. An excellent introduction argues that the files place Malcolm in the context of American racial politics of the 1950's and 60's.
THE BLACK MUSLIMS IN AMERICA. By C. Eric Lincoln. (Beacon Press, 1961; out of print.) The classic treatment of the Nation of Islam during the leadership of Elijah Muhummad and Malcolm X; unsurpassed as a sociological study of the sources of Black Muslim belief and practice.
BLACK NATIONALISM: A Search for an Identity in America. By E. U. Essien-Udom. (University of Chicago, 1962; out of print.) An excellent standard treatment of black nationalist belief and practice in the 50's, including an analysis of the Black Muslims.
WHEN THE WORD IS GIVEN: A Report on Elijah Muhammad, Malcolm X, and the Black Muslim Movement. By Louis E. Lomax. (Greenwood Press, 1963.) An informal and perceptive attempt by a journalist to unveil the mysterious shroud of religious rituals, puritanical behavior and unorthodox beliefs that have to this day intimidated and intrigued outsiders. The author's lucid prose and imaginative reporting evoke the electricity and immediacy of the events he describes.
MALCOLM X: IN OUR OWN IMAGE. Edited by Joe Wood. (St. Martin's, 1992.) A collection of essays by 14 black intellectuals that expands the critical dialogue about Malcolm X's legacy, and an excellent companion to John Henrik Clarke's more hagiographical collection.
MALCOLM X: A SELECTED BIBLIOGRAPHY. Compiled by Lenwood G. Davis with the assistance of Marsha L. Moore. (Greenwood Press, 1984) and MALCOLM X: A COMPREHENSIVE ANNOTATED BIBLIOGRAPHY. By Timothy V. Johnson. (Garland Publishing, 1986; out of print.) Two bibliographies that give an indication of the wide debate about Malcolm carried on in journals, magazines, books and the popular press (especially alternative news papers).
THE END OF WHITE WORLD SUPREMACY: Four Speeches by Malcolm X. Edited by Imam Benjamin Karim. (Arcade Publishing, 1971.) As a historical document that records Malcolm's thought during the crucial year preceding his departure from the Nation of Islam, these speeches are invaluable. However, the book is marred by the attempt of the editor (who served under Malcolm, and introduced him on the day of his assassination) to validate through Malcolm's words the views he lived to reject -- making the book an act of ironic ventriloquism achieved by editorial manipulation.
MALCOLM X: THE LAST SPEECHES. Edited by Bruce Perry. (Pathfinder, 1989.) A valuable collection of six speeches and interviews, including two delivered during the last week of Malcolm's life, that is especially helpful in documenting his maturation as a social activist and intellectual who began to view the problems of black Americans within an international framework.
BY ANY MEANS NECESSARY: The Trials and Tribulations of the Making of "Malcolm X." By Spike Lee with Ralph Wiley. (Hyperion, 1992.) The director of "Malcolm X" chronicles the obstacles he faced in completing the film; the book also contains insightful comments from the movie's principals (including the producers, cinematographer and actors) and the script, written by James Baldwin, Arnold Perl and Mr. Lee.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Malcolm X, right, with Elijah Muhammad, founder of the Nation of Islam, at a rally in New York in 1961. (Eve Arnold/Magnum)

SECTION: Section A; Page 14; Column 4; Editorial Desk

HEADLINE: Let's Reform Health Care Before It's Too Late

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Eli Ginzberg, who was kind enough to quote me in "The Health Swamp" (Op-Ed, Nov. 12), is right when he says that not everyone wants health care reform. Certainly, insurance companies and other powerful lobbies find the present system profitable enough. However, I think more Americans are dissatisfied with our system than Dr. Ginzberg suggests.
Every year thousands of families are impoverished by our health care system, yet few are getting their money's worth. Last year we spent more than $800 billion on health care, nearly half of it on administration and unnecessary tests and procedures. Our public hospitals are overburdened and will become more so as poverty, drugs and the AIDS epidemic take a further toll. 
 Many of our schoolchildren aren't ready to learn, in part because of poor or neglected health. Many adults who can afford to see a doctor can't find one because of an acute shortage of primary-care physicians in rural areas and inner cities, or they are disheartened by physician insensitivity or indifference.
At least 37 million people lack health insurance, a number bound to grow if unemployment increases. Medicare, which Dr. Ginzberg implies is chockablock with satisfied customers, falls far short of the real needs of our older population. Most of the elderly are not affluent -- the majority of women older than 65 have an income of less than $10,000 a year -- and even the poorest must spend their own money before Medicare kicks in. Even then, the system will not provide the medication or treatment many need to stay alive, and it makes no provision for long-term care or for avoiding or postponing nursing home costs by providing services to the elderly at home.
Medicaid, the other major government health insurance program, is even worse. It excludes most of the poor it is meant to serve by simply calling them too well off. Even those who qualify for Medicaid often find it does them little good: because the system pays physicians only a fraction of the real cost of treatment, more and more doctors refuse to see Medicaid patients.
It's no wonder then that, according to one national poll, 67 percent of those who voted for Bill Clinton ranked health care reform as their most pressing concern, ahead of education and jobs, and just behind the environment. How should the new administration begin the job of reforming our faltering health care system?
I have four modest suggestions. The first is to go slowly. The President-elect has said he will not be pressured into making hasty decisions on serious issues. I hope he maintains his resolve. America has voted for change, but not for haphazard change or for poorly drawn legislation that could take a generation to undo. Some adjustments, such as eliminating unnecessary paperwork, can be accomplished relatively quickly. However, more sweeping reforms, such as eliminating unnecessary tests and procedures, and overhauling the insurance industry, will take much longer -- a decade or more, even if everything goes smoothly.
Second, the new Administration should look to the states for guidance, rather than trying to impose its will on local jurisdictions through a national health board that would regulate the cost of everything from tongue depressors to bypass surgery. After 12 years as Governor of Arkansas, President-elect Clinton understands better than most the stresses state governments suffer when asked to absorb the economic and human cost of ill-conceived Federal mandates. He also knows that some of the most imaginative and effective contributions to health care reform have come from our states and cities, which have moved forward as our Federal Government fiddled.
Third, the President-elect needs to take the leadership in developing a national consensus on a plan of action. The most practical way is to appoint a bipartisan national health commission, representing doctors, patients, business, Congress, state and local governments and other interested groups to assess conflicting proposals, to choose the best and most balanced, to weigh and make clear the trade-offs, and to present a rational plan to Congress. The commission will succeed if its Congressional appointees commit themselves to the task, rather than delegating the work to their staffs, and if they take the proposals back to the House and Senate, lead the debate and enable the legislation. That's how we salvaged Social Security.
Finally, what the new administration needs most is the political will and the moral courage to challenge the interests that hold our health care system hostage. As Dr. Ginzberg notes, there's a crisis in the offing, and unless we reform the system now, before it reaches chaos or collapse, we will end up with government-run medicine, exorbitantly expensive and insensitive to patient needs, or have private medicine run amok.
 
C. EVERETT KOOP, M.D.
Senior Scholar, C. Everett Koop
Institute at Dartmouth
Hanover, N.H., Nov. 14, 1992

GRAPHIC: Drawing.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 6; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 1632 words

HEADLINE: TOUGHER MEASURES TO FIGHT TB URGED BY NEW YORK PANEL

BYLINE: By MICHAEL SPECTER

 BODY:
Wading into one of the most contentious issues in modern medicine, a panel of 34 scientists, ethicists and public health leaders yesterday released a report calling for broad new measures to stem the rampant spread of tuberculosis in New York City and around the nation.
Two of the panel's recommendations are likely to fuel angry policy debates: that all tuberculosis patients allow someone to watch them take their medication once they leave the hospital and that all patients receive treatment until they are completely cured rather than just no longer contagious. The recommendations would apply to all patients no matter what their economic background or the cause of their illness.
 
Curing the Sick
 In its report to the United Hospital Fund, a New York philanthropy that focuses on health-care issues, the panel also rejected controversial and growing demands for mandatory AIDS testing, saying it is not necessary for effective TB screening. People with the virus that causes AIDS are 40 times more likely to develop active cases of tuberculosis once they are infected with TB than those without H.I.V., and health officials say if the TB epidemic is not quickly contained by curing those who are sick, it could spread far more rapidly in the coming years. 
 New York City reported nearly 4,000 new cases of tuberculosis last year, and many of the patients had strains resistant to drug treatment. Many of those who are sick are homeless or drug addicts who rarely complete their treatments.
"This is an extremely cogent analysis of many of the issues raised by the resurgence of tuberculosis," New York City's Health Commissioner, Dr. Margaret A. Hamburg, said of the report.
 
Legal Questions
 The central recommendations and others by the panel, which include extensive workplace education and measures to protect those infected with H.I.V. from discrimination, would have enormous impact if they are adopted by the New York City Health Commissioner. And they would add millions of dollars to a deeply strained budget.
Requiring patients to have people watch them take their medicine each day -- called directly observed therapy -- would force health officials to spend millions of dollars on supervising many who may be willing to take medicine without any such supervision or incentives. And the possibility of new laws that could strengthen public health officials' ability to detain patients has fueled intense debates about the murky line between civil liberties and the right of healthy people to stay that way.
"We absolutely agree that completing therapy has to be our No. 1 priority," Dr. Hamburg said. "But the reality is that it simply makes no sense in terms of our limited resources to force everyone to have directly observed therapy. I understand the problems of civil liberties and I understand who is most likely to get sick in New York. But we cannot afford to hold off on TB control efforts and wait until a damaged society is repaired. And we won't."
Many of the report's recommendations have been strongly endorsed by the Health Department. Others, like the far-reaching recommendation on directly observed therapy, would require legislative approval. Dr. Hamburg said that because there was no precedent for such laws, it was unclear who would have to approve them.
The group that wrote the report, titled "The Tuberculosis Revival: Individual Rights and Societal Obligations in a Time of AIDS," included representatives from the city, the state, leading medical schools, law firms, New York's largest labor unions and such organizations as the Gay Men's Health Crisis. Because it is perhaps the broadest such study released since deadly new strains of TB have proliferated in many American cities and because it coincides with some plans already approved by the Department of Health in New York City, where the epidemic is by far the worst, the findings are expected to wield influence far beyond the city.
The panel, led by New York physicians and ethicists, worked for more than six months on the document, which it envisioned as a way to look beyond the medical facts of the TB epidemic to the more difficult social issues of poverty and class that the disease has again underscored. Curing new strains can require patients to swallow a dozen pills a day for up to two years, and the difficulties of treatment have made the resurgence of tuberculosis particularly hazardous in poor communities.
 
'Lessen the Stigma'
 "It is absolutely essential that we never again draw lines in health care between the haves and the have nots," said Nancy Dubler, the director of the division of bioethics at Montefiore Medical Center in the Bronx and a main author of the report. "If everyone is forced to take their medicine in the same way, it will lessen the stigma for all. And that is a sacrifice and a goal worth seeking."
Dr. Dubler said she hoped the recommendations, which were developed with extensive testimony from New York Health Department officials, would help erase the sharp divisions in the quality of health care for many urban residents. But some city health officials and advocacy organizations suggested they seemed more a wistful reminder of how bad services are than a reasonable way to change them.
"We are all dealing with huge problems and little money," said David Hansell, deputy director for government affairs at Gay Men's Health Crisis. "Why on earth would we put everyone on monitored therapy when clearly there are many who don't need it?"
The panel, however, claims it is unfair to suggest that only poor, black and Hispanic people -- who make up the great majority of TB patients -- fail to complete the treatment necessary for a cure. A goal of the panel is to create a single tier health system, and it sees the recommendation of directly observed therapy for all patients as an important step in that direction.
"We are not saying hold the epidemic hostage to our sense of social obligation," Dr. Dubler said. "What we are saying is for once let's act with fairness in a crisis."
As always, when threats to public health bump up against civil liberties imperatives, the question of fairness arises. Is it fair to lock people away from their families until they are not simply contagious but completely cured? Many community advocates say no, but public health experts are uniform in their insistence that the answer is yes. Is it fair to require H.I.V. testing of all workers who might come into contact with TB patients? Public health officials say that testing should be a personal decision, but many health-care workers think it should be mandatory.
"These are not easy issues," said Bruce C. Vladeck, president of the United Hospital Fund.
The document essentially makes a simple case: It is time to stop blaming victims of poverty for a disease that has always been primarily a result of destitution and urban crowding. It argues that without fundamental changes in the way poor people receive health care, measures like arresting those who do not take medicine will never really do any long-term good. It suggests that New York provide incentives -- including payments -- to get them to complete treatment.
 
Members of the Panel on Tuberculosis

Here is a list of the panelists who recommend broad new measures to stem the spread of tuberculosis.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 1710 words

HEADLINE: Promises Aside, States in Region Fight With One Another for Jobs

BYLINE: By STEVEN PROKESCH

 BODY:
A little more than a year after Connecticut, New Jersey, New York State and New York City signed an agreement intended to end the luring of companies from one another, the interstate war for jobs is intensifying and the so-called nonaggression pact seems close to collapse.
In recent months, New Jersey and Connecticut have fattened the packages of financial aid they offer companies that relocate there. New York State, which has had the biggest arsenal of financial incentives, has been forced to increase sharply the amount it doles out to keep companies from leaving. 
 This month, for instance, New York City and New York State had to offer $80 million in incentives to prevent the New York Mercantile Exchange from moving to Jersey City. New York officials say that without the competition from New Jersey, they would have had to pay only $50 million.
"Maybe the key players have got to sit down and look each other in the eye and say, 'Do we have a regional cooperative agreement or is it truly economic warfare?' " said Barry F. Sullivan, New York City's Deputy Mayor for Finance and Economic Development. "If we mean what we say about fostering a cooperative economic-development agenda, then we have to get together and reinforce it."
The aim of the nonaggression pact signed by the four governments in October 1991 was to prevent companies from playing one part of the region against another to obtain public subsidies like tax breaks, grants, low-cost financing and energy discounts. The hope was that these resources would instead be used to invest in things like transportation, training and marketing to make the region more competitive against other parts of the country.
But promises of cooperation have gone by the wayside as the recession-ravaged states have desperately tried to stop the plunge in jobs. Since the pact was signed, New York has lost 134,000 nonfarm jobs, New Jersey 93,000 and Connecticut 53,000.
In October 1992, the number of nonfarm jobs remaining in New York was 7,752,000; in New Jersey, 3,392,000, and in Connecticut, 1,504,000.
From the sketchy data available, New York State -- especially New York City -- is the biggest loser in the intensifying war. Rather than luring companies from New Jersey or Connecticut, New York is losing businesses to those states or having to pay ever-higher incentives to keep them.
Stanley Brezenoff, executive director of the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey and a strong advocate of regional cooperation, contends that the incentive war is hurting the entire region. "It does not create new jobs or economic activity in the region," he said. "What it does is enrich private-sector entities."
Despite such calls for cooperation, the fighting seems to be escalating. Vincent Tese, New York State's director of economic development, is threatening to retaliate against New Jersey by making a special effort to attract its companies.
Although he expressed concern about such new Connecticut subsidies as tax credits for relocating companies, he is outraged by recent New Jersey measures like a development bank that offers companies a variety of aid.
 
New Program in New Jersey
 Today, Gov. Jim Florio of New Jersey is expected to announce a new program in which the state will make investments -- and will even take partial ownership -- in privately owned buildings to enhance the ability of their owners to attract tenants.
New Jersey did just that in September when it persuaded a unit of the First Chicago Corporation to move 1,000 jobs from lower Manhattan to the empty, new Newport Tower in Jersey City. Now, Mr. Florio plans to expand the practice significantly. He is also expected to announce today that the USA cable network is moving operations with 100 employees in New York City and Long Island to Newport Tower, in a deal similar to First Chicago's.
The 1991 pact was actually fairly limited in its promises. The four merely vowed not to use negative advertising against one another and to avoid programs designed exclusively to lure companies from the others. They also promised to increase cooperation in marketing, to develop a regional development strategy and to create a committee of policymakers that would meet at least quarterly to deal with regional economic problems and to undertake initiatives.
A ban on soliciting companies within the region and reductions in subsidies used to woo companies were long-term goals.
The governments have refrained from negative advertising. But some annoyed officials are concerned that state-regulated utilities like Public Service Electric and Gas Company of New Jersey and the Brooklyn Union Gas Company have been running ads that promote their service areas and knock neighboring states.
There has also been some increased cooperation. For example, New Jersey joined New York City and New York State in a successful effort to bring the 1998 Goodwill Games to metropolitan New York.
But the four governments have yet to begin work on a regional economic-development strategy, and the committee of policy makers has yet to meet. By some accounts, turnover among senior economic officials in the region is partly to blame. Mr. Sullivan, who assumed his position on May 1, said he became aware of the pact only last month.
Barbara W. McConnell, who became New Jersey's Commissioner of Commerce and Economic Development less than a year ago, also called for a regional meeting. But with states in other regions increasing incentives, she added, "in these tough times it's a lot more difficult to sit still."
Joseph J. McGee, Commissioner of Connecticut's Department of Economic Development, agreed. "I would welcome another meeting, but let's also be realistic," he said. "We're all protecting our job base."
Connecticut and New Jersey officials insisted that it was better for the region if companies interested in leaving New York went to one of their states instead of leaving the region.
 
Differing State Strategies
 To hear officials of the three states tell it, the incentive programs are mainly intended to help companies already in their states expand.
Connecticut recently lowered its corporate tax rate, eased taxes on manufacturers and introduced tax credits for research and development. All three states have increased aid to companies for job training.
Playing catch-up to New York, Connecticut and New Jersey have expanded their ability to help companies obtain low-cost financing, which they see as vital at a time when commercial banks have been reluctant to lend.
Connecticut and New Jersey have also formed venture-capital groups to invest in companies and help them develop new products. New York State has invested heavily in collaborative research centers for universities and industry that are intended to promote the commercial use of inventions.
But what has become clear is that programs whose original purpose was not to steal jobs from a neighbor may do just that as companies play one state off the other. As the states and New York City acknowledged in 1991, the only way to avert that is for members of the region to harmonize their incentives.
While some concede that they failed to take meaningful steps down that road, they still seem skeptical that cooperation is possible. "No matter what we have in common as a region, we are not identical in our economic base, our work force and our demographics," Ms. McConnell said.
New York officials say NGersey is most responsible for the escalating economic war. They cite its efforts to xfive commodity exchanges, the headquarters of Prudential Securities Inc. and the First Chicago unit from Manhattan.
New Jersey succeeded only with First Chicago. But New York officials say New Jersey forced them to offer much more in incentives to keep other businesses.
To a lesser extent, New York officials are worried about tax credits that Connecticut is offering to entice companies. The Morgan Stanley Group, which considered moving its headquarters from Manhattan to Stamford, Conn., would have received as much as $100 million in credits over seven years to do so.
Instead, the investment bank agreed to stay in Manhattan after Mayor David N. Dinkins agreed to a four-year freeze of New York City's corporate-income-tax rate and the city and state offered a 10-year, $40 million package of incentives.
 
Attacks on a New Fund
 New York City and New York State also prevailed over New Jersey when The New York Times decided to build a printing and distribution plant in College Point, Queens, to replace its midtown Manhattan press operations. But Mr. Tese emphasized that The Times did not aggressively try to play one state against the other. "I think The Times wanted to stay in New York," he said. "What The Times was asking for was not excessive."
New York officials are especially angry about New Jersey's use of its new $224 million Economic Recovery Fund to woo New York businesses. The fund can be used to make grants, loans, investments or loan guarantees.
"In theory, its purpose is broader than stealing New York City businesses," said Carl Weisbrod, president of New York City's Economic Development Corporation and one of the city's main warriors in its battle to retain companies. "In practice, that is what it seems to be aimed at."
The fund will provide $6 million to outfit Newport Tower for First Chicago. In return, the fund will receive as much as 8 percent ownership in the building. New York officials estimated that it would have cost the city and state more than $50 million in incentives to beat the New Jersey package.
New York and New Jersey officials say they are negotiating with what they describe as several high-profile companies threatening to move operations out of New York City. New York officials fear New Jersey is using the Economic Recovery Fund to woo them.
"We are not actively going across the river, picking the pockets of the city and the state," said Ms. McConnell, pointing out that the fund mainly provides capital for small and medium-sized companies already in New Jersey and for local public construction. "But if there are companies in New York City looking to move out, I don't think the agreement called for us to sit on our hands."

HEADLINE: The Vietnamese Speak Softly Of Their Own 300,000 M.I.A.

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LANG SON, Vietnam

 BODY:
Me Thi Ha believes she is a widow. But apart from the faded, red-bordered Government certificate proclaiming her husband a "Vietnamese martyr in the struggle against America," she has no proof of his death.
Mrs. Ha worries about calling herself a widow because she still does not know how or when or where her husband died, and she says she probably never will. 
 "Of course I understand how the Americans feel," she said. "When I read in the newspapers how the Americans come here to search for the missing soldiers, I know exactly the pain of the families. We share the same grief."
 
Missing and Presumed Dead

In Vietnamese homes, the red-bordered certificates are typically displayed in the finest wooden frames the families can afford, and as household decorations they are as common here as wall calendars and family photographs. Like the families of many Vietnamese soldiers, Mrs. Ha received the certificate instead of a body that the Vietnamese Army could never find.
According to the Hanoi Government, her husband, Capt. To Van Thinh, and more than 300,000 other Vietnamese soldiers are still missing and can only be presumed dead from what many Vietnamese in the north still call the War Against America.
About a million and a half Vietnamese were killed in the war -- 185,000 South Vietnamese soldiers, 924,000 North Vietnamese and Vietcong and 415,000 civilians. About 58,000 Americans died.
More than three-quarters of the Vietnamese missing are from central or north-central provinces, although the figure of 300,000 includes Vietcong as well as tens of thousands of soldiers who served in the South Vietnamese Army.
 
Careful With Americans
 There are death certificates on display everywhere in Lang Son, a city of merchants and small farmers on Vietnam's mountainous northern border with China, about 80 miles northeast of Hanoi. Lang Son is the capital of Lang Son Province, home to nearly 2,400 soldiers listed as missing from the war.
The Vietnamese Government, which is eager to resume diplomatic and economic ties with the United States, is careful not to offend American visitors by suggesting directly that the sacrifice of Vietnamese families was greater than that of families in the United States.
But when American investigators come to Hanoi to press the Vietnamese to cooperate in the search for 2,265 Americans still listed as unaccounted for in Indochina, the Vietnamese will turn the conversation -- slowly, carefully, inevitably -- to the fact that so many more Vietnamese are also missing.
 
Few Dental Records
 Hanoi's recent announcement that it will step up cooperation in the search for missing Americans by opening up its war archives is thought likely to result in a relaxation of a 17-year-old American trade embargo on Vietnam.
Vietnam's Ministry of Labor, War Invalids and Social Affairs is responsible for trying to help Vietnamese families determine the fate of missing soldiers, but there is little it can do.
"Vietnam is a very poor country, and so the people must take responsibility for this themselves -- this must be a people's campaign," said Nghiem Xuan Tue, a military spokesman. "Even when we have a soldier's remains, we have none of the modern technology to identify them."
Dental records, among the most valuable tools the Pentagon has for identifying the remains of American troops, are usually not available for North Vietnamese soldiers, most of whom had never had a dental X-ray.
 
Families Keep Searching
 As a North Vietnamese soldier in the early 1970's, Mr. Tue fought on some of the most grisly battlefields of the south, and he said it was common for northern soldiers to be buried hurriedly in shallow dirt graves marked only by a few stones.
"In the war there was no time to bury a man properly," he said. "I buried so many myself. You would leave stones on the grave and hope to go back someday to find it." For thousands of soldiers who were hit by bombs dropped from B-52's, he said, there were no remains at all.
While the chances of finding a soldier's remains are virtually nonexistent, nearly 1,000 northern Vietnamese families apply to the Government each month for the chance to travel south to look for clues to the fate of missing relatives.
 
Proper Burial Is Essential
 Many go armed only with a one-page typewritten letter that was commonly sent to families by the army informing them of little more than the fact that a soldier had died; if a family was lucky, the letter mentioned a province where the body might have been buried.
During the war, communication from the southern battlefields was difficult and dangerous, and mail from soldiers did not routinely travel north. Many northern families were not notified for years that a soldier had died or was missing.
Tran Van Ke, a 64-year-old shipping clerk from Lang Son, waited five years before he learned that his son had died of wounds suffered in South Vietnam.
"His friends came home from the war in 1975 and told us our brave son died in 1970 and that he had been quickly buried," Mr. Ke said, reaching up to remove a small black-and-white photograph of his son, Tran Ban Tiep, from the wall of the front room of the family home. The 1968 photo showed a boyish Mr. Tiep, then 18, in full military uniform, carrying an AK-47 rifle.
"His friends cannot remember where he was buried," Mr. Ke said. "It is my great hope that we will someday discover where he is buried."
Ancestor worship dominates the spiritual life of the Vietnamese, and it is considered essential that the remains of the dead be properly buried and venerated.
"If I could afford the trip, I would go south to look for my husband," said Mrs. Ha, a retired army cook whose only income, apart from gifts from her three children, is a $5-a-month pension. "Even if I could not find him, I would like to try. I am not a religious woman, but I do know I would feel relief in my dreams if I know where my husband was buried."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Tran Ve Ke, a shipping clerk from Lang Son, Vietnam, holding a picture of his son. Mr. Ke waited for five years before he learned that his son had died of wounds suffered in South Vietnam. (Johanna Sherry for The New York Times) (pg. A6)

HEADLINE: Clinton May Carry the Campaign Into Office

BYLINE: By MICHAEL KELLY

DATELINE: SANTA BARBARA, Calif., Nov. 29

 BODY:
Running along the beach here Saturday, Bill Clinton came upon a volleyball game, a group of bronzed young Californians at play. The President-elect briefly joined in. Gawkers marveled.
"Which one is the President?" a woman asked. 
 "He's the one with the white legs," another replied.
But Mr. Clinton, so much paler than the typical beach devotee as to seem of a different species, has one great point in common with the worshipers of sun and sea in Southern California. Like the surfers in "The Endless Summer," the 1966 movie that helped define California beach culture, Mr. Clinton is chasing his own version of the perfect wave: the perfect encounter with a voter. His endless summer is an endless campaign.
 
Continuing Courtship
 When Mr. Clinton was elected, it seemed reasonable to assume that he would stop campaigning. Instead, he has done something unique for a President-elect. He has segued from the last race into the next one. The emerging style of his Presidency is the style of the endless campaign.
At its heart, the style is personal, a ceaseless, tireless effort to maintain and extend a connection between Bill Clinton and the people. The connection is intimate and emotional and far more physical than is the Presidential norm. It has already led to extraordinary scenes of the President-elect moving impulsively through barely controlled crowds, and it has clearly become a concern for the Secret Service agents assigned to protect him.
The Clinton touch with the public is an article of faith among his advisers, who liked to boast during the campaign that he almost invariably won the support of anyone he actually met. And they have high hopes that this approach will work in the long haul of the Presidency.
But others say the tactic carries risk as well. While calling what he termed the "post-inauguration extension of the campaign" a good idea in the short term for a President who will take office without a majority of voters behind him, Kevin Phillips, a Republican political analyst, warned against a prolonged reliance on public relations.
 
'Risk of Trivializing'
 "If he reaches the point where this is seen as an attempt to camouflage non-action on the economic front, it could be counterproductive," Mr. Phillips said. "He also runs the risk of trivializing his mandate and damaging the gravitas of the Presidency if he monkeys around too much."
Mr. Clinton's style as he prepares to enter the White House is the successor to the style that got him there. With the possible exception of the Rev. Jesse Jackson, no Presidential candidate of recent years campaigned so aggressively, so physically, among the public.
While George Bush mostly limited his excursions among the people to brief visits with small, well-controlled groups, Mr. Clinton reached out to as many people as possible. In a 16-hour day, he would touch and be touched by perhaps several hundred people, and would speak directly to many thousands.
 
Selling 'Retail Politics'
 Since winning office, he has continued to campaign in this manner, which political experts call "retail politics." On his daily run or exercise session at the Little Rock Downtown Y.M.C.A., he is open, for instance, to anyone who approaches him for a private word or an autograph.
On a grander scale, he has developed the habit of making an ostensibly informal, but actually carefully arranged, walk among the people once every week or so. These events, known among Mr. Clinton's aides as "meet and greets," are the heart of retail politics, and Mr. Clinton does them very well.
The third of these, following walks on Georgia Avenue, in Washington, and through a poor area of Albany, Ga., came Friday, when Mr. Clinton toured the Glendale Galleria, a gigantic mall in a suburb of Los Angeles.
The event illustrated the great political and emotional lift such visits offer as well as the danger they pose. Thirty thousand people were so tightly crammed in the mall's two floors that movement along the areas where Mr. Clinton was to walk became almost impossible.
 
No 'Prisoner of the White House'
 As Mr. Clinton made his way, talking and listening and touching, the crowd reacted with a level of excitement more usually accorded to movie stars. They screamed and surged as Mr. Clinton approached, straining against the line of obviously worried police officers and Secret Service agents, who made them wave their hands in the air to show they carried no weapons.
Mr. Clinton's top aides say he will continue this sort of thing after he assumes the Presidency.
"He does not intend to become a prisoner of the White House," said Bruce Lindsey, a senior adviser. "This is one of the ways that he maintains a sense of what's going on -- talking to real people about their real problems is a way for him to maintain a sense of what the national problems are, and whether the solutions are working."
Discussing "meet and greets" like the walk in the Glendale Galleria, Mr. Lindsey said Mr. Clinton "learns from these sort of events, by talking to people and finding out what their needs are," adding, "It's nice to read studies that tell you that this or that is what the people need, but it's better to go talk to the people directly."
Meeting large numbers of people also has an emotional benefit for Mr. Clinton, Mr. Lindsey suggested.
"For years, other politicians have told him that he can't keep working crowds so energetically, that it would exhaust him, drain him," Mr. Lindsey said. "Clearly, it doesn't drain him. It re-charges him. It energizes him."
On one level, the accuracy of Mr. Lindsey's analysis can be seen in five minutes of watching Mr. Clinton in public action. He bounds from encounter to encounter, leaning forward into conversation. He seems deeply comfortable with very intimate moments, willing to allow strangers to hug and kiss him. He beams at the evident adulation; in the Glendale Galleria he seemed as much and as happily in his natural element as a man could possibly be.
 
Political Benefit, Too
 But the psychological needs of a career politician -- and Mr. Clinton has been campaigning toward the White House for almost the whole of his adult life -- often dovetail with the political ones.
Mr. Clinton's aides do not generally cite as the model for his first term John F. Kennedy, the President their boss most obviously evokes in mannerisms of gesture and speech. Rather, they point to Franklin D. Roosevelt.
Like Mr. Roosevelt, Mr. Clinton will take office at a point of history's cusp. Like Mr. Roosevelt, he is widely admired but also widely mistrusted. Like Mr. Roosevelt, Mr. Clinton will have to deal with competing interests pressuring him in contradictory directions.
If Mr. Clinton is to emulate Mr. Roosevelt's success, his advisers say, he must build a base of support strong and deep enough to allow him to bend Congress to action and to override criticism from interest groups and the press.
Mr. Roosevelt built his base with fireside chats that bypassed the press to forge a strong, intimate bond with millions of voters. But Mr. Clinton must use television, in which pictures, not words, serve to establish emotional connection.
 
Power of Television
 What Mr. Clinton and his advisers appear to have grasped is that the power of television allows a politician who understands the camera to gain a wholesale benefit from a moment in retail politics. A hug with a weeping woman at a roadside stop in the Midwest, a few minutes of volleyball on a California beach, a walk through a poor black neighborhood in Washington -- these moments, captured on camera and played nationwide on the evening news, can serve to build a sense of kinship.
"There's a tremendous effect beyond just the community we go to on one of these visits," Mr. Lindsey said. "We find that in communities like the ones where the visit took place there is a feeling that Bill Clinton is one of them because of the visit. If he goes down and visits a town in Georgia, people see that not just in Georgia, but in places like that all across the South, and it moves them."
Mr. Clinton's approach clearly worked in the 1992 campaign and was, in a modest way, revolutionary. Going against accepted political wisdom, he ran a retail campaign in the season of wholesale politics, understanding that the broadening effect of television would make small moments large.
Whether it can work in the White House is a new matter. No one, including Mr. Clinton, can know right now if the public will still have an appetite for the endless campaign a year from now.

LENGTH: 1423 words

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Vying for Committees, Freshmen Mimic Insiders

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
After a campaign focused on fighting the status quo, it did not take long for the freshmen in Congress to start wheeling and dealing for coveted positions in the insider network.
Even before they have been sworn in, the members of the largest freshman class to enter Congress in more than 40 years are falling into the clutches of incumbency -- fighting like political pros for the committee assignments that will determine how much influence they will wield over the next two years. 
 In fact, the jockeying is more intense than ever, inflamed by the many retirements and defeats of incumbents that left scores of empty seats on Congressional committees.
"It's overachievers' hell," said Representative Barney Frank, Democrat of Massachusetts.
Representative-elect Jack Kingston, Republican of Georgia, said he was taking a "chin-first, Rocky 1" approach in his bids for a seat on one of three gold-plated committees: Appropriations, Energy and Commerce, or Ways and Means: "I just go in there and say, 'Guys, this is what I need.' You write them, you call them, you harass them. If it's politically advantageous for all those concerned, that's what they all want to know."
 
Suddenly Popular
 Others just grovel.
"I've been called at home, paged at airports, and I've never been so popular," said one of the few Democratic leaders who would discuss the subject, but only on condition that he remain unidentified.
Although the selection process has only begun, some incoming lawmakers are already expressing disgust, or at least dismay. Representative-elect Sam Coppersmith, Democrat of Arizona, said he wished the leaders would just leave it up to the new members themselves to make the committee selections.
"There would be a certain poetic justice if they just threw three juicy steaks into a room full of 65 hungry dogs and see which three dogs come out with the steaks," he said. "It would be an amazing labor-saving device."
And while the incoming members are running their fax machines on overdrive and shuttling to Washington to lobby Congressional leaders, battles have broken out among sophomore and junior members in the California, Connecticut, Massachusetts and New York delegations for assignments on the House Appropriations committee, the panel that disburses pork.
 
Patrons Are Desirable
 Because of the huge turnover -- 122 new members in the Senate and House in all -- the large number of committee vacancies (no one knows exactly how many) is making the byzantine process of obtaining desired assignments more frenzied than ever.
There is no precise formula for going about it, but finding a senior patron is of prime importance. Then junior lawmakers must flatter other members of their state delegations and sweet-talk the titans like the Speaker of the House, or at least a member of the party committee in charge of assignments.
Finally, they must beat out other junior members when the four party committees -- one for each party in the respective chambers -- take their votes around the second week of December.
Members of one assigning panel, the House Democratic Steering and Policy Committee, say some junior lawmakers are writing pandering letters to Speaker Thomas S. Foley of Washington and other leaders, reminding them of their help on past issues or pledging their fealty.
Most explain why they are qualified for one position or another, but several of the most effective letters, according to a member of the steering committee, note that they are in competitive districts and therefore need a chance to shine.
"The more independent, the more of a maverick you are, the worse off you are in this insider process," the steering committee member said.
No matter what their seniority, all lawmakers know that their re-elections could well depend on the new highway project they bring home from the Senate Appropriations Committee, the loophole for a local business they sneak into a tax bill from the House Ways and Means Committee, or even the occasional televised repartee with a controversial Supreme Court nominee from a seat on the Senate Judiciary Committee.
On the other hand, most are running away from unwanted assignments on the Committee on House Administration or the Committee on the District of Columbia, which can mean handling complaints about service at the House restaurant or delays in garbage pickups in the nation's capital.
This year, leaders are practically begging House members to fill more than 20 vacancies on the House Foreign Affairs Committee, a reflection of the fact that foreign issues are on the political back burner. Meanwhile, scores of members are throwing their names into play for the 10 or so open seats on the House Energy and Commerce Committee, one of the most powerful panels in economic affairs.
"It's like in the Army," said Representative Scott L. Klug of Wisconsin, a member of the Committee on Committees, the Republican House panel that makes committee assignments.
"The people with the best experience don't get the assignments," said Mr. Klug, a critic of the process. "It's who is better at making deals, not who knows energy and tax issues best."
 
Heeding His Instincts
 Members design strategies that fit their temperaments. Senator-elect Russell D. Feingold, Democrat of Wisconsin, said he has received numerous offers from well-placed advocates for various causes to lobby senior members on his behalf for choice assignments. "My instincts told me that's not the way to go," said Mr. Feingold, who added he could be satisfied with a more modest assignment on the Budget Committee or the Special Committee on Aging. "That's not a good first impression. Immediate gain can lead to long-term pain."
Representative-elect Blanche Lambert, Democrat of Arkansas, has taken the unconventional tack of holding back her assignment requests until the last minute to see how the demands for openings shake out. "It's a little bit like a board game," she said, "or like in bridge, you always have to know what cards have been played."
Insiders do not take Ms. Lambert lightly. At age 32, she is an experienced Capitol Hill lobbyist who began her career as a receptionist for Representative Bill Alexander, the man she beat in a primary last spring on her way to Congress.
Complicating the process is the fact that seats are assigned according to a member's state or region. When more than one member from the same state wants the same seat, seniority comes into play, as well as race, sex and ideological orientation.
Carolyn B. Maloney, a City Councilwoman in New York who defeated Representative Bill Green, Republican of Manhattan, promised to make a run for his Appropriations Committee seat. But she ran into the stone wall of four, more senior, delegation members who want the same position and the extra staff that goes with it.
The job of mediating among those four colliding egos fell to Representative Charles B. Rangel, Democrat of Harlem and the dean of the delegation. To whittle down the field, Mr. Rangel called Representative Dan Rostenkowski of Illinois, the chairman of the Ways and Means Committee, to ask if he could make room for Michael R. McNulty of Albany.
 
A Three-Way Showdown
 Mr. Rostenkowski said yes, setting up a three-way showdown -- among Representatives Floyd H. Flake of Queens, Nita M. Lowey of Westchester and Jose E. Serrano of the Bronx -- for the delegation's secret-ballot recommendation to the steering committee before Dec. 8. The three are vigorously lobbying their colleagues for at most two openings.
Mr. Serrano has enlisted the Hispanic Caucus to help him, while Ms. Lowey has beseeched several fund raisers and even rabbis to make calls on her behalf. Although Mr. Flake has the most seniority, his case is hurt by a previous rejection by the Steering Committee as well as the excess baggage of a 1990 indictment, later dismissed, accusing him of evading taxes.
Boldness is called for when a junior member is opposed by party leaders for a desired seat.
Representative Arthur Ravenel Jr., Republican from a South Carolina coastal district, threatened to change parties a few years ago if he did not get on the Merchant Marine and Fisheries Committee. A ranking Republican on the panel, Representative Don Young of Alaska, was blocking him because Mr. Ravenel opposed offshore oil drilling in Alaskan waters.
Mr. Ravenel's bluff worked. Already hopelessly in the minority, the Republicans acceded and granted the plucky South Carolinian his seat.
