HEADLINE: Chinese Premier Making a Rare Visit to Hanoi

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BEIJING, Nov. 29

 BODY:
Prime Minister Li Peng is to travel to Vietnam this week for the first such visit by a Chinese leader in more than two decades.
Despite the normalization of relations a year ago, dealings between the Communist neighbors remain so strained that they threaten the peace in the region. 
 Mr. Li is to visit Vietnam from Monday through Friday, in part to build on the resumption of normal diplomatic relations. But his visit is not expected to achieve any breakthrough on the territorial disputes along the countries' land border and in the South China Sea.
China and Vietnam fought a brief border war in 1979, and there have been periodic skirmishes since then. This year China has challenged Hanoi by taking a more assertive posture toward its territorial claims.
Sensitive to charges that they have been bullying Vietnam, Chinese officials are eager to portray the trip as a sign of friendship between the former enemies.
 
'A Very Important Visit'
 "It will be a very important visit," said Wu Jianmin, the Foreign Ministry spokesman. "There is far more common ground between China and Vietnam than there are differences and problems between them."
One of the most important topics during Mr. Li's visit will be the dispute over the Spratly and Paracel islands in the South China Sea. The islands are claimed fully or in part by six nations in the region, but China is in the best position to back its claims by force.
In May, China signed a contract with an American company to explore for oil in a region of sea that is much closer to Vietnam than to China. Then in September, a Chinese ship began drilling near the Gulf of Tonkin, in waters that Vietnam claims as its own.
Western diplomats take no position on whose territory it is, but they warn that the dispute could lead to another Chinese-Vietnamese war -- particularly if oil is found in the South China Sea.
China's trade minister, Li Lanqing, is to accompany Prime Minister Li to Hanoi, and economic ties are also expected to be on the agenda.
"We expect our trade and economic collaboration will receive a shot in the arm from the talks," Wei Xiaorong, a trade official, told the China Daily newspaper.
Since relations were formally normalized in November 1991, air links have been restored and trade has soared. In the first nine months of 1992, trade totaled $104 million, more than three times the figure in the similar period of 1991.
In fact, total trade is far higher, because many goods are smuggled by merchants across narrow mountain trails or by fishermen in the South China Sea.
Rail links were supposed to have been restored as well, but China annoyed Vietnam by building a station in a buffer zone at "Friendship Pass" along the border. In addition, China announced that a strip of several hundred yards of what used to be Vietnam was now its own territory.
Another obstacle for trade is the recent practice by the Chinese border police of seizing cargo ships plying between Hong Kong and Vietnam. At least 19 ships have been seized, and the total is probably higher since some shipowners try to avoid publicity. The ships are normally freed after an investigation, but some cargoes have been confiscated.
China says its purpose is to curb smuggling, and Hong Kong officials say there may be something to that. But the seizures have focused on ships heading for Vietnam, and some see them as official piracy -- as well as a way of showing Vietnam who is stronger.
China was a firm supporter of the Communist Government in Hanoi during the 1960's and the early part of the 1970's. According to Chinese estimates, 300,000 Chinese volunteers took part in the Vietnam War on the side of Hanoi, with 1,100 killed.
Vietnam later angered China by expelling and often severely mistreating its ethnic Chinese inhabitants. The friendship collapsed completely at the end of 1978, when Vietnam's Army invaded Cambodia to oust the regime of the Khmer Rouge, China's ally.

HEADLINE: Revamping U.S. Commerce Agency

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
The Malcolm Baldrige National Quality Award -- three rectangles of foot-high crystal mounted on an aluminum base -- sits on a coffee table in the office of Commerce Secretary Barbara H. Franklin.
But the base of the nation's highest award for corporate quality does not hold the crystal blocks tightly and it comes apart, Ms. Franklin said apologetically, adding: "It's the way it was designed." 
 Many people, some of them close to President-elect Bill Clinton, say the same thing about the Commerce Department.
The agency looks after a potpourri that includes economic statistics, unfair trade cases, weather forecasts, the census and dead dolphins. After reviewing the obstacles to making sense out of the department, the Clinton transition team now leans toward shifting the missions of its many agencies, Democrats said.
 
Old Plans on Hold
 Previous Democratic plans to take some agencies out and substitute new ones have been put on a back burner.
By installing his own people in existing agencies at the Commerce Department and elsewhere, Mr. Clinton should be able to emphasize Government assistance in the research and development of new commercial products and Government aid to exporters without fighting a political battle to reorganize the department, Democrats say. The agency may even be renamed the Department of Commerce and Technology, they add.
The heart of the department's mission will be a broad new interpretation of trade policy, Democrats say, with Government trying to help new American industries into the international marketplace and help flagging industries before they run into trouble from imports.
The leading candidate to replace Ms. Franklin and carry out this task is John A. Young, who has announced plans to retire soon as president and chief executive of the Hewlett-Packard Company. Details of the Democratic plan have not been worked out.
Under the Bush Administration, trade policy has consisted mostly of international agreements negotiated by Carla A. Hills, the United States trade representative. The Commerce Department has started several programs to help exporters by telling them where to find information and money in the Federal bureaucracy. But its main role has been the occasional imposition of steep import taxes or quotas to protect struggling American industries.
Democrats and Republicans across the political spectrum agree the agency now serves a hodgepodge of unrelated functions. The late Commerce Secretary Malcolm Baldrige, for whom the award is named, tried in vain in the early 1980's to expand his department to include the Office of Trade Representative, which is part of the White House.
Senator John Glenn of Ohio and Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the majority leader, introduced the best known and most influential Democratic plan in 1988. It would have put now-independent Federal export financing agencies into the department, created a civilian technology agency in the department to back private-sector research, transferred the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration to the Environmental Protection Agency and set up an Economic Security Advisory Board to oversee trade and international economic policy.
The department did create a Technology Administration three years ago, but its role and budget have been kept small.
 
Change in Mission Sought
 Mr. Clinton has expressed interest in reorganization and in changing the missions of existing agencies as ways to shake up the Commerce Department.
Mr. Clinton said at a news conference on Nov. 12 that too often "Presidents-elect have been in a rush to fill the Cabinet positions before they decided whether they wanted these departments to pursue a different mission than had been pursued in the past, and without considering perhaps how these departments should fit one with the other."
Actually transferring agencies faces a daunting obstacle: the enormous number of Congressional committees involved.
"To reorganize anything in this town, you have to reorganize the Hill, and the jurisdictions of the committees, and I don't think that's in the cards," Ms. Franklin said.
A few Democrats still think reorganization possible. "I don't think the jurisdiction issue really bothers members," said Representative Robert Matsui, Democrat of California and senior member of the House Ways and Means Committee's subcommittee on international trade, "because we don't see the Commerce Department as being relevant."
But large corporations are the main constituency of the Commerce Department, and they have expressed little enthusiasm over the years for reorganization.
The department's International Trade Administration alone reports to the House and Senate Commerce Committees, the House Ways and Means Committee and the Senate Finance Committee on trade issues, and the House and Senate Judiciary Committees on breaking the Arab economic boycott against Israel.
"There are very few committees or subcommittees that don't wind up involved in some aspect of Commerce," said a department official who insisted on anonymity. "No matter how you split the baby, you're going to take away jurisdiction from a powerful Congressional committee chairman."

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Clinton transition team wants to shift the missions of the Commerce Department's agencies. The current Commerce Secretary, Barbara H. Franklin, is skeptical. "To reorganize anything in this town, you have to reorganize the Hill, and the jurisdictions of the committees, and I don't think that's in the cards," she said. (Stephen Crowley/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:
The Economy
Business executives say the Clinton economic expansion may have arrived even before the President-elect moves into the White House. But they add that the pace of the rebound will be far slower than in past recoveries and that too few jobs will be created to bring unemployment down quickly. [Page A1.]
Retailers assessing the Thanksgiving shopping period have altered their outlook for the holiday selling season as a whole from "cautiously optimistic" to "pretty optimistic." An informal survey showed sales up 5 to 8 percent from last year. [D1.] 
 Individual investors are ardently buying stocks, propelling stock indexes, especially for smaller issues, to new highs. These investors are forsaking cash and some high-yielding, high-risk bonds to pour money into stock mutual funds at a record rate. [D1.]
The Government has embarked on one of the biggest land sales in recent history, selling some 2,300 square miles of acreage from the portfolios of failed savings and loans. The sale signifies that the savings and loan cleanup effort is nearing an end. [D1.]
The Clinton transition team leans toward shifting the missions of the Commerce Department's many agencies rather than taking some agencies out and substituting new ones. [D4.]
Orders for American-made machine tools declined 24.9 percent in October as the weak economies of the nation's trading partners overseas cut exports in half. [D5.]
The interstate war in which New York, New Jersey and Connecticut try to lure corporate jobs from one another is intensifying. The so-called nonaggression pact signed by the states about a year ago seems near collapse. [A1.]
 
International
The Commerce Department will announce new taxes today on some steel imported from a dozen foreign countries said to be unfairly subsidizing their domestic steelmakers. But the taxes will not cover enough of the nation's steel imports to help the American industry much, people with knowledge of the case said. [D4.]
 
Companies
Employees' rights to privacy have become an issue in Trans World Airlines' investigation of worker abuses of sick-leave policies, a source of problems for many companies. [A12.]
 
The Media Business
"The Jackie Thomas Show" will be closely watched by network executives because its star is Tom Arnold, husband of Roseanne Arnold, star of the hit series "Roseanne." [D1.]
A new business dictionary lists information like who runs the Bank for International Settlements and how the gold standard worked in the 19th century. The book costs $595. [D1.]
A meeting of newspaper executives this week with representatives of minority groups comes amid a mood of growing frustration among minority journalists over the pace of change. [D8.]
The problem of trying to find viewers on Saturday night is leading two networks in different directions. CBS believes in the traditional western, while ABC favors something new. [D8.]
Nestle awarded Publicis-FCB Communications in Paris the advertising account for Perrier. More than 30 agencies around the world will lose Perrier work. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D9.]
 
Today's Columns
A computer that processes information more like a human brain than a machine is closer to reality because of two University of Pennsylvania scientists. Edmund L. Andrews: Patents. [D2.]
With many large Japanese electronics companies in a slump, some analysts see worthwhile investments in companies less well known than Sony, Hitachi and other giants. Market Place. [D6.]
The municipal bond market continues to march ahead, but if the rally advances much further, some analysts will raise warnings of a correction. Jonathan Fuerbringer: Credit Markets. [D7.]

HEADLINE: Retailers Buoyed by First Holiday Shoppers

BYLINE: By ADAM BRYANT

 BODY:
As a group, retailers rarely describe business forecasts in terms that are not upbeat. But if any message can be gleaned from their reports on the first two days of the all-important holiday shopping season it is this: "Cautiously optimistic" has been upgraded to "pretty optimistic."
Although only a few retailers offered hard numbers yesterday, a consensus appeared to be emerging that sales were up from 5 to 8 percent over the same period last year. For example, a survey of about 10,000 retail outlets by Telecheck Services suggests that retail sales on Friday rose 5.1 percent compared with the day after Thanksgiving last year. 
 And Younkers Inc., a chain of department stores in seven Midwestern states, reported a 6 to 8 percent increase in sales on Friday and Saturday. "There's not been a big increase in traffic," said Tom Gould, chairman and chief executive, "but the customers who are out, are buying."
If such jumps are borne out when all the cash register slips from Friday and Saturday are tallied and reported, it will mean a slight improvement over most retailers' predictions of sales increases of 4 to 6 percent. That would compare with gains last year of a little more than 1 percent for the two days after Thanksgiving.
To some degree, holiday shopping gains will be easier this year, because there are two more shopping days than there were last holiday season. In 1991, Christmas fell on a Wednesday. This year, being a leap year, Christmas is on a Friday. Retailers hope that will translate into at least incremental increases for the holiday shopping season, when they typically ring up as much as 40 percent of their annual sales and half of their profits.
This year's sales will compare with depressed sales in the previous three holiday seasons. Even so, "the numbers we're looking at are really good by any standard," said Robert E. Nourse, president and chief executive of the Bombay Company of Fort Worth. "This is a good one."
As many industry observers had predicted, the West Coast states that are still heavily yoked by a sluggish economy did not share in many of the gains reported yesterday.
 
Sears Is 'Quite Pleased'
 One exception, however, was Sears, which had some of its strongest sales jumps in the Southwest and California. Matthew A. Howard, a senior vice president of marketing for Sears, attributed the gains in part to the company's exclusive sponsorship of broadcasts of the movies "Ghostbusters" and "E.T." on the Spanish-language network Telemundo on Thursday and Friday nights. For the second year, Sears was also the sole sponsor of "E.T." on Thanksgiving night on CBS.
Although Mr. Howard would not disclose details about Sears's results from Friday and Saturday, he said they exceeded expectations of increases in the mid-single digits. "It's only two days, and the next few weeks will tell us how we do," he said. "But we are quite pleased. There was excellent traffic in the stores and in the malls." That included areas in the Northeast, he said.
Popular items varied from store to store and chain to chain, but most retailers roundly trumpeted the importance of value.
Mr. Gould of Younkers said his chain had increased the use of its "V.P." promotion, an abbreviation of "value priced." Displays typically show a photograph of the item, its price and three features. Mr. Gould said the tactic had proved so effective that the company had affixed such labels to 10 times as many goods this year as it had in years past.
"The customer is looking for four components: fashion, quality, value and service," he said. "You're dealing with very savvy customers."
 
'Strong' Sales at Dayton Hudson
 For the first time, the Dayton Hudson Corporation has expanded a value-oriented "Great Gift" promotion to its Mervyn's chain of stores. With prices that range in $5 increments up to $30, the store is selling gift-wrapped goods that the company says are particularly appealing to consumers this year. For example, a product that combines an A.M.-F.M. radio, clock and flashlight sells for $10.
Dayton Hudson, the nation's fourth-largest retailer, with discount and department stores in 33 states, did not disclose statistics on sales.
But Kenneth A. Macke, the chairman and chief executive, said yesterday: "We expected strong sales for the post-Thanksiving weekend, and our sales were strong. With the exception of California, our Target stores led our companies with double-digit comparable store increases."
Tactical Retail Solutions, a retail consulting firm, has estimated that discount chains like Target and Wal-Mart will see a 6 percent increase in sales, while specialty stores and department stores will reap smaller gains of 1 to 2 percent.
The survey by Telecheck, based in Houston, indicated that the biggest increase in spending on Friday came in the Midwest, up 10.2 percent from the comparable day a year ago. The rise was 6.3 percent in the Southeast, 5.4 percent in the Southwest, 3.5 percent in the Northeast, 2.5 percent in the Middle Atlantic region, and 2.2 percent in the West.
But New York sales were down 3.3 percent and New Jersey's fell 3.7 percent. Los Angeles showed a 3.1 percent decline, and Hawaii's sales fell 4.3 percent, the company said.
"Although one day doesn't make a season, the percentage increase in national sales indicates that this season's shopping should be better than last year's," said William Ford, Telecheck's senior economic adviser. "Retailers should do considerably better than the 1.8 percent increase recorded during the 1991 holiday season and probably will post a national increase of about 6 percent."

LENGTH: 1618 words

HEADLINE: Signals of the Clinton Recovery: Rebound Is Seen, but a Slow One

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
A number of business executives say the Clinton expansion may have arrived even before the President-elect moves into the White House. But they are quick to add that the pace of the economic rebound will continue to be far slower than in past recoveries and that too few jobs will be created to bring unemployment down quickly.
In interviews over the Thanksgiving weekend, business owners and executives said they were seeing signs of a recovery -- from more classified advertising to more business travel -- in their own businesses and communities. Taken together, the various indicators suggest that the American economy is at last turning a corner. 
 "It seems that this recovery is finally under way," said George N. Hatsopoulos, chief executive of the Thermo Electron Corporation, a maker of industrial instruments in Waltham, Mass. "I believe it's going to catch on this time."
 
Heartened by New Activity
 Business people say they are heartened by a surge in consumer confidence since the Presidential election, by last week's surprisingly strong economic reports and by the crowded stores on the first days of the holiday shopping season. A consensus appeared to be emerging that holiday sales were up 5 to 8 percent so far from a year earlier. [Page D1.]
That is not to say that most businesses are not still struggling; that gains -- particularly on the job front -- will be anything but slow, or that good times are imminent. Indeed, business people stress that competition will remain as brutal as at any time in memory.
"It's tougher to make money whether you're a cab driver or an entrepreneur," said Jerry Della Femina, the New York advertising executive who created the Joe Isuzu ads in the 1980's and who started a restaurant, a real estate company and an ad agency this year. "Everybody's coming off the memories of the 1980's, when people made fortunes by just showing up. Now that's past."
Although many Republicans are quick to point out that President Bush had long predicted this recovery, several executives said the election of Gov. Bill Clinton had done the most to raise their confidence in the economy. "A lot of this is psychological," Mr. Della Femina said. "Almost all of it, in fact. We talk ourselves into recessions. This is Clinton's chance to allow us to talk ourselves into a recovery."
 
 
A Jump in Sales
 In San Francisco, Ruth Owades, chief executive of Calyx & Corolla, a fresh flower catalogue company, reported a jump in sales immediately after the election. "We did more than twice as much business this year as we did last Thanksgiving Day," she said.
But business is also clearly responding to stronger sales and rebounding profits.
Economic growth more than doubled from the glacial 1.2 percent pace of the first nine months of the recovery, which most economists reckon began in the spring of 1991, to a moderate 2.8 percent in the last three quarters. Apart from the effect of Hurricanes Andrew and Iniki, after-tax corporate profits are back to a record high.
One reason is that consumers, who account for roughly two-thirds of the gross domestic product, are making purchases again. Consumer spending rose at a strong 3.7 percent annual rate last summer and surged at an 8.4 percent annual rate in October.
Business confidence in the economy -- reflected in start-ups of businesses and in loan demand, as well as in surveys -- has been making a quiet comeback for months. At least some executives talk about adding employees, restocking inventories and spending money on equipment that would raise productivity.
Paul West, the proprietor of Paul West Heating Inc., a heating, ventilation and air-conditioning contractor in San Francisco, said business had been building since August and that he had hired two workers since then. "My telephone is ringing off the hook now," he said. "I've got too much work. There's some old customers I can't take care of."
He added: "If the phone keeps ringing after the first of the year, I'll know we are on to something. What we are seeing now could be seasonal. I've had false starts before."
In Cleveland, Sandra Pianalto, the chief operating officer of the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland, said that bankers in the area were seeing more demand for loans in recent weeks. "Bankers around here seem to be a little more optimistic," she said.
 
Better Sales at Ford
 The Ford Motor Company has seen its car sales turn up. "Sales of cars to individuals were up 8 percent in August, 15 percent in September and 30 percent in October," said Joel Pitcoff, manager of research and analysis.
Ford has been increasing its production schedules. Its North American assembly plants have been running 94 percent of straight time, compared with 78 percent of straight time last year. Straight time means two eight-hour shifts, five days a week.
Housing has been a bright spot, too. The eagerness of first-time home buyers to put deeds in their pockets, and fears that mortgage rates are likely to go up, helped push sales of existing houses in October to their highest level in four years. And building permits for new single-family houses recently picked up as well.
"We really don't have a whole lot to complain about, " said Ginny Constable, a real estate agent at Coldwell Banker Horsley & Constable, in Harrisonburg, Va. "We had almost-record months for our company four months through October. Our inventory is really down now, and we're struggling to find people properties in the first-time-buyer range."
Business is spending more money to make money. Rodger Ballou, president of the travel services group of the American Express Company, said that business travel was up.
So is advertising. Heinz Prechter, chairman of ASC Inc., an automotive supplier and owner of several small newspapers southwest of Detroit, said the regional newspaper business "has been incredibly strong, reflecting local optimism." He added, "All the advertisers are coming back, and linage is up."
 
 
Spending for Investment
 Thanks to its drive to lift productivity and cut costs, businesses have been on a capital-spending spree. Outlays on computers, telephone systems and other high-technology equipment has climbed at an 8.5 percent rate after inflation in the last three quarters. In September and October, orders for capital equipment jumped 11 percent to a record high.
In Waltham, Mr. Hatsopoulos of Thermo Electron said business had been improving for months. The equipment division, which was losing money, is again profitable. "We are going into another round of planning for next year in early January," he said. "It looks as if we're going to revise our investment plans up."
Peter Wayne, a computer consultant and physician in Yonkers, decided that the recovery had arrived when he suddenly started having trouble reordering computer equipment and parts from his usual suppliers. "They didn't answer their '800' number," he said. "They were out of parts. They had to back-order. A few weeks ago, they were shipping orders the following day."
But for him, what really clinched the idea of a recovery was that two of the half-dozen suppliers he had called said that they were hiring more help to handle the flood of orders.
Mr. West, of the San Francisco heating company, said that in order to expand, "I need new trucks and to build up capital to cover the payroll until we get paid" for a job.
"I'm still trying to save as much as possible," he said. "We are on a very strict diet. We don't take anyone out for business dinners, for example."
For all these signs of strength, few executives or economists expect the economy to keep growing at the summer's perky 3.9 percent pace, much less the 5 or 6 percent pace typical of the early phase of previous recoveries. Most economists expect the growth rate to settle at 2.5 percent or so.
 
Exports Could Fade
 Among the forces that are restraining spending: Consumers are still nervous about their debt loads and their low saving rates. State and local governments are cutting budgets. And military contractors will be slimming down for years to come.
At the same time, exports, which have been one of the economy's sources of growth in the last year and a half, may fade unless the recessions in Europe and Japan ease before too long. Machine tool orders fell 24.9 percent in October as exports dropped by half.
The key to the recovery, virtually everyone agrees, is jobs.
"The public is only going to declare the recession over when there's sustained and convincing job growth," said Roger E. Brinner, chief economist at DRI/McGraw-Hill, an economic forecasting firm in Lexington, Mass.
Although private companies have added just 300,000 jobs since last winter there are signs that the worst of the layoffs are behind and that companies are adding workers by the ones and twos.
The unemployment rate has edged down to 7.4 percent, from 7.8 percent. Initial unemployment claims have fallen in six of the last eight weeks and are now below 400,000, the dividing line between a labor market that is stagnating or contracting and one that is gradually expanding.
 
Hiring Plans Improve
 What is more, hiring plans continue to improve modestly. A survey released yesterday by Manpower Inc., a Minneapolis-based temporary-help company, shows that more companies are planning to add employees in the first quarter and fewer are anticipating staff cuts.
Still, despite the upbeat mood and the hopeful signs, business is not going to let President-elect Clinton off the hook. "Clinton promised to focus on the economy like a laser beam," said Ms. Owades of Calyx & Corolla. "That's what the country's looking for."

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:

ECONOMIC WAR BEGINS ANEW
A little more than a year after Connecticut, New Jersey, New York State and New York City signed an agreement intended to end the luring of companies from one another, the interstate war for jobs is intensifying and the so-called nonaggression pact seems close to collapse. In recent months, New Jersey and Connecticut have sweetened the packages of financial aid they offer companies that relocate there, and New York State has been forced to increase sharply the amount it doles out to keep companies from leaving. This month, for instance, New York City and New York State had to offer $80 million worth of incentives to prevent the New York Mercantile Exchange from moving across the Hudson River to Jersey City. A1.
 
NEW YORK CITY
 
SCIENTISTS URGE STRONG TB MEASURES
Wading into one of the most contentious issues in modern medicine, a panel of 34 scientists, ethicists and public health leaders is calling for broad new measures to stem the rampant spread of tuberculosis in New York City and around the nation. Two of the panel's recommendations are sure to ignite angry policy debates: that all tuberculosis patients should have to let people watch them take their medication once they leave a hospital and that new laws be passed requiring all patients to receive treatment until they are cured. A1.
 
O'CONNOR DENIES PRESSURING CLINTON
Denying published reports, John Cardinal O'Connor says that the Roman Catholic Archdiocese of New York is not trying to sway the Clinton transition team from considering Schools Chancellor Joseph A. Fernandez, with whom the Cardinal has often been at odds, as a potential Secretary of Education. The Cardinal says that he does not know of any effort by Catholics to block consideration of Mr. Fernandez, and, if it existed, that "the archdiocese would have no part of it." B3.
 
TROLLEY PLAN HITS ROADBLOCKS
A plan to have privately run trolleys shuttle riders along 42d Street is mired in a political deadlock, despite widespread support. The electric trolleys, seen as an environmentally sound way to reduce congestion along one of the New York City's showcase thoroughfares, has fallen victim to a larger battle between aides to Mayor David N. Dinkins and New York City Council leaders over who has the power to approve future transit franchises. B3.
 
MAKING MONEY BY SPECIALIZATION
Though his only business education was in the school of hard knocks, Seymour Rosenberg exemplifies one of the hottest trends in today's industrial sector: flexible manufacturing. His arcane specialty, as chairman of the New York Sewing Machine Company, is taking ordinary-looking sewing machines and refashioning them for specialty work. The idea is to tailor production exactly to a customer's needs, anticipate his emerging problems and be there as a continuing and necessary part of his business. Strictly Business, B2.
 
REGION
 
A CANTOR'S JOYS PROVE ENDURING
Lawrence Avery, who is celebrating his 40th year as cantor at Beth El synagogue in New Rochelle, is marking another milestone: his 1,000th bar or bat mitzvah student. Parents praise not only his skills as a cantor, but also the enthusiasm he brings to every task. New Rochelle Journal, B6.
 
CHASE IN NEW JERSEY
A wild car chase over 60 miles in New Jersey and New York State involving dozens of police cars ended when two Newark men in a car the police said was stolen crashed into another vehicle in North Arlington, N.J. B6.
 
PASSAIC COUNCIL VOTES TO OUST MAYOR
The Passaic City Council has voted unanimously to remove Mayor Joseph Lipari from office, one day after a Federal jury found the Mayor guilty of conspiracy and tax evasion charges. The Council instructed the City Attorney, A. William Sala, to meet with the Passaic County Prosecutor to devise the legal strategy for removing the Mayor. B6.

LENGTH: 1508 words

HEADLINE: Trying to Mend the Ties That Bind

BYLINE: By FELICIA R. LEE

 BODY:
For the last six months, Shaquesha O., her mother and her seven brothers and sisters have lived in a shelter in Brooklyn. Because of a computer error, the family's welfare checks were reduced to $12.10 every two weeks for over four months. Then Shaquesha, 13 years old, and her older sister were arrested and charged with larceny in a subway robbery.
That's when Dwight Melhado, a slight man who speaks with a Jamaican lilt, came into their lives. Carrying the slightly prim job title of "family preservationist," Mr. Melhado was assigned by a Family Court judge to work with Shaquesha and her family, arranging counseling for her and trying to solve the family's other problems, like getting the welfare checks straightened out. 
 Beyond the friendship that he offers to the struggling family in Brownsville in Brooklyn, Mr. Melhado is part of a small but growing program in New York City's Department of Juvenile Justice that many experts say represents the future of juvenile justice across the nation. Family preservationists, who are on call around the clock, are specially trained counselors who work intensively with a troubled family for up to eight weeks, helping to solve problems ranging from a lack of skills as parents to finding housing.
 
Started as Pilot Program
 Now in 30 states, family preservation programs are modeled on Homebuilders, developed 17 years ago in Washington State as an alternative to foster placement. Colorado, Alabama, Connecticut and New Jersey are among the first few states to use family preservation programs as part of their social-service system.
New York City's program was begun as a pilot effort in 1989. Currently in Brooklyn, the Bronx and Manhattan, it is to expand to Queens in January. So far, statistics seem to prove the case for the program. Of the 250 families that have gone through Family Ties, 70 percent of the children have averted placement with the State Division for Youth.
A follow-up study showed that a year later about 70 to 75 percent of those on probation were still at home after going through "intensive supervision." That means they spend more time with their probation officers and are offered more services than other juvenile probationers at less risk. What happens to these children two, three or more years later, only time and more studies will tell.
Next year, there will be four family preservationists for each New York City borough except Staten Island. Families enter the program with a judge's approval after children, like Shaquesha, are charged with crimes and the case enters Family Court. She and her family, like others in Family Ties, agreed to be interviewed only on the condition that their full names not be used.
The idea behind Family Ties is simple: with some professional intervention, children who are at high risk of being locked up because of their crimes can stay at home and be placed on probation. Detention costs the city $70,000 annually per child, compared with about $7,500 for Family Ties.
Moreover, a growing chorus of juvenile-justice experts say that confining adolescents to detention centers does little to rehabilitate them and to stem what is a growing wave of juvenile crime. Family Ties tries to help children by using resources in the community and to alter any underlying family dynamics that might contribute to future problems.
 
Avoiding the 'Treadmill'
 "Family Ties is a very good program," said Mark Soler, executive director of the Youth Law Center, a public-interest law center in San Francisco. "It's a landmark program. It's the first program in the U.S. to apply family preservation in a juvenile-justice program.
"Juvenile justice has been geared toward individual kids and behavior modification. It's not geared toward the family although the problem is in the family. This program can get kids out of the system instead of being on the treadmill they are on. There are more kids going into the juvenile-justice system and more kids being locked up, but juvenile crime is worse than ever."
Nationally, the number of boys between age 10 and 17 who were arrested for violent crimes rose 40 percent between 1987 and 1991, the Federal Bureau of Investigation's uniform crime report shows. For girls in the same age group, the rate rose 49 percent. Violent crimes were defined as homicide, forcible rape, robbery and aggravated assault.
In New York City, the number of juvenile offenders who are in secure centers on an average day has grown 81 percent in the last two years -- to 148 in fiscal 1991 from 82 in fiscal 1989. Juvenile offenders are those accused of crimes so serious that they are to be tried in adult courts rather than Family Court. The number of juveniles tried in Family Court has risen to 12,700 in 1991 from 6,700 in 1986.
"We saw parents who did not have control over their kids; we saw parents who did not have parenting skills; we saw families overwhelmed by economic pressures," said Rose W. Washington, the city's Commissioner of Juvenile Justice. "I was really tired of the fact that I was seeing children who I said that if they had someone working with them, they wouldn't be there. I vowed we would focus on the family and prevent children from coming in."
 
Budget Constraints
 Although Family Ties has won generally good reviews from those within the juvenile-justice system, its size and therefore its impact have been limited by the constraints of a tight city budget. And the city has enough money to pay for only 7 of the 12 preservationists the program calls for currently. It expects to have the money next year to pay for all 16, including 4 for Queens.
There were some concerns early in the Family Ties program that after children had gone through the program, judges were pressured to recommend them for probation instead of placement to prove the effectiveness of Family Ties.
Kathyrn McDonald, the administrative judge for New York City Family Court, said that although Family Ties was limited in scope, "I think it's a splendid concept."
"This is the future," she said. "It's cheaper, the children learn more and it's safer. The child has learned to behave within the community. If you place a child in a synthetic environment and then release them back into the community, they may not be any better in the future."
In New York City, the Human Resources Administration last year shifted $12 million from its foster-care budget to pay for 240 caseworkers to act as family preservationists for up to two families at a time. In the next few weeks city officials are expected to assess the effectiveness of that program.
 
Setting Family Goals
 The preservationists spend a great deal of time just talking to families, two-thirds of whom are already known to the city's Child Welfare Administration for various reasons. They then set family goals, often enlisting the help of others, like extended-family members, school counselors and church members, to help the family to meet them. At the end of the intervention, they make a recommendation to the judge for probation or placement.
Mr. Melhado, who became a family preservationist in 1990, said he was still in touch with the families he has helped. And Shaquesha, a lively girl who enjoys reading, is convinced that Mr. Melhado is one of the best things that has ever happened to her. He has taught her, she said, that anger is not the most constructive way to deal with her problems and also how to make decisions and choose her friends more carefully.
Shaquesha contends that she and her sister did not participate in the subway robbery but were simply in the wrong place at the wrong time. She acknowledged that she had been suspended from school many times for talking back to adults, but Mr. Melhado has got her to try talking respectfully to her teachers and guidance counselor at school.
"I've learned that if somebody tells me something that makes me feel bad, I can't jump to conclusions," she said.
 
Happy With Progress
 Mrs. O. said she initially resented the idea of a stranger intruding on the family, but is happy with Shaquesha's progress with Mr. Melhado in the last few weeks.
"At first I said I wasn't the one in trouble," she said. "I said why do we need someone coming in here? But she's more calm now."
Sometimes, parents need as much help as their child. Marysa Besse, the family preservationist working with Damien W. and his mother, observed that Ms. W. never set goals or punished her son. Damien, who is 14, was involved in a gang called Brooklyn's Most Wanted and had been charged with criminal trespassing and shoplifting. Mrs. W. complained that Damien never came home on time, but then acknowledged that she never set a curfew.
"She buys him a lot of expensive items," Ms. Besse said. "She threatens to discipline him, but never follows through."
Kathy Watson, the program coordinator for Family Ties in Manhattan, put it this way: "Kids don't exist outside families. Whenever a kid has a crisis, you can assume the family has a crisis."

HEADLINE: Tool Orders Fell 24.9% In October

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Nov. 29

 BODY:
Orders for American-made machine tools declined 24.9 percent in October as the weak economies of the nation's trading partners cut exports in half, an industry group said today.
Compared with a year earlier, October tool orders dropped 7.8 percent, according to the Association for Manufacturing Technology, which compiles the monthly report. 
 Orders for machine tools, which include metal cutting and metal forming instruments, are a barometer of industrial production and capital spending by factory owners.
"The drop-off in export orders, although disappointing, is not surprising given poor economic growth in our major export markets," said Albert Moore, the association's president.
Foreign machine tool orders will probably deteriorate further before recovering, Jean Sundrla, a private economist in Alexandria, Va., said. The higher dollar and recessions overseas are likely to hurt orders, she said.
She added, though, "We should see domestic demand increase as the economy improves, and turn things around over the next few months."
Monthly domestic orders declined 18.3 percent in October to $184.15 million, the industry group said. Monthly export orders plunged 54.4 percent to $22.95 million in October.
But year-to-date total orders were 2.3 percent ahead of 1991.

HEADLINE: Individuals Pushing Stocks Up

BYLINE: By ALLEN R. MYERSON

 BODY:
Individual investors, their confidence reviving with the economy, are ardently buying stocks, propelling stock indexes, especially for smaller issues, to new highs.
These investors are forsaking cash and some high-yielding, high-risk bonds to pour money into stock mutual funds at a record rate. 
 All told in 1992, for the first time in 30 years, individual investors will be net buyers of stocks, whether directly or through mutual funds, said Steven G. Einhorn, chief strategist at Goldman, Sachs & Company. In recent years, their sales of individual stocks have more than outweighed their purchases of mutual funds.
With individuals holding only about 22 percent of their financial assets in stocks, near a record low, Mr. Einhorn said, low yields on other investments and an accelerating economic recovery could bring about a fundamental shift. "We are in the early stages of a re-entry by individuals into the stock market that will last several years," he said.
Brokerage firms and mutual funds report a strong revival of interest.
 
One Fund Up 13% in Month
 "We've picked up a lot in equities just in the last few days," Diane M. Coffey, director of corporate communications for the Dreyfus Corporation mutual fund group, said last week. The New Leaders Fund, which buys smaller stocks, has grown 13.5 percent, to $218 million, in just one month, she said.
The election of a new President has apparently amplified the effects of an improving economy. A monthly index of investor sentiment that Fidelity Investments compiles from University of Michigan consumer surveys rose from 75.7 in October to a preliminary 88.9 in November, the sharpest gain and highest reading since the Allied success in the Persian Gulf war in early 1991.
Individual investors still have $3.5 trillion, or 35 percent of their financial assets, in money market funds and certificates of deposits, Mr. Einhorn said. Yields have fallen to virtually nothing, after inflation, and while stocks are more volatile in the short run, a well-diversified portfolio tends to outperform other common investments by several percentage points a year over the longer run.
 
One Investor's Story
 Dan Tooker, a New York City teacher who had invested in money market funds, bond funds and apartments, began planning to redeploy his sliver of that $3.5 trillion on Friday.
First the real estate crash persuaded him to invest no more in apartments. Then he watched money market rates shrivel. On Friday, as crowds filled New York's stores, he gave up shopping and went to the Dreyfus office in the lobby of what everyone still calls the Pan Am Building. There he flipped through pamphlets for mutual funds that invest in stocks, initially pleased but then increasingly distressed by the variety.
Enough investors are following through to virtually guarantee that 1992 will be a record year for stock funds. Net investments in equity funds, subtracting redemptions from sales, topped $5 billion in October, up from $4.4 billion in September, according to the Investment Company Institute's latest report.
With $58.8 billion of net cash inflows for the first 10 months of the year, and reports of a strong November, only huge, last-minute withdrawals could leave 1992 gains below the record $38.4 billion set last year.
 
Bond Funds Gain Less
 The popularity of stock funds comes as some investors are calling off their quest for high income from bonds. Bond funds, which have steadily attracted about $8 billion in net cash flow a month, gained only $2 billion in October. Junk bond funds and global income bond funds -- some of the highest yielding but riskiest categories -- suffered net cash withdrawals.
William J. Cooney, who dispenses advice at Dreyfus, says savvy investors who piled into bond funds early this year were seeking to reduce their risks in case prices suddenly plummeted as the economy picked up.
"Prices are the highest we've seen in a long time," he said. "There are only two other places you can invest: money markets or stocks."
Individual investors have already helped push several stock indexes to record highs. The Nasdaq composite index, primarily of smaller stocks, reached its third straight record Friday, closing at 649.49, up more than 15 percent over the last three months.
 
Institutional Buying Seen
 Some market analysts cite the gains among larger stocks within this index as evidence of institutional buying, but the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, which includes many more larger stocks, has gained only 4 percent in the same period. It also set a record of 430.16 on Friday.
The Dow Jones industrial average, which includes only large stocks, has risen less than 1 percent since late August, to 3,282.20, still below its high of 3,413.21 set on June 1.
Individuals tend to favor smaller stocks, which are often unable to absorb the huge amounts of money that pension funds and other large institutions move around.
"We're seeing a tremendous amount of activity in the secondaries," said Robert Dineen, the national sales manager for Merrill Lynch's western region. "Three months ago nobody wanted to go near them."
Rather than a sudden stampede, Mr. Dineen has seen investors' confidence building on what had already been a healthy year for purchases of stocks. "There's a sense of optimism we haven't seen in a while," he said.
And what of Mr. Tooker, puzzling over his choices among Dreyfus funds? He decided to take home a clutch of pamphlets, then make his selection.
"They've got 97 things in there," he said, exaggerating only somewhat.

HEADLINE: Neediest Fund Helps Victims of AIDS

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY

 BODY:
Ever since Simon Rutledge learned that he was infected with the virus that causes AIDS, he has worried that his illness would also drag down his two children, as if the mistakes he had made -- dropping out of school and stealing to support a heroin habit -- would linger after his death.
His children, Alicia and Simon Jr., did not get the virus, H.I.V., from him or their mother, who died of AIDS three years ago. But every day they saw the disease eating away at their parents. The children became withdrawn and resentful, apparently afraid of getting close to people who would soon be taken away from them. 
 In recent months, though, Alicia, 10 years old, and Simon Jr., 6, have opened up to their father because of help from the Children's Aid Society, one of seven charities aided by The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund.
"Things have turned around completely," Mr. Rutledge, 42, said the other day, referring to a home-care worker provided by the society. "Before we had a lot of tension. We were losing each other. Now we have a much better rapport with each other."
Mr. Rutledge said he had spent hours and hours talking to the children about his life, trying to break through the barriers they had erected during the years their mother was dying. He also said his health has improved because he was taking AZT, a drug that slows the onset of AIDS symptoms.
During last year's Neediest Cases campaign, Mr. Rutledge described the rift his illness caused in his family in an article in The New York Times. He said at the time that with the help of the Children's Aid Society, he was taking the first steps toward re-establishing his relationship with his children. Alicia was beginning to do better in school and Simon Jr. was starting to accept his mother's death.
Since then, the Children's Aid Society has stood by the Rutledge family, insuring that the bond Mr. Rutledge was trying to build did not crumble. The society has also paid for therapy, food and medicine.
Mr. Rutledge's story underscores how the charities assisted by The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund not only rescue the ailing in times of crisis, but also continue to provide support afterward.
The fund began its 81st campaign yesterday at a time when charities across New York City are confronting growing responsibilities. They must help New Yorkers like Mr. Rutledge in addition to people who are for the first time in their lives facing homelessness, disease and unemployment.
The Neediest Cases was born at the beginning of the century when Adolph S. Ochs, the publisher of The Times, decided that his readers would be eager to improve their city if only they knew about the misery around them. This year's campaign ends on Feb. 28. The Times pays for the fund's overhead, so all contributions are given directly to the seven charities.
The change in the Rutledge family is most apparent in Alicia. She delights in going to school, in helping to cook dinner, in playing with her brother, and, most importantly, in spending time with her father.
"When he is sick, I take care of him and I bring him his medicine," she said. "Sometimes, when he goes to sleep, I worry that he won't wake up, so I go in and check on him every night."
Mr. Rutledge, who is now working odd jobs around his Tremont neighborhood in the Bronx, has been bolstered by children's happiness.
"I haven't had too much of a good life, using drugs and all," Mr. Rutledge said. "I'm hoping that my children will get a good education and try to have the things that I didn't have. I also talk to them about being close to one another. They have to stay close. They have to understand that one of these days, Daddy's not going to be there."
 
HOW TO AID FUND
 Checks should be made payable to The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund and sent to P.O. Box 5193, General Post Office, New York, N.Y. 10087, or to any of these organizations:
BROOKLYN BUREAU OF COMMUNITY SERVICE, 285 Schermerhorn Street, Brooklyn, N.Y. 11217.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Baffled by Vector Analysis? Here's Help in 3 Volumes

BYLINE: By PETER PASSELL

 BODY:
Care to know who runs the Bank for International Settlements in Switzerland or, for that matter, what the bank really does? Perhaps you would like to discover what a martingale is (hint: not a bird). Or maybe you just want to learn, without enrolling in night school, how the gold standard worked in the 19th century.
The Macmillan Press Ltd. of London is betting $2 million and four years' effort that a lot of people will plunk down $595 to find out. They have crammed the answers -- and just about everything else anyone might want to know about matters financial -- in the three-volume, 2,621-page New Palgrave Dictionary of Money and Finance, introduced last week by the Stockton Press, Macmillan's publishing arm in New York. 
 Is there, in fact, a market for this nifty, if not thrifty, reference work? It certainly comes with an imposing imprimatur. Two of the editors, Peter Newman, formerly of Johns Hopkins University, and Murray Milgate of the University of Bradford in England, are academic heavyweights.
The third, John Eatwell of Trinity College of Cambridge, is a political mover and shaker as well as a distinguished scholar. He served as the principal economic adviser to Britain's Labor Party until the defeat of Neil Kinnock and recently became a Labor member of the House of Lords.
Moreover, a sampling of the 803 contributors who wrote the 1,008 encyclopedia-style essays reads like a roll-call of past and future Nobel laureates: Franco Modigliani on bond markets, William Baumol on economies of scale, Jacob Frenkel on fiscal policy, Steven Ross on arbitrage, Thomas Sargent on rational expectations, Joe Stiglitz on the principal-agent problem, James Tobin on money.
Nor have the editors neglected the stars of business economics. Among the luminaries in print: Alan Walters of AIG Trading, Stephen Axilrod of Nikko International, Helmut Kaiser of Deutsche Bank, Pedro-Pablo Kuczynski of First Boston International and Albert Wojnilower of First Boston Asset Management.
 
Aimed at a Broad Audience
 Some of the essays may prove very rough going for folks who never mastered college math. But Mr. Newman expects that most of the entries will be accessible to a broad audience -- "everyone from central bankers to academics to businessmen" -- because most are written in plain, jargon-resistant English.
Actually, Macmillan's investment was based on more than just a guess that sufficient numbers of readers would shell out big money for big names and big, handsome reference volumes. Macmillan has reportedly done very well as the publisher of the equally technical 20-volume New Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians, which sells for $2,300. And The New Palgrave Dictionary of Money and Finance itself has a long and glorious lineage dating to 1894.
Macmillan orginally published it as the Dictionary of Political Economy under the guidance of R. H. Inglis Palgrave, the legendary editor of The Economist magazine. It was updated a number of times through the 1920's. The idea of a comprehensive survey of economics was resurrected in 1987 as a four-volume work edited by Messrs. Eatwell, Newman and Milgate.
More than 5,000 copies of this New Palgrave Dictionary of Economics have been sold worldwide at $750 a set. Soon after publication, Macmillan went back to the three editors in search of a companion work that would appeal to a broader audience.
 
An Editorial Compromise
 "They wanted a dictionary of business," said Mr. Newman, who like his fellow editors had little taste for assembling a nuts-and-bolts guide to the corporate world. The subject of money and finance, offering wiggle room for theoretical essays as well as practical ones, was the compromise -- or, as Mr. Newman put it, "an intersection of interests."
The primary appeal of the dictionary will no doubt be the fact-packed essays on traditional and relatively timeless topics ranging from securitization to futures trading to the "capital asset pricing model." But Mr. Newman notes that in contrast to the 1987 Dictionary of Economics, some topics are as current as the savings and loan crisis and the breakup of the Soviet Union.
This is likely to add to the value of the work. The essay on German monetary unification, for example, is a remarkably lucid and compact piece of analysis that could save the reader a tedious afternoon of research.
But the decision to cover unfolding events does carry some risks: the three otherwise excellent entries on the European monetary system end the story in 1991, and thus make no mention of the system's virtual collapse this summer.
 
The Odd Tidbit
 While the dictionary is first and foremost a capitalist's tool, it is also a browser's delight. Trolling through the first volume (A-E), one discovers that the Emperor Caligula auctioned his furniture to pay his debts; that Japanese chief executives are, on average, seven years older than their American counterparts; that 32 of the necessary 34 state legislatures have called for a convention to approve a balanced-budget amendment to the United States Constitution.
And if such arcana do not clinch the sale, the editors hope a few truly daffy entries will. Where else, asks Mr. Newman, are you likely to read that "The Wonderful Wizard of Oz" by L. Frank Baum is an elaborate parable about the bimetallic currency controversy of the 1890's, with Grover Cleveland cast as the Wicked Witch of the East and William Jennings Bryan as the Cowardly Lion? (In the book, unlike the movie, Dorothy wears silver slippers as she follows the Yellow Brick Road.)
And where else are you likely to discover that the outcome of the Super Bowl predicts changes in prices in foreign stock markets as well as domestic ones, but is utterly useless in forecasting bond prices?
Oh, yes: back to those martingales. A martingale is a betting strategy in which the bettor tries to come out ahead by doubling the sum lost. But according to the essayist, Alan F. Karr of Johns Hopkins, economists have appropriated the term to mean "a mathematical model of a stochastic process that is incrementally noise." Aren't you glad you asked?

HEADLINE: America's Great S.& L. Land Sale

BYLINE: By LESLIE WAYNE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
The Government has embarked on one of the biggest land sales in recent history. At a time when real estate is still in the doldrums, the Government is selling some 2,300 square miles of land -- an amount twice the size of Rhode Island.
From land suitable only for cattle grazing to vast tracts outside fast-growing cities, the land comes from the portfolios of failed savings and loans taken over by the Government. It is the wreckage of the real estate excesses of the 1980's and, for the Government, signifies even more: that its savings and loan cleanup effort is nearing an end. 
 For the last three years, the Government has been selling office buildings, shopping centers, mortgages and homes from failed savings and loans. Now, with some $144 billion of these assets sold, the Government is turning to the bottom of its real estate barrel and selling raw land, some of the toughest real estate to sell.
 
Opening 'the Faucet'
 "We've been trickling land into the market," said Lamar C. Kelly Jr., senior vice president for asset sales at the Resolution Trust Corporation, the agency charged with the savings and loan cleanup. "Now we're going to open the faucet. Some of the land is real pretty. But, there's a lot of other parcels where the highest and best use is just to hold the earth together."
The sale of the land, valued on the Government's books at $17.5 billion, will put only a slight dent in the price of the savings and loan bailout, which has already topped $100 billion and is rising. But it will rid the Government of assets that are hard to value, costly to maintain and tricky to market.
It is hard to turn dirt in the ground into cash in the pocket. With the economy still slumping, it may be years before there is enough demand to warrant the construction of new offices or homes -- which is how developers make money when they buy raw land. In the interim, owners of land, whether it is the Government or private buyers, face bills for taxes, insurance and maintenance that each year can amount to about 10 percent of the parcel's market value.
"Land eats at your table three times a day, in property taxes, insurance expenses, and it produces no income," said Scott Shay, a managing director at Ranieri Wilson & Company, an investment firm that has bought Resolution Trust assets. "Raw land is the riskiest part of real estate. With so many empty buildings in this country, who wants to buy raw land and build more?"
 
Several Tactics Planned
 With a Congressional mandate to sell all assets as soon as possible, without waiting for real estate prices to bounce back, the agency is experimenting with a variety of selling strategies and will begin in earnest this December. It will auction some of the land. It will package portions into big tracts and sell them to sophisticated investors. It will sell small lots to individual developers. It will provide mortgages to help buyers finance the purchase.
Most important, it plans to discount the properties greatly at a time when most other owners of land, typically banks, insurance companies and real estate concerns, are sitting on their portfolios, waiting for better times.
"We're going to use every sales mechanism known to man," Mr. Kelly said. "We're willing to price it so low that we can dispose of this land, cut our carrying costs and realize whatever value there is. We're willing to recognize the true value of this land, which we estimate is around 30 percent of book value. And there's not many sellers out there willing to take a 70 percent loss."
Unlike office buildings or shopping malls, raw land lacks "cash flow," in business parlance, because it consumes cash instead of generating it. As a result, it cannot be securitized by large Wall Street firms, which have made a lucrative business of buying packages of troubled real estate assets and turning them into marketable securities.
 
Wall Street Not Interested
 Nearly 90 percent of Resolution Trust's real estate assets have been securitized, and this financial maneuver has turned some of Wall Street's biggest names, like Goldman, Sachs & Company and the GE Capital Corporation, into the agency's biggest customers.
But, these deep-pocket customers are not expected to buy land. "You can't securitize something without any cash flow and without any known value, " said Andrew Stone, an executive vice president at Daiwa Securities. "The buyers of R.T.C. real estate assets have largely been institutional bond players, and bond players aren't interested in raw land because you can't securitize it."
Moreover, there is skepticism over the desirability of the land being sold. By Resolution Trust's own admission, it is a strange lot: Everything from vast patches of desert land in the Southwest that the agency has dubbed "rock farms" to desirable lots in the middle of fast-growing Boca Raton, Fla., or scenic La Jolla, Calif.
"Their portfolio ranges from some parcels that are enticing, to others that are completely useless," Mr. Stone at Daiwa Securities said. "And that makes it hard to value. How can you figure out what an empty parcel of Arizona desert is worth? Is it 5 cents on the dollar or 10?"
The biggest single asset on the block is the Banning-Lewis Ranch, 35 square miles of unimproved land that has been annexed to the city of Colorado Springs. This parcel, which stretches 14 miles from north to south and is adjacent to the city's municipal airport, has spectacular views of Pike's Peak and is one of the last remaining big parcels of developable land in the Rocky Mountain region.
 
White Elephant Ranch
 Bought in the land boom of the 1980's by a Colorado Springs developer, the Banning-Lewis Ranch was to become a huge residential planned community. But the developer and savings and loan went through $235 million in loans and direct investments before the Colorado economy soured and demand for new houses vanished. When the developer defaulted and the savings and loan failed, the ranch reverted to Resolution Trust.
Now the ranch is a white elephant. Some developers estimate that it will take more than $100 million to develop, with an uncertain market for the houses, shopping centers and office buildings that could be put on it. "You're going to have to put a ton of dough into that property," said Jeffrey Gault, president of the Solus Property Company in Los Angeles, a leading developer. "And you have to ask whether there's the demand for that kind of development in Colorado in 1992."
As a result, the best offer so far for the Banning-Lewis ranch is $24 million -- but it is still tentative and no contract has been signed.
Some of the land in Resolution Trust's portfolio has environmental problems, like a parcel in Northern California that is home to a rare butterfly that lives for only 45 days. Developers will have to wait 10 months to determine whether the butterfly will return this year.
Or there is the uncertainty of the unknown. For instance, will toxic contaminants be found on land the Government has for sale? Or will environmentalists oppose development there? And there are political questions: Will a new local government change the existing zoning on a given parcel?
"Land is the highest form of speculative asset that we have to offer," Mr. Kelly said. "And so far we've been too conservative and it hasn't worked, so we are going to become a lot more liberal."
 
Joint Venture Fund
 One of Resolution Trust's most innovative approaches is a new $2 billion fund called the Land Fund. This is a joint venture between the Government and private investors in which the private investors will be the general partners and Resolution Trust a passive limited partner entitled to a share of any future profits. The private investor group would put up an equity investment of $100 million to $200 million and the Government puts in the land. It is similar, in structure, to many real estate partnerships.
Louis Dubin, the manager of the Land Fund at Resolution Trust, says that investors in the fund can expect a return in the range of 25 to 30 percent a year as the land is developed -- if all goes well. So far, Mr. Dubin said that more than 900 investors had called for information. "There's not a major land development company that hasn't called," he said.
But it remains to be seen whether these calls will translate into offers. "Sure, a lot of people are looking at it," Mr. Gault at Solus Property said. "But someone still has to write a check for $100 million, and that's a lot of money."
 
Avoiding Problem Parcels
 David Porter, head of corporate finance at LaSalle Partners, a real estate firm advising Resolution Trust on the fund, said the Government was screening assets to keep out problem parcels and would allow investors in the fund to "put back" any assets to Resolution Trust that later develop serious problems.
"For the Resolution Trust, this is not a gamble," Mr. Porter said. "After all, they've got the assets already. And for the investor, they are getting the assets at a low price. For them, it's like buying an option way out of the money on future development values."
For investors, the rewards could be as great as the risks, real estate developers say. "The con side for an investor is that this is all speculative," Mr. Gault said. "But the pro side is that you can buy this land really cheap and if the economy rebounds under a Clinton Administration, you could be hitting a home run out of the ball park."

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Some Performers In Japan's Slump

BYLINE: By Andrew Pollack

DATELINE: TOKYO

 BODY:
THE once mighty Japanese electronics companies have been in such a bad slump lately that one securities analyst gave a speech here last week questioning whether the industry will become the "Rust Bowl of the 90's."
Big electronics companies have been reporting huge profit drops or even losses and many have announced plans for a gradual trimming of thousands of people from their work forces. 
 But analysts say that if investors look to companies less well known than Sony, Hitachi and other giants, they will find some that seem to be defying the hard times and are worthwhile investments.
Several analysts point to Kyushu Matsushita, which is half owned by the Matsushita Electric Industrial Company, the giant consumer electronics company known for its Panasonic brand.
Kyushu Matsushita has experienced vibrant demand for its cordless phones and for a combination telephone-fax machine. When the company announced its earnings for the first fiscal half recently, it revised its forecast for the year upward and raised its dividend. Many big companies are revising forecasts downward and cutting dividends.
"I don't know anyone else that raised their dividend," said Steven W. Myers, electronics analyst for Jardine Fleming Securities in Tokyo. Since announcing the first-half results, the company's stock has risen more than 20 percent, he said. And some analysts think the stock, which closed Friday at 2,380 yen, a little over $19, can rise some more.
Chuck Goto of S. G. Warburg likes Shinko Elecric Industries, a Fujitsu affiliate that is a Japanese play on the success of the Intel Corporation, the American company that makes the microprocessor chips used in most personal computers.
Shinko makes leadframes, which are devices that connect a computer chip to a circuit board and help dissipate heat from the chip. It designed a special advanced unit that Intel uses with its 386 microprocessor.
The stock, which was traded at 2,200 yen in March, peaked at 3,460 in July but has dropped, closing Friday at 2,840 yen. That decline is on fears that Intel will not use the Shinko technology with its newer 486 chip, which is supplanting the 386 as Intel's best seller, Mr. Goto said.
But he said those fears were unfounded and the stock should rise. "I figure it can go back to the previous peak anyway when people realize that growth will come back," he said. Shinko Electric Industries, he cautioned, is not to be confused with the Shinko Electric Company, a different company, which makes heavy electrical machinery.
The biggest successes of the year are the two video game companies, Nintendo and Sega Enterprises. Nintendo's profits for this fiscal year are expected to exceed those of Matsushita, which is the world's largest consumer electronics company. Sega, which has emerged as a strong competitor to Nintendo in the last year or so, is growing at a breakneck rate despite the recession. Both stocks are well below their yearly highs, and some analysts say they are worth buying.
Most analysts still do not recommend investing in the largest computer and consumer electronics companies. The combination of weak demand in consumer electronics, the lack of new hit products and restructuring of the computer industry will continue to depress earnings for another year if not longer, they said. The only bright spot is that demand for computer memory chips, which these companies also make, appears to be picking up.
But for investors who prefer a big company, Michael Jeremy of Baring Securities thinks the safest stock is Toshiba, which does not have exposure to the ailing mainframe computer business. Toshiba also seems active in preparing for the future of computing, having formed a venture with Apple to make portable video computers and with I.B.M. to make flat computer screens.
Toshiba also has a business in heavy electrical equipment and has received some big contracts for nuclear power plant equipment. "This gives them a little bit more stability than some of the consumer electricals," Mr. Jeremy said. Toshiba's stock has been trading in a narrow range recently while many others have been falling.
Takatoshi Yamamoto, electronics analyst for Morgan Stanley & Company in Tokyo, rates the big industrial electronics companies as "holds," but agrees that Toshiba is the best of the group, which also includes Fujitsu, Hitachi, NEC and Mitsubishi Electric. Among consumer electronic companies, he thinks Matsushita might be worth a gamble because its stock price has dropped to the company's book value.
In the poor business climate, Mr. Yamamoto recommends stocks of companies that are "global winners" in their business and can be expected to prosper when good times return. He said Citizen Watch had stable earnings from watches and is expanding into personal computer manufacturing. Another winner, he said, is Canon, which is a leader in printers and color copiers.

SECTION: Section C; Page 11; Column 1; Cultural Desk; Word and Image Page
