HEADLINE: Reviewing the NC-17 Film Rating: Clear Guide or an X by a New Name?

BYLINE: By WILLIAM GRIMES

 BODY:
The NC-17 rating, a rarity since being created two years ago to replace the X rating, has been applied to five films in the last four months, raising questions about the criteria for applying it and its usefulness as a consumer guide to a film's content.
The most visible recipient of the rating is "Damage," a tale of sexual obsession directed by Louis Malle and starring Jeremy Irons and Juliette Binoche, which is to open on Dec. 23. 
 New Line Cinema, the distributor of the film, appealed to the Rating Appeals Board of the Motion Picture Association of America in New York City this month, but failed to muster the two-thirds vote needed to overturn the decision. New Line's president, Michael Lynne, says his company is now conferring with Mr. Malle to see whether the director will agree to make cuts in the film to qualify for an R rating.
Four other films have also received NC-17's, although two of those ratings were later changed to R. Those changed are "The Lover," based on the Marguerite Duras novel about a 15-year-old girl's affair with an older man, and "Body of Evidence," a courtroom drama that includes sexual scenes between two of its stars, Willem Dafoe and Madonna. Abel Ferrara's "Bad Lieutenant," a gritty morality tale starring Harvey Keitel, which opened in New York on Nov. 20, and "The Wide Sargasso Sea," based on the novel by Jean Rhys, still have their original NC-17 ratings.
Mr. Ferrara and Aries Film Releasing declined to contest the rating for "Bad Lieutenant." Likewise, Fine Line Features, the art-film division of New Line Cinema, has accepted the rating for "Wide Sargasso Sea," which includes male frontal nudity. The film is to open in April.
MGM appealed the NC-17 rating for "The Lover" and won the change to an R rating without making cuts, so it was released as an R. The company is also distributing "Body of Evidence," but decided to forego an appeal and make the necessary cuts to change the rating to an R. The film, which is to open in mid-December, is destined for a wider audience than "The Lover," and it was crucial to the studio that it be acceptable to exhibitors throughout the country.
The varied responses of the producers and distributors affected by the rating suggest the complexity and the ambiguity surrounding NC-17. The rating, which means that no one under 17 may be admitted, was created to allow film makers to turn out films for an adult audience without suffering the stigma of the X rating, which had become a synonym for pornography. Instead, some film makers and distributors say, the stigma has remained and the dividing line between an R and an NC-17 remains unclear. An R rating, for "restricted," means that children under 17 are admitted only when accompanied by an adult.
"There has to be a rating that says this is a mature film, or sophisticated film, and perhaps not for children, but certainly you shouldn't go in and expect to see an overtly sexual or an overtly violent movie: you will see thought-provoking cinema," said Paul Cohen, the president of Aries Film Releasing.
The Motion Picture Association's guidebook on the rating system states that in judging any film, the rating association considers "theme, violence, language, nudity, sensuality, drug abuse and other elements."
Jack Valenti, the chairman of the Motion Picture Association, reiterated his oft-stated position that the NC-17 rating does not make any qualitative judgment about a film or imply that it is pornographic. "This rating means what it has always meant," he said. "It says, This is a movie that is off-limits for children.
"I think most directors know when they are pushing the envelope back."
The economic consequences of an NC-17 rating can be severe. Many theaters refuse to show an NC-17 film, and many newspapers and radio and television stations will not carry advertising for one. Blockbuster Video, Kmart and Walmart, which account for more than half of the video sales in the United States, will not handle NC-17 titles.
Although studios are theoretically free to release an NC-17 film, the industry rule of thumb is that it would be restricted to about 300 theaters across the country and would probably not turn a profit with a budget of more than $3 million. For that reason, the major studios, which count on their bigger-budget films playing in a thousand or more theaters, have invariably chosen to cut films rather than face the dread rating. The only NC-17 film released by a major studio, Universal, is "Henry and June," the first film to be given the rating. It was shown in only 307 theaters and grossed a disappointing $11.6 million.
 
Of 'Baser Instincts'
 New Line had wanted a wide release on "Damage," which is based on Josephine Hart's novel about sexual obsession, but the rating has thrown a wrench in the works. "We did not expect an NC-17," Mr. Lynne said, pointing out that Mr. Malle had never received an X or NC-17 rating.
"Louis Malle does not make films that are pornography, or that pander to baser instincts," said Mr. Lynne.
His reference to "baser instincts" might have been a Freudian slip. Critics of the rating system have long argued that violence is treated more leniently than sex, and that the major studios do not suffer the same scrutiny as smaller independent companies.
"Basic Instinct," an R-rated erotic thriller that could be described as "Damage" plus ice picks and lesbianism, has become Exhibit A in this argument.
Kathie Berlin, the senior vice president for production and marketing at MGM, argued her appeal for "The Lover" with the film's director, a child psychologist and a letter of support from the editor of Seventeen magazine in hand. She also played the "Basic Instinct" card.
"I talked about 'Fatal Attraction' and 'Basic Instinct,' which showed violent lovemaking and lesbianism, and most of the lovemaking is Michael Douglas throwing someone over the back of a chair," Ms. Berlin said.
Ms. Berlin said she was told by the chairman of the ratings board, Richard D. Heffner, "Well, we made a mistake on 'Basic Instinct,' and we can't make this mistake again."
In fact, the producers of "Basic Instinct" submitted the film, or segments of it, for more than a dozen preliminary screenings before the ratings board, each time with additional cuts, to achieve the R rating. (Some gamesmanship is undoubtedly involved, since a film that reaps pre release publicity as a sizzling NC-17 and then trims down to an R gets the best of both worlds.)
 
How Ratings Are Given
 The board that applies ratings, the Classification and Rating Administration, is composed of 10 members and a director appointed by Mr. Valenti. Mr. Heffner has served for 14 years. He and Mr. Valenti appoint the raters to terms of one to several years. The only qualification demanded of the raters is that they be parents. Their names are not made public.
Films are screened before the rating administration, and, by a majority vote, a rating of G (General audiences; all ages admitted), PG (Parental guidance suggested), PG-13 (Parents strongly cautioned; some material may be inappropriate for pre-teen-agers), R or NC-17 is applied. Some film makers choose to submit a film for an unofficial screening, receive an unofficial rating and proceed to make changes before submitting the film officially.
The ratings board does not give specific reasons when a film has been judged NC-17 rather than R. Sometimes it is clear that specific scenes of violence or sex are causing the problem. Often, however, film makers are told that the NC-17 is a result of the cumulative effect of scenes, none of them enough to trigger an NC-17 on their own.
"They said 'Ms. 45' had the mood of an X, which sounds like a Duke Ellington composition to me," said Mr. Ferrara, referring to a female-revenge film he directed. "I said, 'I can't edit a mood: tell me which scenes to cut.' " Mr. Lynne said: "You don't get enough information in the process to know why the rating has been decided on."
The maker of a film that receives an NC-17 has two choices: cut or alter the objectionable scenes, or appeal the decision before the Rating Appeals Board in New York City, a 21-member body of representatives from the film industry.
In an appeal, the film is screened for the board. Then the director of the rating board makes his case for the original rating, after which the film maker and supporting witnesses argue their case. A quorum of 11 members is required to hand down a decision, and it takes a two-thirds vote to overturn a ruling by the rating board.
Mr. Cohen, of Aries, said he hoped "Bad Lieutenant," which opened strongly in New York, would focus attention on the problems inherent in the NC-17 rating.
"By labeling a film NC-17, you're associating it with pornography and ultraviolent films," he said. " 'Bad Lieutenant' may put a spotlight on that problem, and help the ratings board achieve a more accurate and articulate representation of the films they rule on, which I believe in their hearts they want to do."
 
Not for Children
 Mr. Cohen agreed that the film, which includes a graphic rape scene, masturbation, profanity and nudity, is not for children.
Ira Deutchman, the president of Fine Line Features, says he is willing to give NC-17 a chance.
"Given the difficulty of trying to be everything to a population as diverse as America's, it's a lot to ask of the M.P.A.A. to be much better than they are," Mr. Deutchman said. "They are making an honest attempt to be representative of the society, and I don't see why we can't accept that there is certain material that is suitable for adults and not for children."

SECTION: Section A; Page 14; Column 4; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 373 words

HEADLINE: How They Drove Three-Card Monte Out of the Carnival Circuit

 BODY:
To the Editor:
As a strategy consultant and adjunct professor of economics, I found your Nov. 11 article on three-card monte too pessimistic. 
 The often-advanced claim that three-card monte is a harmless game of skill between the dealer's dexterity and the bettor's powers of observation is usually a smokescreen. An honest dealer cannot object to the first five strategies suggested below, while a cheat will balk because each gets in the way of making a fast buck.
One can cheat at three-card monte by working four or more cards as three and concealing the target card in the palm or often up a sleeve.
Better known strategies to end the problem include the following:
(1) Publicize how to beat the game. First, insist the dealer keep both hands on the table after laying out the cards, or the deal doesn't count. Second, decide on the card you think is the target card, but don't touch or name it. Third, declare that the two other cards are not it and quickly turn both of them over at the same time. If the dealer hid the target card, he dare not turn over the third card, and you win. There are risks of harm from shills or the dealer, but undercover police could then move in and arrest the dealer and more aggressive shills on obvious charges.
(2) Introduce competition with honest games. One might, for example, provide the popular sites with all-weather three-card monte machines.
(3) License the dealers at very high fees and bar unlicensed dealers.
(4) Sell daily rights to locations for very high fees.
(5) Heavily tax each play.
(6) Arrest and heavily fine anyone playing three-card monte.
The police long ago found the above were effective against dishonest dealers at carnivals, fairs and exhibitions. That's why three-card monte and the shell game have been passe for 30 or 40 years. A new generation may have to pay a premium to learn from experience to respect an accomplished prestidigitator's skills as the price of failing to study now-obscure branches of entertainment history and art. That brings us to the last strategy. Redefine the situation as an educational experience, and let the gullible learn how fast they can be fleeced.
 
DAVID I. GOLDENBERG
Hackensack, N.J., Nov. 13, 1992
